CASE 01 — Acute Follicular Conjunctivitis
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 42-year-old woman with no past ocular history presents with bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and morning eyelid crusting. Symptoms have occurred intermittently for 2 years but recently worsened. She denies ocular medications and systemic illness including allergies, rosacea, or flulike symptoms.
History Narrative: A 42-year-old woman with no past ocular history presents with complaints of bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning. Although she has experienced these symptoms intermittently over the last 2 years, her symptoms have recently become worse. She denies taking any ocular medications. Her symptoms are unrelated to any systemic illness including allergy, rosacea, or flulike symptoms.
Symptom Onset: Over the last 2 years, with recent worsening.
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Worsening over recent period (symptoms recently became worse)
Characteristics: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, likely adenoviral; bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, morning eyelid crusting; mild eyelid margin erythema with scaling/crusting, trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis; mild conjunctival hyperemia with inferior papillary reaction; diminished tear lake, inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions, mild aqueous tear deficiency.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Cool compresses and unpreserved lubricants; strict hygiene (hand washing, isolating soiled tissues/washcloths/pillowcases)
Associated Symptoms: itching, burning, foreign body sensation, crusting eyelids, eyelid erythema, scaling, crusting at lash base, trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, mild conjunctival hyperemia, mild inferior papillary reaction, diminished tear lake, inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions (corneal), mild aqueous tear deficiency
Previous Episodes: Intermittent symptoms over the last 2 years
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — symptoms (itching, burning, foreign body sensation, morning crusting) cause intermittent discomfort but vision remains 20/20 and treated with supportive measures; activities minimally affected.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Contact with infected individuals / exposure to contagious conjunctivitis; poor eyelid hygiene (crusting at base of lashes) leading to blepharitis; recurrent eyelid disease (chronic blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction); possible ocular surface disease/tear deficiency (aqueous tear deficiency).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (20/20)
Visual Acuity – Method: 20/20 (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Mild erythema of the eyelid margin with scaling and crusting at the base of the eyelashes; evidence of trichiasis, madarosis, and poliosis; no ulceration of eyelid noted.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival hyperemia with mild inferior tarsal papillary reaction
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions (inferior SPK)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet (no cells or flare)
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no specific size stated)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild erythema of the eyelid margin with scaling and crusting around lash bases; trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, and eyelid ulceration; mild conjunctival hyperemia with mild inferior tarsal papillary reaction; diminished tear lake bilaterally; inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions on rose bengal staining.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, most consistent with adenoviral conjunctivitis — bilateral ocular itching, burning, foreign body sensation, morning crusting, mild eyelid margin erythema with scaling and crusting, trichiasis/madarosis/poliosis, mild conjunctival hyperemia with inferior tarsal papillary reaction, diminished tear lake, inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions on rose bengal, and mild aqueous tear deficiency on Schirmer testing. Managed with observation, cool compresses and unpreserved lubricants; patient counseled on contagiousness, hygiene, and return in 4–6 weeks or sooner for membranes, worsening corneal involvement, or persistent symptoms.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, likely adenoviral
Secondary Diagnoses: Blepharitis; Aqueous tear deficiency (mild); Meibomian gland dysfunction (inferred)
Differential Diagnosis: Adenoviral (viral) conjunctivitis; Bacterial conjunctivitis; Chlamydial (inclusion) conjunctivitis; Allergic/blepharitis-related conjunctival inflammation
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: loteprednol povidone iodine dexamethasone trifluridine vidarabine ganciclovir cidofovir 2′3′-dideoxycytidine interferon β interferon γ immunoglobulin N-chlorotaurine acyclovir valacyclovir tetracycline doxycycline erythromycin sulfisoxazole azithromycin mitomycin C
All Procedures: removal of conjunctival membranes or pseudomembranes with forceps or cotton swab placement of a self-retained amniotic membrane exam (slit-lamp examination) rose bengal dye examination Schirmer test fluorescein, lissamine green, or rose bengal corneal staining noninvasive tear osmolarity and MMP-9 point-of-care testing meibomian gland imaging (e.g., LipiView) excision/removal of conjunctival membranes to prevent symblepharon excimer laser phototherapeutic keratectomy with low-dose mitomycin C
Follow-Up Instructions: Examine in 4–6 weeks to confirm symptom improvement, follicle regression, and fading corneal opacities; earlier/more frequent visits if visually threatening corneal opacities or conjunctival membranes/pseudomembranes develop; ensure proper medication adherence.
Patient Education: The condition is likely viral and contagious; practice strict personal hygiene (frequent hand washing), avoid sharing tissues/washcloths/pillowcases, use cool compresses and preservative‑free lubricating drops for comfort, expect symptoms to persist 1–2 weeks (or longer), return sooner if membranes, worsening vision, or increased pain occur, and follow up in 4–6 weeks or sooner if symptoms worsen.
Work Restrictions: Avoid close contact with others and maintain strict hygiene; no specific work restrictions documented.


CASE 02 — Chronic Follicular Conjunctivitis
===========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: photophobia, discomfort, and headache centered around her right eye for 2 weeks
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I specifically deny any sexual contact."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 37-year-old female with 2 weeks of right-eye photophobia, discomfort, and headache; 1 week prior sudden onset right-eye redness and watering after returning from vacation in Hawaii; noted swelling in front of right ear; initially seen and treated with 2-week topical tobramycin–dexamethasone with temporary node decrease, but 2 days after stopping drops developed corneal epithelial infiltrates and persistent follicles; vision 20/25 OD, 20/20 OS; right-sided nontender preauricular node, mild right upper‑lid ptosis, conjunctival injection and moderate pretarsal follicles OD, left eye quiet.
History Narrative: A 37-year-old female office worker presented with a 2-week history of photophobia, discomfort, and headache centered around her right eye. One week into the course she developed sudden onset redness and watering of the right eye on the first day back at work after a vacation in Hawaii; she also noted swelling in front of her right ear. She had been seen by an eye‑care practitioner who found unilateral follicular conjunctivitis with a mildly tender preauricular node and was treated with a 2‑week course of topical tobramycin–dexamethasone drops. The node became slightly smaller but, two days after stopping the drops, she developed corneal epithelial infiltrates and persistent follicles. She denied sexual contact and systemic/genital symptoms; she had used hotel facilities and swimming pools during her vacation. Visual acuity was 20/25 right and 20/20 left. Examination showed a right nontender preauricular node, 1–2 mm right upper‑lid ptosis, mild bulbar conjunctival injection, moderate medium‑sized pretarsal follicles and papillae on the right (fewer follicles inferiorly in the left), and diffuse midperipheral and a few central corneal epithelial infiltrates in the right eye with no stromal involvement; left cornea and anterior segment were normal. Prior topical corticosteroid use likely altered the clinical presentation. Tests (adenovirus, HSV, chlamydia) were negative. Medication history notable for topical tobramycin–dexamethasone and later brimonidine, which was ultimately discontinued as the suspected offending agent.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks before presentation
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Improved — follicles resolved by 2 months after stopping brimonidine.
Characteristics: Unilateral chronic follicular conjunctivitis with pretarsal follicles and papillae, mild bulbar injection, right-sided (1–2 mm) ptosis, right corneal epithelial infiltrates (diffuse midperipheral and a few central), and a (initially) nontender preauricular lymph node; onset after topical tobramycin–dexamethasone and associated with brimonidine exposure (toxic etiology).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Use of topical tobramycin–dexamethasone (and prior topical corticosteroids), and brimonidine (topical glaucoma medication)
Alleviating Factors: Discontinuation of brimonidine; preservative-free lubrication (artificial tears/ointment) and cold compresses
Associated Symptoms: photophobia, discomfort, headache, redness, watering (epiphora), eyelid swelling (ptosis), preauricular lymph node enlargement
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes noted
Prior Treatments: Topical tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension drops (2-week course); brimonidine (discontinued)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — photophobia, discomfort, and headache affecting work activities; improved after stopping offending agent.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: Hawaii
Risk Factors: Recent use of topical ophthalmic medications (tobramycin–dexamethasone, brimonidine), prior topical corticosteroid use, exposure to fomites/swimming pools during travel, recent viral prodrome/possible adenoviral exposure.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (right), N5 (left)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 1- to 2-mm right upper‑lid ptosis; left lid normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild injection of the bulbar conjunctiva in the right eye; left eye quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates, no stromal involvement
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open/wide
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerosis, mild


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderate number of medium-sized pretarsal follicles and papillae in the right eye; right cornea with diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates, no stromal involvement; mild bulbar conjunctival injection; 1–2 mm right upper lid ptosis; left eye quiet with substantially fewer inferior fornix follicles; left cornea and anterior segment normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Disc appears normal (no optic nerve swelling or cupping noted)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Unilateral toxic chronic follicular conjunctivitis likely secondary to topical brimonidine use, with prior topical tobramycin–dexamethasone possibly masking corneal findings; infectious causes (adenovirus, HSV, chlamydia) were ruled out by negative testing.
Primary Diagnosis: Unilateral toxic chronic follicular conjunctivitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Nontender right preauricular lymphadenopathy; epithelial corneal infiltrates (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Adenoviral keratoconjunctivitis; Chlamydial (adult inclusion) conjunctivitis; Herpes simplex/EB virus keratoconjunctivitis; Toxic (medicamentosa) follicular conjunctivitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: punctal stenosis; subconjunctival scarring; conjunctival scarring with fornix obliteration/symblepharon; loss of conjunctival goblet cells; severe ocular surface disease (superficial punctate keratopathy, epithelial erosions, corneal ulceration/melting)


Plan
All Medications: tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension; topical tobramycin; dexamethasone; topical corticosteroids; brimonidine; preservative-free artificial tears/ointment; topical cyclosporine A 1%; topical tacrolimus ointment; topical anesthetics; trifluridine; idoxuridine; gentamicin; pilocarpine; timolol; apraclonidine; epinephrine; dipivefrin
All Procedures: Cultures and diagnostic testing of conjunctival scrapings (adenovirus, HSV, chlamydia) including direct fluorescent antibody testing and McCoy cell culture; conjunctival scrapings with Dacron swab for PCR/DFA and inoculation into tissue culture transport medium; clinical observation and follow-up examinations every 2–3 weeks.
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up every 2–3 weeks until clinical improvement is noted; monitor closely for occult infection and for signs of progressive conjunctival scarring or ocular surface disease.
Patient Education: Stop the suspected offending eye drop (brimonidine) immediately. Use preservative‑free artificial tears or ointment for comfort and apply cool compresses as needed. Avoid topical corticosteroids or other eyedrops unless directed by your eye doctor. Return for follow‑up every 2–3 weeks until improvement is seen, and sooner if symptoms worsen (increasing pain, vision loss, marked redness, pus, or corneal changes). If the original medication is still needed, switch to a preservative‑free alternative. Early removal of the offending agent typically results in resolution over weeks; contact your provider if no improvement by 2–3 weeks.


CASE 03 — Acute Bacterial Conjunctivitis
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine follow-up visit 15 months after penetrating keratoplasty; mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling.
DURATION: 15 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had a fever and swollen glands, particularly on the right side of my face, about 6 weeks ago — my doctor thought it was 'strep throat' or 'mononucleosis'."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old woman, 15 months post-right penetrating keratoplasty and anterior segment reconstruction, presents for routine follow-up with comfortable eyes but mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling. Six weeks earlier she had a febrile illness with right-sided swollen glands (internist suspected strep throat or mononucleosis); throat culture and EBV serology were negative and she was treated with oral penicillin for 14 days with symptomatic recovery except persistent preauricular lymphadenopathy. No ocular symptoms during that illness. Currently unilateral right ocular findings include visual acuity 20/60 improving to 20/40 with pinhole, IOP 28 mm Hg, mild lid puffiness and scurf, prominent follicles and papillae in inferior fornix and superior pretarsal conjunctiva, mild diffuse keratoplasty epitheliopathy; left eye essentially normal. On chronic topical ocular medications (loteprednol 0.5% BID, brimonidine 0.2% BID, timolol 0.5% gel BID, methylcellulose at bedtime). Denies other eye drop use, contact with animals, or travel; otherwise healthy. Symptoms improved significantly after starting topical trimethoprim–polymyxin B four times daily for 7 days.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old female patient presents for a routine follow-up visit 15 months after penetrating keratoplasty and anterior segment reconstruction for trauma to the right eye. Her eyes are comfortable, but she complains of mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling. She recalls that 6 weeks previously she developed a fever and swollen glands, particularly on the right side of her face, and her internist suspected “strep throat” or “mononucleosis.” She had no ocular symptoms at that time. Throat culture and Epstein–Barr virus serology were taken and were negative. She was treated with oral penicillin for 14 days, and she recovered symptomatically except for the persisting preauricular lymphadenopathy. The patient is otherwise healthy with a negative review of systems. Her past ocular history is also significant for medically treated traumatic angle recession glaucoma of her right eye. Her left eye is normal with no ocular history. Her ocular medications for the right eye were loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily, brimonidine 0.2% twice daily, timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily, and methylcellulose at bedtime. She had last been seen 3 months previously, and no ocular inflammation was seen. Her current examination reveals a visual acuity of 20/60 + with pinhole to 20/40 in the right eye. Intraocular pressures were 28 mm Hg in the right eye and 14 mm Hg in the left eye. She has a slightly tender right preauricular lymph node. External exam reveals slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left. Biomicroscopy of the right eye shows mild lid scurf and no other lesions. The conjunctiva reveals prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area. No granulomas are present. The keratoplasty has mild diffuse epitheliopathy. The anterior segment exam is otherwise noncontributory. The left eye shows mild pretarsal papillae, without follicles, and an otherwise normal exam. She denied using any eye drops other than the prescription medications already listed. She denied close contact with animals, including cats and birds, and had not traveled to potentially endemic areas for other infectious etiologies. She denied gynecologic complaints and reported being monogamous.
Symptom Onset: Slow (days to weeks)
Symptom Duration: 15 months
Progression: Acute (hours to days)
Characteristics: Red eye with conjunctival injection, chemosis, purulent or mucopurulent discharge, ocular irritation, possible eyelids stuck together in morning; onset unilateral progressing to other eye within 24–48 hours; may include conjunctival papillae and inflammatory (pseudo)membranes; typically self-limited 1–2 weeks.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Warm compresses and artificial tears; topical antibiotic drops (trimethoprim–polymyxin B)
Associated Symptoms: mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling; fever 6 weeks prior; swollen glands; eyes comfortable; mild pretarsal lid puffiness; ocular irritation; eyelids stuck together in the morning due to discharge (reported as typical)
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of conjunctivitis reported
Prior Treatments: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily; brimonidine 0.2% twice daily; timolol 0.5% gel-forming solution twice daily; methylcellulose at bedtime; prior 14-day oral penicillin (for presumed strep throat)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — eyes comfortable, routine follow-up; symptoms improved after 2 days of treatment and no major functional impairment mentioned
Systemic Symptoms: Fever and swollen glands (lymphadenopathy) 6 weeks prior; otherwise negative review of systems
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No contacts with similar symptoms reported
Risk Factors: Contact with infected individuals; recent upper respiratory infection; prior ocular surface disease or surgery (penetrating keratoplasty); chronic topical medication use with preservatives; use of topical corticosteroid; immunocompromise; neonates; close contact/poor hygiene (sharing towels); sexually transmitted infection exposure (e.g., Chlamydia, gonorrhea)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60 +
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60 + (pinholes to 20/40)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart with pinhole
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left; mild lid scurf
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area; mild diffuse epitheliopathy of the keratoplasty; no granulomas; otherwise noncontributory
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos; slight right periorbital lid puffiness (right > left), otherwise external orbit exam within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild diffuse epitheliopathy
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: open (prior penetrating keratoplasty with traumatic angle recession glaucoma)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia in the right eye; phakia in the left eye


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (no abnormal size noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: mild lid scurf, slight lid puffiness, prominent follicles and papillae in inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area, mild diffuse keratoplasty epithelial punctate epitheliopathy; anterior segment otherwise noncontributory. Left eye: mild pretarsal papillae, otherwise normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Streptococcal pneumoniae acute bacterial conjunctivitis of the right eye with inflammatory pseudomembrane formation. Unilateral conjunctival injection with papillary and follicular response, mild lid scurf, mild diffuse keratoplasty epitheliopathy, elevated IOP in the right eye (28 mm Hg). Treated with topical trimethoprim–polymyxin B q.i.d. with significant improvement after 2 days. Differential includes viral follicular conjunctivitis, chlamydial inclusion conjunctivitis, toxic drug-related follicular reaction, and masquerade syndromes; laboratory testing (viral cultures, chlamydia culture and DFA) was negative, supporting bacterial etiology. Continued topical antibiotic therapy and conservative management recommended; monitor for escalation suggesting hyperacute or gonococcal infection.
Primary Diagnosis: Streptococcal pneumoniae bacterial conjunctivitis with inflammatory pseudomembranes
Secondary Diagnoses: 1. Penetrating keratoplasty (status post, right eye) 2. Traumatic angle recession glaucoma (medically treated, right eye) 3. Ocular hypertension (IOP 28 mm Hg in right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Viral conjunctivitis (adenovirus, HSV, EBV); Chlamydial (adult inclusion) conjunctivitis; Toxic/medication-induced follicular conjunctivitis; Conjunctival lymphoma (masquerade syndrome)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Corneal involvement including keratitis and risk of corneal perforation - Spread of infection to the other eye - Transmission to contacts - Rare systemic extension in severe cases (e.g., with gonococcal infection) - Formation of inflammatory membranes or pseudomembranes - Potential vision loss if severe or untreated


Plan
All Medications: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% (eye drops) twice daily; brimonidine 0.2% twice daily; timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily; methylcellulose (eye drops) at bedtime; oral penicillin (14-day course, previously); trimethoprim sulfate–polymyxin B drops four times daily for 7 days
All Procedures: Conjunctival scrapings for pathologic examination; viral cultures (HSV and adenovirus) from conjunctival scrapings; chlamydia culture on McCoy cells and conjunctival smear for DFA staining; treatment with topical trimethoprim–polymyxin B drops (medical therapy)
Follow-Up Instructions: Return to clinic if symptoms worsen, vision decreases, copious purulent discharge, increasing pain, or no improvement after 48–72 hours.
Patient Education: - Use prescribed topical antibiotic drops (trimethoprim–polymyxin B) four times daily for 7 days as directed. - Expect symptoms to improve within a couple of days; most cases resolve in 1–2 weeks. - Use warm compresses and artificial tears for comfort. - Avoid touching eyes and do not share towels, washcloths, or pillows while symptomatic to prevent spread. - Return sooner if symptoms worsen, vision decreases, pain increases, heavy purulent discharge, or if hyperacute signs (rapid worsening, copious discharge, marked eyelid swelling) occur. - If concerns about sexually transmitted infection or persistent/recurrent symptoms, testing and partner treatment may be indicated.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate emergency care for hyperacute symptoms—copious purulent discharge, marked eyelid swelling, severe pain, rapid vision loss, or signs suggesting gonococcal infection (risk of corneal involvement); do not delay—go to ER or ophthalmology urgently.


CASE 04 — Pterygium
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in her right eye over the past 3 days
DURATION: 3 days
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "Decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in my right eye over the past 3 days."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 60-year-old woman with 3 days of decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in her right eye; eye stuck shut for 2 days requiring a warm, wet washcloth to open; denies trauma, surgery, fevers, chills, malaise, ill contacts; past medical history notable for hypertension and hysterectomy; no medication allergies; takes metoprolol; wears glasses.
History Narrative: A 60-year-old woman presents with complaints of decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in her right eye over the past 3 days. She reports that her eye has been stuck shut for the past 2 days, requiring a warm, wet washcloth to open the eye. She denies any trauma or surgery. She denies any contacts with ill individuals and does not have any fevers, chills, malaise, or any other ill symptoms. Her past medical history includes hypertension and a hysterectomy 10 years ago. She reports no allergies to medications and uses metoprolol for hypertension. She denies any family history of eye disease. The patient is healthy with a negative review of systems other than her ocular complaints. She is wearing glasses.
Symptom Onset: 3 days
Symptom Duration: 3 days
Progression: Most pterygia either grow very slowly or enter a quiescent phase; occasionally they may become actively inflamed or grow rapidly and progressively.
Characteristics: Fibrovascular overgrowth of bulbar conjunctiva, typically nasal interpalpebral fissure; may be fleshy or quiescent, can have epithelial irregularity, corneal invasion, Stocker’s iron line, induces astigmatism, prone to inflammation and recurrence.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exposure to ultraviolet light and chronic dry eye disease
Alleviating Factors: Wearing ultraviolet‑blocking sunglasses and frequent use of preservative‑free artificial tears
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision; irritation; discharge; eye stuck shut (due to crusting)
Impact on Daily Activities: Decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in right eye causing eye to be stuck shut for 2 days — likely moderate impact on daily activities (reduced vision OD 20/40–20/30; symptomatic interference).
Systemic Symptoms: No fevers, chills, malaise, or any other ill symptoms
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No
Risk Factors: - Ultraviolet light (sun) exposure - Chronic dry eye disease - Living closer to the equator (increased UV exposure) - Male sex (higher incidence) - Wind exposure


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J16/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Manifest refraction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Right conjunctiva injected and edematous; left conjunctiva normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear corneas, no scarring or inflammation.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Right: mild cataractous changes; Left: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light; no afferent pupillary defect (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: PERRL (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – RAPD: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right conjunctiva injected and edematous with mucopurulent discharge; inferior fornix covered with a fibrin–mucus pseudomembrane; inferior and superior palpebral conjunctiva with 2+ papillary reaction; corneas clear without scarring or inflammation; anterior chambers quiet; right lens with mild cataractous changes; left eye slit-lamp exam normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.4 in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Pterygium, bilateral — small, quiescent lesion(s) not currently sight‑threatening. Right eye also has acute bacterial conjunctivitis with mucopurulent discharge and an inferior fornix pseudomembrane due to Streptococcus pneumoniae; cornea clear, mild right cataract, IOPs normal. Plan: treat conjunctivitis with appropriate topical antibiotic guided by culture sensitivity, consider short course topical steroid if significant inflammation, lubricants and UV protection for pterygia, observe pterygium size/progression and consider surgical excision if progressive growth, visual axis involvement, induced astigmatism, or refractory symptoms.
Primary Diagnosis: Pterygium, bilateral
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; History of hysterectomy; Mild cataract (right eye); Bacterial conjunctivitis due to Streptococcus pneumoniae
Differential Diagnosis: Pinguecula; Pseudopterygium (conjunctival scar from trauma/chemical injury/infection); Conjunctival intraepithelial neoplasia/squamous cell carcinoma
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Persistent epithelial defects; scleral necrosis


Plan
All Medications: metoprolol preservative-free artificial tears topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory medication topical steroid (e.g., ophthalmic corticosteroid) penicillin ciprofloxacin vancomycin trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole mitomycin C topical antibiotic (postoperative) topical corticosteroid (postoperative)
All Procedures: Gram stain and conjunctival cultures on blood and chocolate agar Conjunctival mucus collection Observation with slit-lamp measurement of pterygium Topical preservative-free artificial tears Topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory medications Topical corticosteroid (short course, e.g., qid tapered over 2–3 weeks) Ultraviolet-blocking sunglasses (protective measure) Pterygium excision under local subconjunctival anesthesia (lidocaine infiltration) Removal of Tenon’s capsule Primary conjunctival anastomosis / rotational flap / conjunctival autograft Amniotic membrane transplantation Securing graft with sutures or fibrin glue Application of mitomycin C (adjunctive antimetabolite, titrated use) Sending excised tissue for histopathologic examination Postoperative topical antibiotic qid until re-epithelialization Postoperative topical corticosteroid with taper Follow-up examinations to monitor inflammation, IOP, and recurrence
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for medically managed pterygia is dictated by the severity of symptoms and the proximity to the visual axis; if topical steroids are used for inflammatory pterygia or during the postoperative period, earlier follow-up is indicated to assess response and to evaluate the intraocular pressure.
Patient Education: A pterygium is a benign, fibrovascular growth of the conjunctiva often related to UV exposure and chronic dry eye. If small and not causing symptoms it can be observed. To reduce irritation and slow progression, use preservative‑free artificial tears regularly and wear ultraviolet‑blocking sunglasses and wide‑brimmed hats outdoors. For active inflammation, a short course of topical steroid drops (typically four times daily tapered over 2–3 weeks) may be used under physician supervision; follow‑up is needed to monitor response and intraocular pressure. If the pterygium grows onto the cornea, induces astigmatism, causes vision loss, or produces unacceptable cosmetic or symptomatic problems, surgical removal can be offered but carries a risk of recurrence (commonly up to ~35–40% without adjuncts). Adjunctive measures (conjunctival autograft, amniotic membrane, or mitomycin C) can lower recurrence but have risks that should be discussed. Seek prompt care for rapid growth, atypical appearance, severe pain, or persistent redness/discharge.


CASE 05 — Recurrent Erosion/Epithelial Basement Membrane Dystrophy
==================================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old lifeguard with intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; episodes consistent with recurrent epithelial erosions; visual acuity 20/20 OU.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old lifeguard with intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; visual acuity 20/20 OU; slit‑lamp exam shows fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes.
Symptom Onset: Intermittent (recurrent) — chronic episodes beginning prior to presentation (patient is 32)
Symptom Duration: Intermittent (chronic), present for an unspecified/recurring duration
Progression: Recurrent
Characteristics: Most common corneal dystrophy; map, dot, and fingerprint (map‑dot‑fingerprint) epithelial irregularities; autosomal dominant with female predominance; often bilateral; causes recurrent corneal erosions and irregular astigmatism/haze.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eyelid traction on loosely adherent epithelium (dryness, lid rubbing/traction)
Alleviating Factors: Avoiding epithelial trauma; regular lubrication with nonpreserved artificial tears (4–8×/day) and nighttime ointment; hypertonic 5% sodium chloride drops during day and 5% ointment at night; bandage contact lens or self‑retained amniotic membrane grafts; pressure patching/antibiotic ointment and cycloplegia during acute episodes; surgical interventions (debridement, micropuncture, PTK) for recurrent cases.
Associated Symptoms: red eye, dryness, irritation, foreign body sensation
Previous Episodes: Recurrent erosions (history of spontaneous recurrent episodes)
Impact on Daily Activities: Intermittent symptoms (redness, dryness, irritation, foreign body sensation) with preserved 20/20 vision — mild to moderate impact (occasional discomfort that may interfere with work duties as a lifeguard, especially during acute erosions)
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - History of corneal trauma/injury - Epithelial basement membrane (map-dot-fingerprint) dystrophy (family history/autosomal dominant) - Prior recurrent erosions - Contact lens wear (implied risk for tight-lens complications) - Pterygium-induced corneal surface irregularity (induced astigmatism) - Dry eye/poor lubrication


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen distance vision (ETDRS)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg

Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea in both eyes (bilateral pterygium-like conjunctival fibrovascularization)
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Epithelial basement membrane dystrophy with map-dot-fingerprint changes; recurrent epithelial erosions (recurrent erosion syndrome)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open/normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear, no lens abnormality detected

Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: Pupils equal and reactive; no RAPD
Pupils – Other: Equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes; slit‑lamp reveals superficial gray‑white map and dot epithelial changes characteristic of epithelial basement membrane (map‑dot‑fingerprint) dystrophy with diffuse sloughing of epithelium as a large sheet.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome secondary to epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (map-dot-fingerprint dystrophy) — bilateral involvement with intermittent red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; visual acuity 20/20 OU; slit-lamp shows characteristic map and dot epithelial changes and diffuse epithelial sloughing consistent with recurrent erosions. Primary plan: acute management to promote epithelial healing and prevent infection (topical antibiotic ointment, pressure patch or bandage contact lens or self‑retained amniotic membrane as indicated, cycloplegia for pain), consider epithelial debridement if needed; for recurrent/recalcitrant disease discuss anterior stromal micropuncture or superficial PTK; long‑term lubrication with preservative‑free artificial tears and nocturnal ointment, and consider 5% sodium chloride drops/ointment for maintenance; follow closely for wound healing and infectious keratitis, consider corneal topography if vision affected by irregular astigmatism.
Primary Diagnosis: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome, epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (map‑dot‑fingerprint dystrophy)
Differential Diagnosis: Recurrent corneal erosion from epithelial basement membrane dystrophy; Traumatic recurrent corneal erosion; Infectious keratitis (superficial bacterial/viral); Dry eye–related epithelial breakdown
Severity: Mild to moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: delayed corneal wound healing; refractive changes; “tight lens” syndrome; secondary infectious keratitis; subepithelial scarring; recurrence of erosions


Plan
All Medications: topical antibiotic ointment cycloplegia soft bandage contact lens (not a medication) N/A self-retained amniotic membrane grafts (Prokera, AmbioDisk) N/A topical antibiotic ointment (post-debridement) cycloplegia (post-micropuncture) nonpreserved artificial tears artificial tear ointment (bedtime) 5% sodium chloride drops 5% sodium chloride ointment
All Procedures: - Epithelial debridement (manual scraping/peeling) - Bandage contact lens application - Self-retained amniotic membrane graft placement (cryopreserved Prokera or dehydrated AmbioDisk) - Pressure patching - Anterior stromal micropuncture (25‑gauge needle micropunctures) - Excimer laser phototherapeutic keratectomy (PTK)
Follow-Up Instructions: Careful follow-up until epithelial defect resolution; monitor for infectious keratitis. After healing, maintain lubrication with nonpreserved artificial tears 4–8×/day and artificial tear ointment at bedtime. Consider 5% sodium chloride drops during day and 5% sodium chloride ointment at bedtime for ≥3 months as alternative/adjunct. Obtain corneal topography if irregular astigmatism or visual disturbance suspected.
Patient Education: Educate the patient that they have epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (map‑dot‑fingerprint) which commonly causes recurrent corneal erosions—episodes of pain, tearing, redness, and foreign‑body sensation that often recur and can affect either eye. Acute care focuses on protecting the surface and preventing infection (topical antibiotic ointment, patching or bandage contact lens, possible cycloplegia for pain); clinic‑placed self‑retained amniotic membrane grafts are an option. If erosions recur despite medical therapy, procedures such as gentle epithelial debridement, anterior stromal micropuncture, or phototherapeutic keratectomy (PTK) can produce longer symptom‑free periods. After healing, maintain frequent lubrication with preservative‑free artificial tears (4–8×/day) and ointment at night; hypertonic 5% sodium chloride drops/ointment may help adherence and reduce recurrences for several months. Be vigilant for increasing pain, discharge, worsening vision, or signs of infection and seek urgent care if these occur. Consider evaluation of the fellow eye and discuss potential hereditary risk to family members.
Emergency Instructions: Careful follow-up until the epithelial defect has healed; seek immediate ophthalmic care for increasing pain, worsening redness, decreased vision, discharge, or signs of infection (possible infectious keratitis). If severe pain or vision loss occurs, go to emergency department or eye emergency clinic promptly.


CASE 06 — Fuchs’ Corneal Dystrophy
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: sudden onset of unilateral pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, and blurred vision upon awakening
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "I awoke with sudden pain, foreign body sensation, tearing, light sensitivity, and blurred vision as soon as I opened my eyes."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35‑year‑old woman with a history of multiple prior similar episodes awoke with sudden onset of unilateral pain, foreign body sensation, tearing, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening her eyes; symptoms began upon awakening with no antecedent trauma and have occurred in either eye previously.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman awoke from sleep with sudden onset of unilateral pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening her eyes. The patient had a previous history of multiple similar episodes occurring in either eye. The patient’s mother has also been affected with similar episodes. She had no history of prior ocular trauma. Visual acuity is 20/400 in the involved eye and 20/20 in the fellow eye. Slit-lamp examination of the involved eye revealed a large, discrete area of epithelial sloughing. The edges of the epithelial defect were remarkable for a “heaped-up” appearance. No infiltrate was apparent. Fluorescein dye revealed pooling over the epithelial defect. Examination of the fellow eye was remarkable for diffuse, superficial gray-white opacities in a “map” and “dot” configuration.
Symptom Onset: Upon opening her eyes while awakening (sudden upon awakening)
Progression: Slowly progressive
Characteristics: Slowly progressive endothelial dystrophy causing loss of endothelial cells and corneal guttata with stromal and epithelial edema (worse in morning) often with diurnal vision fluctuation, increased corneal thickness on pachymetry, familial occurrence, may be managed medically early (hypertonic saline, ointment, dehydration) and surgically with endothelial transplantation (DSAEK, DMEK) or PKP in advanced/scarred cases


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Morning (worse vision/diurnal vision fluctuation — symptoms worse upon awakening/morning blur)
Alleviating Factors: Using hypertonic sodium chloride (e.g., NaCl 5%) drops (especially upon awakening) and hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime; brief corneal dehydration with a hair dryer on low setting directed at the cornea at arm’s length for several minutes
Associated Symptoms: morning blur, diurnal vision fluctuation (worse vision in morning), pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, blurred vision
Previous Episodes: Multiple similar episodes occurring in either eye
Impact on Daily Activities: Morning blur from corneal edema causing worse vision on awakening; may need drops/ointments or hair-dryer measures; progressive disease can significantly affect visual function requiring transplantation — moderate to severe visual impact, especially mornings.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Mother
Risk Factors: Age (older age), female sex, positive family history/genetic predisposition (autosomal dominant tendencies), endothelial cell loss/low endothelial cell density, corneal guttae, prior ocular surgery (risk for progression), cataract (coexisting lens changes)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 82 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear without injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy with corneal stromal and epithelial edema, central guttata, increased pachymetry (thickened cornea) and signs of bullous keratopathy in advanced disease
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open, normal appearing (wide) angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: Cataract OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near (normal accommodative pupillary constriction)
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD
Pupils – Other: briskly reactive to light, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp examination: corneal guttate with or without corneal edema; in case example large discrete area of epithelial sloughing with “heaped-up” edges and pooling of fluorescein; fellow eye with diffuse superficial gray-white opacities in map‑dot‑fingerprint configuration; no infiltrate apparent.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 1. Fuchs’ corneal endothelial dystrophy OU — slowly progressive endothelial cell loss with guttata and variable stromal/epithelial edema causing diurnal visual fluctuation (worse in the morning). Corneal pachymetry likely increased; specular microscopy useful for endothelial assessment. Manage conservatively with hypertonic saline drops/ointment and ocular surface measures while monitoring progression. 2. Bilateral cataracts — lens opacities present OU; consider combined cataract extraction with endothelial intervention when vision is significantly affected. 3. Surgical considerations — when medical measures fail or visual function is significantly impaired, consider endothelial transplantation (DMEK preferred when applicable for better visual/refractive outcomes and lower rejection; DSAEK as alternative) for isolated endothelial dysfunction; consider penetrating keratoplasty if there is visually significant stromal scarring or concurrent advanced pathology. When planning endothelial procedures with cataract extraction, target slightly myopic IOL power to offset anticipated hyperopic shift (approx. +1.0–1.5 D with DSAEK). Postoperative care includes topical antibiotics and prolonged steroid taper, supine positioning after DSAEK/DMEK, and suture management/topography-guided astigmatism control after PKP.
Primary Diagnosis: Fuchs’ corneal dystrophy OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Cataract OU
Differential Diagnosis: Recurrent corneal erosion syndrome due to map‑dot‑fingerprint (epithelial basement membrane) dystrophy; Traumatic corneal abrasion; Other epithelial basement membrane dystrophies (Meesmann’s, Reis–Bücklers); Anterior stromal dystrophies causing recurrent erosion (lattice, macular, granular)
Laterality: OU
Complications: - Progressive corneal edema leading to bullous keratopathy - Decreased visual acuity (morning blur, progressive vision loss) - Corneal scarring (can limit postoperative visual outcome) - Graft rejection after transplantation - Graft failure or detachment (after DSAEK/DMEK) - Hyperopic shift after DSAEK (~1.0–1.5 D) - Accelerated cataract formation (especially after PKP)


Plan
All Medications: NaCl 5% ophthalmic solution (hypertonic sodium chloride) ointment (hypertonic sodium chloride) topical antibiotic drops topical corticosteroid drops nonpreserved artificial tears
All Procedures: Hypertonic sodium chloride drops (NaCl 5%) Hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime Use of a hair dryer to dehydrate the cornea (thermal desiccation) Descemet stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK) Descemet membrane endothelial keratoplasty (DMEK) Penetrating keratoplasty (PKP) Simultaneous cataract extraction with intraocular lens (IOL) placement Topical antibiotic drops (postoperative) Topical corticosteroid drops (postoperative) Ointments and/or nonpreserved artificial tears (ocular surface rehabilitation) Selective suture lysis and/or running suture adjustment (post-PKP astigmatism management) Rigid gas permeable contact lens fitting Astigmatic keratotomy, compression sutures, wedge resection, or repeat PKP (surgical astigmatism correction) Laser in situ keratomileusis (LASIK) (post-suture removal refractive surgery)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Postoperative topical antibiotic and corticosteroid drops as prescribed; discontinue antibiotic after re-epithelialization. - Slowly taper topical steroid over 6–12 months. - Use ointments and/or nonpreserved artificial tears as needed to rehabilitate ocular surface; minimize preserved drops. - After DSAEK/DMEK: remain supine until air bubble resolves to allow graft attachment. - After PKP: manage astigmatism with selective suture lysis/running suture adjustment guided by topography; prescribe glasses or RGP lenses when appropriate. - Return for routine postoperative visits for epithelialization, graft position, IOP, and suture management; monitor long-term for graft rejection and cataract progression.
Patient Education: Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy is a slowly progressive condition in which the cells that keep the back surface of your cornea clear gradually decline, causing corneal swelling and blurry vision — often worse in the morning. Early measures to reduce symptoms include using hypertonic saline drops (5% NaCl) when you wake and a hypertonic ointment at bedtime, and briefly directing a hair dryer on low from arm’s length toward the eyes to help evaporate surface fluid. Regular follow-up is important to monitor vision, corneal thickness, and cell status. If swelling progresses and vision is significantly affected despite these measures, surgical options to replace the diseased endothelial layer (DSAEK or DMEK) or, rarely, a full-thickness transplant (PKP) can restore clarity; these have different recovery times and risks which your surgeon will review. Also discuss cataract management if present, because combined procedures and intraocular lens power planning may be needed. If you notice suddenly worse vision, pain, or signs of infection, seek prompt care.


CASE 07 — Keratoconus
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: slowly progressive loss of vision, worse in left eye (vision worse on awakening initially)
DURATION: slowly progressive (chronic)
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have slowly lost vision, worse in my left eye than my right. It used to be worse when I woke up and cleared during the day, but now it stays poor all day."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 60-year-old woman with slowly progressive loss of vision, worse in the left than right eye; initially worse on awakening and improving during the day, but more recently persistently poor vision throughout the day.
History Narrative: A 60-year-old woman presented with a history of slowly progressive loss of vision in the left greater than the right eye. Initially, her vision was worse upon awakening and gradually cleared as the day went on; more recently, however, her vision remained poor throughout the day.
Symptom Onset: Slowly progressive; initially worse on awakening and cleared during day, more recently remained poor throughout the day
Symptom Duration: Slowly progressive (gradual) — long-standing; present for years (chronic)
Progression: progressive
Characteristics: Noninflammatory ectasia of the central cornea with thinning, irregular astigmatism, iron (Fleischer) ring, breaks in Bowman’s membrane, often bilateral and asymmetric, progressive (may be mild in this patient)


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Vision improved gradually as the day went on (worse on awakening, clears during day)
Associated Symptoms: Progressive decreased vision (worse in one eye), blurry vision that improves during day initially, monocular glare/halos, and difficulty with contact lens or spectacle correction.
Prior Treatments: Rigid gas permeable contact lenses (hard contact lenses)
Impact on Daily Activities: Impaired — significant difficulty with vision-dependent tasks in daily life (left eye CF at 4 ft; right eye 20/200 uncorrected but functional with RGP to 20/30), limiting activities without contact lens correction
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Eye rubbing; atopy/allergic eye disease; family history/genetic predisposition; young age at onset (typical); male sex (slightly higher risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Counting fingers at 4 feet
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Counting fingers at 4 feet
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white and quiet
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Keratoconus with central corneal thinning, irregular astigmatism; right eye mild keratoconus (correctable to 20/30 with RGP), left eye more advanced/poorer vision (CF) with potential progression and risk of hydrops/scarring
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and deep on exam (normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (about 3–4 mm)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; extraocular movements intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Central microcystic epithelial edema and stromal edema with folds in Descemet’s membrane; corneal guttatae and endothelial pigmentation centrally in both eyes; anterior chamber deep and quiet; iris normal; moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts bilaterally.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal through a hazy view
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal caliber and course
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Remarkable only for hazy view; posterior poles otherwise normal on dilated exam — peripheral retina not specifically described, infer no peripheral abnormalities detected (no tears/holes/rhegmatogenous pathology noted).


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Keratoconus, bilateral but asymmetric, greater in the left eye. Patient has mild disease: poor spectacle/soft contact correction but achieves 20/30 with rigid gas-permeable lenses in the involved eye; right eye managed successfully with RGP. Potential for progression in left eye; discuss collagen cross-linking to halt progression and surgical options (INTACS, DALK, PKP) if contact lens intolerance, poor fit, progressive thinning, or central scarring occur. Continue contact lens rehabilitation and periodic monitoring with topography/pachymetry; consider surgical referral if vision or contact lens tolerance worsens.
Primary Diagnosis: Keratoconus
Differential Diagnosis: Fuchs endothelial corneal dystrophy; Pseudophakic/aphakic bullous keratopathy; Corneal hydrops from keratoconus; Herpes simplex stromal keratitis
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: residual astigmatism, myopia, glaucoma, cataract, mydriasis, graft rejection


Plan
All Procedures: rigid gas permeable contact lens fitting; ultraviolet light/riboflavin corneal collagen cross-linking; intracorneal ring segment implantation (INTACS); deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty (DALK); penetrating keratoplasty (PKP)
Follow-Up Instructions: The left eye may eventually show changes consistent with keratoconus; continue rigid gas-permeable contact lens for the right eye and monitor the left eye for progression.
Patient Education: Keratoconus is a progressive thinning and bulging of the cornea that causes irregular vision. Early disease can often be managed with glasses or specialized contact lenses (rigid gas-permeable, scleral, or hybrid lenses) to improve vision. Collagen cross-linking (UV light + riboflavin) can strengthen the cornea and slow progression. If contact lenses no longer provide good vision or scarring occurs, surgical options include intracorneal ring segments (INTACS) to flatten the cornea or corneal transplantation (DALK or PKP); DALK preserves the patient’s endothelium and has fewer rejection risks. Regular follow-up with corneal measurements/topography and prompt care for changes in vision are important. If proceeding with cataract surgery, corneal thickness and endothelial health guide whether a combined endothelial procedure is needed.


CASE 08 — Microbial Keratitis
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "see clearly." "Vision in both eyes was clear as a child and young adult and until last year was correctable to 20/20." "He does not 'see clearly.'"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 19-year-old man with a 9-year history of glasses and contact lens wear presents with gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year. Despite a recent change in prescription and new contact lenses, his right eye vision remains poor. He denies trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection. He reports vision was clear and correctable to 20/20 until last year. He has seasonal allergies, is allergic to penicillin, and uses acetaminophen occasionally. He is otherwise healthy with a negative review of systems and is currently wearing contact lenses.
History Narrative: A 19-year-old man presents with complaints of gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year. He has worn glasses and contacts for 9 years, and despite a recent change in his prescription and new contact lenses, he does not “see clearly.” He denies any trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection. He reports his vision in both eyes was clear as a child and young adult and until last year was correctable to 20/20. His past medical history is significant for seasonal allergies. He reports allergies to penicillin and uses acetaminophen occasionally. He denies any family history of eye disease. The patient is healthy with a negative review of systems. He is wearing contact lenses.
Symptom Onset: gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Characteristics: central, dense, gray-white, necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with overlying epithelial defect and partial re-epithelialization on progression


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Previous contact lens wear, history of trauma, and compromised ocular surface (e.g., dry eye/ocular surface disease)
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision, pain (implied by keratitis), redness, photophobia, tearing, discharge, contact lens wear (risk factor)
Previous Episodes: Denies any trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection.
Impact on Daily Activities: Impaired — decreased vision OD (20/50 with contacts, 20/100 with manifest refraction) causing reduced spectacle-corrected acuity and need for rigid gas-permeable lenses to achieve functional vision; likely difficulty with tasks requiring clear distance vision without specialty contact wear.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative review of systems / patient is healthy (no systemic symptoms).
Risk Factors: Previous contact lens wear; history of trauma; compromised ocular surface; contact lens wear; immunocompromise implied (ocular surface disease); topical steroid use caution (implied risk); corneal scarring/hydrops/perforation risk with ectatic disorders (keratoconus associations: atopy, eye rubbing, Down’s syndrome, sleep apnea, floppy eyelids)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Manifest refraction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with overlying epithelial defect, stromal thinning and partial re-epithelialization; corneal scarring risk.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full motility (intact extraocular movements)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Lids and lashes normal. Adequate tear lake and tear breakup time. Corneas clear with no evidence of scarring or inflammation. On careful inspection of the cornea, mild central thinning, Vogt’s striae on Descemet’s membrane, and a partial Fleischer’s ring (more prominent with cobalt blue illumination). Anterior chamber quiet. Lens clear. Posterior pole normal with healthy foveal light reflex. No afferent pupillary defect.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: The cup-to-disc ratio is 0.3 bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal with a healthy foveal light reflex
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal periphery.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Streptococcal corneal ulcer of the left eye with central dense stromal infiltrate and epithelial defect consistent with bacterial keratitis. Risk factors include probable contact lens wear. Corneal integrity at risk; vision threatened. Plan: aggressive topical broad‑spectrum antibiotic therapy (fortified antibiotics ± fluoroquinolone) with frequent dosing, obtain corneal scrapings for Gram stain, culture and sensitivities, consider subconjunctival antibiotics or hospitalization if severe or noncompliant, and use adjunctive measures (cycloplegia, doxycycline, enzyme inhibitors). Avoid topical steroids until adequate antimicrobial therapy and clinical improvement confirmed; if improvement occurs, consider cautious steroid taper per SCUT trial timing. Monitor closely for response and signs of thinning or impending perforation; if perforation or medical failure occurs, use tissue adhesive or perform therapeutic penetrating keratoplasty removing all infected tissue. Follow-up frequently until resolution and then address risk factor modification (halt contact lens use, treat lid/tear dysfunction) and consider optical rehabilitation for residual scarring.
Primary Diagnosis: Streptococcal corneal ulcer, left eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Seasonal allergies; Penicillin allergy; Contact lens wear
Differential Diagnosis: Bacterial keratitis (contact lens–associated); Acanthamoeba keratitis; Fungal keratitis
Severity: Central (severe)
Laterality: Left eye
Complications: - Corneal scarring leading to decreased vision - Corneal thinning and ectasia - Corneal perforation and globe compromise - Need for penetrating keratoplasty or other surgical repair - Graft failure or compromised long-term graft survival - Persistent irregular astigmatism - Recurrence of infection


Plan
All Medications: acetaminophen penicillin aminoglycosides cephalosporins fluoroquinolones erythromycin bacitracin polymyxin chloramphenicol vancomycin tetracycline sulfonamides rifampin moxifloxacin prednisolone sodium phosphate doxycycline
All Procedures: Diagnostic stains, corneal culture and sensitivities, ultrasound pachymetry, computerized videokeratoscopy/topography (EyeSys, Pentacam), rigid gas permeable contact lens over‑refraction, slit‑lamp examination, dilation with posterior pole exam, topical fortified antibiotic therapy, topical fluoroquinolone therapy, subconjunctival antibiotic injection, intravenous antibiotics (hospitalization), topical corticosteroid drops (adjunctive), cycloplegics, doxycycline (matrix metalloproteinase inhibition), collagen shields impregnated with antibiotics, bandage contact lens, self‑retained amniotic membrane grafts, corneal glue, penetrating keratoplasty, deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty, conjunctival flap (not indicated), phototherapeutic keratectomy, contact lens rehabilitation (rigid gas permeable and scleral lenses)
Follow-Up Instructions: Once infection is successfully treated, evaluate and correct risk factors for recurrence ( discontinue or modify contact lens wear ), treat lid/lacrimal/conjunctival dysfunction as needed, and consider surgical options (rigid/scleral contact lenses, PTK, DALK/PK) for severe scarring.
Patient Education: Avoid wearing contact lenses until the infection has fully healed and follow strict contact-lens hygiene if resuming; recognize and report worsening symptoms (increased pain, redness, decreased vision, discharge, or light sensitivity) immediately. Adhere exactly to prescribed topical antibiotic regimen and return for follow-up so treatment can be adjusted based on cultures and clinical response. Do not use steroid drops unless specifically instructed by your ophthalmologist. Use prescribed cycloplegic drops for comfort if recommended, and expect possible need for additional treatments (glue, surgery, or corneal transplant) if the ulcer perforates or does not respond. Address modifiable risk factors (improve eyelid hygiene, treat dry eye or lid disease) to reduce recurrence risk.


CASE 09 — Keratoconjunctivitis Sicca—Dry Eye
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in the left eye for 48 hours
DURATION: 48 hours
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 39-year-old woman with history of contact lens wear presenting with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and purulent discharge in the left eye. Vision initially 20/20 OD and CF 3′ OS; left cornea with central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with epithelial loss, indistinct edges, 2+ AC cell and flare and 1-mm hypopyon. Subsequent course: day 4 vision 20/400 OS with corneal opacity showing defined borders and re-epithelialization; day 11 continued stromal opacity with re-epithelialization; 1 month later vision improved to 20/50 OS with central corneal stromal scar.
History Narrative: A 39-year-old woman with a history of contact lens wear presented with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and purulent discharge in the left eye. Visual acuity was 20/20 OD and CF 3′ OS initially; right eye unremarkable. Left external exam: mild lid and conjunctival edema, papillary conjunctival reaction, purulent discharge. Cornea: central, dense, gray-white, necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with epithelial loss; indistinct edges extending beyond stromal opacity. Anterior chamber: 2+ cell and flare with 1-mm hypopyon. Iris, lens, and retina unremarkable. Cultures ultimately grew a Streptococcus species. Follow-up at 4 days: VA 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS; left eye with minimal lid/conjunctival edema, scant purulent discharge; cornea showed condensing gray-white opacity with defined borders and epithelial healing at edges. At 11 days: VA 20/20 OD, 20/400 OS; further condensation of stromal opacity with continued re-epithelialization. At 1 month: VA 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS with a central corneal stromal opacity. Past ocular history notable for contact lens wear; systemic history notable for diabetes mellitus contributing to neurotrophic keratopathy in diagnosis.
Symptom Onset: 48 hours
Symptom Duration: 48 hours
Progression: Improved
Characteristics: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca due to aqueous tear deficiency (neurotrophic keratopathy from diabetes) with delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Contact lens wear (including overnight wear), reduced blink rate, floppy eyelids, diabetes mellitus–induced neurotrophic keratopathy
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, discharge
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — decreased vision OS (CF to 20/50 over month), pain, photophobia, redness, discharge and tear/ocular surface dysfunction likely impaired reading, driving, and contact lens use.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Contact lens wear
Risk Factors: Contact lens wear; overnight lens wear (implied); contact lens hygiene issues; ocular surface disease; lacrimal dysfunction; lid dysfunction (floppy eyelids, reduced blink rate); diabetes mellitus (neurotrophic keratopathy).

Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus; Floppy eyelids (lid laxity)

Follow-Up Instructions: Return for follow-up as directed; monitor vision and corneal healing with repeat examinations (including visual acuity and corneal exam) at regular intervals (e.g., 4 days, 11 days, 1 month) and sooner if worsening symptoms (increased pain, decreased vision, increased redness, or discharge).
Patient Education: Use preservative-free artificial tears frequently; apply gels or ointment at bedtime. Practice lid hygiene and warm compresses to improve meibomian gland function. Blink frequently and consider protective eyewear to reduce evaporation. Avoid eye rubbing and ensure proper contact lens hygiene (or discontinue lenses until healed). For persistent or severe symptoms, follow-up for possible punctal plugs, prescription anti-inflammatory drops (topical cyclosporine or short course topical steroid as directed), tear-stimulating pills, or specialty therapies (autologous serum drops, scleral/contact lens, LipiFlow). Return promptly for worsening vision, increased pain, or eye redness.


CASE 10 — Postsurgical Corneal Edema
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: ocular irritation in both eyes
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have burning, foreign body sensation, a sandy-gritty feeling, and redness, more in my right eye. These symptoms are worse in the morning and later in the day and make me unable to read or drive comfortably. I still have preserved emotional lacrimation. I sleep on my stomach, preferring my right side."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 56-year-old man with diabetes mellitus referred for a second opinion for ocular irritation in both eyes after cataract surgery, complaining of burning, foreign body sensation, sandy‑gritty feeling, redness (worse in right eye), symptoms worse in morning and later in day impairing reading and driving, reduced blink rate, preserved emotional lacrimation, sleeps on right side, reduced ocular sensitivity, tear breakup time <2s, superficial punctate and rose bengal staining, low tear meniscus, delayed fluorescein clearance, and decreased tear secretion; postop poor visual outcome in operated eye due to corneal edema with preexisting low endothelial cell density and final BCVA 20/20 after treatment.
History Narrative: A 56-year-old man with diabetes mellitus presented with bilateral ocular irritation and a referral for a second opinion for presumed unstable ocular surface/dry eye in the right eye. He complained of burning, foreign body sensation, sandy-gritty feeling, and redness, worse in the right eye, worse in the morning and late in the day, interfering with reading and driving. He had preserved emotional lacrimation and reported sleeping on his stomach, preferring his right side. Blink rate was reduced bilaterally. He had no external signs of rosacea, no palpable preauricular nodes, normal lid position and globe relationship, and loose/floppy lid tension (2+ right, 1+ left). Meibomian secretions appeared clear and easily expressible. Tear meniscus height was low in upper and lower lids both eyes. Tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae were diffusely injected with a mixed papillary and follicular response. Ocular sensitivity was markedly reduced by Cochet–Bonnet esthesiometry. Tear breakup time was <2 seconds OU. Fluorescein showed superficial punctate staining and rose bengal staining positive over the exposure zone, more prominent OD. Intraocular pressures were 15 mmHg OD and 16 mmHg OS. Fluorescein clearance test showed marked delayed dye clearance and decreased tear secretion in both eyes. Lens and fundus were unremarkable OU. Specular microscopy of the fellow eye showed low endothelial cell density without guttata; the operated eye had unexpected immediate postoperative corneal edema attributed to preexisting low endothelial density of undetermined etiology. He was treated with topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline four times daily for one week, with complete resolution of corneal edema and final best-corrected visual acuity of 20/20.
Progression: Resolved


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Sleeping on his right side (prone/stomach sleeping) leading to exposure/more symptoms on that side, reduced blink rate, low tear meniscus/poor tear film (dry eye)
Alleviating Factors: Resolution of corneal edema after topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline four times daily for 1 week, resulting in final BCVA 20/20
Associated Symptoms: burning, foreign body sensation, sandy‑gritty feeling, redness, worse in morning and later in day, decreased reading/driving comfort, reduced ocular sensitivity, photophobia (implied by difficulty driving/reading)
Prior Treatments: topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline solution four times a day for 1 week
Impact on Daily Activities: Unable to determine
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: preexisting low endothelial cell density; Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy (corneal guttata); complicated surgery (direct endothelial trauma, Descemet’s membrane detachment, prolonged phacoemulsification/ultrasound exposure); history of acute IOP rise (e.g., acute angle-closure); diabetes (endothelial abnormality); toxic intraocular/operative agents; excessive postoperative inflammation; postoperative IOP elevation; long-term use of topical ophthalmic medications with preservatives


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Both tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae were diffusely injected
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No palpable preauricular nodes
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Postsurgical corneal edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Unremarkable


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (intact)
