Slit Lamp Exam Description: Meibomian secretion clear and easily expressible; low tear meniscus height in upper and lower lids OU; tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae diffusely injected with mixed papillary and follicular response; reduced ocular sensitivity; tear breakup time <2 sec OU; fluorescein shows superficial punctate staining and rose bengal staining positive over exposure zone (greater OD); lid position normal; lid tension loose/floppy (2+ OD, 1+ OS); lens and fundus unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal optic nerve, no abnormalities detected
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 56-year-old man status post cataract extraction with immediate postoperative decreased vision due to corneal edema. Exam and specular microscopy of the fellow eye revealed low endothelial cell density without guttata, indicating preexisting endothelial dysfunction (likely non‑Fuchs, possibly related to diabetes). Differential included surgical endothelial trauma, toxicity, inflammation, and elevated IOP, but clinical course and fellow‑eye findings support low preoperative endothelial reserve as the cause. Treated medically with topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline qid; edema resolved and final BCVA returned to 20/20. Surgical intervention deferred given resolution.
Primary Diagnosis: Postsurgical corneal edema
Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Preexisting endothelial disease/dysfunction (e.g., Fuchs’ dystrophy, low endothelial cell density); surgical trauma to endothelium or Descemet’s detachment; toxic endothelial injury (intracameral agents, contaminants); postoperative IOP elevation causing endothelial decompensation
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: wound leak; flat anterior chamber; iris incarceration in the wound; IOP (intraocular pressure elevation); primary endothelial failure; endophthalmitis; epithelial defect; down-growth


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1%; topical corticosteroids; topical hyperosmotic 5% sodium chloride (solution or ointment); topical ophthalmic aqueous suppressants; topical antiglaucoma medications; systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; subconjunctival corticosteroid injection; preservative-free epinephrine (recommended); preservative-free lidocaine (recommended); antibiotics (topical or subconjunctival)
All Procedures: Topical corticosteroid therapy (prednisolone acetate 1% qid for 1 week) and topical hypertonic saline solution 5% qid for 1 week
Follow-Up Instructions: The patient was managed with topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline solution four times a day for 1 week; follow up as needed to confirm resolution and final visual acuity (edema resolved and final BCVA 20/20).
Patient Education: - Corneal edema after cataract surgery is often temporary and can improve with time and treatment. - Risk factors include low endothelial cell density, Fuchs’ dystrophy, complicated surgery, diabetes, and certain medications or intraoperative toxic exposures. - Treatments aim to reduce inflammation, lower intraocular pressure if elevated, and draw fluid out of the cornea: frequent topical corticosteroids (eg, prednisolone acetate 1%), topical hypertonic saline (5% sodium chloride drops or ointment), and glaucoma medications or oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors when needed. - Additional supportive measures: a therapeutic soft (hydrophilic) contact lens can improve comfort and vision in mild edema. - If edema persists despite medical therapy for 2–3 months, surgical options include endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK or DMEK) or, less commonly now, penetrating keratoplasty. - Report immediately: worsening vision, new severe pain, light sensitivity, increased redness, or sudden eye discharge. - Follow-up schedule: frequent visits initially to monitor inflammation, intraocular pressure, and corneal clarity; adhere to prescribed drops and return as instructed.


CASE 11 — Dellen
================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: right eye
PATIENT QUOTES: "decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year" "persistent blurred vision without major discomfort"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old man presented with a 1-year history of decreased vision (worse at night) in both eyes; past ocular history unremarkable. He had diabetes mellitus for 10 years, well controlled on oral medications. Examination showed bilateral moderate nuclear sclerosis (left worse), best-corrected VA 20/50 OD and 20/100 OS, and cataract surgery was recommended for the left eye. Postoperatively after uncomplicated left phacoemulsification with temporal clear corneal incision and PCIOL, on postoperative day 1 his vision was 20/100 with IOP 18 mm Hg and slit-lamp showed diffuse central corneal edema and edema around the incision; he complained of persistent blurred vision without major discomfort.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man presented with a chief complaint of decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year. Past medical history was remarkable for diabetes mellitus for 10 years, which had been well controlled with oral medications. Past ocular history was unremarkable. There was no family history of systemic or ocular diseases. Best corrected visual acuity was 20/50 in the right eye and 20/100 in the left eye. Pupillary examination and ocular motility were unremarkable. The intraocular pressures (IOPs) were 12 and 13 mm Hg in the right and left eyes, respectively. There was no visual field loss by confrontation finger counting. Slitlamp examination was normal other than moderate nuclear sclerosis of the crystalline lens in both eyes, left worse than right. Fundus examination did not show any evidence of diabetic retinopathy. Potential acuity testing revealed a visual potential of 20/25 in each eye. A clinical diagnosis of cataract in both eyes was made and cataract surgery of the left eye was recommended for visual rehabilitation. The patient underwent an uncomplicated phacoemulsification via temporal clear corneal incision with implantation of a posterior chamber acrylic intraocular lens in the left eye. The visual acuity was 20/100 with an IOP of 18 mm Hg on the first postoperative day. Slit-lamp examination revealed diffuse corneal edema in the central cornea as well as around the incision site, and the patient complained of persistent blurred vision without major discomfort.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Relatively benign, usually nonprogressive but may persist or worsen if underlying causes (dry eye/adjacent elevation) untreated
Characteristics: Dellen are localized peripheral corneal thinning from desiccation of epithelial and subepithelial tissues adjacent to a limbal elevation (e.g., filtering bleb, dermoid, conjunctival elevation), noninflammatory, often well‑circumscribed, associated with interrupted tear film and dry eye, typically without stromal infiltration, may pool fluorescein, and usually benign but can progress if untreated.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: dry eye; inadequate tear distribution; lid disease (meibomitis, blepharitis); limbal elevation (filtering bleb, dermoid, conjunctival elevation/chemosis); meibomian gland dysfunction; post‑surgical conjunctival elevation
Alleviating Factors: - Lubrication with preservative-free artificial tears and bland ophthalmic ointment - Punctal occlusion - Treatment of meibomian gland dysfunction (warm compresses, lid massage, LipiFlow) - Bandage contact lens or self-retained amniotic membrane graft - Oral doxycycline and vitamin C (for prevention of keratolysis) - Secured amniotic membrane grafting and/or limited tarsorrhaphy for refractory cases
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision (especially at night), persistent blurred vision, ocular surface dryness/discomfort from dry eye (e.g., irritation)
Impact on Daily Activities: Decreased vision (worse at night) in both eyes causing reduced visual acuity (20/50 OD, 20/100 OS) prior to surgery, with postoperative blurred vision in the left eye—moderate impact on daily activities, especially night driving and tasks requiring good visual acuity.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Inadequate tear distribution - Dry eye - Lid disease (meibomitis, blepharitis) - Presence of limbal elevation (e.g., filtering bleb, dermoid, conjunctival elevation after surgery) - Meibomian gland dysfunction/insufficiency - Postoperative conjunctival elevations (e.g., after muscle or scleral buckling surgery)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (Jaeger 4)
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Filtering bleb present (5 to 8 o’clock) adjacent to peripheral corneal dellen; otherwise unremarkable conjunctiva/sclera.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Dellen (localized peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to limbal elevation)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Lens: Moderate nuclear sclerosis in both eyes, left worse than right


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD)
Pupils – Other: unremarkable
Extraocular Movements: unremarkable
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Diffuse corneal edema in the central cornea and around the temporal clear corneal incision; otherwise slit‑lamp exam normal (moderate nuclear sclerosis of both crystalline lenses, left worse than right).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits for age; no optic nerve pallor or cupping noted (optic nerve appeared normal).
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No diabetic retinopathy; periphery without abnormal findings


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Delle in right eye due to localized corneal thinning from inadequate tear distribution/dry eye in the setting of an adjacent limbal elevation (filtering bleb).
Primary Diagnosis: Delle in right eye due to dry eye in the setting of a filtering bleb
Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus (type 2), dry eye, meibomian gland dysfunction/blepharitis
Differential Diagnosis: Dellen (desiccation-related peripheral corneal thinning); Peripheral ulcerative keratitis from autoimmune/inflammatory disease (e.g., rheumatoid arthritis, Mooren’s ulcer, vasculitis); Degenerative peripheral thinning (e.g., Terrien’s marginal degeneration, pellucid marginal degeneration)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right eye
Complications: Dellen (peripheral corneal thinning) adjacent to a filtering bleb leading to localized corneal thinning and risk of progressive keratolysis; potential need for bandage contact lens, amniotic membrane graft, tarsorrhaphy, or surgical intervention if refractory.


Plan
All Medications: oral doxycycline; vitamin C; preservative-free artificial tears; bland ophthalmic lubricating ointment; topical punctal occlusion (procedural)
All Procedures: phacoemulsification with posterior chamber acrylic intraocular lens implantation; specular microscopy (noncontact or contact) of fellow eye; corneal pachymetry; punctal occlusion; application of preservative‑free artificial tears and ophthalmic ointment; oral doxycycline; oral vitamin C; bandage contact lens placement; self‑retained amniotic membrane graft; secured amniotic membrane grafting; limited tarsorrhaphy; surgical excision of offending conjunctival mass (e.g., pyogenic granuloma or dermoid); LipiFlow thermal pulsation for meibomian gland dysfunction
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe and treat long term for signs and symptoms of dry eye contributing to dellen formation; regular follow-up visits to monitor healing and check for progression or recurrence.
Patient Education: Dellen are localized areas of corneal thinning caused by poor tear coverage, often next to a raised area at the limbus (e.g., bleb or conjunctival mass). They are not the same as inflammatory ulcers but need monitoring. Treatment focuses on improving lubrication and tear stability: use preservative‑free artificial tears frequently, apply lubricating ointment at night, consider punctual occlusion if recommended, and treat lid disease (warm compresses and gentle lid massage for meibomian gland dysfunction). If thinning persists, additional measures (oral doxycycline and vitamin C, a bandage contact lens, or an amniotic membrane graft) may be needed; rarely, surgery (tarsorrhaphy or excision of a causative mass) is required. Return promptly for worsening pain, increasing redness, new discharge, a growing area of thinning, or any sudden vision change. Follow long‑term for dry eye management to prevent recurrence.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if you develop sudden worsening pain, increased redness, a new or enlarging area of corneal thinning, any vision loss, a persistent epithelial defect, discharge, or signs of infection. If severe symptoms occur outside office hours, go to the nearest emergency department or eye urgent care.


CASE 12 — Graft Rejection Following Penetrating Keratoplasty
============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision, red eye, photophobia, and foreign body sensation (new symptoms after PK)
DURATION: Two weeks
LATERALITY: OD
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old man with a history of glaucoma status post multiple filtration procedures who underwent repeat trabeculectomy with a new flap at 7 o’clock producing a moderately elevated filtering bleb from 5 to 8 o’clock; two weeks postoperatively he developed peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to the filtering bleb with fluorescein pooling and a small area of epithelial cell loss.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man with a history of glaucoma that required multiple filtration procedures in the right eye was found to have elevated intraocular pressure on maximal medical therapy. A repeat trabeculectomy procedure was performed. In order to avoid the previous surgical site, the trabeculectomy flap was established at the 7 o’clock position, which resulted in a moderately elevated filtering bleb extending from the 5 to 8 o’clock positions. Two weeks after surgery, peripheral corneal thinning was noted adjacent to the filtering bleb. Fluorescein instillation revealed pooling with a small area of epithelial cell loss.
Symptom Onset: Two weeks after surgery
Symptom Duration: Two weeks
Characteristics: - Peripheral corneal thinning (delle): well-circumscribed, “saucerlike” with sloping edges; localized interruption of tear distribution adjacent to an elevation (e.g., bleb); epithelium usually intact at base but may show breakdown with chronic desiccation; fluorescein may pool in depression; rehydrates and thickens with viscous lubricant and patching resulting in resolution and mild localized stromal edema/opacification.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Broken sutures, suture abscess, perioperative/any inflammation (iritis, trauma), stromal vascularization, large/eccentric/repeat grafts, inadequate tear film (dry eye/meibomian gland dysfunction), adjacent elevated filtering bleb causing localized tear disruption
Alleviating Factors: Rehydration (application of viscous lubricating agent such as 1% methylcellulose or ophthalmic ointment and patching the eye), prompt topical corticosteroids for rejection, removal of broken sutures and prophylactic antibiotics, cycloplegic agents for comfort and blood–aqueous stabilization
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, foreign body sensation, red eye, decreased vision
Prior Treatments: Multiple filtration procedures in the right eye; repeat trabeculectomy (with flap at 7 o'clock).
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision, photophobia, foreign body sensation causing difficulty with reading, driving, and comfort until treated
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: stromal vascularization; large-diameter grafts; eccentric grafts; repeat grafts; any postoperative inflammation (iritis, trauma, epithelial or subepithelial rejection, broken sutures)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 80 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J+ (Jagger print)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes with no notable crusting, madarosis, or lash abnormalities.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: injection (ciliary)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Moderate peripheral corneal thinning (dellen) adjacent to a superior-temporal filtering bleb with localized fluorescein pooling; area initially epithelialized but at risk for epithelial breakdown and stromal thinning; later findings include stromal edema and keratic precipitates consistent with endothelial graft rejection.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Cell and flare (anterior chamber reaction)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Round, reactive to light, normal size (approx. 3–4 mm)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: broken suture with suture abscess, ciliary injection, keratic precipitates, stromal edema, and anterior chamber cell/flare consistent with endothelial corneal graft rejection. Left eye: keratoconus.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: OD: Broken suture with suture abscess and endothelial corneal graft rejection — patient presents with signs of endothelial rejection including ciliary injection, keratic precipitates, stromal edema and anterior chamber inflammation likely triggered by the broken suture/suture abscess; high risk for graft failure if not treated promptly. OS: Keratoconus.
Primary Diagnosis: OD: Endothelial corneal graft rejection
Secondary Diagnoses: (1) Broken suture with suture abscess. (2) Endothelial corneal graft rejection.
Differential Diagnosis: Broken suture with suture abscess; Endothelial corneal graft rejection; Iritis (post‑PK anterior uveitis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: Broken suture with suture abscess; Endothelial corneal graft rejection


Plan
All Medications: 1% methylcellulose; ophthalmic lubricating ointment; Polytrim; bacitracin ointment; topical broad-spectrum fluoroquinolone (e.g., ciprofloxacin); prednisolone acetate 1%; scopolamine 0.25%; oral prednisone (e.g., 80 mg daily); subconjunctival/transseptal triamcinolone (40 mg/mL); systemic cyclosporin A
All Procedures: repeat trabeculectomy; trabeculectomy; penetrating keratoplasty (PK); suture removal at the slit lamp; culture of suture abscess; topical antibiotic administration (e.g., Polytrim, bacitracin, topical fluoroquinolone); topical corticosteroid therapy (prednisolone acetate 1%); cycloplegic agent (scopolamine 0.25%); systemic corticosteroids (oral prednisone); subconjunctival/transseptal steroid injection (triamcinolone); repeat penetrating keratoplasty; Descemet’s stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK); perioperative prophylactic systemic steroids; perioperative cyclosporin A; ABO blood type matching
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up every 2–4 days for severe rejection or ~1 week for less severe cases until rejection is controlled, then less frequent visits as topical steroids are tapered with attention to visual rehabilitation (suture lysis, glasses, or rigid contact lens fitting as appropriate).
Patient Education: Seek immediate evaluation within 24 hours for any new photophobia, foreign-body sensation, red eye, or decreased vision; understand that timely treatment is critical to prevent irreversible graft damage. Broken sutures should be removed promptly and may require short-term topical antibiotic prophylaxis; report any persistent irritation or discharge. For endothelial rejection, expect intensive topical steroid treatment (possibly hourly) and close follow-up (every 2–7 days depending on severity); cycloplegics or systemic/subconjunctival steroids may be used for severe cases. Follow prescribed steroid tapering and attend scheduled follow-ups to monitor response and for visual rehabilitation (suture adjustments, glasses, or rigid contact lens as needed).
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate evaluation within 24 hours for any new photophobia, foreign body sensation, red eye, or decreased vision.


CASE 13 — Blepharitis
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in the right eye for 1 week
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Right eye
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 30-year-old man with a history of right penetrating keratoplasty for keratoconus 5 months prior presented with 1 week of redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in the right eye. He had prior right-eye VA 20/60 improving to 20/30 with pinhole one month earlier. On exam the right eye had VA 20/400 without pinhole improvement, 2+ ciliary flush, a broken exposed suture with surrounding infiltrate at the graft 11 o’clock, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft with a line of keratic precipitates at the central edge, and 1+ anterior chamber cell and flare. The left eye was asymptomatic and previously used a rigid gas-permeable contact lens. He also had signs of associated dry eye (inferior punctate keratopathy, diminished tear lake, positive Schirmer’s).
History Narrative: A 30-year-old man presented with complaints of redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in his right eye for 1 week. Past ocular history included keratoconus, for which he had undergone penetrating keratoplasty (PK) in the right eye 5 months earlier. At his examination 1 month earlier, a visual acuity of 20/60 in the right eye with improvement to 20/30 with pinhole had been recorded.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Chronic, relapsing
Characteristics: Staphylococcal anterior blepharitis with eyelid crusting, disrupted lid margin architecture, madarosis, associated dry eye/aqueous tear deficiency (inferior punctate keratopathy, diminished tear lake), possible meibomian gland dysfunction features and chronic relapsing course.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Frequent or rough handling of the eyelids; contact lens wear; dermatologic conditions (eczema, rosacea); seasonal/allergic exposures; bacterial colonization (staphylococcal)
Alleviating Factors: Eyelid hygiene (mechanical debridement/lid scrubs), warm compresses, gentle eyelid massage to express meibomian secretions, preservative‑free artificial tears, topical antibiotics/ointment at bedtime, brief topical steroids (short pulse), omega‑3 supplementation, LipiFlow/intense pulsed light for refractory MGD
Associated Symptoms: redness; photophobia; blurred vision; ocular surface discomfort; dry eye symptoms (inferior punctate keratopathy, diminished tear lake)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant ocular discomfort (redness, photophobia, blurred vision in right eye) limiting activities such as reading/driving intermittently, especially during flares.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Staphylococcal eyelid colonization (S. aureus, S. epidermidis); seborrheic dermatitis; meibomian gland dysfunction; dry eye disease/aqueous tear deficiency; rosacea/acne rosacea; contact lens wear; dermatologic conditions (eczema); prior ocular surgery (e.g., penetrating keratoplasty)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 17 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 17 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Crusting debris at the base of the eyelashes with disruption of normal eyelid margin architecture and loss of eyelashes (madarosis)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 2+ ciliary flush in the right eye; left eye quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal lacrimal drainage on irrigation; no epiphora or lacrimal system abnormality noted
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft from a broken, exposed interrupted suture at the 11 o’clock meridian; line of keratic precipitates demarcating the central edge of stromal edema; 1+ anterior chamber cell and flare; inferior punctate epithelial erosions (associated); graft rejection features after PK.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 1+ cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2+ ciliary flush OD; broken, exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at 11 o'clock on the graft; marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft with a line of keratic precipitates at its central edge; anterior chamber 1+ cell and flare; left eye quiet with RGP in place, central corneal thinning and mild protrusion, Fleischer ring present.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Anterior blepharitis, staphylococcal type, with associated aqueous tear deficiency and signs of ocular surface disease; chronic, relapsing condition requiring eyelid hygiene, topical antibiotics/short-pulse steroids as needed, and maintenance therapy (artificial tears, warm compresses); consider culture if refractory or biopsy if unilateral refractory disease to exclude malignancy.
Primary Diagnosis: Anterior blepharitis, staphylococcal type
Secondary Diagnoses: Keratoconus; Status post penetrating keratoplasty (right eye) 5 months prior; Dry eye disease (aqueous tear deficiency); Ocular rosacea (mentioned as associated — inferred not confirmed)
Differential Diagnosis: Allergic conjunctivitis; Dry eye syndrome (aqueous tear deficiency); Giant papillary conjunctivitis; Medicated (medicamentosa) or contact lens–related conjunctivitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Structural damage (poliosis, madarosis, lid ulceration, eyelid/eyelash malposition and scarring) - Corneal complications: inferior punctate epithelial erosions, marginal infiltrates, phlyctenules, keratitis, neovascularization, thinning, graft rejection/ stromal edema (in post-PK patient) - Chronic relapsing disease requiring long-term therapy - Potential for misdiagnosed malignancy (sebaceous cell carcinoma, basal cell carcinoma, squamous cell carcinoma, melanoma)


Plan
All Medications: hypochlorous acid 0.01% (lid cleansing solution), erythromycin ointment, sulfacetamide ointment, bacitracin ointment, topical steroids (short-pulse), preservative-free artificial tears, topical azithromycin ophthalmic solution, oral minocycline, doxycycline (50–100 mg orally twice a day), tetracycline (250 mg orally four times a day), oral omega-3 fatty acid (DHA/EPA, 1–3 g/day)
All Procedures: Penetrating keratoplasty (prior, right eye) Slit-lamp examination Ultrasonic pachymetry (ancillary, as indicated) Eyelid hygiene/lid scrubs (mechanical debridement with cotton‑tip applicator or lid scrub) Application of topical antibiotic ointment (bedtime) Warm compresses and eyelid massage Topical steroid short‑pulse therapy (if indicated) Topical azithromycin ophthalmic solution or erythromycin ointment (as treatment) Oral doxycycline/minocycline/tetracycline antibiotic therapy (as treatment) Omega‑3 fatty acid supplementation (oral) Eyelid bacterial culture and sensitivity testing (if refractory) LipiFlow thermal pulsation system (therapeutic option) Intense pulsed light (therapeutic option) Eyelid biopsy (for atypical/refractory unilateral disease) Surgical management of eyelid/eyelash malposition (if structural damage)
Follow-Up Instructions: Return visit in 3–6 weeks for moderate/severe disease; earlier follow-up if started topical steroids to monitor response and intraocular pressure. Mild cases: follow as needed or at next routine visit. Continue maintenance lid hygiene, artificial tears, and warm compresses; tailor regimen to symptoms.
Patient Education: Blepharitis is a chronic, relapsing eyelid condition; meticulous daily eyelid hygiene is essential (lid scrubs with a mild shampoo or commercial cleansers, warm compresses, gentle massage). Use preservative‑free artificial tears 4–8×/day for dry eye symptoms. Apply topical antibiotic ointment (e.g., erythromycin) at bedtime for severe staphylococcal blepharitis as directed. Short courses of topical steroids may be used for marked inflammation—follow-up is needed to monitor response and eye pressure. Avoid frequent/rough handling of the lids. Consider omega‑3 supplementation (1–3 g/day DHA/EPA) for meibomian gland dysfunction. Return in 3–6 weeks for reassessment or sooner if symptoms worsen or unilateral/refractory disease suggests possible underlying malignancy.


CASE 14 — A Nearly Mature Cataract in a Patient with Glaucoma
=============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Nearly mature cataract with decreased vision in the only seeing eye (monocular)
DURATION: Two years
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 10-day-old infant noted by pediatrician to have bilateral white pupils; poor wince to light in both eyes, reactive pupils, soft globes, clear corneas, central white lens opacities bilaterally with no view of posterior segment; referred for ophthalmologic evaluation and workup.
History Narrative: A pediatrician noticed white pupils in a 10‑day‑old infant and diagnosed bilateral cataracts. The mother’s pregnancy was uncomplicated with no rash or febrile illness; the infant was born at term and maternal serologies were negative. There was no family history of congenital or childhood cataracts. On examination the child had poor wince to light in both eyes, reactive pupils without a relative afferent pupillary defect, normal extraocular movements, and soft globes to palpation. Eyelids and conjunctiva were normal and the corneas were clear and normal in diameter. The lenses had central white opacities bilaterally and there was no view to the posterior segment in either eye; B‑scan ultrasonography showed no retinal detachment or mass. Laboratory testing and genetic consultation failed to reveal an underlying genetic syndrome.
Symptom Onset: 10 days after birth
Characteristics: Mature cataract with zonular compromise, small/atonic iris with radial sphincterotomies, shallow anterior chamber risk, prior trabeculectomy with functioning bleb, monocular (blind fellow eye), advanced glaucoma, phakic with nearly mature central white opacity, poor preop view of posterior segment.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: poor wince to light
Prior Treatments: multiple prior intraocular surgeries including a trabeculectomy with a functioning filtering bleb; intermittent use of glaucoma medications and massage of the bleb
Impact on Daily Activities: She uses a computer at work, grows flowers, and is very aware of colors and the brightness of objects.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: mature cataract, zonular weakness/compromise, congenital glaucoma, prior multiple intraocular surgeries (trabeculectomy/functional bleb), atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies/small pupil, monocular status (phthisical fellow eye), tenuous corneal endothelium/corneal endothelial cell loss, prior bleb (risk of bleb trauma), potential for postoperative inflammation and IOP spikes


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonometry (measured during exam under anesthesia)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear and normal in diameter
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Partially formed with viscoelastic in place (shallow centrally with soft chamber due to viscoelastic protection)
Anterior Segment – Iris: atrophic with radial sphincterotomies and corectopia
Anterior Segment – Angle: Narrow/compromised with functioning filtering bleb and prior trabeculectomy
Anterior Segment – Lens: Mature cataract with zonular compromise


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive pupils with corectopia (atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Reactive
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD)
Pupils – Other: reactive with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The lenses had central white opacities bilaterally; pupils reactive with no RAPD; corneas clear and normal in diameter; eyelids and conjunctiva normal; poor wince to light in both eyes.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Mature cataract with zonular compromise in a monocular patient with advanced glaucoma status post multiple intraocular surgeries (functional trabeculectomy bleb), atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies; high risk for intraoperative zonular failure, posterior lens dislocation, corneal endothelial decompensation, and postoperative IOP elevation.
Primary Diagnosis: Mature cataract with zonular compromise, advanced glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: congenital glaucoma; zonular weakness/compromise; functioning trabeculectomy with filtering bleb; atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies; advanced glaucoma; monocular status (blind phthisical fellow eye); prior multiple intraocular surgeries; corneal endothelial cell loss (risk for corneal decompensation)
Differential Diagnosis: Mature congenital cataract; Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (PHPV); Posterior capsular/lamellar cataract from intrauterine infection (TORCH)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Vitreous prolapse through a superior nasal iridectomy (requiring anterior vitrectomy); potential corneal endothelial compromise (risk of corneal decompensation); intraoperative zonular instability with risk of dropped lens (managed with capsular tension ring); transient postoperative IOP spike (managed medically).


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate, ofloxacin, diclofenac, timolol, brimonidine, prostaglandin analog (avoided), carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (avoided), Viscoat (dispersive viscoelastic), Provisc (cohesive viscoelastic), Healon 5 (ultraviscous viscoelastic)
All Procedures: - Paracentesis and clear corneal incision with globe fixation using a 16-mm Fine/Thornton ring - Side-port incision - Viscoat (dispersive) and Provisc (cohesive) viscoelastic administration (soft-shell technique) - Lysis of posterior synechiae - Insertion of Morcher iris-expander ring - Continuous curvilinear capsulorrhexis - Insertion of Morcher capsular tension ring - Cortical-cleaving hydrodissection and hydrodelineation - Phacoemulsification using chopper—"choo choo chop and flip" technique (phaco with reverse Kelman/30° bevel-down tip) - Cortical irrigation/aspiration (tangential cortex stripping) - Injection and implantation of 6.0 D foldable silicone IOL into the capsular bag - Closure of main wound with suture - Separate paracentesis and split-port anterior vitrectomy (anterior vitrectomy with vitrector) - Removal of iris-expander ring - Stromal hydration to seal incisions
Follow-Up Instructions: Topical prednisolone acetate, ofloxacin, and diclofenac TID; close IOP monitoring — examine twice daily for 3 days postoperatively; start prophylactic timolol and brimonidine to control IOP (avoid prostaglandin analogs and carbonic anhydrase inhibitors); follow routine postoperative visits (week 1 and ongoing monitoring for IOP and corneal status).


CASE 15 — Retained Lens Material after Cataract Extraction
==========================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in the right eye with blurred vision and elevated intraocular pressure.
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 34-year-old Caucasian woman with a complex ocular history including a phthisical left eye and congenital glaucoma status post multiple prior surgeries in the right eye (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, and trabeculectomy) was referred by a glaucoma specialist for evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in the right eye. She had progressive decreased vision in the right eye; visual acuity was count fingers in the right and no light perception in the left. On exam the right eye used a superior nasal peripheral iridotomy as an entrance pupil, had a functioning thick-walled superior nasal filtering bleb, IOP 20 mm Hg off medications, few corneal guttae, deep quiet anterior chamber, diffusely atrophic iris with 360° posterior synechiae preventing dilation (pupil ~3 mm) with multiple sphincterotomies and a large radial cut at 12 o’clock, a dense nearly mature cataract, absent zonules visible through the superior nasal iridectomy and questionable zonular status elsewhere, and no view of the right fundus.
History Narrative: A 34-year-old Caucasian woman was referred by a glaucoma specialist for evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in her right eye. She had a complex ocular history including a phthisical left eye and congenital glaucoma status post numerous surgical procedures in the right eye including goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, and a trabeculectomy. On examination, visual acuity was finger counting in the right and no light perception in the left. Examination of the right eye suggested that the patient was using her superior nasal peripheral iridotomy as an entrance pupil. There was a relatively thick-walled but functioning filtering bleb in the superior nasal quadrant. Intraocular pressure (IOP) was 20 mm Hg without glaucoma medications. A few corneal guttae were observed. The anterior chamber was deep and quiet. The iris was highly atrophic throughout its entire periphery, with very little sphincter tissue. The pupil measured 3 mm and did not dilate due to 360 degrees of posterior synechiae. There were multiple sphincterotomies at the pupillary margin and a large radial cut at the 12 o’clock position. There was a dense, nearly mature cataract, an absence of zonules visible through the large superior nasal iridectomy, and questionable zonular status in the areas of broad peripheral iridectomies in two other quadrants. There was no view of the right fundus.
Symptom Onset: Several weeks after disruption of the lens capsule
Symptom Duration: Several weeks


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: eye pain, blurred vision
Prior Treatments: Numerous glaucoma surgeries in the right eye (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy); topical timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine, topical atropine and prednisolone acetate prior to vitrectomy/lensectomy/IOL placement.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate; initial vision reduced to counting fingers in affected eye with pain and elevated IOP limiting activities, improving postoperatively to 20/30 allowing normal daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: - Complex ocular history including congenital glaucoma with multiple prior intraocular surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy) - Phthisical fellow eye - Extremely long axial length (30 mm) - Large horizontal white-to-white (>14 mm) - Poor zonular support/questionable zonules and absence of zonules in areas - Nearly mature/dense cataract - Posterior synechiae and small non-dilating pupil with sphincterotomies/iridectomies


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: A few corneal guttae
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Highly atrophic throughout its entire periphery with very little sphincter tissue; multiple sphincterotomies and posterior synechiae; pupil 3 mm non-dilating; large radial cut at 12 o'clock; using superior nasal peripheral iridectomy as entrance pupil.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open with retained lens material visible on gonioscopy (open angle)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Dense, nearly mature cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm; entrance pupil via superior nasal peripheral iridectomy (irregular/atrophic with multiple sphincterotomies)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Nondilating to light (no pupillary dilation due to 360° posterior synechiae)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: 3 mm, did not dilate
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: 360 degrees of posterior synechiae
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: few corneal guttae; deep, quiet anterior chamber; highly atrophic iris with 360° posterior synechiae, small 3 mm nonreactive pupil using superior-nasal peripheral iridectomy as entrance pupil; dense nearly mature cataract; functioning thick-walled superior-nasal filtering bleb. Left eye: phthisical, no light perception.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Flat


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lens particle glaucoma secondary to retained lens fragments in the right eye following cataract extraction, with elevated IOP despite medical therapy, significant anterior segment abnormalities (360° posterior synechiae, atrophic iris, prior filtering surgery), dense nearly mature cataract and questionable zonular support; B-scan showed attached retina. Acute IOP elevation and need for IOL placement prompted surgical management—pars plana vitrectomy, lensectomy, and IOL implantation—performed 4 days after presentation, resulting in postoperative IOP normalization (12 mm Hg off medications) and good visual rehabilitation.
Primary Diagnosis: Lens particle glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Phthisical left eye; congenital glaucoma status post numerous surgical procedures (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy); corneal guttae; 7 D against-the-rule astigmatism; high axial myopia (axial length 30 mm)
Differential Diagnosis: Angle-closure glaucoma; Phacolytic glaucoma; Phacoantigenic (phacoanaphylactic) glaucoma; Iridocyclitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: glaucoma; inflammation; retinal detachment; hemorrhage; chronic inflammation; disabling visual floaters; refractory cystoid macular edema; secondary glaucoma


Plan
All Medications: timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine, atropine, prednisolone acetate, topical beta-blocker, alpha-2-adrenergic agonist, topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, glycerin, isosorbide, mannitol, prostaglandin analogs (not used), triamcinolone acetonide
All Procedures: pars plana vitrectomy; lensectomy; intraocular lens (IOL) placement; topical timolol; topical dorzolamide; topical brimonidine; topical atropine; topical prednisolone acetate
Patient Education: You have lens particle glaucoma from retained lens fragments after cataract surgery. Immediate goals are to lower eye pressure and reduce inflammation to protect your optic nerve. Treatment may include multiple eye-pressure drops (beta-blocker, alpha‑2 agonist, topical and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors), frequent steroid eye drops, and cycloplegic drops to ease pain and prevent adhesions. If pressure or inflammation do not respond, pars plana vitrectomy and lensectomy to remove the lens fragments (and placement of an intraocular lens if needed) is often performed—timing is individualized but commonly within days to weeks. Urgently return for worsening pain, sudden vision loss, new floaters, flashes, or a marked rise in eye pressure. Follow all medications exactly, attend scheduled follow-ups for pressure checks, and avoid activities that increase eye pressure until cleared.
Referrals: a glaucoma specialist


CASE 16 — Fibrin Deposition on Intraocular Lenses
=================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye
DURATION: 9 months
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 47-year-old man with history of bungee-cord trauma to the left eye and complicated left cataract surgery 9 months ago, lost to follow-up, presenting with a 2-month history of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye.
History Narrative: A 47-year-old man with a history of bungee cord trauma to the left eye had undergone a complicated cataract surgery 9 months earlier. He was lost to follow-up and presented to the retina clinic with a 2-month history of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye. His visual acuity was 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg OD and 38 mm Hg OS. Pupils were reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect. Right eye examination was unremarkable aside from a mild cataract. Slit-lamp biomicroscopy of the left eye revealed mild conjunctival injection, 2+ anterior chamber cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous. Gonioscopy of the left eye demonstrated a wide open angle with lens fragments noted inferiorly. Dilated fundus examination of the left eye revealed a posterior vitreous detachment with his crystalline lens subluxated inferiorly in the anterior vitreous; macula, retinal vessels, and optic nerve were unremarkable.
Symptom Onset: 2 months ago
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Worsening over 2 months leading to decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye with elevated IOP (chronic progressive since surgery 9 months earlier)
Characteristics: Fibrin strands or dense fibrinous membrane over IOL/pupil; cellular AC reaction (2+ cell and flare) without hypopyon; presents days 2–14 post‑surgery; may cause decreased vision, redness, pain, photophobia; immune‑mediated from blood–aqueous barrier breakdown.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: surgical manipulation of the iris; incomplete removal of lens cortex/epithelial cells; intraoperative use of long-acting miotic agents; prior or prolonged intraocular surgery; conditions increasing vascular permeability (diabetes, hypertension, pseudoexfoliation, uveitis)
Alleviating Factors: Topical corticosteroids (eg, prednisolone acetate), systemic steroids if severe, intracameral tPA, anterior chamber washout/fibrin membranectomy when indicated, preoperative NSAIDs/steroids, intraoperative subconjunctival steroid injection
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, red eye, pain, photophobia
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — decreased vision OS to 20/400 with pain, redness, and photophobia for 2 months impairing daily function.
Risk Factors: diabetes; hypertension; pseudoexfoliation syndrome; uveitis; prolonged surgery; previous intraocular surgery; surgical manipulation of the iris; incomplete removal of lens cortex and epithelial cells; intraoperative use of a long-acting miotic agent; prior history of intraocular surgery (recent)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (OS 20/400 distance → near approx. N24)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 38 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild corneal edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 2+ cell and flare, fibrin strands/membrane over IOL with mild conjunctival injection, prolapsed vitreous in the AC, no hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Iris: iridodonesis; ectopia lentis; prolapsed vitreous; pupillary membrane/fibrin covering pupil (2+ AC cell and flare)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Wide open angle with lens fragments inferiorly
Anterior Segment – Lens: Ectopia lentis; subluxated crystalline lens inferiorly with lens fragments inferiorly; prolapsed vitreous; prior intraocular lens implant with fibrin deposition on IOL


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive to light; no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no extraocular motility deficit)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild conjunctival injection, 2+ anterior chamber cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Macula: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Periphery flat, no retinal breaks or holes; vitreous detached with crystalline lens subluxated inferiorly in anterior vitreous.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 47-year-old man with history of complicated left cataract surgery 9 months prior, now presenting with 2 months of decreased vision, redness, and pain OS. VA 20/400 OS, IOP 38 mmHg OS. Exam OS: mild conjunctival injection, 2+ AC cell and flare, iridodonesis, lens subluxation/ectopia lentis with prolapsed vitreous and lens fragments inferiorly on gonioscopy, and posterior vitreous detachment with subluxated crystalline lens in anterior vitreous on dilated exam. Findings consistent with postoperative fibrinous anterior chamber reaction on the IOL with secondary lens particle glaucoma causing markedly elevated IOP. Right eye unremarkable aside from mild cataract. Plan: aggressive topical corticosteroids (prednisolone acetate 1% q1–q6h depending on inflammation), consider intracameral tPA if fibrin persists, manage elevated IOP medically and consider washout/lens removal or surgical intervention for dislocated lens and retained lens fragments once inflammation controlled. Monitor for complications including infectious endophthalmitis, corneal edema, secondary glaucoma, and cystoid macular edema.
Primary Diagnosis: Postoperative fibrin deposition on the IOL
Secondary Diagnoses: Traumatic cataract with prior complicated cataract surgery; ectopia lentis (subluxated crystalline lens); prolapsed vitreous; elevated intraocular pressure/glaucoma (left eye, IOP 38 mm Hg)
Differential Diagnosis: Fibrinous postoperative anterior uveitis (fibrin deposition on IOL); Postoperative endophthalmitis; Lens-particle glaucoma (lens fragment–induced inflammation/IOP rise); Persistent intraocular inflammation from retained lens material or IOL malposition
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: AC turbidity, corneal edema, elevated intraocular pressure, bleeding, corneal toxicity, band keratopathy, endophthalmitis, posterior synechiae, loss of iris function, membrane formation on the IOL, IOL dislocation, glaucoma, cystoid macular edema, posterior capsular opacification, infectious keratitis


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% (topical); topical nonsteroidal antiinflammatory agents (topical NSAID); systemic steroids; intracameral recombinant tissue plasminogen activator (tPA)
All Procedures: Intracameral tPA injection; anterior chamber washout with fibrin membranectomy; removal of intraocular lens (IOL) if causative (IOL explantation)
Follow-Up Instructions: Regular eye examinations at regular intervals to monitor visual acuity, IOP, and for signs of infection or recurrent inflammation.
Patient Education: Fibrin on the intraocular lens is inflammation after eye surgery that can cause decreased vision, redness, pain, and light sensitivity. It usually appears days to weeks after surgery and is treated primarily with strong topical steroid drops (for example prednisolone acetate 1%) given frequently to reduce inflammation and restore the blood–aqueous barrier. In more severe or persistent cases, an injectable medication (intracameral tPA) can dissolve fibrin, but it carries risks (temporary corneal clouding, corneal edema or toxicity, raised eye pressure, bleeding, and infection). Rarely, surgery (anterior chamber washout or IOL removal) is needed if the IOL is causing ongoing inflammation or vision is significantly affected. Follow-up exams are important to monitor vision, eye pressure, and signs of infection or cystoid macular edema; contact your eye doctor right away for worsening pain, sudden vision loss, increasing redness, or discharge.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if you develop sudden severe eye pain, marked vision loss, increasing redness, new discharge, flashes/floaters, or if your eye becomes more swollen; for sudden very high eye pressure symptoms (severe pain, nausea/vomiting, halos around lights) or signs of infection (fever, worsening pain) go to the emergency department or contact your eye surgeon/ophthalmologist right away.


CASE 17 — Subluxated Crystalline Lens
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photophobia
DURATION: 5 days
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "photophobia"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 59-year-old Caucasian woman presented 5 days after combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye complaining of photophobia; she had undergone the same procedures in the left eye one month earlier.
History Narrative: A 59-year-old Caucasian woman presented to the eye clinic after intraocular surgery in the right eye, with a chief complaint of photophobia. Five days earlier, she had undergone combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens (IOL) implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye. Her left eye had undergone the same procedures 1 month prior to presentation. Her past medical history was significant only for hypertension. Her ocular medications were prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours and ofloxacin four times a day in both eyes. Her visual acuity was 20/60 OD and 20/200 OS. Slit-lamp examination of the right eye revealed mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb. The cornea was clear. The anterior chamber had moderate cells and flare with no hypopyon. There was a meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL. Examination of the left eye revealed a low bleb, clear cornea, and rare cells in the anterior chamber. The IOL was in good position. Dilated fundus exam was unremarkable in both eyes except for moderate and severe glaucomatous cupping of the right and left optic nerves, respectively. The poor vision in the left eye was secondary to advanced glaucoma.
Symptom Onset: 5 days earlier
Symptom Duration: 5 days
Characteristics: Upward (superotemporal) lens subluxation associated with Marfan’s syndrome


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Reclining the dilated patient so the lens falls back behind the pupil (and pharmacologic miosis to trap it posteriorly)
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia
Prior Treatments: combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in both eyes (right eye 5 days prior, left eye 1 month prior)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — visual impairment (photophobia, decreased acuity OD 20/60, OS 20/200) and postoperative inflammation likely interfere with reading, driving, and daily tasks, especially with OS advanced glaucoma causing poor vision.
Systemic Symptoms: tall stature, long thin extremities (arachnodactyly), joint laxity, pectus excavatum, kyphoscoliosis, decreased subcutaneous fat, and cardiac abnormalities (mitral or aortic valve disease, progressive ascending aortic dilation)
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Marfan’s syndrome; hereditary zonulopathy; tall stature/arachnodactyly (systemic features of Marfan’s)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: JND/E (20/40)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: moderate cells and flare, no hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Angle: Narrow/occludable angle with risk of pupillary block — peripheral laser iridotomy may be indicated
Anterior Segment – Lens: Upward subluxation of the crystalline lens (ectopia lentis)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion bilaterally (EOM intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb; clear cornea; anterior chamber with moderate cells and flare, no hypopyon; meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: moderate cupping OD, severe cupping OS
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unremarkable (macula normal)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and no abnormalities detected
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: no peripheral retinal detachment; examination unremarkable except predisposition noted — peripheral retina otherwise unremarkable.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) associated with Marfan’s syndrome.
Primary Diagnosis: Lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) associated with Marfan’s syndrome
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Trauma (unilateral) including angle recession; Marfan syndrome (hereditary ectopia lentis); Homocystinuria (metabolic, downward subluxation); Pseudoexfoliation-associated zonulopathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Life-threatening cardiac abnormalities (aortic dissection, mitral/aortic valve abnormalities) associated with Marfan’s syndrome - Retinal detachment - Glaucoma (including angle‑closure from pupillary block) - Corneal endothelial cell loss and corneal decompensation from anterior dislocation - Amblyopia (in pediatric patients) - Vitreous prolapse - Intraocular hemorrhage - Endophthalmitis - Hypotony - IOL-related complications: optic tilt/decentration, pigment dispersion, unintended refractive error, suture degradation/breakage and scleral knot erosion, hemorrhage and haptic damage with intrascleral fixation


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours; ofloxacin four times a day
All Procedures: combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (right eye); combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (left eye, 1 month prior)
Follow-Up Instructions: Return to clinic for postoperative check within 1 week for IOP, anterior chamber inflammation, wound/bleb status, and IOL position; earlier evaluation sooner if increased pain, decreasing vision, photophobia, hypopyon, or signs of endophthalmitis. Continue topical prednisolone acetate 1% and ofloxacin as prescribed; taper steroids per postoperative protocol. Arrange ongoing glaucoma and retinal monitoring; consider echocardiography if Marfan’s suspected for systemic evaluation.
Patient Education: A subluxated (partially dislocated) lens means the natural lens has shifted because its supporting fibers (zonules) are weak or broken. Causes include trauma, age-related conditions, or inherited disorders like Marfan’s syndrome — if bilateral or unexplained, further medical evaluation (including heart testing) is important. Small shifts can often be managed without surgery using glasses, contact lenses, or pupil‑changing drops to reduce symptoms (double vision or glare). If the lens threatens the cornea, causes pupillary block/angle closure, significantly blurs vision, or risks other complications, surgery is recommended. Surgical options include phacoemulsification with special capsular support devices, or pars plana lensectomy with vitrectomy; IOL placement may be in the bag, in the sulcus, sutured to the iris or sclera, or an anterior chamber/iris‑claw lens depending on support. After surgery, expect steroid and antibiotic eye drops, follow‑up visits to monitor inflammation, pressure, and IOL position, and report sudden pain, vision loss, increasing redness, or discharge immediately. If Marfan’s or another systemic condition is suspected, family screening and cardiology evaluation (echocardiogram) are recommended because of possible life‑threatening aortic or valve disease.
Referrals: Echocardiography for evaluation of aortic/valvular disease (cardiology)


CASE 18 — Congenital Cataract
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in the left eye
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 41-year-old man with Marfan’s syndrome presenting with variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye; no history of trauma; symptoms correspond with left lens subluxation and myopic refractive error (VA 20/70 with −5.00 D in left eye) discovered on exam.
History Narrative: A 41-year-old man with Marfan’s syndrome presents with variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye. He has a family history of Marfan’s syndrome and no history of trauma. Visual acuity is 20/70 in the left eye with −5.00 D correction and 20/20 in the right eye with −0.75 D correction. The left crystalline lens is superiorly subluxated with diaphanous zonules exposed across at least six clock hours inferiorly; there is no phacodonesis and no vitreous prolapse. Dilated exam of the asymptomatic right eye revealed a very subtle inferior zonular dialysis. Intraocular pressure and fundus examination are normal.
Symptom Duration: Variable (chronic)
Characteristics: Bilateral, idiopathic congenital central nuclear lens opacities causing visually significant deprivation amblyopia risk; often symmetric, present at birth/within first year, may be associated with systemic disease in bilateral cases.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: variable decreased vision; bothersome glare
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision (20/70 in left eye) with glare impacting activities that require good monocular vision; overall functional but some limitations (glare sensitivity, reduced depth perception).
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Family history of Marfan’s syndrome - Systemic syndromes/chromosomal abnormalities - Metabolic disorders - Intrauterine infections - Trauma - Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (PHPV) - Microphthalmia - Uveitis - Early surgery (age <4 weeks) increasing glaucoma risk


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/70
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/70
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: Normal
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes (no abnormality noted)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris, no abnormality detected
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Subluxated crystalline lens superiorly with at least six clock hours of inferior zonular dialysis; diaphanous/exposed zonules; no phacodonesis; posterior capsule intact; central nuclear congenital cataract (bilateral idiopathic)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: reactive, round, equal (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupil constriction confirms excellent centration of the IOL
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU (normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Left crystalline lens is subluxated superiorly with diaphanous zonules exposed across an area of at least six clock hours inferiorly; no phacodonesis and no vitreous prolapse; right eye with a very subtle inferior zonular dialysis; otherwise intraocular pressure and fundus normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal peripheral retina


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Bilateral idiopathic congenital cataract — visually significant lens opacities present since infancy with high amblyopia risk. No medical treatment available; surgical extraction recommended early (bilateral by ~10 weeks) with consideration of timing of IOL implantation (usually avoided <1 year). Postoperative management requires prompt optical correction (glasses for bilateral aphakia or contact lens for unilateral), aggressive amblyopia therapy, and long-term surveillance for complications such as posterior capsular opacification and secondary glaucoma.
Primary Diagnosis: Bilateral idiopathic congenital cataract
Secondary Diagnoses: Marfan syndrome; Zonular dialysis (lens subluxation)
Differential Diagnosis: Congenital cataract; Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (PHPV/retrolental fibroplasia); Retinoblastoma; Coats’ disease
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: posterior capsular opacification; secondary membrane formation; glaucoma; retinal detachment


Plan
All Medications: atropine
All Procedures: Phacoemulsification of subluxated crystalline lens with insertion of capsule retractors, cortical cleanup, implantation of capsular tension ring (CTR) with scleral fixation using a Cionni/Malyugin-modified CTR (double-armed 9–0 polypropylene/CV‑8 Gore‑Tex suture through eyelet) and placement of three‑piece foldable IOL in ciliary sulcus with optic‑capsulorhexis capture
Follow-Up Instructions: Excellent refractive correction and appropriate occlusion (patching) therapy; if no IOL, immediate fitting with contact lenses or glasses (bilateral: glasses preferred with myopic target; unilateral: contact lens preferred); prolonged monitoring for glaucoma with long‑term follow‑up; frequent postoperative visits for refractive management and amblyopia therapy.
Patient Education: This chapter reviews congenital cataract: it can cause leukocoria and amblyopia if untreated; early detection and timely surgery (unilateral ~4–6 weeks, bilateral by ~10 weeks) improve outcomes but surgery before 4 weeks raises glaucoma risk. No medical cure exists; topical mydriatics (low‑dose atropine) may help selected central cataracts temporarily. Surgery in infants often requires posterior capsulotomy and close amblyopia therapy afterwards. IOLs are usually avoided under age 1 and targeted to leave initial hyperopia; risks include inflammation, membrane/capsular opacification, glaucoma (may occur years later), and retinal detachment. Postop rehabilitation requires prompt refractive correction (contacts for unilateral, glasses for bilateral) and intensive patching/occlusion therapy to prevent irreversible amblyopia. Long‑term follow‑up is essential for refractive changes and late glaucoma.


CASE 19 — Ocular Hypertension
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: shadow blocking her temporal vision
DURATION: 20-year history
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "a 'shadow' since her surgery, which was blocking her temporal vision."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: An 80-year-old woman with a 20-year history of glaucoma presented 8 months after combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye complaining of a temporal “shadow” since surgery that was blocking her temporal vision and causing near-miss driving incidents; vision in the affected eye was 20/30 and IOP 7 mm Hg.
History Narrative: An 80-year-old woman with a 20-year history of glaucoma presented for consultation 8 months following combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye. She complained of a “shadow” since her surgery, which was blocking her temporal vision. This was so debilitating that she almost ran over a small boy with her car. The visual acuity was 20/30 in the affected eye and the intraocular pressure (IOP) was 7 mm Hg. A large, ischemic filtering bleb was present with a negative Seidel’s test. The cornea was clear, the anterior chamber deep and quiet, and the cup-to-disc ratio was 0.8. The peripheral fundus was not visible due to a small and fibrotic pupil. A B-scan ultrasound was performed, which showed 360-degree ciliochoroidal detachments with serous fluid inside the detachments. There was a large nasal choroidal detachment that measured 8 mm. There was no retinal detachment overlying the choroidal detachment.
Symptom Onset: 8 months ago
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Characteristics: Ocular hypertension characterized by elevated intraocular pressure with normal-appearing optic nerves and visual fields, risk of progression to glaucoma, may be primary or secondary (e.g., pigment dispersion, pseudoexfoliation), assessed with gonioscopy, OCT, visual fields, pachymetry; management individualized based on risk factors and age.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: shadow blocking her temporal vision
Prior Treatments: combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye (8 months prior)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — shadow blocking temporal vision so debilitating she almost ran over a child while driving
Risk Factors: - Elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) - Thin central corneal thickness (CCT) - Race (particularly black populations) - Positive family history of glaucoma - Older age (affects treatment considerations)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 7 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 7 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: large, ischemic filtering bleb present
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: small and fibrotic pupil
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and deep (normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Intraocular lens (IOL) implant


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: small and fibrotic pupil
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – Other: small and fibrotic
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The cornea was clear, the anterior chamber deep and quiet, and the cup-to-disc ratio was 0.8.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.8 (large optic cup)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Not visible due to a small and fibrotic pupil


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ocular hypertension — elevated intraocular pressure without evidence of glaucomatous optic nerve damage or visual field loss; patient is a glaucoma suspect for possible progression to primary open-angle glaucoma and should be monitored and considered for treatment based on risk factors and IOP level.
Primary Diagnosis: Ocular hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Idiopathic ocular hypertension; Primary open-angle glaucoma; Secondary glaucoma from pigment dispersion or pseudoexfoliation syndrome
Laterality: Right and left
Complications: large, ischemic filtering bleb with choroidal detachment (serous ciliochoroidal detachment)


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C
All Procedures: combined cataract extraction with intraocular lens implant and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; B-scan ultrasound; slit-lamp examination; gonioscopy; spectral domain optical coherence tomography (SD-OCT); visual field testing (perimetry); pachymetry; laser trabeculoplasty; glaucoma filtration surgery; dilated optic nerve examination
Follow-Up Instructions: Initially every 6–12 months. Yearly visual field testing and/or OCT and dilated optic nerve exam. If started on IOP-lowering therapy, recheck within 4 weeks; after stability, exam every 4–6 months for ≥2 years. If stable long-term, every 6–12 months.
Patient Education: Explain that ocular hypertension means your eye pressure is higher than normal but your optic nerve and vision are currently normal, so you are being monitored as a glaucoma risk. Discuss risks and benefits of treatment versus observation, emphasizing individualized decisions based on age, risk factors (family history, race, corneal thickness), and potential side effects of medications. Recommend follow-up schedule: initial visits every 6–12 months (or sooner if treatment started), yearly visual field and/or OCT with dilated optic nerve exam, and if started on eye drops return in ~4 weeks to check pressure and then every 4–6 months while stable for at least 2 years. Advise to report new vision changes, increasing shadow/field loss, eye pain, or medication side effects promptly.


CASE 20 — Open-Angle Glaucoma
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Referred for elevated intraocular pressure (possible glaucoma)
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 42-year-old Caucasian man was referred after an optometrist found elevated eye pressures and suspected glaucoma; he has no systemic or family history, takes no medications, and reports no visual symptoms—visual acuity 20/20 OU, normal pupils and motility, clear anterior segment, IOPs 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS, angles open 360° on gonioscopy, normal optic discs with cup-to-disc 0.2 OU, and normal 24–2 Humphrey visual fields.
History Narrative: A 42-year-old Caucasian man was referred to the eye clinic after being told by an optometrist that his eye pressures were high and that he might have glaucoma. Past medical history and family history were unremarkable. He was not taking any medications. Ocular examination revealed visual acuity of 20/20 OU without correction. Pupils and motility were normal. Anterior segment biomicroscopic examination was unremarkable with a clear cornea and lens. Intraocular pressures (IOPs) were 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy revealed angles open to the ciliary body band 360 degrees with moderate pigmentation of the trabecular meshwork. Dilated funduscopic examination revealed symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping. The cup-to-disc ratio was 0.2, with an intact neuroretinal rim OU. The macula, vessels, periphery, and vitreous were normal in appearance. A 24–2 Humphrey automated perimetry was performed, which revealed no visual field defects in either eye.
Progression: No progression detected
Characteristics: Primary open-angle glaucoma: age 42, Caucasian male, bilateral open angles 360°, IOPs 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS, visual acuity 20/20 OU, cup-to-disc ratio 0.2 OU with intact neuroretinal rim, no visual field defects on 24-2 Humphrey, moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation, unremarkable anterior segment and media, no family or systemic history.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: None reported
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: None — the patient has normal visual acuity, no visual field defects, and intact optic nerves, so there is no impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Elevated intraocular pressure (ocular hypertension) and moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation; age 42 (younger adult), Caucasian race, family history negative (so not a risk), thin/normal CCT not stated (N/A)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 27 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 28 mm Hg
