Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal lid position and lashes; no abnormalities noted
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open to the ciliary body band 360°
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: normal
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment biomicroscopic examination unremarkable with a clear cornea and lens
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping; cup-to-disc ratio 0.2 OU with intact neuroretinal rim, no focal thinning or hemorrhages.
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 42-year-old Caucasian man with ocular hypertension and findings consistent with early primary open-angle glaucoma. Visual acuity 20/20 OU, IOPs 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS, gonioscopy 360° open to ciliary body band with moderate TM pigmentation, C/D 0.2 OU with intact neuroretinal rim, and 24-2 Humphrey fields without defects. No secondary causes identified. Impression: primary open-angle glaucoma (or ocular hypertension at high risk for POAG) requiring risk stratification (including CCT and RNFL imaging) and initiation of IOP-lowering management and close follow-up to achieve at least a 20% IOP reduction from baseline.
Primary Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma (POAG); Secondary open-angle glaucoma (pigmentary or pseudoexfoliation); Chronic angle-closure glaucoma; Optic nerve atrophy from anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (AION)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: OU


Plan
All Medications: β-blockers (selective and nonselective) topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors prostaglandin analogs adrenergic agonists miotics apraclonidine 5-fluorouracil mitomycin C
All Procedures: Goldmann applanation tonometry Tonopen/pneumotonometer measurement Central corneal thickness measurement (ultrasound pachymetry, anterior segment OCT) Gonioscopy Dilated stereoscopic funduscopic examination (fundus contact lens, Hruby lens, 78-D/90-D lenses, red-free imaging) 24-2 Humphrey automated perimetry (automated static threshold visual field testing) Manual kinetic and static perimetry SD-OCT and other optic nerve head/retinal nerve fiber layer imaging (confocal scanning laser ophthalmoscopy, scanning laser polarimetry) Selective laser trabeculoplasty (SLT) Argon laser trabeculoplasty (ALT) Laser peripheral iridotomy Trabeculectomy (with possible 5‑fluorouracil or mitomycin C) Seton implant surgery (Baerveldt, Molteno, Ahmed) Endoscopic cyclophotocoagulation Traditional transscleral cyclodestructive procedures (cryoablation/laser) Micropulse transscleral cyclophotocoagulation Minimally invasive glaucoma surgeries (trabecular meshwork micro‑bypass stents, intracanalicular scaffolding devices, supraciliary microstents, ab interno trabeculotomy, ab interno canaloplasty, subconjunctival filtering devices) Stereoscopic optic nerve photography Panretinal photocoagulation (as indicated)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up frequency based on disease severity, target IOP achievement, evidence of progression, and duration of control; at each visit assess visual acuity, IOP, medication side effects and compliance; periodically evaluate optic nerve (stereoscopic photos/bio) and repeat visual field testing as indicated; adjust therapy for failure to meet target IOP, progressive optic nerve/visual field loss, or medication side effects/compliance issues.
Patient Education: You have primary open-angle glaucoma (high eye pressure with open drainage angles). Although your vision and optic nerves are currently normal, untreated high pressure can damage the optic nerve over time. Key points: - Purpose of treatment: lower eye pressure to a safe “target” (typically ≥20% below current pressures) to prevent future nerve damage. - Tests and monitoring: regular IOP checks, central corneal thickness measurement, gonioscopy, optic nerve examination (stereoscopic photos and/or OCT), and automated visual fields are needed to track for progression. - Medical therapy: eye drops (prostaglandin analogs, β-blockers, topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, α-agonists, or miotics) are commonly used; often start in one eye to assess response. Take drops exactly as prescribed; report side effects and adherence problems. - Procedural options if needed: laser trabeculoplasty (SLT) can lower pressure and may be used before or instead of drops; trabeculectomy or tube shunts and minimally invasive glaucoma surgeries are options if medications/laser fail. Some procedures require pretreatment and short-term IOP checks after the procedure. - What to watch for: sudden vision changes, eye pain, severe redness, or signs of infection after any procedure — seek prompt care. - Lifestyle and follow-up: keep all scheduled visits and visual field/OCT testing; inform providers of other medical conditions or medications. Smoking cessation and general cardiovascular health are beneficial. You currently have 20/20 vision and no field or optic nerve damage, but ongoing monitoring and treatment adherence are essential to prevent future vision loss.
Referrals: Bac Tien Nguyen and Sally Byrd


CASE 21 — Primary Angle-Closure Glaucoma
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine eye exam
DURATION: Acute (onset described as acute)
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 51-year-old African American man presented for a routine eye exam with no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints; PMH notable for mild emphysema treated with inhalers. Visual acuities 20/20 OU; refractions -3.00+2.25x110 OD and -2.25+2.00x86 OS. Pupils reactive without APD. Slit-lamp unremarkable. IOPs 26 mm Hg OD and 32 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy: grade IV angles OU with clear ciliary body band 360°, 1+ trabecular meshwork pigment. Fundus and automated visual fields obtained. On exam the midperipheral iris of the left eye was bowed anteriorly (iris bombé) with peripheral corneal touch consistent with acute angle-closure in the left eye; fellow eye had a narrow, potentially occludable angle. No medications associated with angle closure; no signs of lens-induced or secondary causes.
History Narrative: A 51-year-old African American man presented for a routine eye exam. He had no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints. Past medical history was remarkable for mild emphysema for which he used inhalers.
Symptom Onset: Acute onset (left eye)
Symptom Duration: acute onset (sudden), single episode — duration: acute (onset at presentation)
Progression: Stable

Associated Symptoms: None reported

Impact on Daily Activities: No impact — patient was asymptomatic with 20/20 vision OU and no visual complaints.
Systemic Symptoms: Mild emphysema; uses inhalers; no other systemic symptoms reported.

Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma. Left eye: Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma; Left eye: Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Mild emphysema
Differential Diagnosis: Primary pupillary-block primary angle-closure glaucoma; Plateau iris–related angle closure; Lens-induced (phacomorphic/aphakic/subluxation) angle closure; Secondary angle-closure (neovascular, uveitic, malignant glaucoma, choroidal effusion)
Severity: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma (right); Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma (left)
Laterality: OS


Plan
All Medications: inhalers (for emphysema); isosorbide; glycerol; intravenous mannitol; acetazolamide (IV and oral); topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; pilocarpine 2%; topical beta‑blockers; alpha‑2 agonists; prostaglandin analogs; topical corticosteroids; analgesics; antiemetics
All Procedures: Gonioscopy; Applanation tonometry; Slit-lamp examination; Funduscopic exam (optic nerve evaluation); Automated visual fields; Laser iridotomy (recommended/definitive therapy)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Measure IOP within 30–120 minutes after laser surgery. - Follow-up exam within 1 week. - Additional follow-up at 4–8 weeks postoperatively. - More frequent visits if response inadequate. - Taper topical corticosteroids during postoperative period. - Perform pupillary dilation with postdilation IOP check and gonioscopy to confirm angle remains open; consider provocative testing if needed.
Patient Education: You have acute primary angle‑closure glaucoma in your left eye and a narrow, at‑risk angle in the right eye. Immediate goals are to lower eye pressure, relieve pain/blur, and open the blocked drainage angle to prevent optic nerve damage. Initial treatment may include oral or IV medications (osmotic agents like isosorbide or mannitol, and carbonic anhydrase inhibitors), topical drops (pilocarpine to constrict the pupil if effective, beta‑blockers or alpha‑2 agonists), and topical steroids for inflammation. Definitive treatment is a laser peripheral iridotomy to create an alternate fluid pathway; the fellow eye usually gets a prophylactic iridotomy because half of such fellow eyes can have an acute attack within 5 years. You may need additional procedures (laser iridoplasty, goniosynechialysis, or cataract surgery) if angle closure persists or a lens contributes. Expect close follow‑up shortly after any laser/surgery (IOP check within 30–120 minutes, then within a week, and at 4–8 weeks) and temporary steroid drops. Seek immediate care for sudden eye pain, vision loss, nausea/vomiting, or worsening redness.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care for sudden eye pain, vision loss, severe headache, nausea or vomiting; go to nearest emergency department or urgent eye clinic for urgent IOP-lowering treatment (topical agents, systemic acetazolamide or osmotics as appropriate) and prompt definitive therapy (laser peripheral iridotomy).


CASE 22 — Pigmentary Glaucoma
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye
DURATION: several hours
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had several hours of discomfort and my left eye is blurry."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old woman presented with several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye. She reported decreased vision to 20/60 in the left eye; IOPs were 14 mm Hg OD and 52 mm Hg OS. The left pupil was middilated and sluggish. Onset was acute over several hours; associated symptoms included mild eye redness and corneal epithelial edema. No prior similar episodes documented in the history provided.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old woman presented with several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye. Vision was 20/20 OD and 20/60 OS. Intraocular pressures were 14 mm Hg OD and 52 mm Hg OS. The left pupil was sluggish and middilated. Slit-lamp exam of the left eye showed mild conjunctival and episcleral hyperemia, mild corneal epithelial edema, a shallow peripheral anterior chamber, and a markedly convex (bombé) midperipheral iris. The lens had mild nuclear sclerosis. Gonioscopy of the left eye revealed marked convexity of the iris contour with no visible anterior chamber angle structures; the right eye showed an open but narrow anterior chamber angle. The optic nerve cups were small in both eyes. The left eye was treated with medical therapy and laser peripheral iridotomy, which opened the anterior chamber angle and reduced the IOP to normal.
Symptom Onset: several hours
Symptom Duration: several hours
Progression: Moderate-stage pigmentary glaucoma with progression evidenced by visual field defects (right: superior arcuate; left: early nasal step)
Characteristics: Secondary open-angle glaucoma affecting young myopic men with abnormal posterior iris bowing causing iris–zonule chafe, pigment dispersion (Krukenberg spindle, Sampaolesi’s line), densely pigmented trabecular meshwork, exercise-induced IOP spikes and blurry vision, responsive to medications, laser trabeculoplasty, and laser peripheral iridotomy.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exercise (physical exertion), close work, emotional state, and ambient light level
Alleviating Factors: Laser peripheral iridotomy (which opened the anterior chamber angle and reduced IOP to normal) and medical therapy (IOP-lowering medications)
Associated Symptoms: discomfort, blurred vision, eye redness, colored halos around lights, blurry vision with exercise
Previous Episodes: several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye (no prior similar episodes mentioned)
Prior Treatments: medical therapy and laser iridotomy
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely mild-to-moderate limitation from intermittent blurry vision with exercise and risk of acute IOP spikes; may restrict strenuous activity and require frequent follow-up and treatment adherence.
Risk Factors: - Young age (most commonly affects young adults) - Male sex (affects men ≈2× more than women) - Myopia (typically myopic eyes) - Exercise (can induce pigment liberation and IOP spikes) - Family history of angle-closure glaucoma increases risk for angle-closure (not pigmentary) but noted as relevant in evaluation


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen (ETDRS equivalent) testing: Right eye 20/20, Left eye 20/60
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 52 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival and episcleral hyperemia
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild corneal epithelial edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Left eye: Shallow peripheral anterior chamber with marked iris convexity (iris bombé) and absent peripheral anterior chamber; Right eye: Open but narrow anterior chamber angle.
Anterior Segment – Iris: Midperipheral iris markedly convex (iris bombé) with anterior bowing; pupil middilated and sluggish; midperipheral transillumination defects present.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Left eye: closed (marked convexity/iris bombé with no visible angle structures pre-iridotomy; opened after laser iridotomy) Right eye: narrow but open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Mild nuclear sclerosis


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Left pupil middilated and sluggish; right pupil not described (assumed normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: sluggish in left eye; (right eye not stated)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Sluggish
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: middilated and sluggish
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The conjunctiva and episclera were mildly hyperemic, and the cornea was mildly edematous. The pupil was middilated and the iris had a markedly convex configuration.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: The optic nerve cups were small in both eyes.
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular abnormality detected / macula grossly unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions identified; peripheral retina was flat and without retinal breaks or detachments.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 55-year-old woman with acute left eye IOP spike to 52 mm Hg, mid-dilated sluggish pupil, corneal epithelial edema, conjunctival/episcleral injection, shallow peripheral anterior chamber and marked anterior bowing of the midperipheral iris with closed angle on gonioscopy. Right eye with open but narrow angle. Optic nerves with small cups. Left eye responded to medical therapy and laser peripheral iridotomy with opening of the angle and normalization of IOP. Impression: Left eye acute angle-closure attack with pupillary block (iris bombe) relieved by iridotomy. Coexisting features consistent with pigment dispersion in general discussion but current presentation and exam most consistent with primary angle-closure from pupillary block in the left eye; fellow eye narrow angle at risk — consider prophylactic iridotomy.
Primary Diagnosis: Pigmentary glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma; Pseudoexfoliative glaucoma; Uveitic (inflammatory) glaucoma; Intraocular/ocular melanotic lesions (ocular melanosis or melanoma)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: pilocarpine (miotic) prostaglandin analogs beta-blockers carbonic anhydrase inhibitors alpha-adrenergic agonists
All Procedures: medical therapy; laser iridotomy; gonioscopy; slit-lamp biomicroscopy; laser peripheral iridotomy (LPI); argon laser trabeculoplasty (ALT); selective laser trabeculoplasty (SLT); incisional glaucoma surgery (trabeculectomy, Ex-Press shunt, glaucoma drainage implant); minimally invasive glaucoma surgery (iStent, gonioscopy-assisted transluminal trabeculotomy, endocyclophotocoagulation)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up individualized to treatment response and disease stability; schedule visits based on effectiveness of therapy, severity of disease, and patient reliability.
Patient Education: Explain that pigmentary glaucoma is caused by pigment rubbing off the back of the iris, which can block drainage and raise eye pressure; it most commonly affects younger, myopic men but can occur in others. Describe symptoms to watch for—blurry vision, colored halos, eye pain, redness, and sudden vision changes with exercise—and advise seeking urgent care for sudden vision loss or severe pain. Explain diagnostic tests they may undergo: visual acuity, intraocular pressure checks, slit-lamp exam, gonioscopy, optic nerve evaluation, and visual fields. Review treatment options and goals: eye drops (to lower pressure), possible laser procedures (laser trabeculoplasty or peripheral iridotomy) to reduce pigment liberation or improve outflow, and surgery if needed; emphasize that treatment aims to lower pressure to prevent optic nerve damage. Discuss common side effects of typical medications (e.g., ocular irritation, blurred vision, systemic effects with beta-blockers) and the rationale for miotics historically. Advise avoiding vigorous exercise that provokes symptoms until controlled, and report new/worsening symptoms promptly. Emphasize the need for lifelong monitoring: regular IOP checks, optic nerve exams, and visual field testing; inform about frequency based on disease stability but stress adherence to follow-up and medications. Offer reassurance that many treatments control pressure and preserve vision when followed.
Emergency Instructions: Return to emergency department or ophthalmologist immediately for sudden vision loss, severe eye pain, nausea/vomiting, new halos, marked redness, or if eye drops/laser fail to lower IOP. If IOP remains very high or vision worsens, go to the ED now.


CASE 23 — Neovascular Glaucoma
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine eye examination.
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old myopic man presenting for routine eye examination with no complaints, denying significant past ocular or medical history; found to have elevated intraocular pressures (28 and 26 mm Hg) on applanation and on exam had vertically oriented pigment on corneal endothelium, numerous spokelike iris transillumination defects, fully open angles with dense trabecular meshwork pigmentation, RNFL thinning and visual field defects consistent with early/moderate glaucoma.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old myopic man presents for routine eye examination with no complaints and denies any significant past ocular or medical history. He has best corrected visual acuity of 20/20 OU, no afferent pupillary defect, and IOPs by applanation of 28 mm Hg OD and 26 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp exam shows vertically oriented pigment deposition on each corneal endothelium and numerous spokelike iris transillumination defects OU. Gonioscopy reveals fully open angles (360°) OU with dense trabecular meshwork pigmentation bilaterally. Fundoscopy shows a right optic nerve cup-to-disc ratio ~0.7 with inferotemporal rim thinning and a left cup-to-disc ratio of 0.55; retina is otherwise normal. SD-OCT RNFL analysis demonstrates moderate inferotemporal thinning OD and mild thinning OS. Automated visual fields show a moderate superior arcuate defect OD and an early nasal step OS.
Characteristics: Neovascularization of anterior segment structures leading to secondary angle-closure glaucoma with iris and trabecular meshwork neovascularization, fibrovascular membrane formation, elevated IOP, pain/inflammation, often associated with ocular ischemia (e.g., diabetic retinopathy, retinal vein occlusion).


Subjective (cont.)
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely minimal to moderate impact — patient asymptomatic with 20/20 vision but elevated IOP and early glaucomatous damage, requiring treatment and monitoring that may transiently limit activities.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies any systemic symptoms
Risk Factors: Diabetic retinopathy; retinal vein occlusion (e.g., central retinal vein occlusion); retinal artery occlusion; carotid occlusive disease; sickle cell retinopathy; chronic uveitis; intraocular tumor; chronic retinal detachment; ocular ischemia (general)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 26 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation (Goldmann)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external examination of the orbits.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Vertically oriented pigment deposition on the corneal endothelium bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Numerous fine, irregular neovascular vessels at the pupillary margin progressing toward the iris root (iris neovascularization/NVI)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (360 degrees)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, clear crystalline lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: no afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: No afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Pupils – Other: no afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Extraocular Movements: Full initially (EOMI)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Vertically oriented pigment deposition on each corneal endothelium and numerous spokelike iris transillumination defects in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Right optic nerve: cup-to-disc ratio ~0.7 with inferotemporal rim thinning; Left optic nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.55
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma (NVG) due to anterior segment neovascularization; elevated IOPs requiring prompt medical lowering (oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, topical aqueous suppressants) with consideration of intravitreal anti‑VEGF and panretinal photocoagulation once posterior pathology evaluated; likely need for glaucoma filtering surgery (aqueous shunt) if IOP not controlled, with anti‑VEGF preoperatively to reduce bleeding risk; pain/inflammation control with topical steroids and cycloplegics; cyclodestructive procedures or enucleation reserved for blind, painful eyes.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Neovascular glaucoma; Acute angle-closure glaucoma; Fuchs’ heterochromic iridocyclitis; Traumatic/angle-recession glaucoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Angle closure with permanent synechial closure - Marked intraocular pressure elevation leading to glaucomatous optic neuropathy/vision loss - Hyphema from fragile neovascular vessels - Intraoperative and postoperative bleeding during glaucoma surgery - Failure of filtering surgery (high failure rate) - Corneal edema from high IOP - Painful blind eye possibly requiring cyclodestruction or enucleation - Recurrent neovascularization and need for repeated anti-VEGF or interventions


Plan
All Medications: oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; topical beta-blockers; topical alpha-2 agonists; topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; topical prednisolone; topical cycloplegics; prostaglandin analogs (use debated); intravitreal anti-VEGF agents; hyperosmotic agents (use with caution)
Follow-Up Instructions: Return for prompt panretinal photocoagulation and ophthalmology follow-up for glaucoma surgery evaluation; monitor IOP and retinal status; consider intravitreal anti‑VEGF prior to surgery.
Patient Education: - Neovascular glaucoma (NVG) is a serious form of glaucoma caused by abnormal new blood vessels in the eye that can block fluid drainage and raise eye pressure. - It often stems from underlying retinal ischemia (common causes: diabetic retinopathy, retinal vein occlusion); finding and treating the underlying cause is essential. - Urgent treatment is needed to lower intraocular pressure (IOP) to protect vision and reduce pain; initial care often includes oral and topical pressure-lowering medications. - Anti-VEGF injections into the eye can rapidly reduce neovascularization and help control bleeding and inflammation before definitive treatment. - Laser treatment (panretinal photocoagulation) to ischemic retina is commonly used when the view permits to decrease the VEGF drive. - Many patients will require surgical drainage (glaucoma tube/shunt) because medical therapy alone is often insufficient; surgery has higher risk of bleeding when vessels are active. - If vision is already nonfunctional and painful, procedures to reduce pain (cyclodestruction, steroids, or, rarely, enucleation) may be considered. - Attend urgent and frequent follow-up visits; report sudden vision loss, severe eye pain, redness, or nausea/vomiting immediately. - Inform your doctor about systemic conditions (diabetes, vascular disease, sickle cell) and medications; some treatments (oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, hyperosmotics) may have risks or require precautions. - Keep blood sugar and vascular risk factors well controlled and have regular eye exams to detect high-risk changes early.
Emergency Instructions: Lower intraocular pressure immediately (oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor ± topical aqueous suppressants such as beta-blocker, alpha‑2 agonist, topical CAI); consider topical steroid and cycloplegic for inflammation/pain; administer intravitreal anti‑VEGF once posterior causes like tumor/retinal detachment are excluded; consider anterior chamber paracentesis if pressure remains very high; arrange urgent ophthalmology/glaucoma referral for possible surgical intervention (aqueous shunt) and prompt panretinal photocoagulation when feasible.


CASE 24 — Inflammatory Glaucoma
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: a bad headache on the right side of my head, extreme redness and light sensitivity of the right eye, and nausea (sick to my stomach)
DURATION: Half a day
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "a bad headache on the right side of my head." "a sick to my stomach." "pink eye." "colored halos around lights."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 72-year-old man with acute half-day onset of severe right-sided headache, extreme redness, photophobia, nausea and one episode of vomiting; reports abrupt, painless severe decline in vision in the right eye ~3 months earlier diagnosed as ischemic central retinal vein occlusion.
History Narrative: A 72-year-old man presents to the emergency department complaining of “a bad headache on the right side of my head.” He also notes extreme redness and light sensitivity of the right eye and feels “sick to my stomach.” His symptoms have developed quickly over half a day. Fearing that this may be more than “pink eye,” the emergency department physician requests that an ophthalmologist evaluate the patient. Past medical history is remarkable for hypertension, diabetes, and arteriosclerosis. A review of systems is positive for nausea and one episode of vomiting. The patient had experienced an abrupt, painless, severe decline in vision in the right eye approximately 3 months earlier. He was diagnosed at that time with an ischemic central retinal vein occlusion by his ophthalmologist.
Symptom Onset: half a day
Symptom Duration: half a day
Characteristics: Inflammation-associated elevated intraocular pressure due to reduced trabecular outflow; may present acutely with corneal edema, conjunctival injection, severe pain, headache, nausea/vomiting, hyphema, iris neovascularization, and open angle (secondary open-angle/inflammatory glaucoma)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nausea and vomiting, exposure to bright light (photophobia), corneal edema, prior central retinal vein occlusion leading to iris neovascularization and hyphema, active intraocular inflammation (uveitis)
Associated Symptoms: bad headache on the right side, extreme redness, light sensitivity (photophobia), nausea, vomiting, blurry vision, eye pain
Previous Episodes: Abrupt, painless, severe decline in vision in the right eye ~3 months earlier (diagnosed as ischemic CRVO)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — severe vision loss and pain (right eye hand‑motions vision, corneal edema, nausea/vomiting, headache) likely greatly limit activities and cause marked functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: nausea and one episode of vomiting
Risk Factors: Hypertension; diabetes; arteriosclerosis; prior ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); intraocular inflammation/uveitis; neovascularization of the iris (NVG)


Objective Exam

Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm

Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: HM

Visual Acuity – Method: Hand motions
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 67 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 19 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes without lesions or ptosis

Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: The optic disc appears normal in the left eye; right optic nerve not visualized due to corneal edema (only a dull red reflex).
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize macula due to corneal edema; only a dull red reflex appreciated.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Severe hypertensive retinopathy


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 72-year-old man with acute right eye pain, redness, photophobia, nausea/vomiting and markedly elevated IOP OD (67 mm Hg) with corneal edema, conjunctival injection, small hyphema, anterior chamber cell/flare, minimally reactive iris with fine irregular branching surface vessels and history of prior ischemic CRVO OD.
