Findings most consistent with neovascular (secondary) glaucoma of the right eye (NVG) likely due to prior ischemic CRVO, with concomitant herpetic anterior uveitis considered in differential given KP pattern and iris atrophy features. Diagnosis: secondary open-angle inflammatory glaucoma/NVG OD, with possible herpetic anterior uveitis contributing; left eye without acute disease.
Primary Diagnosis: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Diabetes; Arteriosclerosis; Ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (right eye); Hypertensive retinopathy (left eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Neovascular glaucoma from ischemic CRVO; Herpetic anterior uveitis (herpes simplex/zoster) with secondary glaucoma; Fuchs’ heterochromic iridocyclitis; Posner–Schlossman syndrome
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: glaucomatous optic neuropathy; cataract formation; peripheral anterior synechiae (PAS) leading to secondary angle closure; corneal edema; cystoid macular edema (CME); band keratopathy; recurrent/persistent elevated IOP requiring glaucoma surgery; hyphema; vision loss


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone 1%; dexamethasone 0.1%; rimexolone; loteprednol; flurbiprofen; ketorolac; diclofenac; methotrexate; azathioprine; timolol; levobunolol; dorzolamide; brinzolamide; apraclonidine; dipivefrin; latanoprost (prostaglandin analogs); trifluridine; acyclovir; atropine; cyclopentolate; 5‑fluorouracil; mitomycin C
All Procedures: gonioscopy slit-lamp examination B-scan ultrasonography ophthalmoscopy serologic workup body imaging topical corticosteroid administration (e.g., prednisolone 1%, dexamethasone 0.1%) topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory agents (e.g., flurbiprofen, ketorolac, diclofenac) oral systemic immunosuppressive therapy (e.g., methotrexate, azathioprine) topical antivirals (trifluridine) oral antiviral therapy (acyclovir) cycloplegic agents (atropine, cyclopentolate) intraocular pressure-lowering topical medications (beta-blockers, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, adrenergic agonists, prostaglandin analogs) laser peripheral iridotomy surgical iridectomy trabeculectomy with antimetabolite (5-fluorouracil or mitomycin C) glaucoma drainage device implantation (e.g., Ahmed valve, Molteno implant) laser trabeculoplasty (contraindicated in active inflammation) laser cyclophotocoagulation
Follow-Up Instructions: Frequent follow-up with monitoring of inflammation and IOP; regular optic nerve exams and visual field testing; gonioscopy at each visit to assess for neovascularization or peripheral anterior synechiae; monitor for complications (CME, band keratopathy) and treat as needed; continue/adjust steroids to control inflammation despite risk of steroid-induced IOP elevation.
Patient Education: Your sudden severe eye pain, redness, light sensitivity, nausea, and very high eye pressure (67 mm Hg) are signs of a serious eye condition that can threaten vision. The likely cause is neovascular glaucoma (new abnormal blood vessels on the iris) related to your prior retinal vein occlusion. Key points you should know and do now: - This is an urgent problem: prompt treatment is needed to lower the eye pressure and control inflammation to prevent permanent vision loss. - You may need a combination of medicines: topical and/or oral pressure-lowering drugs, topical steroids to reduce inflammation, and dilating drops to lessen pain and prevent pupil adhesions. If herpetic infection is suspected, antiviral medication may be used. - If medications do not control the pressure or the eye remains inflamed, eye surgery (such as a glaucoma drainage device or filtering surgery) may be required. - Expect close, frequent follow-up visits for pressure checks, eye exams, and gonioscopy to monitor the angle and look for new vessels or synechiae. - Because some treatments (steroids) can raise eye pressure, your doctor will balance inflammation control with glaucoma management and adjust therapy as needed. - Report immediately any worsening pain, sudden vision loss, new floaters, or increased redness/nausea. - Share your full medical history (diabetes, hypertension, prior retinal vein occlusion) and all medications; systemic conditions can affect eye treatment and prognosis. If you have questions about the proposed medications or surgery, ask your ophthalmologist to explain the expected benefits, risks, and follow-up plan.
Referrals: Ophthalmology (glaucoma/uveitis specialist)


CASE 25 — Primary Congenital Glaucoma
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain of the left eye (2 days)
DURATION: 2 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 38‑year‑old Caucasian man with a 2‑day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain of the left eye; prior ocular history notable for one episode of left herpes simplex keratitis 12 months earlier treated with trifluridine.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain involving his left eye. His ocular history is notable for one episode of herpes simplex keratitis involving the left eye 12 months ago, which resolved after treatment with trifluridine eye drops.
Symptom Onset: 2-day history
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Characteristics: Cloudy corneas, tearing (epiphora), photophobia, corneal enlargement (buphthalmos), Haab’s striae (Descemet breaks), elevated IOP, enlarged axial length, iris thinning/high flat insertion with peripheral anterior stromal thinning, open angles with prominent iris processes, moderate trabecular pigmentation, asymmetric (often bilateral) presentation, presents within first 2 years of life, autosomal recessive inheritance risk, male predominance


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain
Previous Episodes: one episode of herpes simplex keratitis involving the left eye 12 months ago
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — activities affected by blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain in the left eye for 2 days (reduced vision to 20/60, improved to 20/40 with pinhole), limiting comfortable visual tasks and causing discomfort.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: - Family history of glaucoma (10–40% of cases) - Consanguinity (higher risk in consanguineous populations) - Autosomal recessive inheritance (genetic predisposition) - Male sex (slight male predominance)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 46 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Left cornea: moderate central corneal haze with fine stellate keratic precipitates; Right cornea: enlarged with subtle epithelial edema (better seen with microscope)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep centrally and peripherally
Anterior Segment – Iris: Patchy atrophy of the pupillary sphincter with transillumination defects; no nodules, no heterochromia, no posterior synechiae
Anterior Segment – Angle: open to the ciliary body with high flat insertion and prominent iris processes (angles open to ciliary band)
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Round, reactive pupils of normal size in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric reaction to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: unremarkable. Left eye: cornea with fine, stellate diffuse keratic precipitates; anterior chamber deep centrally and peripherally with 2+ cells and aqueous flare; patchy iris pupillary sphincter atrophy on transillumination; no iris nodules, heterochromia, posterior synechiae; lens clear.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic discs were symmetric with healthy rims
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 38-year-old Caucasian man with acute 2-day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain in the left eye. Ocular history notable for remote (12 months) left herpes simplex keratitis treated successfully with trifluridine. Exam: OD 20/20, OS 20/60 improving to 20/40 with pinhole. IOPs 12 mm Hg OD and 46 mm Hg OS. Left slit-lamp: fine stellate KPs diffusely, 2+ anterior chamber cells and flare, patchy iris sphincter atrophy on transillumination, deep AC, no posterior synechiae, clear lens. Gonioscopy: angles open to ciliary band OU with moderate TM pigmentation and prominent iris processes, no PAS. Fundus: clear vitreous, symmetric healthy optic discs. Impression: Acute unilateral inflammatory hypertensive episode of the left eye most consistent with herpetic anterior uveitis/keratouveitis leading to markedly elevated IOP. Prior history of HSV keratitis supports herpetic etiology. Secondary causes such as Fuchs or other uveitides less likely given acute painful presentation, KPs and iris atrophy. Management plan: urgent IOP-lowering therapy and anti-inflammatory/antiviral treatment tailored to presumed herpetic uveitis; close follow-up with IOP monitoring and consider glaucoma surgery only if IOP remains uncontrolled despite medical therapy.
Primary Diagnosis: Primary Congenital Glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Herpes simplex keratitis (history, left eye; resolved)
Differential Diagnosis: Primary congenital glaucoma; Peters anomaly (anterior segment dysgenesis); congenital corneal endothelial dystrophy (CHED); secondary glaucoma from uveitis or ocular tumor
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Unilateral (left)


Plan
All Medications: trifluridine eye drops; topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (dorzolamide); brimonidine; selective beta‑blockers (topical, reduced 0.25% dose in young children); topical alpha‑2 agonists (contraindicated in infants) ; oral acetazolamide (5–10 mg/kg every 6–8 hours)
All Procedures: goniotomy; trabeculotomy; drainage device placement (Baerveldt shunt); trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (rare/with antimetabolite); cyclodestructive procedures; examination under anesthesia; anterior chamber examination including gonioscopy; A-scan ultrasonography; Tonopen/hand-held applanation IOP measurement; topical/ oral medical therapy administration (preoperative)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Topical cycloplegics and steroids for 1–2 weeks postoperatively. - Second exam under anesthesia at 8–10 weeks with repeat measurements; if IOP acceptable, dilate for disc photography and fundus exam. - Exams under anesthesia every 2–3 months until IOP stable or child can be examined in clinic. - Once examable in clinic: every 3–4 months, then extend to every 6 months when stable. - By age 3–4 years, routine office examinations.
Patient Education: Primary congenital glaucoma (PCG) is a condition in infants/young children where high eye pressure causes enlarged, cloudy eyes, tearing, and light sensitivity. Early diagnosis and prompt surgical treatment (goniotomy or trabeculotomy) are essential to protect vision. Before surgery, medicines (topical or oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; cautious low‑dose beta‑blocker use) may be used to lower pressure and clear corneal edema. Surgery is typically done under general anesthesia and often at the same time as the full exam under anesthesia. After angle surgery, short‑term steroid and cycloplegic eye drops are used; follow‑up exams under anesthesia are usually needed at 8–10 weeks and then every 2–3 months until the child can be examined in clinic. If pressure remains high after complete angle treatment, options include glaucoma drainage implants (preferred in infants) or, rarely, trabeculectomy with mitomycin C or cyclodestructive procedures. Long-term monitoring of eye size, corneal clarity, intraocular pressure, and optic nerve health is required; visual rehabilitation (amblyopia treatment, glasses) may be necessary. Seek urgent ophthalmic care for increasing cloudiness, tearing, photophobia, or eye enlargement.
Referrals: Pediatrician and anesthesiologist clearance for general anesthesia; surgeon experienced in pediatric angle surgery (for examination under anesthesia and operative treatment).


CASE 26 — Ocular Hypotony
=========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: "cloudy eyes and tearing" for 3 to 4 weeks
DURATION: 3 to 4 weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "cloudy eyes and tearing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 3-month-old female referred after her mother noted “cloudy eyes and tearing” for 3–4 weeks; photophobic and more comfortable in dim light; diffuse corneal haze OU (R>L); corneal diameters ~12mm OD and 11mm OS; IOP measured by iCare while nursing 45 mm Hg OD and 36 mm Hg OS.
History Narrative: A 3-month-old female infant was referred after her mother noted “cloudy eyes and tearing” for 3–4 weeks. She was a full-term, uneventful pregnancy and delivery with no forceps use. No family history of glaucoma; a healthy 3‑year‑old brother. On office exam the infant was photophobic and more comfortable in dim illumination. Both eyes had diffuse corneal haze (R>L). Estimated corneal diameters were 12 mm OD and 11 mm OS. IOP measured with iCare while the infant nursed was 45 mm Hg OD and 36 mm Hg OS.
Symptom Onset: 3–4 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 to 4 weeks
Progression: Resolved—improved after scleral flap suturing with IOP stabilized in low teens (no recurrent hypotony at 6 years)
Characteristics: Hypotony with maculopathy; shallow anterior chamber; chorioretinal folds and disc swelling; low IOP (profound), overfiltration-related bleb (exuberant, elevated)


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Cycloplegic agents, discontinuation of aqueous suppressants, topical corticosteroids (with caution), bandage contact lens, autologous blood patch, bleb compression sutures, resuturing of scleral flap (surgical revision), observation/conservative management
Associated Symptoms: cloudy eyes; tearing; photophobia; more comfortable in dim illumination
Prior Treatments: Cycloplegic agents; conservative observation; autologous blood patch; surgical revision with placement of two additional scleral flap sutures (bleb not excised); later suture lysis
Risk Factors: Filtration surgery (trabeculectomy) leading to primary overfiltration, wound leak, cyclodialysis cleft, use of aqueous suppressants, postoperative conjunctival thinning (bleb formation), inflammation, ocular ischemia, prior ocular surgery/trauma.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (measured with fixation and age-appropriate testing under anesthesia when needed)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 45 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 36 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: iCare tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Diffuse corneal haze (right greater than left)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Shallow
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear crystalline lens bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Diffuse corneal haze, right greater than left; photophobic; both corneas with swollen/edematous appearance; shallow anterior chamber (noted in discussion of AC shallowing)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Disc swelling and chorioretinal folds consistent with optic disc edema
Fundus Exam – Macula: Maculopathy with chorioretinal folds (hypotonous maculopathy)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Hypotony due to primary overfiltration following filtration surgery, with persistent hypotony and maculopathy refractory to medical therapy and autologous blood patch, requiring surgical resuturing of the scleral flap with subsequent normalization of IOP and restoration of axial length, refraction, and visual acuity.
Primary Diagnosis: Hypotony due to primary overfiltration
Differential Diagnosis: Primary overfiltration; Hyposecretion of aqueous (pharmacologic or inflammatory); Bleb leak; Cyclodialysis cleft
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Hypotony with maculopathy


Plan
All Medications: aqueous suppressants;cycloplegic agents;topical corticosteroids;autologous blood (for blood patch)
All Procedures: Seidel test; fundoscopic examination; gonioscopy; intraocular pressure measurement with iCare; examination under anesthesia (pressure measurement, corneal diameter, ultrasonography/axial length, anterior segment exam, gonioscopy); cycloplegic therapy; topical corticosteroids; autologous blood patch; placement of two additional scleral flap sutures (scleral flap resuturing/bleb revision); suture lysis.
Follow-Up Instructions: Cycloplegic agents and conservative observation; if no improvement, autologous blood patch (attempted) followed by surgical revision with placement of additional scleral flap sutures; postoperative monitoring of IOP, axial length, refraction, and visual acuity with suture lysis as indicated (one month postrevision in this case) and long-term IOP follow-up.
Patient Education: Explain what ocular hypotony is, why it can happen after glaucoma filtration surgery, and the potential symptoms (blurred vision, shallow anterior chamber, light sensitivity). Emphasize steps in evaluation: history of medications, Seidel test for bleb leak, fundus exam for choroidal effusion, gonioscopy for cyclodialysis cleft, and ultrasound if media opaque. Advise immediate actions: stop all aqueous‑suppressing eye drops, use cycloplegic drops and topical corticosteroids as directed, and seek prompt ophthalmology follow‑up. Describe possible treatments depending on cause: observation for mild cases; bandage contact lens, autologous blood patch, or bleb compression for overfiltration; wound repair or scleral flap resuturing for persistent hypotony; reformation of the anterior chamber with viscoelastic if shallow. Explain risks of untreated hypotony (choroidal effusion, maculopathy, vision loss) and the importance of timely care. Provide return‑precautions: worsening vision, increasing pain, new floaters, or persistent drainage warrant urgent evaluation.


CASE 27 — PosttrabeculectomyWound Leak
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Worsening vision in the right eye for 1 year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "worsening vision"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 45-year-old woman with aphakic right eye and history of inflammatory glaucoma, referred for surgical management. Preop vision 20/20 with aphakic correction, IOP 40 mm Hg on maximal medical therapy, CCT 535 µm, 1+ AC cell and flare, keratic precipitates, vitreous at pupillary plane, optic disc cupped to 0.9 with inferior notch and superior altitudinal field defect. Underwent trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy; IOP 9–12 mm Hg first postoperative year. One year after surgery developed worsening vision to 20/60 (improved to 20/20 with +1.50 D), noted highly elevated avascular cystic bleb, moderate chorioretinal folds at posterior pole and macula, and IOP 3 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman with inflammatory glaucoma in her aphakic right eye was referred for surgical management. Her left eye had only light perception since birth from persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous. Preoperatively, with aphakic correction her visual acuity was 20/20 and intraocular pressure (IOP) was 40 mm Hg on maximum medical therapy including oral agents. Central corneal thickness was 535 μm in each eye. The anterior chamber had 1+ cell and flare with scattered fine keratic precipitates and vitreous at the pupillary plane. The optic disc was markedly excavated with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.9 with an inferior notch, and the visual field showed a superior altitudinal defect. A trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy was performed without incident. Postoperatively visual acuity remained 20/20 and IOP was 9–12 mm Hg for the first postoperative year. One year after surgery she presented with worsening vision: visual acuity was 20/60, which improved to 20/20 with a +1.50 D hyperopic correction. Exam showed a highly elevated, avascular, cystic bleb; moderate chorioretinal folds at the posterior pole and macula; and an IOP of 3 mm Hg.
Symptom Onset: 1 year after surgery
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Worsening vision over 1 year after surgery (late progression leading to hypotony maculopathy)
Characteristics: Highly elevated, avascular, cystic bleb (large, avascular, Seidel-negative)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Use of antimetabolites (mitomycin C/5‑FU) leading to thin, avascular cystic bleb and overfiltration causing chronic hypotony
Associated Symptoms: Worsening vision
Prior Treatments: Maximum medical therapy including oral agents; trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — temporary visual blurring to 20/60 (correctable to 20/20 with +1.50 D) and hypotony-related maculopathy causing transient visual disturbance, likely interfering with activities requiring sharp central vision until resolved.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Use of antimetabolites (mitomycin C/5‑FU) leading to thin, avascular cystic blebs; young age; high myopia; prior inflammation/aphakia; prior vitrectomy; overfiltration (large ostium/loose sutures); conjunctival ischemia/thin conjunctiva; wound trauma or buttonhole; chronic topical/ systemic aqueous suppression.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: LP
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart at distance
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 3 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: highly elevated, avascular, cystic bleb
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris with no synechiae; vitreous at pupillary plane noted (aphakic eye)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The bleb was highly elevated, avascular, and cystic; the eye was quiet and noninflamed; moderate chorioretinal folds involving the posterior pole and macula were noted.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Markedly excavated with cup-to-disc ratio 0.9 and an inferior notch
Fundus Exam – Macula: Moderate chorioretinal folds involving the posterior pole and macula; maculopathy with macular striae
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Engorgement and tortuosity
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal hemorrhage; peripheral exam unremarkable/NORMAL


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Late bleb leak with chronic hypotony and hypotony maculopathy in the right aphakic eye following trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (IOP 3 mm Hg, highly elevated avascular cystic bleb, Seidel-negative at presentation but bleb ischemic; decreased vision from macular chorioretinal folds and +1.50 D hyperopic shift).
Primary Diagnosis: Late bleb leak
Secondary Diagnoses: Inflammatory glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Late bleb leak; Overfiltration causing chronic hypotony; Choroidal effusion with hypotony maculopathy
Severity: Late
Laterality: right
Complications: worsening vision; hypotony (IOP 3 mm Hg); moderate chorioretinal folds involving posterior pole and macula; hypotony maculopathy; hyperopic shift (+1.50 D); bleb leakage risk (late bleb leak)


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C topical corticosteroids topical aqueous suppressants systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors topical phenylephrine beta-blockers autologous blood systemic corticosteroids cycloplegics
All Procedures: Trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; subtotal vitrectomy; Seidel test; gonioscopy; slit‑lamp and fundus examinations; axial length measurement; refraction; autologous blood injection (described as treatment option)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow closely for recurrent leaks if an ischemic bleb remains; monitor IOP, visual acuity, and bleb appearance; return promptly for any recurrence of leak, hypotony signs, decreased vision, or infection.
Patient Education: Explain that the low eye pressure and vision changes are due to a late bleb leak after trabeculectomy; describe signs to watch for (increased redness, pain, sudden vision loss, discharge) and need to seek immediate care for these symptoms; advise temporary measures taught (use of prescribed aqueous‑suppressing drops or stopping them as directed, placement of a pressure patch or bandage contact lens only if instructed by surgeon); explain possible treatments (observation with aqueous suppression, autologous blood injection, laser treatments, or surgical bleb revision/patch graft) and that definitive repair may be required to prevent infection and permanent vision loss; discuss risks of delayed treatment (hypotony maculopathy, choroidal effusions, infection/blebitis, endophthalmitis); instruct on postoperative follow‑up schedule and importance of adherence; recommend avoiding eye rubbing and trauma to the eye; provide contact information and clear return precautions (worsening vision, severe pain, increasing redness, purulent discharge, or new floaters).


CASE 28 — Failing Filtering Bleb
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Failing Filtering Bleb
DURATION: 5 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 59-year-old woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma who underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye; five months postoperatively she presented with a dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye. On exam BCVA 20/100 OD, 20/30 OS; right anterior chamber very shallow and IOP 2 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 59-year-old woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye. The scleral flap was closed with two 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s capsule was closed with a running locking 8–0 Vicryl suture and the conjunctiva with a running nonlocking 8–0 dissolvable suture. Five months postoperatively she presented with a dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye. Best-corrected visual acuity was 20/100 OD and 20/30 OS. The right anterior chamber was very shallow and the intraocular pressure was 2 mm Hg.
Symptom Onset: 5 months postoperatively
Symptom Duration: 5 months
Progression: Late filtration failure
Characteristics: Late filtration failure; flat (low) filtering bleb with very shallow/flat anterior chamber and hypotony (IOP 2 mm Hg)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: - Young age - Inflammatory conditions (e.g., uveitis) - Prior failed filtering or other conjunctival surgery - Neovascular glaucoma - Aphakia - Iatrogenic factors (excessive cautery, uncontrolled hemorrhage, excessive intraocular manipulation)
Associated Symptoms: dramatic decrease in vision, very shallow anterior chamber, low intraocular pressure (hypotony)
Previous Episodes: 1 prior filtering procedure
Prior Treatments: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C (scleral flap closed with two 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s closed with running locking 8–0 Vicryl; conjunctiva closed with running nonlocking 8–0 Vicryl)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant visual reduction in right eye (20/100) causing decreased vision but not complete disability; likely interferes with reading/driving but some function preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - young age - race - inflammatory conditions such as uveitis - history of prior failed filtering procedure or other conjunctival procedure - neovascular glaucoma - aphakia - iatrogenic surgical factors (excessive cautery, failure to control hemorrhage, excessive intraocular manipulation)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N24/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (best-corrected visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 2 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: flat filtering bleb with shallow/low bleb and conjunctival scarring (episcleral fibrosis)
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Shallow anterior chamber (very shallow)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: very shallow
Anterior Segment – Angle: Very shallow (almost flat) anterior chamber with likely closed/very narrow angle


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, round pupils of normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Flat filtering bleb; anterior chamber very shallow in the right eye.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio increased consistent with advanced glaucomatous optic neuropathy (likely large cup, e.g., C/D ≈ 0.9)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to assess macula — N/A


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Late filtration failure with shallow anterior chamber and persistent hypotony (IOP 2 mm Hg) following trabeculectomy, consistent with a failed/flat filtering bleb.
Primary Diagnosis: Late filtration failure
Differential Diagnosis: Encapsulated filtering bleb (Tenon’s cyst); Tight scleral flap sutures; Occlusion of the sclerostomy (tissue incarceration/membranous occlusion); Episcleral fibrosis/ring of steel
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Late filtration failure


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C, 10–0 nylon (suture) [not a medication], 8–0 Vicryl (suture) [not a medication], topical anti-inflammatory agents, IOP-lowering medication
All Procedures: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C application; scleral flap closure with 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s capsule closure with running locking 8–0 Vicryl suture; conjunctival closure with running nonlocking 8–0 dissolvable suture; gonioscopy; digital ocular massage; argon laser suture lysis; releasable suture removal; needle revision of bleb; more aggressive surgical bleb revision (e.g., surgical reopening of scleral flap/bleb revision)
Follow-Up Instructions: Once IOP is controlled, follow closely for signs of recurrent failure; continue IOP-lowering medication as needed to maintain low IOP and monitor regularly.


CASE 29 — Flat Anterior Chamber
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Flat anterior chamber and elevated intraocular pressure in the right eye
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old African American woman with advanced primary open‑angle glaucoma status post right limbus‑based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C; at 1 year postop IOP was at target with a moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts. Three months later she presented with IOP 28 mm Hg, a deep anterior chamber but an almost flat filtering bleb; subsequently found to have central and peripheral shallowing of the anterior chamber with a patent peripheral iridectomy, no bleb or wound leak, not hypotonous, no choroidal elevation on funduscopy/ultrasound, and visible ciliary processes through the iridectomy — consistent with aqueous misdirection in the right eye.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old African American woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma and a target intraocular pressure of 15 mm Hg underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye. At 1-year postoperative visit the IOP was at target and a moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts was present. Three months later she presented with IOP of 28 mm Hg; the anterior chamber was deep but the filtering bleb was almost flat. There was no bleb or wound leak and the eye was not hypotonous. A patent peripheral iridectomy was present with central as well as peripheral shallowing and no iris bombé. Funduscopy and ultrasound demonstrated no choroidal elevation, and the ciliary processes were easily visible through the iridectomy. These findings led to the diagnosis of aqueous misdirection in the right eye.
Symptom Onset: 3 months after the 1-year postoperative visit (i.e., 15 months after surgery)
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progressive worsening until diagnosis of aqueous misdirection with elevated IOP (progressive)
Characteristics: Aqueous misdirection (ciliary block, malignant glaucoma): flat/very shallow central and peripheral anterior chamber despite patent iridectomy; normal or elevated IOP (may be normal initially); no choroidal effusion/hemorrhage; occurs after intraocular surgery (often in eyes predisposed to angle-closure, short axial length); forward rotation of ciliary body with anterior displacement of lens/vitreous; visible ciliary processes through iridectomy; not responsive to iridectomy alone.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Short axial length/angle‑closure anatomy, prior laser or incisional glaucoma surgery (trabeculectomy), discontinuation of cycloplegics, use of miotics, eyes predisposed to angle closure (phakic eyes)
Alleviating Factors: Cycloplegic–mydriatic therapy (e.g., atropine 1% ± phenylephrine 2.5%), topical aqueous suppressants and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, oral/IV osmotic agents, intracameral viscoelastic injection, laser disruption of anterior hyaloid/posterior capsule (Nd:YAG), argon laser ciliary process ablation, and pars plana vitrectomy with anterior vitreous/ hyaloid removal
Associated Symptoms: Flat (very shallow) central and peripheral anterior chamber; elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) of 28 mm Hg; absence of iris bombé; patent peripheral iridectomy; no choroidal elevations on funduscopy/ultrasound; bleb almost flat (reduced bleb height)
Prior Treatments: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C (right eye)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — visual impairment from elevated IOP and shallow/flat anterior chamber requiring urgent medical/surgical treatment likely limited daily activities until resolved.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Short axial length (predisposition to angle-closure) - Prior laser or incisional intraocular surgery (e.g., trabeculectomy) - Eyes with chronic angle-closure glaucoma (or narrow angles) - Phakic status (presence of natural lens) - Prior use of antimetabolites (mitomycin C) and filtering surgery - Discontinuation of cycloplegics / use of miotics - Advanced glaucoma (history of glaucoma surgery) - African American ethnicity (patient context—listed in case)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: No conjunctival injection; bleb present (moderately elevated, diffuse ischemic with microcysts)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Flat (very shallow)
Anterior Segment – Iris: patent peripheral iridectomy present; iris not bowed (no iris bombé); ciliary processes visible through iridectomy
Anterior Segment – Angle: Closed (appositional closure of the angle)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive (no iris bombe)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts at 1 year post‑op; at presentation 3 months later the filtering bleb was almost flat; anterior chamber initially deep at 1 year, then central and peripheral anterior chamber shallow/flat with no iris bombé; patent peripheral iridectomy; ciliary processes easily visible through the iridectomy; no bleb or wound leak.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio increased consistent with advanced glaucoma (marked optic nerve cupping).
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize macula — no choroidal elevation or hemorrhage; macula grossly flat and unremarkable (no focal macular lesions noted)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No choroidal elevations; fundus otherwise unremarkable in the periphery


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 53-year-old African American woman s/p trabeculectomy OD with MMC now presenting with a flat anterior chamber centrally and peripherally, IOP 28 mm Hg, a low/flat filtering bleb without wound leak, patent peripheral iridectomy, visible ciliary processes through the iridectomy, and no choroidal elevations on funduscopy/ultrasound. Findings and clinical course are most consistent with aqueous misdirection (ciliary block/malignant glaucoma) of the right eye. Medical management with cycloplegics (atropine 1% and phenylephrine 2.5%), topical and oral aqueous suppressants, and consideration of osmotic therapy was initiated; if refractory, anterior hyaloid disruption with Nd:YAG laser or pars plana vitrectomy with anterior vitreous removal and anterior chamber reformation are recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Aqueous misdirection
Differential Diagnosis: Aqueous misdirection (ciliary block/malignant glaucoma); Overfiltration with hypotony; Serous choroidal effusion; Pupillary block
Severity: Advanced
Laterality: Right
Complications: Aqueous misdirection (malignant glaucoma) leading to a flat/shallow anterior chamber with potential elevated IOP, possible need for medical therapy, laser or pars plana vitrectomy; risk of recurrence and need for prolonged cycloplegia


Plan
All Medications: Atropine 1% ophthalmic; Phenylephrine 2.5% ophthalmic; Topical aqueous suppressants (e.g., beta-blocker or prostaglandin—unspecified); Oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (unspecified); Oral or intravenous osmotic agent (unspecified); Intracameral viscoelastic (unspecified)
All Procedures: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C
Follow-Up Instructions: Maintain cycloplegia (e.g., atropine 1% daily) long-term as directed; avoid miotics; monitor IOP and anterior chamber depth regularly; if vitrectomy performed, cycloplegia may be discontinued cautiously in phakic eyes; in fellow eye perform prophylactic laser iridotomy and avoid miotics; return promptly for recurrence of shallowing, pain, or IOP rise.
Patient Education: Aqueous misdirection (also called ciliary block or malignant glaucoma) is a rare complication after eye surgery where fluid is pushed backward into the vitreous, causing the front chamber of the eye to become very shallow and the pressure to rise. Signs to watch for: worsening vision, eye pain, redness, and a very shallow or flat front chamber despite a surgical iridectomy. Initial treatment is medical: strong eyedrops to dilate and rotate the lens/ciliary body (e.g., atropine), medications to lower fluid production (topical aqueous suppressants and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor), and sometimes oral or IV osmotics; do not use miotic drops (they can worsen the problem). If drops and lasers don’t work, procedures (YAG laser or vitrectomy) may be needed to disrupt the anterior vitreous and restore normal fluid flow. If you have had this diagnosis, take drops exactly as prescribed, avoid miotics, report any sudden vision change, severe pain, or increased redness immediately, and attend all follow-up visits because recurrence is possible and further surgery may be required.


CASE 30 — Persistent Choroidal Detachment
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Persistent choroidal detachment
DURATION: 1 day
LATERALITY: Right eye
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old woman with bilateral chronic angle-closure glaucoma who presented 1 day after a mitomycin trabeculectomy with a shallow anterior chamber in the right eye, reduced vision (right eye 20/400, left 20/40), anterior chamber cells 3+, peripheral iridocorneal contact to within 1 mm of the pupil and central shallowing with a posterior chamber IOL 0.5 mm posterior to the endothelium; right eye IOP 34 mm Hg, left 12 mm Hg; filtration bleb elevated and Seidel-negative on the right; B-scan ultrasonography of the right eye showed a flat retina and no choroidal effusions.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old woman with a history of bilateral chronic angle-closure glaucoma presented 1 day after a mitomycin trabeculectomy in the right eye with a shallow anterior chamber. She had right eye visual acuity 20/400 and left eye 20/40; IOPs were 34 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS. She reported recent postoperative status following trabeculectomy; no specific symptoms beyond shallow chamber and decreased vision are recorded.
Symptom Onset: 1 day after a mitomycin trabeculectomy
Symptom Duration: 1 day
Progression: Resolved after observation/surgical drainage as indicated
Characteristics: Serous, bullous, smooth, orange/tan, typically four lobes (one per quadrant), echolucent on B-scan.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Observation (spontaneous resolution), elevation of IOP, discontinuation of systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors and topical aqueous suppressants, discontinuation of topical beta‑blocker in contralateral eye, topical steroids and cycloplegics (trial)
Associated Symptoms: Shallow anterior chamber, decreased vision (20/400 OD), anterior chamber cells (3+), poor fundus view with excellent red reflex, moderate pain not described (no acute pain), hypotony? (IOP 34mm Hg OD — actually high), nausea/vomiting not mentioned
Prior Treatments: mitomycin trabeculectomy
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — vision reduced to 20/400 in affected eye may impair depth perception and tasks requiring bilateral vision, but unilateral disease allows many daily activities to continue with difficulty.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Glaucoma filtration surgery (mitomycin trabeculectomy) leading to postoperative hypotony; chronic angle-closure glaucoma; use of topical/systemic aqueous suppressants (eg, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, beta-blockers); overfiltration or bleb leak; intraocular inflammation (iridocyclitis)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 34 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonometry (Goldmann applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: A moderately elevated filtration bleb, negative Seidel’s test
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Central shallowing with posterior chamber IOL located 0.5 mm posterior to the corneal endothelium; lenticulocorneal touch (iridocorneal contact extending to within 1 mm of the pupillary margin)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Shallow with iridocorneal touch from periphery to within 1 mm of the pupillary margin; central shallowing present (AC depth ~0.5 mm posterior chamber IOL 0.5 mm posterior to endothelium).
Anterior Segment – Iris: Iridocorneal contact from periphery to within 1 mm of pupillary margin; surgical iridectomy patent; anterior chamber shallow centrally and peripherally
Anterior Segment – Angle: Closed (iridocorneal contact peripherally with anterior chamber shallow; history of chronic angle-closure glaucoma)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (pseudophakia)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive, round, mid-dilated (≈3–4 mm)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Small (may prohibit adequate visualization of the posterior pole)
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: moderately elevated filtration bleb negative for Seidel’s testing; shallow anterior chamber with iridocorneal contact from periphery to within 1 mm of pupillary margin; central shallowing with posterior chamber IOL located 0.5 mm posterior to corneal endothelium; anterior chamber cells 3+; surgical iridectomy patent with visible ciliary processes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: moderate glaucomatous cupping
Fundus Exam – Macula: Excellent red reflex; poor view of posterior pole in the right eye (no macular abnormality visualized)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Persistent serous choroidal detachment.
Primary Diagnosis: Persistent serous choroidal detachment
Differential Diagnosis: Overfiltration/bleb leak; Serous choroidal effusion; Suprachoroidal hemorrhage; Retinal detachment
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Persistent serous choroidal detachment


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin, topical steroid, topical cycloplegic agent, topical beta-blocker, topical aqueous suppressants, systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (e.g., oral acetazolamide), oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, balanced salt solution (BSS), viscoelastic, subconjunctival antibiotics, subconjunctival steroids
All Procedures: mitomycin trabeculectomy; surgical iridectomy confirmed patent; B‑scan ultrasonography of the right eye; slit‑lamp examination; Seidel’s testing; paracentesis with anterior chamber reformation (BSS/viscoelastic) and anterior chamber maintainer placement; radial conjunctival incision and 2–3 mm sclerostomy with suprachoroidal drainage and sclerostomy punch; conjunctival closure with absorbable suture; subconjunctival antibiotic and steroid injection; postoperative topical steroid, antibiotic, and cycloplegic therapy.
Follow-Up Instructions: Examine 1 day postoperatively; place on a brief course of topical steroid, topical antibiotic, and cycloplegic (if phakic); monitor for persistent effusion or drainage and confirm prompt resolution and visual recovery.
Patient Education: Choroidal effusions are fluid collections under the choroid that can occur after glaucoma surgery. They often cause a shallow front chamber and may lower vision if large. Most resolve on their own with observation and measures to raise eye pressure slightly (stopping some glaucoma medicines in the affected eye and sometimes the fellow eye). Your surgeon may recommend topical steroid and cycloplegic drops to reduce inflammation and comfort; systemic steroids are generally not used. If the effusion causes persistent vision loss, lenticulocorneal touch, or corneal/retinal folds, a simple drainage procedure can be performed to remove the fluid with rapid visual recovery. Report increasing pain, sudden vision loss, or redness immediately, and expect close follow-up until the fluid resolves.


CASE 31 — Nonproliferative Diabetic Retinopathy
===============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressive visual difficulties at night (nyctalopia), with difficulty driving at night for one year.
DURATION: Since age of onset of trouble was 10 and current age 24, duration = 14 years.
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "I no longer felt safe [driving at night]."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 24-year-old woman with progressive nyctalopia over many years, having trouble with outdoor activities after dusk since age 10; stopped driving at night a year ago because she no longer felt safe; long-standing clumsiness and bumping into objects from poor night vision; otherwise healthy, negative family history for ocular problems, no consanguinity.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old woman with progressive visual difficulties at night was evaluated. Since age 10 she has had trouble performing outdoor activities after dusk. A year ago she stopped driving at night because she no longer felt safe. For many years she has been “clumsy” and often walked into surrounding objects she could not see well. The patient was otherwise healthy. Family history was negative for ocular problems, and there was no consanguinity.
Symptom Onset: Since age of 10
Symptom Duration: Since the age of 10 (approximately 14 years)
Progression: 86%
Characteristics: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy in both eyes, with multiple microaneurysms, intraretinal hemorrhages, venous beading (approaching severe NPDR threshold), and areas of capillary nonperfusion; no proliferative changes noted.


Subjective (cont.)

Associated Symptoms: progressive difficulty with night vision, clumsiness bumping into objects, stopped driving at night due to unsafe vision
Impact on Daily Activities: Stopped driving at night; difficulty with outdoor activities after dusk; clumsiness walking into objects (night-time visual impairment affecting mobility and driving).
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Severity of baseline retinopathy; duration of diabetes; poor glycemic control; hypertension; hypercholesterolemia; possibly type (Type II shows larger treatment benefit)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg

External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal

Plan
