Follow-Up Instructions: Reexamine every 2 to 4 months.
Patient Education: Control blood glucose and blood pressure; understand that tight systemic control lowers risk of retinopathy progression. Recognize symptoms (blurred vision, night vision difficulties) and importance of regular eye exams per severity (annual for minimal, 6–12 months for mild–moderate NPDR, 2–4 months for severe/very severe NPDR; more frequent if macular edema). Be aware laser photocoagulation may be recommended for severe NPDR to reduce risk of progression; report new visual changes promptly.


CASE 32 — Diabetic Macular Edema
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: blurred vision OU for 2 months
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: OU
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old man with a 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presenting with a 2-month history of blurred vision in both eyes (OU); medical history notable for hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, and hepatitis C. Best corrected visual acuity 20/20 OU; IOP 11 mm Hg OD, 12 mm Hg OS; slit-lamp exam unremarkable. Fundus: intraretinal hemorrhages in multiple quadrants OU, cotton-wool spot superior to right macula, no macular edema OU on right, fluorescein angiography with microaneurysms and mild perifoveal capillary dropout, no neovascularization.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old man with a 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented with a 2-month history of blurred vision in both eyes. Medical history is positive for hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, and a history of hepatitis C. Best corrected visual acuity was 20/20 OU. Slit-lamp examination was unremarkable. Intraocular pressure was 11 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS. Funduscopic exam OD showed a normal disc, no macular edema or macular lipid, and intraretinal hemorrhages in two quadrants and a mild degree in the other two quadrants; a cotton-wool spot was present superior to the right macula. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout, but no neovascularization.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: 32 Diabetic Macular Edema; 53-year-old man; 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus; 2-month history of blurred vision OU; hypertension; peripheral neuropathy; history of hepatitis C; best corrected visual acuity 20/20 OU; IOP 11 mm Hg OD, 12 mm Hg OS; fundus: intraretinal hemorrhages in multiple quadrants OU, cotton-wool spot superior to right macula, no macular edema clinically OD but clinically significant center-involving macular edema OU per diagnosis; fluorescein angiography: microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout, no neovascularization; early proliferative diabetic retinopathy OS.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Hypertension; hypercholesterolemia; poor systemic (glycemic) control; peripheral neuropathy (as marker of diabetes severity); history of hepatitis C (systemic comorbidity)
Associated Symptoms: Blurred vision OU for 2 months
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — minimal impact (patient has 20/20 OU vision despite blurred vision history; daily activities largely unaffected)
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Type II diabetes mellitus (10-year history), hypertension, hypercholesterolemia (implied by discussion), peripheral neuropathy, hepatitis C, duration of diabetes, presence of diabetic retinopathy (NPDR/early PDR), poor systemic control risk (inferred)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 11 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet.
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic with no significant lenticular abnormality


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive to light, normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD)
Pupils – Other: PERRL
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal disc
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular edema or macular lipid OU
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal periphery (no peripheral neovascularization or other peripheral lesions noted)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci‑CSME) OU. Early proliferative diabetic retinopathy, OS. Systemic history notable for 10 years of type II diabetes, hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, and hepatitis C. Visual acuity 20/20 OU. Slit-lamp exam unremarkable. IOP 11 mm Hg OD, 12 mm Hg OS. Fundus: bilateral intraretinal hemorrhages in multiple quadrants, cotton‑wool spot superior to right macula; no macular lipid or neovascularization OD. FA: microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout, no leakage consistent with neovascularization. Plan: initiate anti‑VEGF therapy for center‑involving DME OU (three monthly injections to start), consider focal/grid laser for non–center involving areas or adjunctive therapy as needed; monitor closely and reassess for PRP in OS if progression of proliferative changes; optimize systemic control (blood pressure, lipids, glycemic control) and coordinate medical management. Follow‑up in 4–6 weeks with OCT and clinical exam; consider switching anti‑VEGF or intravitreal corticosteroid in poor responders after three injections.
Primary Diagnosis: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci-CSME) OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Peripheral neuropathy; Hepatitis C; Early proliferative diabetic retinopathy, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema (DME) — center-involving; Hypertensive retinopathy with macular edema; Cystoid macular edema (postoperative)
Severity: Moderate nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy (moderate NPDR)
Laterality: OU


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab ranibizumab aflibercept intravitreal corticosteroids (unspecified) argin laser (argon)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in 4–6 weeks after anti‑VEGF (with or without focal laser); if persistent or recurrent edema retreat at that interval. Consider a series of 3 injections before changing therapy; if poor response after 3 consecutive injections, consider switching anti‑VEGF or intravitreal corticosteroid. Repeat laser can be considered 3–6 months after initial treatment. Monitor closely for progression of proliferative disease and adjust PRP timing as needed.
Patient Education: You have clinically significant diabetic macular edema (DME) affecting the center of both eyes. This means fluid has collected in the central part of your retina (the macula) and can threaten vision if not treated. Key points and recommendations: - Cause and risk factors: DME is a complication of diabetes; poor blood sugar, high blood pressure, and high cholesterol increase risk and reduce treatment response. Control of these conditions is important. - Symptoms to watch for: blurred or distorted central vision, difficulty reading or recognizing faces. Report any sudden vision changes immediately. - Recommended treatment: First-line therapy for center-involving DME is intravitreal anti-VEGF injections (medicines injected into the eye) given initially as a series (commonly three injections at 4–6 week intervals) and then repeated as needed based on response. Laser treatment may be used in non–center-involving DME or combined with injections in some cases. Steroid injections are an alternative for certain eyes (e.g., pseudophakic or poor responders) but carry risk of increased eye pressure or cataract. - Monitoring and follow-up: Regular eye exams with optical coherence tomography (OCT) and visits every 4–6 weeks during active treatment to assess response. Longer-term follow-up is needed; multiple treatments may be required. - When surgery is considered: If OCT shows vitreomacular traction causing the edema, a vitrectomy may be recommended. - Systemic care: Work with your primary care physician/endocrinologist to optimize blood sugar, blood pressure, and cholesterol. Smoking cessation and general cardiovascular risk reduction improve outcomes. - Prognosis: Many patients improve with anti-VEGF therapy, but some fluid may persist and multiple treatments are often necessary. Early treatment and good systemic control improve chances of preserving vision. If you have questions about the injection procedure, risks (infection, bleeding, increased eye pressure), or scheduling, please ask so we can provide details and consent information.
Referrals: Medical consultation for optimization of systemic conditions (primary care/internal medicine or cardiology)


CASE 33 — Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision of several weeks' duration
DURATION: several weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had blurred vision for several weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 58-year-old man with 20-year history of type I diabetes, recent-onset hypertension, and chronic hypercholesterolemia presenting with several weeks of blurred vision. Exam: BCVA 20/30 OU, no RAPD, trace nuclear lens opacity, IOP 20 OU. Fundus: moderate microaneurysms OU, clinically significant diabetic macular edema with circinate lipid ring in OD; early neovascularization at the disc in OS. OCT confirmed macular edema OU; fluorescein angiography showed macular leakage OU and NVD OS. Underwent bilateral intravitreal anti-VEGF injections with planned follow-up in 1 month; received a third anti-VEGF and focal laser to OD for persistent edema; OS improved to 20/20 with normal macular OCT and no further treatment.
History Narrative: This 58-year-old man with a 20-year history of type I diabetes, recent-onset hypertension, and chronic hypercholesterolemia sought consultation because of blurred vision of several weeks’ duration.
Symptom Onset: several weeks’ duration
Symptom Duration: several weeks
Progression: Progressive (from NPDR to PDR in the right eye; left eye remained NPDR)
Characteristics: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy with high‑risk characteristics (neovascularization involving over one disc area, extending beyond temporal and superior margins of the disc; no vitreous hemorrhage)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Poorly controlled diabetes (long-standing type I diabetes), hypertension, and hypercholesterolemia
Associated Symptoms: Blurred vision of several weeks’ duration
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal anti-VEGF injections OU (two prior injections, with a third given to OD)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — some blurred vision from macular edema affecting tasks like reading/driving, but best-corrected VA 20/30 OD and 20/20 OS so daily activities largely manageable with occasional difficulty.
Systemic Symptoms: blurred vision of several weeks’ duration
Risk Factors: Any neovascularization; neovascularization at the disc (vs elsewhere); severe neovascularization — (a) neovascularization within one disc diameter of the optic disc exceeding one-quarter to one-third disc area in size (DRS standard photograph 10A), or (b) neovascularization elsewhere exceeding one-half disc area in extent; vitreous hemorrhage.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/90 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 20 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 20 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White conjunctiva/sclera (no injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbit appearance normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea without epithelial defects or infiltrates; no corneal pathology noted (clinically normal).
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: trace nuclear lens opacity


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No relative afferent pupillary defect; pupils normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No relative afferent pupillary defect (i.e., pupils briskly reactive to light)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (intact/normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: trace nuclear lens opacity
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Neovascularization of the disc with involvement over one disc area, extending beyond the temporal and superior margins; elevated neovascular tissue over the retinal surface.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Right: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema with circinate lipid ring surrounding center of macula; macular edema temporal to fovea approaching center causing visual loss; OCT confirmed macular edema. Left: Macular edema with lipid and macular thickening temporal to macula; OCT confirmed macular edema initially, later resolved with normal macular contours on OCT after treatment (VA 20/20)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber, with microaneurysms and intraretinal hemorrhages; no venous beading or abnormal vessel caliber noted.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Moderate microaneurysms and intraretinal hemorrhages scattered in all quadrants; no vitreous hemorrhage; no peripheral neovascularization noted.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 58-year-old man with 20-year type I diabetes, recent hypertension and chronic hypercholesterolemia. Right eye: proliferative diabetic retinopathy with high-risk characteristics — neovascularization involving over one disc area, elevated and extending beyond the temporal and superior disc margins; clinically significant diabetic macular edema (CSDME) with circinate lipid ring and persistent edema despite prior anti-VEGF therapy. Left eye: moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy with early neovascularization at the disc (NVD) on angiography and macular edema; OCT at follow-up showed normal macular contours and 20/20 vision, with no further treatment recommended. Management to date: bilateral intravitreal anti-VEGF injections for macular edema, with planned continued anti-VEGF and focal laser to the right eye for unresolved edema; left eye observed given regression/resolution of edema and stable visual acuity. No vitreous hemorrhage present. Continued close follow-up and consideration of panretinal photocoagulation for PDR as indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy (right eye) with high-risk characteristics; moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy (left eye)
Secondary Diagnoses: Type I diabetes mellitus, diabetic macular edema OU, hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, nuclear sclerotic lens changes (trace)
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic retinopathy (proliferative vs nonproliferative); Branch or central retinal vein occlusion with collateral vessels; Radiation retinopathy; Hypertensive retinopathy
Severity: Proliferative
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Pain in the immediate posttreatment period - Vitreous hemorrhage - Decreased night vision / diminished illumination - Peripheral visual field loss (usually not clinically significant) - Exacerbation or worsening of diabetic macular edema - Early persistent visual loss (≥2 lines)


Plan
All Medications: insulin (for type I diabetes), anti-vascular endothelial growth factor (anti-VEGF) intravitreal injections (bilateral)
All Procedures: Intravitreal anti-VEGF injections OU; focal (macular) laser photocoagulation OD; scheduled follow-up exams; (planned potential third anti-VEGF injection OU)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe within a few weeks after completion of PRP; follow-up PRP and/or anti‑VEGF if retinopathy progresses or neovascularization regression is incomplete; conservative observational follow‑up if retinopathy regresses and vision is stable; consider vitrectomy for progression (recurrent vitreous hemorrhage or traction threatening macula).
Patient Education: Educated about proliferative diabetic retinopathy, the need for prompt treatment (panretinal photocoagulation and/or intravitreal anti‑VEGF) to reduce risk of severe vision loss, the role of anti‑VEGF for macular edema and when vitreous hemorrhage limits laser, potential need for multiple treatments and possible vitrectomy if hemorrhage or traction occurs, expected side effects/complications (pain after PRP, temporary decreased night vision, peripheral field loss, possible worsening of macular edema, risk of vitreous hemorrhage), importance of close follow‑up and monitoring with OCT/clinical exam, and strict systemic risk‑factor control (good glycemic, blood pressure and lipid management).


CASE 34 — Retinal Arterial Occlusion
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Second opinion regarding possibility of diabetic retinopathy; asymptomatic.
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 50-year-old man with sudden visual loss in the right eye; asymptomatic at initial presentation to ophthalmologist seeking second opinion for possible diabetic retinopathy. History notable for 5-year type II diabetes mellitus. Examination later showed findings consistent with an inferotemporal branch retinal artery occlusion in the right eye.
History Narrative: A 50-year-old man with a 5-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented for a second opinion from an ophthalmologist regarding the possibility of diabetic retinopathy. The patient was asymptomatic upon initial presentation. His examination showed vision of 20/20 in each eye. Pressures were 12 and 13 mm Hg in the right and left eyes, respectively. Slit-lamp examination was unremarkable and the lenses were clear. On funduscopic exam of the right eye there were mild hard exudates scattered about the posterior pole, questionable neovascularization elsewhere at the distal portions of both temporal arcades, and definite neovascularization at the disc exceeding one disc area. In the left eye there were intraretinal hemorrhages with microaneurysms in all four quadrants, exceeding standard photograph 2A for hemorrhages in two quadrants.
Characteristics: Retinal edema with retinal whitening and a classic foveal "cherry-red spot"; visible Hollenhorst plaque; limited filling of the affected branch retinal artery on fluorescein angiography; subsequent inner retinal atrophy on OCT.


Subjective (cont.)
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none — the patient is asymptomatic with 20/20 vision in each eye, so daily activities are not impacted.
Systemic Symptoms: Asymptomatic
Risk Factors: Type II diabetes mellitus, atherosclerosis (systemic vascular disease), carotid artery plaque/stenosis, cardiac arrhythmias (emboligenic sources), hypertension (inferred as common vascular risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 138/86 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N36/36
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Perfectly clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near (normal)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all gazes, no motility deficit
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc neovascularization (>1 disc area)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Retinal whitening with a classic cherry-red spot (foveal cherry-red spot)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Right: inferotemporal branch artery occlusion with limited filling of inferotemporal retinal artery and branches, Hollenhorst plaques; Left: intraretinal hemorrhages with microaneurysms in all quadrants (exceeding two quadrants).
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Scattered drusen throughout the posterior pole; peripheral retina with limited filling of inferotemporal retinal artery and branches consistent with inferotemporal branch retinal artery occlusion (no mention of peripheral detachments or tears)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Branch retinal artery occlusion, right eye — acute retinal arterial ischemia with visible Hollenhorst plaque and inferotemporal arterial branch occlusion on fundus exam and fluorescein angiography. Vision better prognosis than CRAO; patient has diabetes mellitus type II (5 years) as systemic risk factor. Recommend urgent systemic vascular workup (carotid evaluation, cardiac rhythm monitoring, atherosclerotic risk factor management) and close ocular follow-up to monitor for retinal and iris neovascularization; consider acute intraocular pressure–lowering maneuvers if within 24 hours and discuss potential interventions (paracentesis, ocular massage) though no proven therapy to improve long-term visual outcome.
Primary Diagnosis: Branch retinal artery occlusion, right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Type II diabetes mellitus, carotid artery atherosclerosis (suspected), systemic vascular disease (atherosclerosis)
Differential Diagnosis: Central retinal artery occlusion; Branch retinal artery occlusion; Retinal vein occlusion (central or branch); Ocular ischemic syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Neovascularization (retinal and possible iris/rubeosis), vitreous hemorrhage, macular edema, inner retinal atrophy leading to permanent visual field defects and hemifield loss, increased risk of stroke/MI from systemic vascular disease.


Plan
All Medications: acetazolamide; topical intraocular pressure–lowering drops; calcium channel blockers; anticoagulation; fibrinolytic agents
All Procedures: digital pressure on the globe (15-second compressions with sudden release); lowering intraocular pressure (intravenous acetazolamide, topical IOP-lowering drops, anterior chamber paracentesis); inhalation of carbogen (5% CO2/95% O2); retrobulbar or systemic calcium channel blockers; full-scatter panretinal photocoagulation; fluorescein angiography; spectral-domain optical coherence tomography; stereoscopic fundus photography; slit-lamp biomicroscopy with precorneal lens contact evaluation
Follow-Up Instructions: Monitor for subsequent neovascularization with regular ophthalmic follow-up; reinforce and ensure control of systemic vascular risk factors as prescribed by the patient’s internist.
Patient Education: Retinal arterial occlusion is a sudden blockage of an artery in the eye that can cause sudden vision loss; it is often a warning sign of systemic vascular disease (like atherosclerosis, carotid plaque, or heart arrhythmias). Immediate actions may include maneuvers to try to dislodge an embolus (digital ocular massage, lowering intraocular pressure with drops, IV acetazolamide, or anterior chamber paracentesis if within 24 hours), but no therapy reliably restores vision. You need urgent medical evaluation and systemic workup (carotid imaging, cardiac evaluation, vascular risk-factor assessment and management). Follow-up eye exams are important to monitor for neovascularization; if new vessels develop, panretinal photocoagulation can be effective. Control vascular risk factors (blood pressure, diabetes, cholesterol, smoking cessation) as advised by your internist to reduce risk of stroke or heart attack. Low-vision aids and rehabilitation (including prisms for certain field defects) can help if vision does not recover.
Referrals: Referral for systemic medical workup (internist) for evaluation of systemic vascular disease, carotid plaque assessment, and cardiac rhythm evaluation (e.g., arrhythmia/embol source).


CASE 35 — Central Retinal Vein Occlusion
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: sudden visual loss in the right eye 4 weeks prior
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 69-year-old woman with sudden visual loss in the right eye 4 weeks ago (presenting 4 weeks after onset); history notable for 6 years of hypertension and 1 year of diabetes; was taking oral contraceptives.
History Narrative: A 69-year-old woman presented 4 weeks after sudden visual loss in the right eye. Medical history included a 6-year history of systemic hypertension and a 1-year history of diabetes mellitus. She had been taking oral contraceptives.
Symptom Onset: 4 weeks prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Characteristics: Intraretinal hemorrhage in all four quadrants with tortuous veins, macular edema, and possible collateralization at the disc; variable ischemia (ischemic vs nonischemic) determining prognosis.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Hypertension; diabetes mellitus; oral contraceptive use; hypercoagulable states (e.g., thrombophilia), cardiovascular disease
Associated Symptoms: sudden visual loss in the right eye
Prior Treatments: Cessation of oral contraceptives; aspirin 80 mg daily; intravitreal triamcinolone and/or anti-VEGF injections (for macular edema)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — some visual impairment (right eye) impacting tasks requiring fine central vision (reading, detailed work), but overall daily activities largely maintained due to preserved fellow eye function.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, diabetes mellitus
Risk Factors: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; oral contraceptive use; hypercoagulable states (e.g., multiple myeloma, macroglobulinemia, coagulation defects, polycythemia vera); cardiovascular disease; atherosclerosis


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 150/92 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 BPM
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: No rubeosis iridis (no iris neovascularization)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open with no neovascularization; angles open and normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: early nuclear sclerosis in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No afferent pupillary defect.
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no EOM deficits
Slit Lamp Exam Description: remarkable only for early nuclear sclerosis of the lens in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, disc normal contour, no neovascularization or hemorrhage
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular edema with intraretinal hemorrhages/retinal whitening in the posterior pole.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Tortuous vessels with intraretinal hemorrhages in all four quadrants; no disc collaterals noted
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Diffuse intraretinal hemorrhages in all quadrants, no peripheral retinal tears or detachment noted


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: CRVO OD with secondary macular edema — 69‑year‑old woman with acute, unilateral central retinal vein occlusion of the right eye, diffuse intraretinal hemorrhages in all four quadrants, tortuous vessels, and OCT-confirmed marked intraretinal/macular edema correlating with decreased vision. History notable for hypertension and diabetes; risk factor modification recommended (cessation of oral contraceptives, systemic evaluation with primary physician). IOPs normal; no afferent pupillary defect. Plan: treat macular edema with intravitreal anti‑VEGF or corticosteroid injections guided by OCT; monitor monthly for 6 months for neovascularization/rubeosis and consider PRP if ischemia ≥5 disc areas or if rubeosis develops. Consider systemic workup as indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: CRVO with secondary macular edema, OD
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Diabetes mellitus; Early nuclear sclerosis of the lens; Prior/oral contraceptive use; Aspirin therapy (80 mg daily)
Differential Diagnosis: Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); Branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) or hemiretinal vein occlusion; Diabetic retinopathy with diffuse hemorrhages; Subhyaloid or subretinal hemorrhage
Laterality: Right
Complications: Macular edema; neovascularization (rubeosis iridis) leading to neovascular glaucoma; vitreous hemorrhage; vision loss (reduced visual acuity); potential media opacities requiring cryopexy or vitrectomy; secondary glaucoma requiring shunt placement


Plan
All Medications: aspirin 80 mg daily; intravitreal triamcinolone; intravitreal anti-VEGF agents; oral contraceptives (cessation recommended)
All Procedures: Cessation of oral contraceptives; recommendation of aspirin 80 mg daily; referral for systemic evaluation/primary care coordination; monthly ophthalmic follow-up for 6 months; consideration of intravitreal anti-VEGF or intravitreal triamcinolone injections for macular edema; panretinal photocoagulation (PRP) if ≥5 disc areas of nonperfusion or rubeosis iridis; glaucoma surgery with shunt device if neovascular glaucoma and medical/laser control fails; possible vitrectomy or cryopexy if media opacity prevents laser.
Follow-Up Instructions: Monthly follow-up examinations for 6 months after diagnosis; thereafter routine follow-up with markedly reduced rubeosis risk after 1 year unless conversion to nonperfused occurs.
Patient Education: You have a central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO) in your right eye causing macular swelling that can reduce vision. Important points: - Control systemic risk factors (blood pressure, diabetes) and stop oral contraceptives as recommended; follow up with your primary care physician. - Macular edema is often treatable; intravitreal anti-VEGF or steroid injections can reduce swelling and often improve vision. Initial frequent injections (every 4–6 weeks) are common, then spaced if improved. - There is a risk of neovascularization and neovascular (rubeotic) glaucoma, usually within 3–6 months, so monthly eye exams for at least 6 months are recommended to detect early signs. - If significant retinal ischemia or rubeosis develops, panretinal photocoagulation (laser) and/or anti-VEGF may be needed; if pressure rises, glaucoma surgery or shunt placement may be necessary. - Report any sudden worsening of vision, eye pain, or redness immediately.
Referrals: Referral to the patient’s primary care physician/internist for systemic evaluation (hypertension/diabetes management and discontinuation of oral contraceptives)
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if sudden worsening of vision, eye pain, redness, or new floaters; return promptly for monthly follow-up for 6 months (or sooner if symptoms) to monitor for neovascularization and rising intraocular pressure.


CASE 36 — Branch Retinal Vein Occlusion
=======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in the right eye for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had decreased vision in my right eye for about three weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45‑year‑old woman with a 3‑week history of gradual decreased vision in the right eye that worsened during the first week then stabilized; no hypertension, on oral contraceptives. Best corrected VA 20/100 OD, 20/20 OS; right afferent pupillary defect; pseudophakic; IOPs 12 and 14 mm Hg. Fundus with segmental intraretinal hemorrhages and macular edema involving the fovea. Prior treatments: intravitreal triamcinolone (initial and repeat) with recurrent edema, subsequently maintained on intravitreal bevacizumab every ~3 months with stable OCT and VA 20/30.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman presented with a 3-week history of decreased vision in the right eye. Her only medication was oral contraceptives and she did not have hypertension. Vision gradually diminished during the first week after onset then stabilized. Best corrected visual acuity was 20/100 OD and 20/20 OS. She had a right afferent pupillary defect. Slit-lamp exam showed no iris neovascularization; she was pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg OD and 14 mm Hg OS. Funduscopic exam OD showed intraretinal hemorrhage distributed throughout each quadrant with mild disc edema, but no definite collaterals; macular edema involved about one disc area at the fovea. Cup-to-disc ratio was 0.3. Left eye exam was normal. Fluorescein angiography showed good perfusion in the midperiphery and late dye leakage at the fovea. OCT showed marked central macular edema. Intravitreal triamcinolone was administered with resolution of edema and vision improvement to 20/30 at 3 months, but edema recurred at 6 months requiring a second triamcinolone with recurrence at 3 months; she was then maintained on intravitreal bevacizumab about every 3 months with stable OCT and visual acuity of 20/30.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Recurrent with periods of resolution; chronic recurrent course stabilized on ongoing treatment
Characteristics: Branch retinal vein occlusion with macular edema, sectoral intraretinal hemorrhages (inferotemporal distribution), mild disc edema, no iris neovascularization, pseudophakic right eye, BCVA 20/100 OD improving to 20/30 after treatment, OCT-confirmed central macular edema, recurrent edema responsive to intravitreal triamcinolone and maintained on bevacizumab every ~3 months.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Oral contraceptives
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision in the right eye for 3 weeks; gradual vision loss then stabilization.
Previous Episodes: Recurrent macular edema with recurrences at 3 and 6 months after intravitreal triamcinolone; multiple recurrences requiring ongoing intravitreal injections (triamcinolone ×2, then bevacizumab every ~3 months; later aflibercept every 6 weeks for other eye)
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal triamcinolone (two injections) and ongoing intravitreal bevacizumab injections (~every 3 months)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — had decreased vision to 20/100 OD initially with improvement to 20/30 with treatment; recurrent edema required ongoing intravitreal injections every ~3 months, implying intermittent functional visual limitation affecting daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: systemic hypertension; diabetes; other cardiovascular disease; glaucoma; increased body mass at age 20; hyperopia; higher serum cholesterol; lower high-density lipoprotein levels; coagulation disorders; alcohol consumption; oral contraceptive use


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbit normal on external exam.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: No corneal abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: no neovascularization
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: Right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: Right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no extraocular movement deficits
Slit Lamp Exam Description: no signs of iris neovascularization; pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.3
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular edema involving the fovea (~1 disc area)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Segmental intraretinal hemorrhages in the distribution of the inferotemporal branch retinal vein with mild disc edema; no definite collaterals; no neovascularization.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Intraretinal hemorrhages distributed segmentally in the inferotemporal quadrant; midperiphery well perfused with no neovascularization noted.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: BRVO (branch retinal vein occlusion) of the right eye with macular edema causing decreased vision to 20/100 OD at presentation. No iris neovascularization; 1+ disc edema and intraretinal hemorrhages in a segmental inferotemporal distribution with macular edema involving the fovea on OCT. Initial good response to intravitreal triamcinolone with recurrence treated again, subsequently maintained on intravitreal bevacizumab approximately every 3 months with stable OCT and vision improved to and maintained at 20/30 OD. No evidence of neovascularization or vitreous hemorrhage; IOPs normal. Recommend continued intravitreal anti-VEGF therapy with OCT monitoring and systemic risk factor management.
Primary Diagnosis: BRVO with macular edema, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic retinopathy; Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); Radiation retinopathy; Sickle cell retinopathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: macular edema; intraretinal hemorrhage; retinal neovascularization; vitreous hemorrhage; disc edema; epiretinal membrane; tractional/rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; neovascular glaucoma


Plan
All Medications: oral contraceptives; intravitreal triamcinolone; intravitreal bevacizumab; aflibercept; aspirin
All Procedures: Intravitreal triamcinolone injection; Intravitreal bevacizumab injections (serial, ~every 3 months); Focal/grid macular laser photocoagulation; Sectoral/scatter retinal laser photocoagulation; Vitrectomy with membrane peeling (for epiretinal membrane or nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage); Vitrectomy for tractional/rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; Optic nerve sheath decompression (experimental); Surgical decompression/lysis of common adventitial sheath (experimental)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow every 3–6 months initially; institute intravitreal therapy (often every 4–6 weeks) for macular edema then extend interval based on response; perform segmental scatter photocoagulation if retinal neovascularization is detected; consider vitrectomy for nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage or vision‑threatening traction.
Patient Education: Explain that a branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) is a blockage of a small retinal vein causing retinal hemorrhage and macular swelling that can reduce vision. Control vascular risk factors (especially blood pressure; inform your primary care doctor). Treatment options aim to reduce macular edema and prevent neovascular complications and include intravitreal injections (anti-VEGF agents or corticosteroids) to improve or stabilize vision, and sectoral or focal laser if unwanted neovascularization or persistent edema occurs. Regular follow-up with optical coherence tomography (OCT) and eye exams is needed (initially every 4–6 weeks while receiving injections, then less frequently when stable). Report any new floaters, sudden vision loss, or pain promptly. Use of aspirin or other systemic treatments should be discussed with your primary care physician.


CASE 37 — Nonexudative Age-Related Macular Degeneration
=======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision OS with a central scotoma for 2 months
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "he reported was well controlled on medications."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 66-year-old man with a 2-month history of decreased vision in the left eye characterized by a central scotoma centered superotemporally; history notable for 20-year hypertension well controlled on medications.
History Narrative: A 66-year-old man with a 2-month history of decreased vision in the left eye characterized by a central scotoma centered superatemporally. He has a 20-year history of well-controlled hypertension. At initial presentation left eye visual acuity was 20/200 and a branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) was diagnosed. The right eye had a prior central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO) treated with multiple anti-VEGF injections and intravitreal triamcinolone with improvement from 20/400 to 20/80 after macular edema resolution. Intraocular pressures were 15 mm Hg OU. On fundus exam the right eye showed a dry macula with collateral vessels at the optic nerve head. The left eye had an anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade with dot-and-blot hemorrhages extending from the midportion of the inferotemporal arcade posteriorly into the macula and moderate to marked macular edema. Intravitreal bevacizumab was given without improvement; subsequent aflibercept every 6 weeks for about a year produced better effect and stabilized left eye visual acuity at 20/70.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Drusen with variable RPE atrophy in the macula (confluent/geographic atrophy), central scotoma, no exudation (no sub/intraretinal fluid or hemorrhage), thin central macula on OCT, fundus autofluorescence may show more widespread atrophy.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: central scotoma, metamorphopsia, decreased vision
Previous Episodes: Previous episodes of CRVO OD treated with multiple anti‑VEGF injections and intravitreal triamcinolone; current BRVO OS onset 2 months prior.
Prior Treatments: several injections of anti-VEGF agents and a couple of intravitreal triamcinolone injections; intravitreal bevacizumab (left) then aflibercept q6 weeks for ~1 year
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — difficulties with detailed near tasks and some distance activities; may require low-vision aids for reading, magnification and focused lighting, but able to perform many tasks with adaptation.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension (well controlled on medications)
Risk Factors: Age (older age), history of hypertension, tobacco smoking (avoidance recommended), bilateral drusen/advanced age-related macular degeneration in fellow eye (exudative AMD in other eye noted as risk context), age-related retinal changes (intermediate/advanced AMD)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear, quiet
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic cataract (mild–moderate)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no deficits
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal-appearing optic nerve head
Fundus Exam – Macula: Dry macula OU with RPE depigmentation and drusen; left macula with moderate–marked edema and dot-and-blot hemorrhages from inferotemporal arcade (OS)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber with collateral vessels at the optic nerve head (OD); anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade with dot‑and‑blot hemorrhages (OS)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina flat, no peripheral lesions detected


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Nonexudative (atrophic) age-related macular degeneration, OU — chronic drusen with RPE atrophic changes causing central visual loss greater in degree than ophthalmoscopic pigmentation alone would predict; no evidence of choroidal neovascularization on OCT/FA. Patient endorsed monitoring and AREDS2 supplementation; low-vision rehabilitation discussed. Follow-up recommended every 3–12 months and prompt return for any new metamorphopsia or visual change.
Primary Diagnosis: Nonexudative age-related macular degeneration, OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Branch retinal vein occlusion (left eye); history of central retinal vein occlusion (right eye); hypertension (well controlled)
Differential Diagnosis: Occult exudative (neovascular) AMD; Pattern dystrophy (macular dystrophies, e.g., Stargardt); Inflammatory or traumatic RPE changes (including prior trauma/uveitis); Optic neuropathy or coexisting vascular occlusive disease (BRVO/CRVO)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OU
Complications: - Progression to exudative (neovascular) AMD (conversion to choroidal neovascularization) - Geographic atrophy with central vision loss - Persistent visual impairment / decreased visual acuity - Need for low vision rehabilitation - Possible fellow-eye involvement and bilateral disease


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab; aflibercept; intravitreal triamcinolone; AREDS2 vitamin supplementation; antihypertensive medications (unspecified)
All Procedures: Intravitreal bevacizumab injection; intravitreal aflibercept injections (every ~6 weeks for ~1 year); intravitreal triamcinolone injections; fundus photography; fluorescein angiography; optical coherence tomography (OCT); fundus autofluorescence (as diagnostic); low vision evaluation (low vision aids); Amsler grid testing; routine ophthalmic examinations (every 3–12 months).
Follow-Up Instructions: Examination every 3–12 months depending on stability; use Amsler grid daily and monitor each eye for new metamorphopsia or visual changes; return promptly for any increased metamorphopsia or other visual symptoms.
Patient Education: - Use an Amsler grid daily to check each eye separately for new distortion or scotomas; report any sudden changes (increased distortion, new blind spots) immediately. - Take AREDS2 vitamin supplements if advised (intermediate or advanced AMD or neovascular AMD in the other eye) and avoid tobacco smoking. - Attend regular follow-up visits every 3–12 months as recommended; more frequent if symptoms change. - Optimize lighting and consider low-vision evaluation and aids (magnifiers, focused lighting, telescopes) for tasks as needed; expect palliative benefits, not cure. - Be aware that development of sudden metamorphopsia or vision loss may indicate conversion to exudative AMD requiring urgent care.
Referrals: Low vision evaluation / low vision services
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic evaluation if you notice any sudden change in vision, new or worsening central visual distortion (metamorphopsia), a new central scotoma, sudden loss of vision, new flashes/floaters, or any other acute visual symptoms.


CASE 38 — Exudative Age-Related Macular Degeneration
====================================================

CASE 39 — Myopic Degeneration
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision in her right eye
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 87-year-old woman with prior bilateral nonexudative age-related macular degeneration presenting with decreased vision in the right eye. Visual acuity 20/40 OD and 20/50 OS. No definite afferent pupillary defect. Pseudophakic OU. IOPs within normal limits. Underwent intravitreal bevacizumab injection and instructed to follow up in 4 weeks.
History Narrative: An 87-year-old woman presented with decreased vision in her right eye. She had a prior history of nonexudative age-related macular degeneration in both eyes. She underwent an intravitreal injection of bevacizumab and was instructed to follow up in 4 weeks.
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Progression: Progressive visual loss from progressive RPE atrophy potentially culminating in CNV (i.e., progressive deterioration)
Characteristics: Myopic degeneration with high myopia (>6 D), lacquer cracks, posterior staphyloma, tilted optic nerve head, peripapillary RPE atrophy, drusen, retinal lattice degeneration, pigment epithelial detachments, subretinal fluid, choroidal neovascularization (occult/juxtafoveal/subfoveal CNVM), Fuchs’ spot, often bilateral involvement, commonly subretinal RPE atrophy surrounding CNV


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision (right eye)
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal bevacizumab injection (one session)
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely significant central vision impairment for reading/detail tasks (need for magnifiers/low-vision aids); difficulty with fine near work and potential distortion, but many daily activities may be partially preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: None
Risk Factors: High axial myopia (high myopia >6 D / axial length >26.5 mm), lacquer cracks/Bruch’s membrane breaks, posterior staphyloma, tilted optic nerve, peripapillary RPE atrophy, retinal lattice degeneration, older age (risk of bilateral CNV increases), prior chorioretinal scars/trauma.

All Procedures: Intravitreal injection of bevacizumab

Patient Education: Patients should monitor central vision with an Amsler grid and promptly report any new distortion or central metamorphopsia. Follow-up after anti‑VEGF injection is typically at 4 weeks, then individualized based on response; OCT is usually performed at each visit to detect macular fluid or schisis. For nonexudative myopic degeneration, routine follow-up about twice yearly is recommended. Discuss low‑vision aids (high‑plus lenses, magnifiers, telescopes, video magnification) and referral for low‑vision rehabilitation as needed.
Emergency Instructions: Return for urgent evaluation if new distortion or central metamorphopsia, or any sudden vision loss; seek ophthalmologic consultation immediately.


CASE 40 — Idiopathic Central Serous Chorioretinopathy
=====================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 1-month history of decreased central vision and distortion (cloudy vision and paracentral scotoma) in the left eye; 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye.
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "finding everything to be cloudy."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old urologist with a 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye; had been told he had a CNVM in the right eye not amenable to treatment. At presentation also had a 1-month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye. One month later (6 years after initial left-eye treatment) he returned with a 1-month history of decreased central vision in the left eye described as everything appearing cloudy.
History Narrative: A 38‑year‑old urologist presented with a 1‑year history of visual loss in the right eye and had been told previously he had a choroidal neovascular membrane in that eye not amenable to treatment. At presentation he reported a 1‑month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye. He was a high myope (spherical equivalent −10 D). He later returned 6 years after initial left‑eye argon laser treatment with a 1‑month history of decreased central vision in the left eye described as everything appearing cloudy. He has a history of glaucoma treated initially with Propine OU and later changed to a topical beta‑blocker OU. He has peripheral retinal lattice degeneration in both eyes.
Symptom Onset: 1 month
Symptom Duration: 1 month
Progression: Chronic/Recurrent
Characteristics: Detachment of the neurosensory retina from the underlying retinal pigment epithelium with subretinal fluid accumulation, typically painless, often self-limited, causing serous macular detachment and possible pigment epithelial defects/leakage on fluorescein angiography.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Steroids, stress/type A personality, pregnancy, psychotropic medications, Cushing’s disease, hypertension, systemic lupus erythematosus, Helicobacter pylori infection
Alleviating Factors: Stopping systemic corticosteroids
Associated Symptoms: Metamorphopsia (visual distortion), central scotoma, decreased central vision, cloudy vision (blurred central vision)
Previous Episodes: Recurrent (prior episodes implied; had episodes over 1 year and 1 month durations)
Prior Treatments: Photodynamic therapy; focal argon laser photocoagulation; indirect laser; systemic corticosteroid cessation (if applicable); observation/medical management options trial (e.g., adrenergic antagonists, mineralocorticoid antagonists)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — difficulty with central vision tasks (distortion, paracentral scotoma, cloudy vision) impairing activities like reading and fine work but unilateral and partially preserved vision (20/30 to 20/200) allowing some function.
Risk Factors: Corticosteroids; Psychotropic medications; Hypertension; Systemic lupus erythematosus; Pregnancy; Cushing’s disease; Helicobacter pylori infection; Male gender; Type A personality; Excess stress; Aldosterone/mineralocorticoid-related factors (e.g., elevated aldosterone); Idiopathic


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/82 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet conjunctiva and sclera (no injection)
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea; no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic with no lens abnormalities


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in both eyes (no extraocular movement restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The slit-lamp examination was unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: tilted (myopic) disc with some peripapillary atrophy
Fundus Exam – Macula: Serous detachment of the macula with subretinal fluid under the fovea (central serous neurosensory retinal detachment)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course (no mention of vessel narrowing, dilation, or hemorrhages)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retinal lattice degeneration in the periphery of both eyes without atrophic holes


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Central serous chorioretinopathy of the left eye with subretinal fluid under the fovea on OCT and fluorescein leakage; chronic/recurrent CSCR considerations discussed, no signs of intraocular inflammation.
Primary Diagnosis: Central serous chorioretinopathy
Secondary Diagnoses: Myopia (high, -10 D) ; Choroidal neovascular membrane (right and left) ; Glaucoma ; Retinal pigment epithelial atrophy ; Retinal lattice degeneration
Differential Diagnosis: Serous retinal detachment from posterior scleritis;Choroidal neoplasm (e.g., choroidal melanoma/nevus with serous detachment);Choroidal neovascular membrane (CNVM) from myopic degeneration or AMD;Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada disease
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left eye
Complications: scotomas; subretinal neovascularization (choroidal neovascular membrane); expansion of laser burns (after treatment); persistent RPE mottling/atrophy leading to chronic visual disturbance


Plan
All Medications: Propine; beta‑blocker (topical); corticosteroids; rifampin; methotrexate; carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; metoprolol; propranolol; ketoconazole; mifepristone; eplerenone; spironolactone; finasteride; anti-vascular endothelial growth factor injections
All Procedures: Argon blue-green laser photocoagulation (left eye) Krypton laser photocoagulation (left eye)
Follow-Up Instructions: CSCR resolves spontaneously in 60–80% of patients within 3 months; monitor for resolution and recurrences (recurrence 18–50%, 20% contralateral involvement). Consider observation for up to 3 months; if persistent/chronic, consider photodynamic therapy or focal laser to leakage point. Stop systemic corticosteroids if possible. Follow-up with clinical exam and OCT/FA as needed.
Patient Education: Central serous chorioretinopathy (CSCR) is a condition where fluid builds up under the central retina (macula), causing blurred or distorted central vision. It often affects young to middle‑aged adults and commonly improves on its own within about 3 months. Risk factors include corticosteroid use, stress/type‑A personality, pregnancy, hypertension, and certain medications. What to expect - Most cases resolve spontaneously; vision often improves but may not return completely and pigment changes can remain. - Recurrence is common (18–50%) and the other eye may be affected in ~20% of patients. Self-care and steps you can take - Avoid systemic or topical corticosteroids if possible—discuss alternatives with the prescribing physician. - Reduce stress and consider counseling or stress‑management techniques. - Report any sudden worsening, new distortion, or central vision loss promptly. Tests and monitoring - You may need OCT and fluorescein (and sometimes indocyanine green) angiography to confirm diagnosis and monitor fluid. - Repeat exams and imaging will be scheduled until the fluid resolves and to detect recurrence. Treatment options (if fluid persists or for occupational needs) - Observation for up to ~3 months initially. - Focal laser or photodynamic therapy (PDT) to the leakage site for chronic or persistent CSCR; PDT has better evidence for resolving subretinal fluid. - Some oral medications (e.g., mineralocorticoid antagonists) are being studied but lack definitive randomized trial proof of benefit. When to call the clinic - New or worsening central blur, increased distortion/metamorphopsia, or sudden vision loss. Follow‑up - Regular follow‑up with OCT/clinical exam until resolution; discuss lifestyle and medication changes to reduce recurrence risk.


CASE 41 — Epiretinal Membrane
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurriness in the left eye for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had blurriness in my left eye for 3 weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35-year-old engineer presented with 3 weeks of blurriness in his left eye, with central metamorphopsia on Amsler grid. He denied trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroid use, or prior surgeries. Vision was 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS (improving to 20/30 with +2.00). Anterior segments and IOPs unremarkable. Dilated fundus: OD normal; OS with elevated circumscribed neurosensory macular detachment, no retinal tears/hemorrhages/exudates. OCT showed subretinal fluid under the fovea OS; FA showed expanding macular leakage. He was observed and the subretinal fluid resolved with vision returning to 20/20 after one month.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old engineer presented with 3 weeks of blurriness in the left eye. He denied past medical history, trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroid use, or prior surgeries. Vision was 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS, improving to 20/30 OS with +2.00 D. Anterior segments and IOPs were normal. Dilated fundus exam OD normal; OS showed an elevated, circumscribed neurosensory macular detachment with no retinal tears, hemorrhages, or exudates. The patient reported central metamorphopsia in the left eye. OCT of the left eye showed subretinal fluid under the fovea. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated an expanding area of leakage in the macula. He was observed; one month later the subretinal fluid resolved and vision returned to 20/20.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Improved (vision returned to 20/20 after observation; later improved to 20/60 postoperatively from preop 20/50)
Characteristics: Idiopathic/secondary epiretinal membrane causing macular pucker with preretinal hyperreflective membrane, foveal contour distortion, retinal thickening, vascular distortion and metamorphopsia (visually significant, BCVA 20/60 postop improvement)


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Observation / close follow-up (symptoms improved with observation)
Associated Symptoms: Metamorphopsia and decreased vision (blurriness)
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision and metamorphopsia in the affected eye causing difficulty with near tasks and visual disturbance, but overall functional (20/50 improving to 20/30 with correction; postoperative improvement to 20/60 noted in case after surgery).
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic symptoms
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Idiopathic posterior vitreous separation; prior peripheral retinal break or retinal detachment; intraocular inflammation; retinal vascular disorders; trauma; prior intraocular surgery; age-related vitreoretinal changes


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: reactive and equal
Extraocular Movements: EOMs full and intact bilaterally
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Elevated, circumscribed neurosensory detachment of the macula with metamorphopsia and foveal contour distortion from epiretinal membrane.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Vascular distortion, most prominent temporally
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No retinal tears, hemorrhages, or exudates in the periphery


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: ERM secondary to previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment, right eye. Symptoms: subacute decreased vision and metamorphopsia. Exam/OCT: preretinal hyperreflective membrane with foveal contour distortion and retinal thickening. Management: observed when mild; indicated vitrectomy with ERM peel (±ILM peel) when BCVA falls to ~20/40–20/60 or with significant metamorphopsia. Patient underwent vitrectomy and ERM removal; at 3 months postop VA improved to 20/60 with marked reduction in metamorphopsia and no recurrent ERM on exam/OCT (decreased foveal thickness, improved contour). Prognosis: good, with typical visual recovery over 3–6 months and low recurrence risk.
Primary Diagnosis: ERM secondary to previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment, right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment
Differential Diagnosis: Cystoid macular edema; Vitreomacular traction from partial posterior vitreous separation; Hypotony-related retinal folds (hypotony retinopathy)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: retinal detachment; progressive nuclear sclerosis (cataract formation)


Plan
All Medications: Topical corticosteroid drops; topical antibiotic drops
All Procedures: Vitrectomy with epiretinal membrane peel (with internal limiting membrane peel performed or removed along with ERM in many cases)
Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative topical corticosteroid and antibiotic drops; routine follow-up with monitoring of visual acuity and OCT over the first 6 weeks, with maximal improvement by 3–6 months; monitor for complications (retinal detachment, cataract progression) and manage cataract surgically if needed.
Patient Education: An epiretinal membrane (macular pucker) is a thin layer of tissue on the retinal surface that can cause blurred vision and distortion (metamorphopsia). Many ERMs are stable and can be safely observed if vision and symptoms are mild. Surgery (pars plana vitrectomy with membrane peel, sometimes with internal limiting membrane peel) is recommended if vision declines to about 20/40–20/60 or worse, or if distressing distortion occurs despite good acuity. Surgery often improves vision and distortion but recovery may take 3–6 months; risks include retinal detachment and accelerated cataract formation in phakic eyes. There is no effective medical therapy for idiopathic or post–retinal-break ERMs. Follow-up with visual acuity, Amsler grid checks, and OCT monitoring is advised; report any sudden worsening, new flashes, or floaters promptly.


CASE 42 — Vitreomacular Traction
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: progressively decreasing vision in the right eye for 18 months with metamorphopsia
DURATION: 18 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had progressively decreasing vision in my right eye for 18 months and I see wavy/distorted vision (metamorphopsia)."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 73-year-old woman with an 18-month history of progressively decreasing vision in the right eye associated with metamorphopsia; best corrected VA 20/200 in the right eye.
History Narrative: A 73-year-old woman presented with an 18-month history of progressively decreasing vision in the right eye with metamorphopsia and best corrected Snellen visual acuity of 20/200. She had a history of a retinal detachment repaired with primary pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas in the right eye 2 years earlier.
Symptom Onset: 18 months
Symptom Duration: 18 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Vitreomacular traction (VMT) with epiretinal membrane (ERM), abnormal foveal contour, progressive decreased vision and metamorphopsia, focal vitreofoveal attachment causing retinal traction and distortion, outer retinal hyporeflective changes on OCT (possible ellipsoid zone disruption), prior pars plana vitrectomy OD (history), symptomatic progressive vision loss to 20/200 OD.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressive VMT with broad vitreomacular attachment, associated intraretinal cystoid changes, presence of epiretinal membrane (ERM), history of retinal disease (uveitis, retinal breaks, retinal vascular diseases), older age (>65), and prior retinal surgery (e.g., prior vitrectomy)
Alleviating Factors: Observation, spontaneous release of traction (especially with focal attachment), intravitreal ocriplasmin, intravitreal expansile gas injection, or pars plana vitrectomy (surgical repair)
Associated Symptoms: Metamorphopsia and progressively decreasing vision (decreased vision)
Prior Treatments: Pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas (retinal detachment repair)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision (20/200 OD) with metamorphopsia causing significant functional limitation in daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Age; epiretinal membrane; uveitis; retinal breaks; retinal vascular diseases (vein occlusions, diabetic retinopathy, macular telangiectasia)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 14 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity (best corrected)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Pleasant-appearing, no injection or scleral icterus
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: brisk, equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Fundus Exam – Macula: Prominent epiretinal membrane with moderate vascular distortion and abnormal foveal contour (vitreomacular traction)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Moderate vascular distortion
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Temporal retinal detachment repair changes with peripheral laser scars and lattice; otherwise peripheral retina without active breaks or new pathology.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 1. Vitreomacular traction (VMT) OD with progressive symptoms (18-month history of decreasing vision, metamorphopsia; BCVA 20/200) with OCT-confirmed abnormal foveal contour and prominent ERM causing retinal distortion. 2. VMT OS, nonprogressive.
Primary Diagnosis: Vitreomacular traction OD
Secondary Diagnoses: Epiretinal membrane (ERM); Prior retinal detachment repaired OD (status post pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas)
Differential Diagnosis: Vitreomacular traction (VMT); Epiretinal membrane (ERM); Full-thickness macular hole (FTMH)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right and Left
Complications: dyschromatopsia; electroretinographic changes; ellipsoid layer disruption; cystoid macular edema; foveal neurosensory detachment; lamellar or full-thickness macular hole; worsening visual acuity


Plan
All Medications: ocriplasmin
All Procedures: Pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) with endolaser and gas; Optical coherence tomography (OCT); Intravitreal ocriplasmin injection; Intravitreal expansile gas injection (pneumatic maculopexy); Observation with Amsler grid self-monitoring
Follow-Up Instructions: Educate patient to self-monitor with Amsler grid and return promptly for worsening metamorphopsia, decreased vision, or new scotoma; periodic clinical and OCT follow-up while observing.
Patient Education: Educate patient to self-monitor vision with Amsler grid testing and to report worsening metamorphopsia, decreased vision, or new scotoma; explain that observation is appropriate for minimal symptoms, spontaneous release can occur, and discuss treatment options (observation, ocriplasmin injection, intravitreal expansile gas, or vitrectomy) with risks/benefits.


CASE 43 — Vitreous Hemorrhage
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye for 3 months, interfering with driving and reading
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 78-year-old woman with a 3-month history of progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye, interfering with driving and reading.
History Narrative: A 78-year-old woman presented with a 3-month history of progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye interfering with driving and reading abilities. The vision was 20/60 OD and 20/40 OS. Slit-lamp examination demonstrated clear cornea, absence of cataract, and intraocular pressure of 13 mm Hg in both eyes. Fundoscopic examination showed focal areas of adherent posterior vitreous to the macula associated with raised retinal tissue, OD, and a nonspecific loss of foveal reflex, OS. Optical coherence tomography (OCT) in OD demonstrated a hyperreflective layer correspondent to the posterior hyaloid partially attached to the macula, leading to elevation and distortion of the retina. The OCT OS also showed focal vitreomacular attachment with distortion of the inner foveal layers. An intravitreal ocriplasmin injection was performed OD without improvement in vision or OCT appearance after 1 month. A pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) was performed OD with release of the vitreomacular traction (VMT) and improvement of the foveal contour. The vision was 20/30 OD 1 month postoperatively. The OS has remained unchanged with vision of 20/40 without clinically important or progressive symptoms, so it has been observed.
Symptom Onset: 3 months
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia over 3 months
Characteristics: Vitreous hemorrhage due to breakthrough bleeding from choroidal neovascularization associated with macular degeneration; presents with progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia, reduced visual acuity (20/60 OD, 20/40 OS), vitreous blood obscuring view, diagnosed/assessed with B‑scan ultrasound and OCT showing VMT and hyperreflective posterior hyaloid partially attached to macula.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Sleeping in a slightly inclined (head-elevated) position
Associated Symptoms: Progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye interfering with driving and reading abilities
Prior Treatments: previous treatment for macular degeneration (prior anti-VEGF phototherapy/laser not explicitly specified)
Impact on Daily Activities: Interfering with driving and reading abilities
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: - Proliferative retinal vascular disease (e.g., proliferative diabetic retinopathy) - Posterior vitreous detachment with retinal tear - Choroidal neovascularization (wet AMD) with breakthrough bleeding - Retinal vein occlusion (branch retinal vein occlusion) - Sickle cell retinopathy - Prior ocular trauma (blunt or penetrating) - Terson’s syndrome - Choroidal melanoma - Retinal macroaneurysm - Inflammatory vitreous opacities (e.g., pars planitis, posterior uveitis)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (N5)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear conjunctiva and normal sclera
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Lens: absence of cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: clear cornea, absence of cataract, and intraocular pressure of 13 mm Hg in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Macula: focal areas of adherent posterior vitreous to the macula with raised retinal tissue and nonspecific loss of foveal reflex (OD: hyperreflective posterior hyaloid partially attached causing elevation/distortion; OS: focal vitreomacular attachment with inner foveal layer distortion)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Not described.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Vitreous hemorrhage due to breakthrough bleeding associated with choroidal neovascularization, OS.
Primary Diagnosis: Vitreous hemorrhage due to breakthrough bleeding from choroidal neovascularization (wet age-related macular degeneration), left eye (OS)
Differential Diagnosis: Spontaneous vitreous hemorrhage from choroidal neovascularization/breakthrough subretinal hemorrhage; Posterior vitreous detachment with retinal tear (± retinal detachment); Proliferative retinopathies (proliferative diabetic retinopathy, branch retinal vein occlusion, sickle retinopathy); Inflammatory vitreous opacities (pars planitis/uveitis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS


Plan
All Procedures: intravitreal ocriplasmin injection OD pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) OD
Follow-Up Instructions: Close follow-up with periodic clinical exam and B‑scan ultrasound as needed; observe for spontaneous clearing and reassess vision; consider vitrectomy if hemorrhage does not clear by 3–6 months (earlier if bilateral vision loss or other indications); counsel patient about visual prognosis and return promptly for any worsening (pain, increased floaters, flashes, or curtain).
Patient Education: Vitreous hemorrhage means blood has leaked into the gel of the eye, most often from abnormal new blood vessels or a retinal tear.
