Important points: - You may notice sudden floaters, haze, or decreased vision; activity that worsens vision may improve as blood settles. - Immediate evaluation is needed to rule out a retinal tear or detachment; B‑scan ultrasound is used when the view is blocked. - If caused by proliferative disease (eg, wet AMD, diabetic retinopathy) or a retinal tear, prompt treatment (laser, injection, or surgery) may prevent further vision loss. - Many small hemorrhages clear on their own over weeks to months; we will monitor vision and perform repeat exams/imaging. If bleeding does not clear or there is a retinal tear/detachment, vitrectomy (surgery to remove the blood) may be recommended. - There is no medication proven to speed clearing of the blood; positioning (slight head elevation) can let blood settle temporarily to improve examination. - Report new flashes, increased floaters, sudden worsening of vision, or curtain/shadow over vision immediately. - Keep follow‑up appointments and treat underlying causes (eg, anti‑VEGF injections or laser for AMD/diabetic disease) to reduce recurrence.


CASE 44 — Retinitis Pigmentosa
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden decreased vision in the left eye for 3 months
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 90-year-old woman with a 3-month history of sudden decreased vision in the left eye; prior vision 3 years earlier was 20/50 OD and 20/200 OS from atrophic AMD OD and previously treated subfoveal CNV OS. Current VA at presentation 20/40 OD and 2/200 OS; left dense vitreous hemorrhage with 2+ nuclear sclerosis, B-scan showing vitreous hemorrhage with PVD OS, no retinal tear or detachment. Observed without treatment for 3 months, then VA improved to 20/400 with visible subfoveal laser scar and contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery. No history of diabetes or hypertension.
History Narrative: This 90-year-old woman presented with a 3-month history of sudden decreased vision in the left eye. Her visual acuity was 20/40 OD and 2/200 OS. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg in each eye. Slit-lamp examination showed a well-positioned posterior chamber lens implant on the right with vitreous adherent to the wound superiorly. In the left eye, she had 2+ nuclear sclerosis and a very dense vitreous hemorrhage precluding a view posteriorly. Past ocular history included vision of 20/50 OD and 20/200 OS three years earlier due to atrophic age-related macular degeneration OD and a previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS. Medical history was negative for diabetes or hypertension. B-scan/A-scan ultrasonography showed vitreous hemorrhage with posterior vitreous detachment OS without retinal tear or detachment, and a disciform scar could not be resolved in the left macula. She was observed without treatment for 3 months; on follow-up visual acuity improved to 20/400 and a subfoveal laser scar with contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery was visible.
Symptom Onset: Between the second and fifth decades of life
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Moderately advanced, gradual progressive visual loss (variable rate)
Characteristics: Genetically heterogeneous inherited retinal degenerative disorder with progressive rod then cone photoreceptor dysfunction and death, presenting with nyctalopia, progressive peripheral vision loss, midperipheral retinal atrophy with vascular attenuation and pigmentary clumping (“bone spicules”), possible posterior subcapsular cataract and cystoid macular edema; most retain central vision until late stages; variable inheritance (sporadic, AR, AD, X‑linked).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exposure to damaging solar ultraviolet light
Associated Symptoms: Gradual progressive nyctalopia and progressive loss of peripheral vision
Prior Treatments: Previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS; posterior chamber intraocular lens OD (prior cataract surgery).
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — progressive peripheral vision loss and nyctalopia causing mobility and night-vision limitations, but central vision relatively preserved allowing many daily tasks until late disease.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Genetic inheritance (autosomal recessive, autosomal dominant, X-linked) and family history (including sporadic cases); advanced age not a risk but disease typically presents earlier; possible associated conditions (syndromic forms: Usher, Bardet–Biedl, Refsum); prior retinal disease not a risk.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 2/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 2/200
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear and quiet
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris
Anterior Segment – Lens: Right eye: posterior chamber intraocular lens, well-positioned. Left eye: 2+ nuclear sclerosis.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils were normal in size and shape (no afferent pupillary defect noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect (pupils reactive to light)
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD)
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no restrictions)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: well-positioned posterior chamber intraocular lens; vitreous adherent to superior wound. Left eye: 2+ nuclear sclerosis. Anterior segments otherwise unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic nerve pallor (mild to moderate)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Subfoveal laser scar with contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery; macular atrophic changes (disciform scar could not be resolved initially)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Attenuated (vascular attenuation)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Midperipheral retinal atrophy with vascular attenuation and bone-spicule pigmentary clumping, more apparent inferiorly extending toward the midperiphery.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Retinitis pigmentosa, sporadic, moderately advanced — patient with midperipheral retinal atrophy, vascular attenuation, and pigmentary clumping with midperipheral ring scotomas on Goldmann VF; central acuity relatively preserved. No family history noted. Recommend counseling, genetic testing discussion, low-vision aids as needed, monitoring for CME and cataract, consider vitamin supplementation and retinal implant options in end-stage disease.
Primary Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa, sporadic, moderately advanced
Differential Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa; Choroideremia; Gyrate atrophy; Vitamin A deficiency
Severity: Moderately advanced
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Progressive visual field loss leading to legal blindness (advanced stages) - Posterior subcapsular cataract (stellate PSC) - Cystoid macular edema (CME) - Retinal vascular attenuation and optic nerve atrophy - Posterior vitreous detachment and vitreous syneresis with vitreous hemorrhage - Pigmentary clumping (“bone spicules”)/retinal atrophy - Increased posterior capsule fibrosis after cataract surgery (requiring multiple YAG capsulotomies) - Potential for total visual loss in minority of patients


Plan
All Medications: Vitamin A palmitate 15,000 IU daily; Vitamin E (not recommended — may hasten progression); Lutein (oral); DHA (docosahexaenoic acid, oral); Acetazolamide (oral); Dorzolamide (topical, less effective for CME)
All Procedures: Observation (conservative management) for 3 months; B-scan/A-scan ultrasonography; slit-lamp examination; indirect ophthalmoscopy with scleral depression (attempted); visual acuity testing; periodic follow-up examinations.
Follow-Up Instructions: If cataract or CME develops, consider treatment (cataract extraction may be challenging; anticipate possible multiple YAG posterior capsulotomies). Periodic yearly liver function tests may be helpful if placed on vitamin A therapy. Provide counseling regarding visual prognosis, genetic testing and low-vision rehabilitation; advise sunglasses to reduce solar UV exposure and routine follow-up for monitoring (eg, annual exams; more frequent if new symptoms).
Patient Education: Counseling regarding visual prognosis and hereditary risk to family members; advice on reducing retinal UV exposure with sunglasses; discussion of low-vision aids and rehabilitation options; inform about potential (but controversial) supplements (vitamin A, lutein, DHA) and risks/benefits and need for genetic testing to clarify inheritance and trial eligibility.
Referrals: Low vision rehabilitation (low-vision services)


CASE 45 — Choroiditis
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision and “blind spots” in both eyes for 1 week
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "blind spots"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with a 1-week history of decreased vision and “blind spots” in both eyes, preceded by fevers and headaches 3 weeks earlier; no eye pain, no redness; best-corrected visual acuity 20/30 OU, normal IOPs, no afferent pupillary defect, normal anterior segment and no anterior or vitreous cellular inflammation; fundus exam showed multiple flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior pole of both eyes.
History Narrative: A 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with a 1-week history of decreased vision and “blind spots” in both eyes. He reported recent fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior to his ocular symptoms.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Characteristics: Bilateral, granulomatous posterior/panuveitic choroiditis with multiple flat deep creamy placoid lesions in the posterior pole; outer retinal and RPE disruption on OCT; early FA hypofluorescence with late staining and corresponding ICG hypofluorescence; associated recent systemic prodrome (fever, headache) in a young adult.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Systemic corticosteroids (oral prednisone or IV steroids), cycloplegic agents, topical prednisolone acetate 1%, H2‑blocker (e.g., ranitidine) to protect the stomach, and if needed systemic immunosuppressants (cyclosporine, cyclophosphamide, chlorambucil, azathioprine)
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision; "blind spots" (scotomas); recent fevers; headaches; photopsias; photophobia; floaters
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision with central scotomas and photopsias affecting reading, driving, and visually demanding tasks; improved with treatment but requires frequent follow-up.
Systemic Symptoms: recent fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior
Risk Factors: Young to middle-aged adults, more common in darkly pigmented individuals (Asian, Hispanic, Native American), and HLA-DR4 association; recent viral prodrome (fever, headache) also reported.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: Normal
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos; orbits normal on external examination.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: clear, no anterior or vitreous cellular inflammation
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal, reactive, no afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – Reaction to Near: normal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The anterior segment was normal; no anterior or vitreous cellular inflammation.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normal with no edema or pallor
Fundus Exam – Macula: Multiple flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior pole involving the macula with outer retinal/RPE disruption and decreased vision (central scotomas)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal caliber and course
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal peripheral retina; no lesions noted


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with 1 week of bilateral decreased vision and scotomata following recent febrile illness and headaches. Examination: BCVA 20/30 OU, normal IOPs, no APD, quiet anterior segment and vitreous. Fundus: multiple flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior poles OU. FA: early hypofluorescence with late staining corresponding to lesions; ICG: areas of decreased fluorescence. OCT: outer retinal hyperreflectivity, disruption of photoreceptor outer segments and RPE correlating with placoid lesions. Clinical presentation and multimodal imaging consistent with Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome (acute posterior placoid-type choroiditis). Recommend prompt systemic corticosteroid therapy and close follow-up for inflammatory control and monitoring for complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada syndrome
Differential Diagnosis: Acute posterior multifocal placoid pigment epitheliopathy (APMPPE); Serpiginous choroiditis; Multiple evanescent white dot syndrome (MEWDS); Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: posterior synechiae; angle-closure glaucoma; choroidal neovascularization; cataract formation; exudative retinal detachment


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% topical; oral prednisone (80–200 mg/day); ranitidine 150 mg twice daily; cycloplegic agent; intravenous steroids (for severe cases); cyclosporine; cyclophosphamide; chlorambucil; azathioprine
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow frequently in the initial stages to adjust corticosteroid treatment and monitor inflammation; once inflammation and exudative detachment decrease, lengthen intervals and taper systemic steroids slowly (over ~4–6 months, often up to 6 months) with careful observation for recurrence; if steroids inadequate or not tolerated, consider systemic immunosuppressants and refer for management; monitor regularly for cataract, choroidal neovascularization, and angle-closure glaucoma.
Patient Education: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome is an inflammatory condition affecting the back of the eye and sometimes other body systems. Prompt treatment with high‑dose oral corticosteroids (and in severe cases IV steroids) is important to reduce inflammation and preserve vision. You may also receive eyedrops (steroid drops and a cycloplegic) and stomach protection (H2 blocker) while on high‑dose steroids. Expect frequent follow‑up initially to monitor response and adjust therapy; steroids are tapered very slowly over months to reduce relapse risk. If steroids are ineffective or cause intolerable side effects, other immunosuppressive medications may be used. Report new symptoms such as worsening vision, eye pain, severe headache, hearing changes, dizziness, or skin/hair changes promptly. Long‑term monitoring is needed for complications like cataract, glaucoma, or choroidal neovascularization.
Work Restrictions: Avoid heavy physical exertion and activities risking head/eye trauma while on high-dose systemic corticosteroids; may require frequent follow-up visits and possible time off work during acute treatment and taper (activity modification as tolerated).


CASE 46 — Retinitis
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: constant pain in the right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had constant pain in my right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week, and a 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old Hispanic woman with 3 weeks of constant right eye pain, 1 week of right eye redness, tearing, and blurred vision, and 1 month of headache and neck stiffness; reported 2 days of preceding flu-like symptoms; no history of trauma. Visual acuity initially 20/20 OU, later decreased to 3/200 OD; exam notable for right anterior chamber cell and flare with granulomatous keratic precipitates, vitreous cells, optic disc edema, serous retinal detachment and macular folds; left eye mild anterior inflammation that progressed with new nasal macular subretinal fluid. CT head normal; lumbar puncture showed mild pleocytosis. Treated initially with topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old Hispanic woman presented with a 3-week history of constant right eye pain, 1 week of blurred vision, redness, and tearing in the right eye, and a 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness. She had a 2-day prodrome of flu-like symptoms and denied any history of trauma. Initial CT brain was normal; lumbar puncture showed a mild pleocytosis. On exam initial visual acuity was 20/20 OU, IOP 18 mm Hg OD and 16 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp showed moderate anterior chamber cell and flare OD with granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium, and a mild anterior chamber reaction in the left eye. Vitreous cells were present OD. Fundus exam OD revealed optic disc edema with focal serous retinal detachment and macular retinal folding; the left eye was unremarkable. B-scan OD showed diffuse low-reflectivity choroidal thickening with a shallow serous macular detachment. She was started on topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics. One week later she returned with persistent pain and markedly decreased vision OD to 3/200; anterior chamber reaction had slightly decreased OD but increased in OS with a few keratic precipitates. Fundus OD showed increased disc edema, subretinal fluid and retinal folds, and focal yellow-white RPE-level lesions in the macula; OS now had a new focal area of subretinal fluid in the nasal macula with vision 20/25. Fluorescein angiography OD demonstrated multiple pinpoint juxtapapillary and macular hyperfluorescent spots leaking in late frames; a similar lesion was seen adjacent to the optic disc in OS.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 1 month
Progression: Progressive, often leading to severe visual loss
Characteristics: Infectious, typically viral/fungal/parasitic (opportunistic) causing progressive retinal inflammation and necrosis leading to severe visual loss; often in immunocompromised (AIDS/HIV) patients; may be unilateral or bilateral; may cause vitreous cells, optic disc edema, serous/exudative retinal detachment, retinal necrosis, possible choroiditis; diagnosis clinical plus PCR/culture; responsive to systemic and/or intravitreal antivirals; high risk of retinal detachment.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Immunodeficiency (HIV/AIDS)
Associated Symptoms: constant right eye pain for 3 weeks; blurred vision; redness; tearing; headache; neck stiffness; flulike symptoms (fever/viral prodrome); decreased vision (markedly in right eye)
Prior Treatments: Topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics
Impact on Daily Activities: Markedly decreased vision in right eye to 3/200 causing significant impairment of daily activities (severe functional limitation).
Systemic Symptoms: headache, neck stiffness, and two days of flu-like symptoms (fever/viral prodrome)
Risk Factors: HIV/AIDS (immunodeficiency), systemic immunosuppression (e.g., immunosuppressive therapy), opportunistic infections, compromised host immune status


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 82 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 3/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Redness (conjunctival injection)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Moderate cell and flare in the right eye; mild anterior chamber inflammatory reaction in the left eye (later increased cell and flare with a few keratic precipitates).
Anterior Segment – Iris: granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium with moderate anterior chamber cell and flare (granulomatous iridocyclitis)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, no cataract apparent


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Reactive to light and near (normal near reaction)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderate anterior chamber cell and flare in the right eye with granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium; mild anterior chamber inflammatory reaction in the left eye.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic disc edema
Fundus Exam – Macula: Right: focal serous retinal detachment with retinal folds and focal yellow‑white RPE lesions in the macula; Left: shallow serous macular detachment nasally with a focal area of subretinal fluid (20/25 vision)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and caliber within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Multiple peripheral areas of retinal necrosis with hemorrhage consistent with CMV retinitis


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: CMV retinitis, OU, in a patient with AIDS.
Primary Diagnosis: CMV retinitis, OU in a patient with AIDS
Secondary Diagnoses: HIV/AIDS, CMV retinitis (bilateral), optic disc edema, serous retinal detachment, granulomatous anterior uveitis
Differential Diagnosis: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome; Sympathetic ophthalmia; Infectious posterior uveitis (toxoplasmosis/CMV retinitis); Sarcoidosis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OU
Complications: Retinal detachment; cataract formation; long-term visual loss; need for long-term rehabilitation/follow-up


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% topical; cycloplegics; intravenous ganciclovir; foscarnet; cidofovir; intravitreal ganciclovir; intravitreal foscarnet; ganciclovir implant; silicone oil (tamponade)
All Procedures: Topical prednisolone acetate 1% administration; Topical cycloplegic administration; Computed tomography (CT) brain; Lumbar puncture with CSF analysis; Slit-lamp examination; B-scan ocular ultrasonography; Funduscopic examination; Fluorescein angiography; Intravenous ganciclovir (induction/high-dose) — standard of care option; Intravitreal ganciclovir injection (2,000 μg) — option; Ganciclovir intravitreal implant insertion — option; Pars plana vitrectomy with silicone oil tamponade — option
Follow-Up Instructions: Close follow-up with photographic documentation.
Referrals: Ophthalmology retina specialist for intravitreal therapy/retinal surgery; Infectious disease/HIV specialist for systemic CMV management and antiretroviral therapy


CASE 47 — The White Dot Syndromes
=================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: multiple black spots in the visual field of both eyes for 2 weeks
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of the right and left eyes for 2 weeks"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32-year-old woman with a 10-year history of AIDS (on HAART) and CD4 count 64 presenting with 2 weeks of new onset multiple black spots (floaters) in both visual fields. She reported no anterior segment symptoms; vision decreased to 20/30 OD and 20/40 OS.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old woman with a 10-year history of acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS) on highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART) and a CD4 count of 64 presented with a 2-week history of new onset multiple black spots in the visual fields of both eyes. She reported no significant anterior segment symptoms; systemic history notable for long-standing HIV/AIDS managed with HAART.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Characteristics: Multiple deep, creamy‑yellow/white placoid lesions at the level of the outer retina/RPE (often bilateral), early hypofluorescence on FA with late staining, minimal anterior/vitreous inflammation, disruption of outer photoreceptor segments on OCT, usually self‑limited with good visual prognosis.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: No known alleviating factors/N/A
Associated Symptoms: new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field (floaters)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — visual symptoms (scotomas, decreased central vision, photopsias) likely interfere with reading, driving, and detailed work but many conditions are self-limited or partially recover with treatment.
Systemic Symptoms: viral prodrome, headaches
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Individuals with recent viral prodrome or upper respiratory infection (e.g., recent fevers, headache)
Risk Factors: - Recent viral prodrome/upper respiratory infection - Young age (20–40s for many syndromes) - Female sex (especially myopic females for MEWDS, MCP, PIC) - Myopia (MEWDS, MCP, PIC) - Caucasian ethnicity (APMPPE, SC, BSCR) - HLA-A29 positivity (birdshot chorioretinopathy) - Immunosuppression/low CD4 count (risk for infectious retinitis like CMV)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 BPM
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: no cells or flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable with no anterior chamber cell or flare
Fundus Exam – Macula: multiple deep cream-colored placoid lesions in the posterior pole with granular macular appearance and disruption of outer retinal architecture (multiple small white and orange specks)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Vascular sheathing superior to the disc extending along the superotemporal arcade; mild retinal vasculitis possible in APMPPE/MEWDS descriptions; retinal vasculitis present in birdshot and MCP contexts.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Creamy yellowish-white retinitis with hemorrhage in the right superior midperiphery and peripheral area of retinitis temporally in the left eye


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 32-year-old woman with AIDS (CD4 64) on HAART presenting with 2 weeks of new black spots bilaterally. Examination notable for BCVA 20/30 OD, 20/40 OS; IOP 10 mm Hg OU; quiet anterior segments. Fundus OD: confluent creamy yellow-white hemorrhagic retinitis with prominent associated hemorrhage extending from superior disc into midperiphery, active vitritis and vascular sheathing along superotemporal arcade. OS: peripheral temporal retinitis without hemorrhage. Findings most consistent with bilateral CMV retinitis (classic hemorrhagic, necrotizing retinitis tracking along arcades) in the setting of advanced immunosuppression. Differential includes acute retinal necrosis and toxoplasmosis, but lack of marked anterior inflammation and hemorrhagic pattern favor CMV. Recommend prompt systemic antiviral therapy and ophthalmic management with frequent funduscopic monitoring; consider intravitreal antivirals and coordination with infectious disease for systemic CMV treatment and evaluation of immune status.
Primary Diagnosis: Cytomegalovirus (CMV) retinitis
Differential Diagnosis: Cytomegalovirus (CMV) retinitis; Acute retinal necrosis (herpes viruses); Ocular toxoplasmosis; Syphilitic retinitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: CNVM; CME; ERM; RPE atrophy and scarring; optic neuropathy; retinal detachment; neovascular glaucoma


Plan
All Medications: highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART) oral corticosteroids periocular steroids systemic steroids immunosuppression / immunomodulatory therapy (e.g., cyclosporine, mycophenolate, azathioprine) antituberculosis therapy intravitreal steroids anti-vascular endothelial growth factor (anti-VEGF)
All Procedures: funduscopic examination; indirect ophthalmoscopy; fluorescein angiography (FA); optical coherence tomography (OCT); indocyanine green angiography (ICG); fundus photography; fundus autofluorescence (FAF); electroretinogram (ERG); Goldmann visual field testing; Humphrey visual field testing; Amsler grid testing; periocular steroid injection; intravitreal steroid injection; anti-VEGF intravitreal injection; laser photocoagulation; systemic corticosteroid therapy (oral corticosteroids); immunomodulatory therapy (IMT) / immunosuppression; HLA-A29 testing; neuroimaging; laboratory evaluation for tuberculosis and syphilis; ophthalmoscopic screening every 3–6 months
Patient Education: These white‑dot syndromes are inflammatory conditions of the retina/choroid that often follow a viral illness, can cause floaters, photopsias, scotomas or decreased vision, and are diagnosed mainly with eye imaging (FA, ICG, OCT). Many are self‑limited (e.g., MEWDS, APMPPE, ARPE) and often improve without treatment; others (e.g., serpiginous choroiditis, birdshot, multifocal choroiditis) can be chronic and may require long‑term immunosuppression to prevent vision loss. Prompt evaluation for systemic causes (e.g., tuberculosis, syphilis) or neurologic symptoms is important. Follow prescribed imaging and follow‑up schedule; report new visual changes immediately. If on immunosuppression or for HIV patients, maintain systemic therapy and routine screening (e.g., ophthalmic exams every 3–6 months if CD4 <100).


CASE 48 — Vascular Tumor
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for 3 days
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for the previous 3 days"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old Caucasian man with a 2-week history of photopsias and 3 days of blurred central vision in the right eye.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-week history of photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for the previous 3 days. Past medical history was notable for hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and skin cancer. Past ocular history was remarkable for macular degeneration. Examination disclosed a visual acuity of 20/60 in the right eye and 20/20 in the left eye. Intraocular pressures were 15 mm Hg in both eyes. There was a 1+ right afferent pupillary defect. Slit-lamp examination was notable for mild nuclear sclerotic alterations in both eyes. Dilated funduscopic examination of the right eye revealed a dome-shaped moderately pigmented inferotemporal mass with an exudative retinal detachment located anterior to the mass. Examination of the left eye was unremarkable. The lesion had a thickness of 4.5 mm and base of 11.9 × 10.1 mm confirmed by echography. B-scan ultrasonography demonstrated a dome-shaped lesion with an exudative retinal detachment and choroidal excavation. There was no evidence of extrascleral extension. A-scan ultrasonography revealed low internal reflectivity and a regular internal structure. Fluorescein angiography of the right eye demonstrated a lesion with intrinsic vascularity and progressive late leakage. The patient underwent a medical workup to rule out the presence of metastasis, including liver function tests and a chest X-ray that was unremarkable. The patient was diagnosed with a medium-sized posterior uveal melanoma. After discussion of alternative treatment options, the patient underwent fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling at the time of iodine-125 radioactive plaque placement. Gene profiling yielded a class 1A tumor, prompting annual liver ultrasound for metastatic evaluation. Seven months following radioactive plaque therapy, visual acuity in the right eye was 20/40 and there was progressive nuclear sclerosis of the lens. The mass had decreased in size posttreatment to a height of 2.3 mm. There were significant pigmentary alterations of the lesion with resolution of the overlying neurosensory detachment. The remainder of the retina remained normal, without radiation-related changes.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Decreased in size (height reduced from 4.5 mm to 2.3 mm) with resolution of overlying neurosensory detachment and progressive nuclear sclerosis of the lens.
Characteristics: Dome-shaped, moderately pigmented inferotemporal choroidal mass with intrinsic vascularity, low internal reflectivity on A-scan, regular internal structure, choroidal excavation, associated exudative (anterior) retinal detachment, decreased in size after plaque radiotherapy with pigmentary alterations.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Photopsias and blurred central vision
Prior Treatments: Iodine-125 radioactive plaque therapy with concurrent fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased central vision in the right eye (20/60 → 20/40) with photopsias and recent blurred central vision causing some functional limitation.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and skin cancer
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: First-degree relatives
Risk Factors: von Hippel–Lindau disease (genetic predisposition), family history of VHL, early-onset retinal angiomas (age), systemic phacomatoses, genetic mutations of VHL (chromosome 3p25–26), prior vascular proliferative ocular conditions, hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, skin cancer (as part of systemic disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal system without external abnormality (no palpable lacrimal mass or swelling)
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbit without abnormality detected
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear corneas, no corneal abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear anterior chamber with mild nuclear sclerotic cataract changes (no cells or flare reported)
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild nuclear sclerotic cataracts (progressive nuclear sclerosis posttreatment)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: 1+
Pupils – Other: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in both eyes (no limitations)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild nuclear sclerotic alterations in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Central vision blurred OD; macula with exudative retinal detachment overlying tumor OD; remainder of macula normal OS (left macula unremarkable)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 1. Right eye: Posterior uveal (choroidal) melanoma, medium-sized (initial thickness 4.5 mm, basal dimensions ~11.9 × 10.1 mm) with exudative retinal detachment and intrinsic vascularity on angiography. A- and B-scan ultrasound consistent with dome-shaped lesion, low internal reflectivity, choroidal excavation, no extrascleral extension. Fine-needle aspiration performed for gene-expression profiling which returned Class 1A (low metastatic potential). Systemic metastatic workup (LFTs, chest X-ray) unremarkable. 2. Post-treatment status (7 months after I-125 plaque radiotherapy): Tumor regression to 2.3 mm thickness with resolution of overlying neurosensory detachment, pigmentary changes at lesion site, visual acuity improved to 20/40 OD, progressive nuclear sclerosis of the lens; no radiation-related retinal vasculopathy observed. 3. Left eye: Unremarkable; visual acuity 20/20 OS, IOPs 15 mm Hg OU. Plan: Continue surveillance for local control and radiation-induced changes; annual systemic metastatic screening (liver ultrasound) given Class 1A result per institutional protocol; monitor cataract progression and visual function; routine ophthalmic follow-up with periodic imaging (B-scan/OCT/fundus photography) and systemic surveillance as indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: Choroidal melanoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Hypercholesterolemia; Skin cancer; Macular degeneration
Differential Diagnosis: Choroidal melanoma; Choroidal hemangioma; Metastatic choroidal tumor
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Exudative retinal detachment - Progressive nuclear sclerosis (cataract) - Vision loss/reduced visual acuity - Radiation-related changes (potential, though not observed)


Plan
All Medications: iodine-125 radioactive plaque (radiation therapy)
All Procedures: fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling; iodine-125 radioactive plaque placement (plaque radiotherapy)
Follow-Up Instructions: Annual to biannual ophthalmic examination for affected individuals and first‑degree relatives; periodic systemic screening with CNS and thoracic/abdominal imaging and 24‑hour urine collection for pheochromocytoma.
Patient Education: Von Hippel–Lindau (VHL) disease is an inherited condition that can cause tumors in the eye and other organs. Because retinal angiomas (tumors of the retina) are often an early sign, it is important to have regular eye exams and systemic screening. You should: - Have periodic ophthalmic exams (indirect ophthalmoscopy) — typically annually to every six months — and ensure first-degree relatives are examined as well. - Undergo genetic counseling and consider genetic testing for VHL, especially if there is a family history or multiple findings. - Receive systemic screening for VHL-associated tumors: imaging of the brain/spine (CNS), chest and abdomen (to evaluate kidneys, pancreas, adrenal glands), and 24-hour urine testing for pheochromocytoma. - Report new symptoms promptly (vision changes, headaches, back pain, high blood pressure, palpitations, abdominal pain). - Coordinate care with a multidisciplinary team (ophthalmology, genetics, neurosurgery, oncology/urology/endocrinology) for surveillance and treatment planning. - Understand treatment options for retinal angiomas: observation for small, stable lesions; laser photocoagulation or cryotherapy for vision‑threatening lesions; possible plaque radiotherapy or external beam radiotherapy for large lesions; and that intravitreal or systemic anti‑VEGF therapies are investigational. - Know that treatment responses can take months and complications (increased exudation, vitreoretinal traction) may occur; surgery may be needed for complications but is often delayed 4–6 months to allow spontaneous improvement. - Maintain regular follow-up and imaging as recommended by your care team to monitor for tumor progression and systemic involvement.


CASE 49 — Choroidal Melanoma
============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Peripheral retinal lesion in left eye found on routine ophthalmic examination
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 49-year-old man asymptomatic found on routine ophthalmic examination to have a peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye; no ocular symptoms, no past ocular history, no systemic medical problems; family history notable for father and two siblings with von Hippel–Lindau disease; visual acuity 20/20 bilaterally.
History Narrative: A 20-year-old man presented for evaluation of a peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye found on routine ophthalmic examination. He was asymptomatic with no past ocular history and no known systemic medical problems. Family history was notable for von Hippel–Lindau disease in his father and two siblings. Visual acuity was 20/20 bilaterally. External exam, pupils (no afferent pupillary defect), visual fields, and motility were unremarkable. Slit-lamp exam was normal. Fundus examination of the left eye showed several dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules emanating from the optic disc and traveling nasally, leading to a collection of reddish‑pink nodules surrounding a large reddish‑orange lesion on the retinal surface with a small area of associated subretinal fluid and adjacent exudate. The right eye fundus was normal.
Symptom Duration: Asymptomatic
Characteristics: - Pigmented (often), dome- or mushroom-shaped choroidal mass with low–to–medium internal reflectivity on A-scan, intrinsic vascularity on B-scan, possible orange lipofuscin hyperautofluorescence, and associated subretinal fluid on OCT.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Asymptomatic
Impact on Daily Activities: None
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: None
Risk Factors: - Light iris color (blue or green) - Fair skin/fair complexion - Increasing age (most common in adults) - Male sex (slightly higher risk) - Ocular/iris/choroidal nevus presence - Familial/genetic predisposition (e.g., BAP1 mutation) - UV light exposure (suspected)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Unremarkable
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No afferent pupillary defect.
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no motility deficits
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp examination is normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal (no macular abnormality)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules emanating from the optic disc and traveling nasally
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Collection of reddish-pink nodules surrounding a large reddish-orange lesion with dilated, tortuous feeder arterioles and draining venules, with a small area of adjacent subretinal fluid and exudate (peripheral, nasally located)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: medium-sized choroidal melanoma.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: medium-sized choroidal melanoma
Differential Diagnosis: choroidal nevus; congenital hypertrophy of the RPE (CHRPE); metastatic carcinoma to the choroid; choroidal hemangioma
Severity: Medium
Laterality: Right
Complications: radiation-related complications including retinopathy, optic neuropathy, and cataract; radiation-vasculopathy and optic neuropathy (occur at ~30–50% at 5 years, increased for tumors adjacent to optic nerve or fovea)


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up every 3 months during the first year, then every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly thereafter; perform clinical exam, fundus photography, and ultrasonography at each visit (use autofluorescence and OCT as indicated); note that ultrasound may show transient post‑treatment height increase in first 6 months; for systemic surveillance use gene‑expression–based risk stratification: class 1A — annual liver imaging; class 1B — annual liver imaging + liver enzymes staggered by 6 months; class 2 — liver imaging and liver enzymes every 6 months staggered by 3 months.
Patient Education: Choroidal melanoma is a cancer in the layer beneath the retina (choroid). It can threaten vision and can spread to other organs (most commonly the liver), so close follow-up and systemic monitoring are important. Treatment depends on size and location and may include plaque brachytherapy (radiation to save the eye), enucleation (removal of the eye) for very large tumors or poor visual prognosis, or less commonly local resection or charged-particle therapy. Before treatment you may need imaging (eye ultrasonography, OCT, fundus photos) and systemic staging (liver imaging, possibly blood tests). After radiation, expect regular follow-up exams with imaging every 3 months the first year, then extending to 6 months and yearly thereafter to monitor tumor regression, detect recurrence, and watch for complications (radiation retinopathy, optic neuropathy, cataract). If tumor is genotyped, follow-up for metastasis is risk-based: low-risk (annual liver imaging), intermediate-risk (annual liver imaging + staggered liver enzymes), high-risk (liver imaging and liver enzymes every 6 months staggered by 3 months). Report new visual changes, eye pain, or systemic symptoms (abdominal pain, weight loss, fatigue) promptly. Discuss fertility, work, driving, and prosthetic/rehabilitation resources if enucleation is needed; referral to ocular oncology and medical oncology for systemic surveillance is recommended.


CASE 50 — Dislocated Posterior Chamber Intraocular Lens
=======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 78-year-old woman presented 4 days after a complicated left cataract surgery with acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye; vision was hand motion OS, IOP 39 mm Hg OS, left eye had conjunctival injection, shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, temporal clear corneal incision with interrupted nylon sutures, and a bullous appositional retinal detachment posterior to the iris; past ocular history notable for high myopia (−9.00), primary open-angle glaucoma, and she was on timolol, dorzolamide, and prednisolone acetate OD/OS.
History Narrative: A 78-year-old woman with myopia (−9.00 D) and primary open-angle glaucoma presented 4 days after a complicated left cataract surgery with acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye. Past medical history included coronary artery bypass grafting 2 years earlier and systemic hypertension. Medications included topical timolol 0.5% OU twice daily, dorzolamide 2% OU three times daily, and prednisolone acetate 1% OS four times daily. On presentation visual acuity was 20/60 OD and hand motion OS; IOP was 12 mm Hg OD and 39 mm Hg OS. Left eye exam showed 2+ conjunctival injection, a temporal clear corneal incision closed with interrupted nylon sutures, a shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, and a bullous appositional retinal detachment posterior to the iris. B-scan ultrasound demonstrated appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages with clotted suprachoroidal blood. The elevated left IOP was managed medically; observation with serial echography was initially recommended. Eight days later liquefaction of the suprachoroidal blood was seen and the patient underwent pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of the suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange; no draining retinotomy was performed because no retinal breaks were identified. At three months follow-up left eye vision was 20/400.
Symptom Onset: 4 days after a complicated cataract surgery
Symptom Duration: 4 days
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) — late occurrence after cataract surgery; may result from zonular compromise (surgical trauma, nonsurgical trauma, pseudoexfoliation); presents with decentration, aphakia, glare, monocular diplopia; may be endocapsular dislocation or complete dislocation into vitreous; associated risks: myopia, glaucoma, advanced age, hypertension, anticoagulation, recent intraocular surgery; management: observation, topical miotics/anti-inflammatory/hypotensives, or surgical options (repositioning with/without scleral sutures, haptic externalization, IOL exchange)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: advanced age; atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease; systemic anticoagulation; hypertension; myopia (−9.00 D); glaucoma; aphakia; recent intraocular surgery
Alleviating Factors: Topical miotics
Associated Symptoms: Severe pain and loss of vision
Prior Treatments: timolol 0.5% OU twice daily; dorzolamide 2% OU three times daily; prednisolone acetate 1% OS four times daily; recent complicated left cataract surgery with temporal clear corneal incision closed with interrupted nylon sutures
Impact on Daily Activities: Severe — acute loss of vision OS (hand motion) with pain; significant functional impairment of affected eye impacting daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: cardiac bypass surgery 2 years previously; systemic hypertension
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: advanced age; atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease; systemic anticoagulation; hypertension; myopia (−9.00 D); glaucoma; aphakia; recent intraocular surgery; compromise of zonular integrity; pseudoexfoliation syndrome; initial surgical trauma; nonsurgical trauma


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 39 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 2 + conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with temporal clear corneal incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: shallow
Anterior Segment – Iris: 2+ conjunctival injection, shallow anterior chamber, aphakia
Anterior Segment – Angle: Narrow/closed angle (shallow anterior chamber)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL), aphakia (OS)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2 + conjunctival injection, a temporal clear cornea incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures, a shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, and the appearance of a bullous appositional retinal detachment posterior to the iris plane
Fundus Exam – Macula: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages with bullous appositional retinal detachment involving the posterior pole; macula elevated (detached).


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens, OS, with aphakia and associated suprachoroidal hemorrhage causing appositional (“kissing”) choroidal detachments, shallow anterior chamber, acute pain, markedly elevated IOP (39 mm Hg) on presentation; managed initially with medical IOP control and observation for clotted suprachoroidal hemorrhage, followed by pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange when liquefaction occurred, resulting in partial visual recovery to 20/400 at 3 months.
Primary Diagnosis: Dislocated posterior chamber IOL, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Myopia (high myopia -9.00), Primary open-angle glaucoma, Hypertension, History of cardiac bypass surgery, Aphakia, Suprachoroidal hemorrhage (with appositional/kissing suprachoroidal hemorrhages), Elevated intraocular pressure (left eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; Exudative (serous) retinal detachment/choroidal effusion; Suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: cystoid macular edema; elevated intraocular pressure; retinal detachment; suture knot erosion; endophthalmitis; hemorrhage; IOL torsion or malposition; recurrent dislocation due to suture breakage; endothelial trauma; postsurgical astigmatism


Plan
All Medications: timolol 0.5% OU twice daily; dorzolamide 2% OU three times per day; prednisolone acetate 1% OS four times per day
All Procedures: Pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange; closure of temporal clear cornea incision with interrupted nylon sutures.


CASE 51 — Cystoid Macular Edema
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after cataract surgery
DURATION: 6 years
LATERALITY: OD
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 64-year-old woman presented with sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after an uncomplicated extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL insertion; intraoperatively a central posterior capsular rupture occurred and the IOL was placed in the ciliary sulcus, and postoperatively she had 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly, moderate anterior chamber cells, vitreous incarceration in the wound, and a freely mobile PCIOL in the vitreous cavity inferiorly.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman presented with sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after an uncomplicated extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL insertion. During the procedure a central posterior capsular rupture occurred and the IOL was placed anterior to the remaining anterior capsule into the ciliary sulcus. Postoperatively her left eye vision was 20/30 with an aphakic correction. Slit-lamp exam showed 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly and moderate anterior chamber cells; the pupil was round but there was vitreous incarceration in the wound. Residual capsule was not noted. Fundus exam disclosed a freely mobile PCIOL within the vitreous cavity inferiorly with attached retina. Surgery was recommended and performed to reposition the dislocated PCIOL into the ciliary sulcus using scleral suturing of the haptics under two partial-thickness superotemporal and inferonasal scleral flaps to cover knots. One month postoperatively the IOL was well positioned with vision 20/60, which returned to 20/20 within 6 months and remained 20/20 at 6 years; the IOL stayed centered with no complications. The right eye vision was 20/400 from nuclear sclerosis.
Symptom Onset: 2 days after surgery
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema (Irvine–Gass syndrome)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Iris trauma, vitreous prolapse/incarceration, retained lens fragments, vitreomacular traction, inflammation from complicated cataract surgery (e.g., posterior capsular rupture)
Alleviating Factors: Resolution often occurs spontaneously within 3–4 months; topical NSAIDs and corticosteroids (and combination therapy) can improve CME; periocular or intravitreal steroid injections and pars plana vitrectomy may alleviate refractory cases.
Associated Symptoms: sudden decreased vision in the left eye, glare, monocular diplopia, pain
Previous Episodes: Yes — this was a new, postoperative CME; no prior episodes reported.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — transient visual impairment (decreased vision, glare) limiting activities like reading/driving for weeks to months, but ultimately returned to normal vision in affected eye.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: - Intraocular surgery (especially cataract extraction) - Posterior capsular rupture - Vitreous prolapse/incarceration in the wound - Retained lens fragments - Iris trauma - Prolonged or complicated surgery - Inflammation (postoperative intraocular inflammation) - Vitreomacular traction or adhesion - Coexisting retinovascular disease - Pseudoexfoliation/zonular dehiscence (leading to late dislocation)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm

Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white conjunctiva, no injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: moderate cells in the anterior chamber
Anterior Segment – Iris: round
