Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) in ciliary sulcus (initially), later dislocated into vitreous then repositioned and scleral‑fixated with haptics under scleral flaps; ultimately centered and well positioned.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and reactive
Pupils – RAPD: Negative
Pupils – Other: vitreous incarceration in the wound
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all cardinal directions without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly; moderate anterior chamber cells; pupil round; vitreous incarceration in the wound; pupillary space clear; residual capsule not noted.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Disc normal.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Cystoid macular edema (thickening with cystic spaces)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retina attached, no peripheral retinal lesions noted (periphery unremarkable)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema of the right eye (OD) following complicated cataract extraction with posterior capsular rupture and vitreous incarceration; exam and imaging demonstrate macular thickening with cystic changes consistent with CME. Initial management with topical NSAIDs and corticosteroids recommended, with consideration of periocular or intravitreal steroid or vitrectomy if refractory or if vitreomacular traction/retained lens material present. Follow-up in 4–6 weeks to assess response.
Primary Diagnosis: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema, OD
Differential Diagnosis: Pseudophakic (Irvine–Gass) cystoid macular edema; Vitreomacular traction–related macular edema; Inflammatory/postoperative uveitic macular edema
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: Cystoid macular edema; vitreous incarceration in the wound; posterior capsular rupture; dislocated posterior chamber IOL into the vitreous (IOL dislocation); corneal microbullous edema; anterior chamber inflammation (cells)


Plan
All Medications: flurbiprofen (topical), suprofen (topical), ketorolac 0.5% (topical), prednisolone 1% (topical), periocular corticosteroid injection, intravitreal corticosteroid injection, ranibizumab (intravitreal anti-VEGF), bevacizumab (intravitreal anti-VEGF), aflibercept (intravitreal anti-VEGF)
All Procedures: extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) insertion; repositioning of dislocated PCIOL with scleral suturing fixation through partial‑thickness scleral flaps (pars plana approach indicated as preferred)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe within 4 to 6 weeks after initiation of medical treatment; follow carefully even after improvement due to risk of recurrence.
Patient Education: Cystoid macular edema (CME) is swelling in the central retina that can occur after cataract surgery and may cause blurred vision. It is usually diagnosed by eye exam and OCT (retinal imaging). Most cases improve on their own over 3–4 months, but treatment often speeds recovery and prevents lasting vision loss. First-line therapy is topical anti-inflammatory eye drops (a steroid plus a nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory) used frequently for several weeks. If topical treatment fails, injections around or into the eye (steroids) or vitrectomy surgery may be recommended, especially if vitreous is pulling on the macula or there are retained lens fragments. You will need follow-up at about 4–6 weeks to check for improvement; some patients need longer treatment or repeat therapy. Report any worsening vision, new floaters, pain, or increasing redness promptly.


CASE 52 — Endophthalmitis
=========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased visual acuity in the right eye
DURATION: 8 weeks
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "decreased visual acuity in the right eye" "visual acuity was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks" "no other contributory medical or ophthalmic history including diabetes mellitus or hypertension"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 66‑year‑old woman presented 8 weeks after phacoemulsification with decreased visual acuity in the right eye. An acrylic IOL was placed in the ciliary sulcus at surgery because of a central posterior capsular rupture. Vision had been excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks. She denied other medical or ophthalmic history including diabetes or hypertension.
History Narrative: A 66-year-old woman presented 8 weeks after phacoemulsification complaining of decreased visual acuity in the right eye. An acrylic intraocular lens was inserted into the ciliary sulcus at the time of surgery due to a central posterior capsular rupture. The patient reported that the visual acuity was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks. The patient reported no other contributory medical or ophthalmic history including diabetes mellitus or hypertension.
Symptom Onset: 8 weeks after phacoemulsification (visual decline began after the first 4 weeks)
Symptom Duration: 8 weeks
Progression: Subacute (symptoms began ~4 weeks post-op, worsened over subsequent weeks)
Characteristics: Painful loss of vision with prominent intraocular inflammation, hypopyon, fibrin on the IOL, conjunctival hyperemia, vitreous opacities on B-scan, onset typically acute postoperative (days–weeks) but can be chronic or delayed depending on organism; may present with decreased visual acuity ranging to light perception.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: - Intraocular surgery (especially cataract surgery with posterior capsular rupture, vitreous loss, retained lens material) - Intravitreal injections - Trauma (open-globe injuries) - Late-onset bleb-associated infections - Presence of indwelling intraocular lens/capsule remnants - Immunodeficiency, diabetes mellitus, intravenous drug use, indwelling catheters, malignancy (for endogenous endophthalmitis) - Use of preservative-containing intraocular steroids (can mimic/predispose) - Lack of povidone-iodine antisepsis or breaches in sterile technique
Associated Symptoms: decreased visual acuity
Previous Episodes: no prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate impairment — decreased right-eye vision to 20/80 causing reduced visual function but not total disability
Systemic Symptoms: No other systemic symptoms reported.
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: - Intraocular surgery (especially cataract surgery) - Intravitreal injections - Posterior capsular rupture, vitreous loss, retained lens material - Trauma (open-globe injuries) - Late-onset bleb-associated (filtering bleb) - Immunodeficiency, diabetes mellitus, intravenous drug use, indwelling catheters, malignancy (risk for endogenous) - Use of prostaglandin analogs (postoperative CME risk factor increasing inflammation) - Lack of povidone-iodine antisepsis, inadequate sterile technique (injection-related)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N16
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: conjunctival hyperemia
External Exam – Lacrimal System: within normal limits
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos; orbit exam within normal limits.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: The cornea was clear and of normal thickness.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: open and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Acrylic intraocular lens in ciliary sulcus, well centered and stable; IOL placed after posterior capsular rupture.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: normal
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact with no restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: conjunctival hyperemia, hypopyon within the anterior chamber, and fibrin on the intraocular lens
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.2
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula thickened with cystic spaces in the fovea (CME)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: The peripheral retina was normal without holes, breaks, or tears.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute infectious postoperative endophthalmitis, left eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute infectious postoperative endophthalmitis, left eye
Differential Diagnosis: Infectious endophthalmitis (acute postoperative); Toxic anterior segment syndrome; Retained lens material–induced inflammation; Exacerbation of preexisting uveitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right
Complications: Endophthalmitis


Plan
All Medications: vancomycin; ceftazidime; corticosteroids (dexamethasone); amikacin; fourth-generation fluoroquinolones; topical vancomycin; aminoglycoside; povidone-iodine; aflibercept; triamcinolone; systemic antibiotics; systemic antifungals
All Procedures: vitreous tap and intravitreal injection of antibiotics (vancomycin and ceftazidime or amikacin) pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) with partial or total capsulectomy and possible IOL exchange/removal intravitreal injection of corticosteroid (dexamethasone) adjunctively vitreous sampling for cultures and smears (vitreous biopsy) B-scan ultrasonography use of povidone-iodine and sterile lid speculum for intravitreal injections
Follow-Up Instructions: Prompt intravitreal antibiotics (vancomycin + ceftazidime or amikacin) after vitreous tap, consider dexamethasone, obtain vitreous cultures and smears, perform B‑scan ultrasound as needed, follow clinically with close monitoring for worsening vision or signs requiring pars plana vitrectomy (especially if light‑perception vision), involve infectious disease for endogenous cases, and arrange urgent surgical PPV with capsulectomy/IOL removal for chronic/atypical cases.
Patient Education: You have an infection inside the eye called endophthalmitis, which can occur after surgery, injections, trauma, or from spread through the bloodstream. It can progress quickly and threaten vision, so prompt treatment is essential. What caused it and what to expect - It most commonly follows cataract surgery or intravitreal injections but can be from other sources. - Symptoms often include decreased vision, eye pain, redness, and sometimes pus or a hypopyon. Some forms (late/chronic or fungal) may be less painful but more indolent. - Rapid treatment improves the chance of preserving vision; prognosis depends on the organism and how soon treatment starts. Treatments you may receive - Immediate sampling of eye fluid (tap) and injection of broad‑spectrum intravitreal antibiotics (commonly vancomycin plus ceftazidime or amikacin). - For severe vision loss (light perception only) or aggressive infections, pars plana vitrectomy (surgical removal of infected vitreous) is often recommended. - Additional treatments may include topical antibiotics/steroids, systemic antibiotics or antifungals for endogenous infections, and possible removal of lens/capsule in chronic cases. - Cultures guide further therapy once results are available. Home care and follow‑up - Expect close follow‑up in the first 24–72 hours with frequent exams; adhere strictly to all medications and appointments. - Report any worsening pain, vision loss, increased redness, or discharge immediately (emergency). - Avoid touching or rubbing the eye, and keep the eye clean and protected per instructions (shield, no swimming). Prevention and risk reduction - For future procedures, measures that reduce infection risk include povidone‑iodine prep and sterile technique; routine topical antibiotic prophylaxis has not been shown to clearly reduce risk. - Control systemic risk factors (e.g., diabetes) and inform providers of any infections or immune suppression. If you have questions or new symptoms, contact your ophthalmologist or go to the emergency department immediately.


CASE 53 — Suprachoroidal Hemorrhage
===================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe left eye pain and decreased vision (sudden onset)
DURATION: 5 days
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I awoke with 10/10 eye pain and decreased vision in my left eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 59-year-old woman who awoke with sudden severe (10/10) left eye pain and decreased vision 5 days after an uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure; exam showed hand-motion vision, pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon and fibrin on the IOL, no view of the posterior pole, and B-scan showing dense membranous vitreous opacities; underwent vitreous tap with intravitreal vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone, cultures grew MRSA epidermidis, treated with hourly fortified topical antibiotics, steroids, and cycloplegic with gradual improvement to 20/40 at 1 week and 20/20 several months after YAG capsulotomy.
History Narrative: This 59-year-old woman awoke with sudden onset severe (10/10) left eye pain and decreased vision 5 days after an uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure. She presented with hand motion vision, pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, a marked anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon and fibrin on the intraocular lens, and no view of the posterior pole; B-scan ultrasonography showed moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities and appositional (kissing) suprachoroidal hemorrhages. She subsequently underwent a vitreous tap with intravitreal vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone; vitreous cultures grew methicillin‑resistant Staphylococcus epidermidis. With hourly topical fortified antibiotics, prednisolone, and cyclopentolate, she improved to 20/40 at 1 week and ultimately 20/20 several months later after posterior capsulotomy.
Symptom Onset: Upon awakening (sudden-onset)
Symptom Duration: 5 days
Progression: Improved to 20/20 several months later after treatment and laser capsulotomy
Characteristics: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhage, dark‑brown/dark‑red choroidal elevations with shallow/flat anterior chamber, sudden severe ocular pain, often after intraocular surgery or trauma, may show dense membranous vitreous opacities on B‑scan and become liquefied over 7–14 days.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Intraoperative or postoperative hypotony, elevated intraocular pressure spikes, hypertension, tachycardia, wound leaks/poor wound closure, axially myopic eye (requiring Flieringa ring), posterior capsular rupture, diabetes, scleral buckle/bleb-related factors (bleb leak, antimetabolite use), vitreous incarceration, and recent intraocular surgery (e.g., cataract surgery/LASIK combined)
Associated Symptoms: Severe ocular pain, decreased vision, conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon, fibrin on the IOL.
Prior Treatments: Uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure 5 days prior; vitreous tap and intravitreal vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone; topical fortified vancomycin and ceftazidime hourly, prednisolone QID, cyclopentolate TID; later laser capsulotomy.
Impact on Daily Activities: Severe; initial hand-motion vision and intense pain would have markedly limited daily activities until recovery to 20/20 over months.
Risk Factors: diabetes; posterior capsular rupture; wound leaks; intraoperative hypotony; myopia (requiring Flieringa ring); uncontrolled intraoperative blood pressure and tachycardia; high-risk eyes (need for preplacement of sutures); bleb leakage; use of antimetabolites; inferior surgical bleb location; vitrectomy-related factors (hypotony during surgery); more than two quadrants of suprachoroidal hemorrhage; vitreous incarceration in the wound/bleb; afferent pupillary defect on presentation; poor presenting visual acuity; retinal apposition >14 days


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/90 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Method: Hand motion
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: pronounced conjunctival hyperemia
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing orbit.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: pronounced conjunctival hyperemia and corneal edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: shallow or flat
Anterior Segment – Iris: iris plane posteriorly displaced with appositional (kissing) choroidal detachments; iris touch/anterior displacement noted in severe cases (iris touch present)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakia


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: Negative
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, shallow anterior chamber with anterior chamber reaction and hypopyon, fibrin on the intraocular lens, temporal clear corneal cataract incision reapproximated with nylon sutures, aphakia.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages with inability to view posterior pole due to choroidal detachments; retina apposed (kissing choroidals) in multiple quadrants


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 59-year-old woman with acute, severe left eye pain and decreased vision beginning 5 days after combined cataract/LASIK surgery. Examination: hand-motion vision OS, marked conjunctival injection, corneal edema, shallow anterior chamber with hypopyon and fibrin on the IOL, no view to posterior pole. B-scan shows appositional (kissing) suprachoroidal hemorrhages with moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities. Clinical course consistent with postoperative suprachoroidal hemorrhage; differential includes endophthalmitis and appositional retinal detachment, but echography confirms suprachoroidal hemorrhage. Management included IOP and pain control, observation with serial echography to document liquefaction; surgical drainage considered if IOP uncontrolled, intractable pain, persistent kissing choroidals, or retinal apposition. Prognosis guarded given appositional hemorrhage, but visual outcome depends on extent and complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Secondary Diagnoses: Suprachoroidal hemorrhage; aphakia; corneal edema; anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon; fibrin on intraocular lens; vitreous opacities; postoperative endophthalmitis (culture-positive MRSE)
Differential Diagnosis: Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; Exudative (serous) retinal detachment; Serous choroidal effusion
Severity: Severe
Laterality: left
Complications: - Elevated intraocular pressure (ocular hypertension) - Severe intractable eye pain - Retinal apposition (“kissing choroidals”) with potential retinal detachment - Macular involvement with anterior retinal displacement and iris touch - Vitreous incarceration in wound/bleb - Afferent pupillary defect - Poor final visual acuity / vision loss


Plan
All Medications: vancomycin; ceftazidime; dexamethasone; topical fortified vancomycin; topical fortified ceftazidime; prednisolone; cyclopentolate; povidone-iodine; intracameral cefuroxime; systemic steroids (mentioned as possible); topical steroids (overlaps with prednisolone); balanced salt solution; air/gas tamponade; perfluorocarbon liquid
All Procedures: combined cataract and LASIK procedure; vitreous tap and intravitreal injection of vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone; hourly topical fortified vancomycin and ceftazidime, topical prednisolone, topical cyclopentolate; laser capsulotomy; wound suturing/closure; anterior chamber paracentesis; placement of Flieringa ring (preventive); sclerotomy/drainage sclerotomy; globe refill with balanced salt solution or air/gas tamponade; 360-degree conjunctival limbal peritomy with rectus muscle isolation; placement of pars plana infusion cannula or 1.5-mm limbal infusion cannula/infusing #23 gauge butterfly needle; limited anterior vitrectomy; injection/use of perfluorocarbon liquid; placement of pars plana infusion cannula and standard pars plana vitrectomy; scleral buckle; intraocular gas tamponade


CASE 54 — Ruptured Globe
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in the left eye after metal-on-metal impact (suspected intraocular foreign body)
DURATION: 25 years
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32‑year‑old man struck a metal banister with a metal hammer while repairing it aboard a docked research ship; he felt minor left eye pain at the time and over the next 2 hours noted a subtle but definite decrease in vision, presenting with 20/30 vision.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old man was working aboard a docked marine biology research ship repairing a metal banister. While striking the metal rail with a metal hammer, he experienced a minor pain in his left eye. Over the ensuing 2 hours, he noticed a subtle but definite decrease in vision. On presentation, his visual acuity was 20/30. Slit-lamp examination showed a deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare. There was a defect in the temporal iris approximately 1 mm from the limbus. Corresponding to this site was a slitlike corneal defect that was not leaking aqueous fluid by Seidel testing. Posterior to the iris, there was a sectoral, white opacity in the peripheral lens. Examination of the posterior pole disclosed a small intraocular foreign body embedded in the retinal midperiphery with a collar of retinal edema surrounding it but no hemorrhage.
Symptom Onset: 2 hours
Symptom Duration: 2 hours
Progression: Resolved/stable (vision recovered to 20/20 and maintained for 25 years)
Characteristics: Ruptured globe with intraocular metallic (magnetic) foreign body involving cornea, iris, lens and embedded in midperipheral retina; self-sealing slitlike corneal entry, sectoral peripheral lens opacity, no afferent pupillary defect, vitreous hemorrhage minimal, retina attached after repair.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Striking metal on metal without protective eyewear
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision; minor eye pain
Impact on Daily Activities: Improved to normal — patient regained and maintained 20/20 vision for 25 years, implying no lasting impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: - Working with metal without protective eye wear (e.g., hammering metal on metal) - Penetrating metallic intraocular foreign body (especially magnetic/metallic) - Prior ocular surgery (previous incision dehiscence) - High-velocity blunt or sharp trauma (coup/contrecoup) - Occupational exposure (marine/industrial settings) - Delayed presentation or missed/occult rupture (chemosis, subconjunctival hemorrhage)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonometry (Goldmann applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Temporal and superior subconjunctival hemorrhage with chemosis.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: slitlike temporal corneal defect (peripheral) approximately 1 mm from the limbus, self‑sealing (Seidel negative)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Defect in the temporal iris approximately 1 mm from the limbus (sectoral iris defect)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Segmental white opacity in peripheral lens (sectoral cortical/traumatic cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect (no APD)
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare; defect in temporal iris ~1 mm from limbus; slitlike temporal corneal defect (Seidel negative); sectoral white peripheral lens opacity posterior to iris; minimal conjunctival/subconjunctival hemorrhage.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula on, attached, no hemorrhage, normal (vision ultimately 20/20)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Small midperipheral retinal impact site temporally with surrounding retinal edema; no peripheral retinal tears or detachment; retina attached throughout periphery.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ruptured globe, left eye (OS) with retained metallic intraocular foreign body involving temporal peripheral cornea, iris, lens and embedded in midperipheral retina with surrounding retinal edema. Vision at presentation 20/30; no afferent pupillary defect. Managed with primary operative intervention: pars plana vitrectomy with lensectomy, intraocular lens implantation, magnetic extraction of IOFB, low encircling scleral buckle, and endolaser around retinal defect. Intraoperative induction of posterior vitreous detachment; mild retinal bleeding at removal site controlled. Postoperative course stable with attached retina and final best-corrected visual acuity 20/20 maintained through 25 years. Prophylactic systemic antibiotics and tetanus as indicated; protective measures and long-term follow-up recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Ruptured globe, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Intraocular foreign body; Traumatic cataract (lens opacity/rupture)
Differential Diagnosis: Intraocular foreign body (penetrating globe injury); Ruptured globe with corneoscleral laceration; Occult scleral rupture with vitreous prolapse; Traumatic endophthalmitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: Intraocular foreign body; lens injury (traumatic cataract/need for lensectomy); retinal impact with localized edema and mild bleeding; risk of endophthalmitis; vitreous hemorrhage; potential retinal detachment; proliferative vitreoretinopathy/fibrous proliferation; possible scleral rupture/dehiscence; aphakia (temporary) and need for IOL implantation; long-term irregular astigmatism.


Plan
All Procedures: vitrectomy with lensectomy and intraocular lens implantation; magnetic extraction of intraocular foreign body; low encircling scleral buckle; laser photocoagulation surrounding retinal defect; induction of posterior vitreous detachment with vitrectomy instrument; primary closure of corneoscleral laceration (corneal 10–0 nylon and scleral nylon/polyglactin sutures)
Follow-Up Instructions: As long as anatomic and visual stabilization is observed, lengthen follow-up intervals; cicatricial response is usually complete by 6 weeks post‑op. Consider additional surgery (epiretinal membrane removal or IOL implantation) at ~3 months for visual rehabilitation if indicated. Recommend shatter-proof safety glasses for the fellow eye and arrange vocational rehabilitation as needed.
Patient Education: Shatter-proof safety glasses are strongly recommended to protect the fellow eye and prevent similar injuries; discuss need for tetanus booster if not up to date; explain the signs of infection (increasing pain, redness, discharge, decreasing vision) and to seek urgent care if present; emphasize postoperative activity restrictions and follow-up schedule, and that further surgeries (IOL implantation, membrane peeling) or visual rehabilitation (rigid contact lens, vocational support) may be needed.
Emergency Instructions: Place a protective shield over the eye to protect it from further trauma and pressure; administer parenteral broad‑spectrum antibiotics; check tetanus status and give booster if not within 5 years; arrange urgent ophthalmic surgical evaluation for globe repair/IOFB management.


CASE 55 — Intraocular Foreign Body
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Poor vision (noticed upon waking the morning after a fall)
DURATION: 3 years
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 80-year-old woman who presented the morning after a fall at her nursing home with periocular ecchymoses after presumed direct ocular trauma on a piece of furniture; initially no pain or visual loss but upon waking the next morning noted poor vision. Prior cataract surgery 3 years earlier; pre-injury vision 20/30 limited by atrophic macular degeneration. On presentation OS vision hand motions, IOP 10 mm Hg, cornea relatively clear, formed AC, conjunctival chemosis temporally/superiorly, scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with posterior chamber IOL seen subconjunctivally, dense vitreous hemorrhage limiting posterior view.
History Narrative: An 80-year-old woman presented the morning after falling at her nursing home. Although the exact history was unclear, periocular ecchymoses suggested direct ocular trauma on a piece of furniture. Initially, the patient had not noticed pain or visual loss, but upon waking the next morning she realized poor vision and sought consultation. Cataract surgery with a nuclear expression extracapsular technique had been performed 3 years previously. Before the injury, the vision had been documented as 20/30, limited by some atrophic macular degeneration changes. Upon presentation, visual acuity was hand motions and the intraocular pressure was 10 mm Hg. The cornea appeared relatively clear with a formed anterior chamber. However, there was conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly. While raising the upper lid, there was no conjunctival laceration, but a scleral dehiscence was evident, apparently at the previous cataract wound; upon examination of the superotemporal quadrant, the posterior chamber implant was seen in the subconjunctival space. The view posteriorly was limited by a dense vitreous hemorrhage. The pupil was not peaked, and there was no vitreous anterior to the iris.
Symptom Onset: 1 day ago
Symptom Duration: <24 hours
Progression: Improved (from hand motions to 20/100 at 3 months)
Characteristics: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body, posterior-segment involvement with vitreous hemorrhage and scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound, associated subretinal hemorrhage (superotemporal midperiphery) and subsequent minimal epiretinal membrane; aphakic after IOL removal.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nursing home fall onto furniture causing direct ocular trauma; prior extracapsular cataract wound (scleral dehiscence) with posterior chamber IOL extrusion; dense vitreous hemorrhage and subretinal hemorrhage; retained magnetic metallic intraocular foreign body; delayed presentation (found next morning).
Associated Symptoms: poor vision; no pain
Prior Treatments: Cataract surgery with a nuclear expression extracapsular technique 3 years prior
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — persistent visual impairment (20/100 OS, aphakic) limiting activities requiring good vision, but retina attached and stable allowing some independence.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Industrial trauma (metal-to-metal impact), lack of protective eyewear, workplace injuries (e.g., farm/field-related), periorbital/furniture-related blunt trauma, prior ocular surgery (cataract wound dehiscence)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 82 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen, best corrected (aphakic correction) 20/100
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Periocular ecchymoses temporally and superiorly
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly; scleral dehiscence at previous cataract wound with subconjunctival intraocular lens (superotemporal)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Periocular ecchymoses temporally and superiorly with conjunctival chemosis in the superotemporal quadrant (no conjunctival laceration), and scleral dehiscence at previous cataract wound with subconjunctival posterior chamber implant.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: relatively clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: formed anterior chamber
Anterior Segment – Iris: scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with no iris prolapse; pupil not peaked; no vitreous anterior to the iris
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakic (intraocular lens removed)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: The pupil was not peaked
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no restriction noted)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Cornea relatively clear with formed anterior chamber; conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly; no conjunctival laceration; scleral dehiscence at previous cataract wound with posterior chamber IOL in subconjunctival space; pupil not peaked; no vitreous anterior to the iris.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: atrophic macular degeneration changes
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Dense vitreous hemorrhage limiting view; focal subretinal hemorrhage in the superotemporal midperiphery; no retinal detachment; three months post-op attached posterior retina with minimal epiretinal membrane and organized subretinal hemorrhage temporally at base of buckle.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 80-year-old woman with globe penetration at prior cataract wound after fall, presenting next morning with hand motion vision OS, IOP 10 mmHg, formed anterior chamber, conjunctival chemosis and scleral dehiscence superotemporally with subconjunctival posterior chamber IOL, dense vitreous hemorrhage limiting posterior view, no pupil peaking, no anterior vitreous. Intraoperative findings: vitreous incarcerated at wound, no retinal detachment but focal superotemporal midperipheral subretinal hemorrhage. Underwent same-day pars plana vitrectomy with lensectomy/IOL removal and placement of encircling scleral band. Postop: attached posterior pole, minimal epiretinal membrane, organized subretinal hemorrhage at buckle base with self-limited traction, aphakic corrected VA 20/100 at 3 months. Diagnosis: retained magnetic intraocular foreign body OS with traumatic open-globe wound at prior cataract incision, surgically repaired with vitrectomy and scleral buckle; monitored for infection and retinal complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Intraocular foreign body (retained magnetic), aphakia, vitreous hemorrhage, subretinal hemorrhage, epiretinal membrane, scleral dehiscence, conjunctival chemosis, periocular ecchymoses
Differential Diagnosis: Retained intraocular foreign body (metallic vs nonmetallic); Globe rupture/perforating ocular trauma with wound dehiscence; Traumatic lens/implant dislocation with vitreous hemorrhage; Endophthalmitis (post-traumatic)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: retained intraocular foreign body; vitreous hemorrhage; scleral dehiscence/wound dehiscence with IOL extrusion (subconjunctival IOL); subretinal hemorrhage; epiretinal membrane; decreased visual acuity (20/100 aphakic); risk of endophthalmitis


Plan
All Medications: prophylactic systemic broad-spectrum antibiotics (initial systemic antibiotics; may include agents covering Bacillus cereus for farm/field injuries); intravitreal antibiotics (in especially suspicious cases); topical corticosteroids; topical antibiotics; topical cycloplegic agents; intravenous antibiotics (postoperative)
All Procedures: vitrectomy; placement of an encircling scleral band (scleral buckle); removal of intraocular lens (IOL) (lensectomy/explanted IOL)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observed at approximately 1- to 2-week intervals in the first month postoperatively, then less frequently thereafter; monitor for signs of endophthalmitis, recurrent retinal detachment, and visually significant epiretinal membrane; if no endophthalmitis and good progress after 24–48 hours, discontinue systemic antibiotics; consider later secondary IOL implantation or aphakic contact lens if aphakic.
Patient Education: An intraocular foreign body (IOFB) is a piece of material that has entered the eye, commonly from trauma. Because metallic IOFBs can damage eye tissues and carry a high risk of infection, prompt evaluation and usually surgical removal are important. Do not undergo MRI until a metallic IOFB has been ruled out. You will likely need imaging (X‑ray or CT) and an urgent ophthalmic surgery (vitrectomy/lensectomy and IOFB removal) to repair the eye and lower infection risk. Broad‑spectrum antibiotics are typically started to prevent endophthalmitis; sometimes intravitreal antibiotics are given. After surgery you will receive topical steroids, antibiotics, and possibly cycloplegic drops, plus short‑term systemic antibiotics if indicated. Follow‑up visits are frequent initially (1–2 weeks) to watch for infection, retinal detachment, or membranes that can affect vision. If left aphakic you may later discuss lens replacement or an aphakic contact lens. Seek immediate care for worsening pain, redness, discharge, sudden vision loss, new floaters, or flashes.
Emergency Instructions: Avoid MRI until metallic IOFB is excluded; treat as open-globe trauma—shield the eye, avoid pressure on globe, give IV broad‑spectrum antibiotics promptly, arrange urgent ophthalmic surgical evaluation for removal (ideally within 6–12 hours).


CASE 56 — Optic Neuritis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden vision loss in a young patient with eye pain on movement and anisocoria
DURATION: several months
LATERALITY: Unspecified
PATIENT QUOTES: "blurry"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old student nurse referred for headaches and anisocoria. Headaches present for several years, worse over past few months, daily, diffuse, not associated with nausea or vomiting. She reports always “blurry” vision, especially with headaches. Denies diplopia or other eye problems. Takes medications only for headaches. Referred after neurologist noted anisocoria. Onset insidious/chronic with recent worsening.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old student nurse was referred for headaches and anisocoria. She had daily, diffuse headaches for several years that worsened over the past few months and were not associated with nausea or vomiting. She reported always having “blurry” vision, especially with her headaches, denied diplopia or other eye problems, and took only medications for her headaches. A neurologist had noted anisocoria and referred her for ophthalmologic evaluation. Examination showed best-corrected visual acuity 20/20 OU, full visual fields, and color vision 10/10 Hardy–Rand–Rittler plates OU. Pupils measured 8 mm OD and 4 mm OS; the right pupil did not react to light or near, while the left pupil reacted briskly to light and near. No relative afferent pupillary defect was documented. Ocular motility was normal without ptosis. Slit-lamp exam revealed both pupils smoothly round without irregularities and no segmental contraction of the right pupil to light. Fundus exam was normal without disc swelling. Past medical history notable only for headaches; no facial sensory or motor deficits.
Symptom Onset: Several years (worse over the last few months)
Symptom Duration: several years (worse over the last few months)
Progression: Acute, usually unilateral onset with improvement over weeks to months (most improve ≥1 line by 15 days and ≥1 line by 30 days; visual recovery often nearly complete by 30 days, continued slow improvement up to 1 year)
Characteristics: Acute unilateral vision loss, periocular pain worsened by eye movement, relative afferent pupillary defect (often), young adult onset (<40), normal or swollen optic disc, typically demyelinating/inflammatory optic neuropathy with eventual visual improvement (most improve ≥1 line by 15–30 days)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye movement (periocular pain worse with eye movement)
Associated Symptoms: headaches, anisocoria, blurry vision (worse with headaches), periocular pain on eye movement not reported (N/A), photophobia N/A
Prior Treatments: intravenous methylprednisolone followed by oral prednisone (per ONTT regimen)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — occasional blurry vision with headaches but 20/20 acuity and no functional visual deficits reported
Systemic Symptoms: Headaches (chronic, worse over last few months), no nausea or vomiting, no diplopia, otherwise denies other systemic symptoms.
Risk Factors: - Young adult age (<40 years) - Prior or concurrent demyelinating white-matter lesions on MRI (≥3 lesions) - History of nonspecific neurologic symptoms - History of previous optic neuritis - Increased cerebrospinal fluid IgG


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jaeger 1 (J1)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white, quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, no periorbital swelling or erythema, no proptosis, no enophthalmos, external adnexa normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep and clear
Anterior Segment – Iris: both pupils smoothly round without irregularities; right pupil dilated (8 mm) , left 4 mm; no segmental contraction of right pupil.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 8 mm, smoothly round bilaterally (right 8 mm nonreactive, left 4 mm reactive)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil did not react to light; left pupil reacted briskly to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Right pupil: no reaction to near; Left pupil: reacted briskly to near
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD detected
Pupils – Other: 8 mm right, 4 mm left; right pupil nonreactive to light or near; left pupil briskly reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU (motility normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: both pupils to be smoothly round without irregularities, and there was no segmental contraction of the right pupil to light
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal with no disc swelling
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 24-year-old woman with acute unilateral visual complaints consistent with typical demyelinating optic neuritis. Exam findings supporting optic neuritis include periocular pain with eye movement, decreased vision acuity and/or visual field defect and an afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) in asymmetric disease; optic nerve head often normal. MRI recommended to evaluate for demyelinating lesions and guide management. Given age and presentation, treat per ONTT protocol with IV methylprednisolone followed by oral prednisone taper if MRI abnormal or rapid recovery desired; avoid standard-dose oral prednisone alone. Consider testing for NMO and MOG antibodies if atypical features, bilateral involvement, poor recovery, or MRI non-diagnostic. Follow-up for visual recovery over weeks to months and monitor risk of MS if MRI shows demyelinating lesions.
Primary Diagnosis: Optic neuritis
Secondary Diagnoses: Anisocoria (pharmacologic mydriasis)
Differential Diagnosis: Pharmacologic mydriasis; Tonic (Adie) pupil; Third cranial nerve palsy (pupil-involving); Optic neuritis (retrobulbar)
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Development of multiple sclerosis (increased risk, especially with abnormal MRI) - Persistent visual loss or incomplete visual recovery (worse prognosis with poor initial acuity or NMO) - Recurrent optic neuritis (including steroid-dependent or steroid-sensitive relapses) - Conversion to neuromyelitis optica (NMO) or association with MOG-antibody disease - Increased risk of new attacks of optic neuritis with standard-dose oral prednisone


Plan
All Medications: headache medications (unspecified) IV methylprednisolone sodium succinate oral prednisone high‑dose oral methylprednisolone (e.g., 500 mg) oral placebo
All Procedures: MRI of the orbits/brain (magnetic resonance imaging)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up for visual recovery; expect most improvement by 30 days with continued gradual improvement up to 1 year if incomplete at 30 days; evaluate for NMO or other etiologies if poor recovery or atypical features; consider MRI for demyelinating lesions and arrange NMO/MOG antibody testing as indicated; monitor for development of MS and follow neurologic care as needed.
Patient Education: Optic neuritis is inflammation of the optic nerve that often causes sudden, usually one-sided vision loss, pain with eye movement, and changes in color vision. Most cases occur in young adults and many improve substantially over weeks to months. Standard evaluation includes an MRI of the brain and orbits to look for demyelinating lesions (risk for multiple sclerosis) and to rule out other causes; additional testing (e.g., NMO or MOG antibody tests) may be needed in atypical or bilateral cases. Treatment options: high‑dose IV corticosteroids followed by an oral steroid taper can speed recovery and may reduce short‑term MS risk in patients with abnormal MRI; standard‑dose oral prednisone alone should be avoided. If vision does not improve or worsens, or if you develop new neurologic symptoms, seek prompt care. Follow-up: close ophthalmology and neurology follow‑up with repeat visual testing and MRI as recommended; most patients recover substantially by 1 month but improvement can continue for up to a year. Contact your clinician promptly for sudden worsening, severe pain, or new symptoms (weakness, numbness, bladder/bowel changes).
Referrals: Neurologist (for headaches/anisocoria)


CASE 57 — Optic Disc Edema with Macular Star (Neuroretinitis)
=============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision in the right eye for 12 days (acute onset)
DURATION: 12 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I noted the acute onset of blurred vision in my right eye 12 days ago."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 35-year-old woman with acute onset 12 days prior of blurred vision in her right eye, associated with moderately severe retro-orbital pain worsened by eye movements. Vision deteriorated over first 3–4 days then stabilized; vision worsens with aerobic exercise (Uhthoff-like). Denies other neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches for years. Otherwise healthy, no family history of visual impairment. Lives with three cats and had a preceding flu-like illness.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman noted acute onset of blurred vision in her right eye 12 days prior, with moderately severe right retro-orbital pain worsened by eye movements. Vision deteriorated over the first 3–4 days then stabilized. She reported worsening vision with aerobic exercise. She denied other precipitating factors, neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches for many years, and had no family history of visual impairment. She was otherwise healthy. She has three cats at home and a preceding flu-like illness.
Symptom Onset: 12 days before presentation
Symptom Duration: 12 days
Progression: Acute onset over 12 days with deterioration over first 3–4 days then stabilized
Characteristics: Acute onset unilateral vision loss with retro-orbital pain, decreased visual acuity (20/80 OD, 20/20 OS), RAPD OD, superior arcuate visual field defect OD, swollen hyperemic optic disc without hemorrhages or exudates, no vitreous cells, macular star (macular exudates) developing consistent with neuroretinitis due to Bartonella (cat-scratch disease)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Aerobic exercise and heat exposure (e.g., hot showers, sauna)
Associated Symptoms: Retro-orbital pain worsened by eye movements; vision deterioration (blurred vision) worse with exercise/heat (Uhthoff’s phenomenon); rare diffuse headaches.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of similar vision loss reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — difficulty with exercise (vision worsens), decreased right-eye acuity (20/80) affecting tasks requiring fine vision; overall daily function likely mildly to moderately impaired.
Systemic Symptoms: flu-like illness
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: 3 cats at home (household contacts with potential exposure)
Risk Factors: Contact with cats (three cats at home); recent flu-like illness; exposure risk for Bartonella henselae (cat-scratch disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (right), N5 (left)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: Significant relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) on the right
Pupils – Other: significant relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) on the right
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp examination was normal.

Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular star (exudates)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal abnormalities noted (periphery within normal limits).


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 57 Optic Disc Edema with Macular Star (Neuroretinitis) — neuroretinitis secondary to cat‑scratch disease (Bartonella henselae), presenting with acute unilateral (right) vision loss, optic disc edema and macular star; infectious etiology confirmed by positive Bartonella IgG and IgM; managed medically with antibiotics, no surgical intervention indicated; prognosis generally good though residual optic atrophy or macular RPE changes possible.
Primary Diagnosis: Optic disc edema with macular star (neuroretinitis)
Secondary Diagnoses: Neuroretinitis (optic disc edema with macular star) secondary to Bartonella henselae infection (cat-scratch disease)
Differential Diagnosis: Demyelinating optic neuritis (typical ON); Infectious neuroretinitis (Bartonella henselae / cat-scratch disease, Lyme, syphilis, toxoplasmosis, tuberculosis); Inflammatory optic neuropathy (sarcoidosis, NMO/NMOSD); Ischemic optic neuropathy (less likely)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Optic atrophy; macular retinal pigment epithelial changes; possible residual visual disability; recurrences in same or fellow eye


Plan
All Medications: antibiotics steroids
Follow-Up Instructions: Reevaluate within 2 weeks for development of a macular star; monitor visual recovery over months with follow-up exams for optic atrophy or macular changes as needed.
Patient Education: Neuroretinitis (optic disc swelling with a macular star) often follows an infection such as cat-scratch disease. It frequently causes sudden, usually one-sided vision loss and eye pain with movement. Many cases improve on their own over weeks to months, but evaluation and testing (blood tests for infections like Bartonella, and sometimes MRI) are important to rule out other causes. If an infectious cause is found, targeted antibiotics may be prescribed. Watch for worsening vision or new symptoms and return promptly. Follow-up eye exams are important because the macular star may appear up to 2 weeks after onset and recovery can be gradual; some residual vision changes may persist. Avoid strenuous overheating activities if they worsen vision; report any new headaches, eye pain, or vision changes immediately.


CASE 58 — Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Nonarteritic
=========================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden painless vision loss in the right eye upon awakening (blurred vision)
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "blurred vision"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 22-year-old woman with 3 weeks of blurred vision in the right eye, onset about 1 week after a nonspecific flu-like illness; minimal right periorbital pain, occasional mild diffuse headaches, no prior medical/ophthalmologic/neurologic history, lives with three cats.
History Narrative: A 22-year-old woman noted onset of blurred vision in her right eye 3 weeks ago. She reported minimal right periorbital pain and recent occasional mild, diffuse headaches. The visual blurring began about a week after a nonspecific “flulike” illness. She denied prior medical, ophthalmologic, or neurologic illnesses. She has three cats at home.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks ago
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Progression of visual loss beyond 2 to 4 weeks
Characteristics: Unilateral sudden painless vision loss on awakening in elderly with sectoral disc edema, small contralateral cup-to-disc ratio, RAPD, color dyschromatopsia, altitudinal visual field defect, associated vasculopathic risk factors (HTN, DM, HLD)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: sudden painless vision loss upon awakening; minimal periorbital pain; recent mild diffuse headaches; preceding nonspecific “flulike” illness; occasional headaches; blurring of vision; dyschromatopsia
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate—some difficulty with tasks requiring central vision (reading, fine detail) in the right eye, but overall daily functioning largely preserved given good vision in the left eye.
Systemic Symptoms: flulike illness and occasional mild diffuse headaches; no prior medical illnesses, no fevers or systemic vasculopathic history mentioned
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: 3 cats at home
Risk Factors: Younger age (22), recent nonspecific viral illness, exposure to cats (cat-scratch disease/Bartonella henselae), vitreous inflammation—no vascular risk factors (no hypertension, diabetes, or hyperlipidemia)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/88 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/15
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/15
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N8 (right), N5 (left)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis, no periorbital swelling or erythema; minimal right periorbital pain reported.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally, equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: equally reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near, with a right relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 1+ vitreous cells in the right eye; otherwise slit-lamp exam unremarkable (no anterior chamber inflammation reported)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Significant optic disc edema with peripapillary exudates and macular star in the right eye; left fundus normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular exudates in a star configuration
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 58 Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Nonarteritic (NAION): A 22‑year‑old woman with 3 weeks of painless (minimal periorbital pain) right visual blurring after a flu‑like illness, right relative afferent pupillary defect, dyschromatopsia, cecocentral scotoma OD, marked sectoral optic disc edema with peripapillary and macular exudates and 1+ vitreous cells OD. Age, vitreous cells, macular star, cat exposure, and positive Bartonella henselae serologies favor infectious neuroretinitis rather than NAION; findings are atypical for NAION (age <40, presence of intraocular inflammation, macular star, lack of vasculopathic risk factors). Final assessment: neuroretinitis (infectious, Bartonella henselae) of the right eye; NAION unlikely.
Primary Diagnosis: Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION), OS
Differential Diagnosis: Neuroretinitis (infectious or idiopathic); Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION); Arteritic AION (giant cell arteritis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: Risk of AION in the fellow eye (≈12% lifetime); potential permanent visual loss including persistent decreased visual acuity, persistent visual field defects, and increased risk of stroke/myocardial infarction related to shared vascular risk factors


Plan
All Medications: phenytoin erythropoietin intravitreal anti-vascular endothelial growth factor therapies topical brimonidine levodopa aspirin corticosteroids
Follow-Up Instructions: The risk of AION in the fellow eye is approximately 12% in the patient’s lifetime; control vascular risk factors and consider aspirin to possibly reduce fellow‑eye/stroke/MI risk (unproven); monitor vision and follow regularly for at least 6 months (expect spontaneous improvement in ~42.7% by 6 months).
Patient Education: Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION) is caused by decreased blood flow to the optic nerve and typically presents as sudden, painless vision loss in one eye. It most often affects people with vascular risk factors (hypertension, diabetes, high cholesterol) and a small optic nerve cup in the other eye. There is no proven treatment that reliably restores vision, although many patients stabilize or improve somewhat over months. Important steps you can take: - Control vascular risk factors: keep blood pressure, blood sugar, and cholesterol well managed, and stop smoking if you smoke. - Take aspirin if recommended by your physician for overall vascular risk reduction (discuss risks/benefits with your doctor). - Report any new vision loss, visual changes, severe headaches, jaw pain, or scalp tenderness immediately (to exclude giant cell arteritis). - Attend follow-up eye exams for visual acuity, visual field, and optic nerve monitoring; many patients show spontaneous improvement over 6 months. - Ask about low-vision services if vision loss affects daily activities; rehabilitation and safety counseling can help maintain function.


CASE 59 — Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Arteritic
======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe visual loss in the left eye on awakening 3 days ago
DURATION: 3 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "on awakening from sleep 3 days ago she noted severe visual loss in the left eye." "She denied any headache, eye pain, jaw claudications, episodes of transient visual loss, or any other neurologic or ophthalmologic complaints." "She related a past history of hypertension, increased cholesterol, and diabetes mellitus."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 65-year-old woman with sudden severe vision loss in left eye noted on awakening 3 days ago; denies headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient vision loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmic complaints; past medical history of hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and diabetes mellitus.
History Narrative: A 65-year-old woman awoke 3 days ago with severe vision loss in the left eye. She denied headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient visual loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmologic complaints. Past medical history significant for hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and diabetes mellitus. Examination: visual acuity 20/20 OD and 20/200 OS; color vision 10/10 OD and 2/10 OS; visual fields normal OD and a dense inferior altitudinal defect OS; pupils 3 mm bilaterally, briskly reactive OD, poorly reactive OS with a left relative afferent pupillary defect; extraocular motility normal. Fundus: right eye normal with cup-to-disc ratio 0.1; left optic disc diffusely swollen with pallor and peripapillary hemorrhages. Laboratory: ESR 70 mm/h and CRP 20 mg/dL. Temporal artery biopsy showed transmural lymphocytic infiltration with multinucleated giant cells and disruption of the internal elastic lamina consistent with giant cell arteritis. Treated promptly with high‑dose corticosteroids.
Symptom Onset: 3 days ago
Symptom Duration: 3 days
Progression: Acute onset with initial severe vision loss on awakening 3 days prior, with no reported improvement and potential for further vision loss without treatment (i.e., acute, recent, potentially progressive without therapy).
Characteristics: Sudden vision loss, headaches, jaw claudication, polymyalgia rheumatica, pallid (chalk‑white) disc edema, elevated ESR and CRP, elderly patient, peripapillary hemorrhages, dense altitudinal visual field defect, relative afferent pupillary defect, cup-to-disc ratio 0.1 in fellow eye, acute unilateral painless vision loss on awakening (arteritic AION/GCA features)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nocturnal hypotension (e.g., low blood pressure during sleep)
Associated Symptoms: sudden severe visual loss in the left eye on awakening, scalp tenderness (possible), jaw claudication (denied), headaches (denied), constitutional symptoms, polymyalgia rheumatica (history), malaise; left eye visual acuity loss, inferior altitudinal field defect, pale swollen optic disc with peripapillary hemorrhages, relative afferent pupillary defect
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — severe left-eye vision loss (20/200) causing marked difficulty with activities requiring depth perception, reading, driving, and increased risk for bilateral vision loss without treatment.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: hypertension, hypercholesterolemia (increased cholesterol), diabetes mellitus, age (>50/elderly)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200

Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no epithelial defect or edema (no abnormalities noted)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Mild nuclear sclerotic cataract (age-related)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right: reacts well to light; Left: reacts poorly to light
Pupils – RAPD: left relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 3 mm bilaterally, reacted well to light on the right and poorly to light on the left, and there was a left relative afferent pupillary defect.
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (extraocular motility normal)

Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and appearance of retinal vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 65-year-old woman with acute painless severe vision loss OS on awakening, VA 20/20 OD, 20/200 OS, dense inferior altitudinal field defect OS, left RAPD, diffusely swollen pale left optic disc with peripapillary hemorrhages; elevated ESR (70 mm/h) and CRP (20 mg/dL); temporal artery biopsy positive for giant cell arteritis. Assessment: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (GCA-related AION) of the left eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (arteritic AION)
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; hypercholesterolemia; diabetes mellitus; polymyalgia rheumatica (history suggested)
Differential Diagnosis: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (giant cell arteritis); Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy; Optic neuritis; Compressive optic neuropathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Further visual loss in the involved or fellow eye (including bilateral blindness); permanent optic atrophy and irreversible vision loss; systemic complications of giant cell arteritis (e.g., stroke, large-vessel ischemia) and corticosteroid-related adverse effects from long-term therapy.


Plan
All Medications: prednisone (oral prednisone 1.0–1.5 mg/kg/day), methylprednisolone (IV methylprednisolone 1,000 mg/day), methotrexate
All Procedures: Temporal artery biopsy
Follow-Up Instructions: Careful monitoring for visual impairment and constitutional symptoms; serial ESR/CRP testing to gauge disease activity; regular ophthalmology follow-up to assess vision and fellow eye; coordinate care with an internist/rheumatologist for steroid management and consideration of steroid‑sparing therapy if needed; monitor for and manage steroid side effects.
Patient Education: You have giant cell arteritis (a form of vasculitis) causing arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy, which has caused sudden vision loss in one eye and can threaten the other eye. Immediate high‑dose corticosteroid treatment is necessary to prevent further or bilateral vision loss; do not delay treatment for biopsy. You will need blood tests (ESR, CRP), a temporal artery biopsy to confirm the diagnosis, close monitoring of vision and laboratory markers, and coordination with your internist or rheumatologist. Report any new symptoms right away (new vision loss, new headache, jaw pain with chewing, scalp tenderness, fever, or fevers/weight loss). Long‑term steroid therapy with gradual tapering is common; steroids have side effects (high blood pressure, high blood sugar, infection risk, osteoporosis, mood changes) so bone protection, blood‑pressure and glucose monitoring, and possibly steroid‑sparing medications (e.g., methotrexate) may be needed. Follow up promptly for visual checks and lab monitoring as instructed.
Referrals: Internist and/or rheumatologist
Emergency Instructions: Start high‑dose corticosteroids immediately to prevent further visual loss; do not delay treatment for temporal artery biopsy. Seek urgent evaluation by ophthalmology/rheumatology and arrange temporal artery biopsy. If severe recent bilateral vision loss or monocular patient, consider IV methylprednisolone (e.g., 1 g/day). Monitor vision, ESR/CRP, and steroid side effects; seek emergency care for any new vision loss, new headaches, jaw claudication, or systemic symptoms.


CASE 60 — Progressive Optic Neuropathy—Tumor
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: severe visual loss in the left eye
DURATION: years
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "Over a period of hours, the vision became markedly blurry." "Over the last 2 weeks, she had noted two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss lasting minutes in the left eye." "She denied any periocular or orbital pain, but noted that over the last several months she had diffuse, dull headaches with occasional superimposed 'ice pick–like' pains affecting her left or right temples." "She reported jaw claudication and polymyalgia rheumatica (PMR)-like symptoms."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 68-year-old woman with progressive painless visual loss in the left eye over hours to severe blurring, with two to three episodes of transient, painless monocular visual loss in the left eye lasting minutes over the prior 2 weeks, and several months of diffuse dull headaches with intermittent sharp “ice pick–like” temple pains; denies periocular/orbital pain. Also reports hypertension, jaw claudication, and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms.
History Narrative: A 68-year-old woman with a history of hypertension presented with severe visual loss in the left eye. Over a period of hours her vision became markedly blurry; during the prior 2 weeks she had experienced two or three painless, transient episodes of visual loss in the left eye lasting minutes. She denied periocular or orbital pain but reported several months of diffuse, dull headaches with occasional “ice pick–like” pains in the temples. She also reported jaw claudication and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms.
Symptom Onset: Over a period of years (progressive visual loss over years)
Symptom Duration: several months (with acute worsening over hours and 2 weeks of transient episodes)
Progression: progressive visual loss over years
Characteristics: Progressive, painless visual loss with afferent pupillary defect, decreased color vision, optic atrophy/pale swollen disc, peripapillary hemorrhages, MRI T1 postcontrast optic nerve sheath enhancement consistent with optic nerve sheath meningioma (compressive optic neuropathy).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressive visual deterioration over months/years (progression of optic neuropathy)
Associated Symptoms: painless transient visual loss, diffuse dull headaches with occasional "ice pick–like" temple pains, jaw claudication, temporal artery tenderness, systemic symptoms of polymyalgia rheumatica (e.g., malaise, weight loss), visual loss (blurred vision), decreased color vision, afferent pupillary defect
Previous Episodes: two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss in the left eye lasting minutes over the last 2 weeks
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — severe visual loss in left eye (20/400) with markedly reduced color vision and visual field loss likely limits depth perception, reading, driving, and other binocular tasks; requires assistance/monitoring.
Systemic Symptoms: headache, jaw claudication, polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms, weight loss, anorexia, fever, malaise
Recent Travel: No recent travel


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis, external adnexa normal, extraocular motility full bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic changes consistent with age-related cataract (mild–moderate)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil reacted well to light; left pupil minimally reactive
Pupils – RAPD: Left relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally; left minimally reactive
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion bilaterally (motility normal)
