Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Compressive optic neuropathy of the right optic nerve secondary to an optic nerve sheath meningioma (MRI T1 postcontrast showing optic nerve sheath enhancement). Recommend close clinical observation with serial visual fields and MRIs; consider stereotactic conformal radiation therapy if progressive visual deterioration. Surgical resection generally not recommended unless exophytic growth or intracranial extension threatens the fellow eye. Follow-up every ~6 months with imaging every 6 months for 2 years then annually if stable.
Primary Diagnosis: Compressive optic neuropathy due to optic nerve sheath meningioma
Secondary Diagnoses: Compressive optic neuropathy (optic nerve sheath meningioma)
Differential Diagnosis: Compressive optic neuropathy from optic nerve sheath meningioma; Pituitary adenoma causing chiasmal/optic nerve compression; Optic nerve glioma (compressive); Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (GCA)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Progressive visual loss leading to optic atrophy and permanent vision loss (severe unilateral vision loss), potential involvement of fellow eye if intracranial extension, and risk of chronic visual field defects.


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Serial clinical reevaluation every ~6 months including visual fields and repeat MRIs (e.g., every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly if no growth)
Patient Education: - Explanation that vision loss is due to compression of the optic nerve by a tumor (optic nerve sheath meningioma) and how that causes symptoms (progressive visual decline, color loss, RAPD). - That MRI with contrast is the key test to evaluate this condition and will be used to monitor tumor size and progression. - Management options: observation with regular monitoring if stable; stereotactic/conformal radiation therapy if vision worsens; surgery is rarely recommended and reserved for select cases (exophytic growth or intracranial extension). - Need for regular follow-up visits with visual acuity, color testing, visual fields, and periodic MRI (e.g., every 6 months for 2 years, then annually if stable). - Signs/symptoms that should prompt earlier re-evaluation (sudden or worsening vision loss, new visual field loss, new pain or headaches, changes in pupil reactions). - Discuss potential outcomes: stabilization or improvement sometimes after treatment; risk of permanent vision loss if compression progresses. - Address common questions: expected side effects and goals of radiation therapy; risks and limited role of surgery; importance of timely reporting of changes. - Offer support resources: low-vision services if needed, and contact information for urgent ophthalmology/neuro-ophthalmology if acute changes occur.


CASE 61 — Papilledema–Pseudotumor Cerebri
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: painless progressive visual loss in the right eye
DURATION: 3 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "vision was no better after the surgery"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A young obese woman with headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, and bilateral disc edema; neuroimaging (MRI/MRV) was normal and lumbar puncture demonstrated an elevated opening pressure with normal CSF contents, consistent with idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri).
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman with a multi-year history of painless progressive visual loss in the right eye. Three years earlier she noted mild visual blurring in the right eye; initial acuity was 20/40 OD and 20/20 OS and was attributed to nuclear sclerotic cataract with observation recommended. Over the next year her right vision worsened to 20/60, and she underwent right cataract surgery with a posterior chamber intraocular lens, but her vision continued to deteriorate thereafter. At presentation her visual acuity was 20/100 OD and 20/20 OS. She denied headache or eye pain. She reported visual color loss (2 of 10 pseudoisochromatic plates OD vs 9 of 10 OS) and diffuse right visual field depression with a normal left field. Examination showed a right relative afferent pupillary defect, pupils 5 mm bilaterally poorly reactive to light on the right and briskly reactive on the left, normal motility, mild right proptosis (Hertel 22 mm OD, 19 mm OS at base 95), a posterior chamber IOL OD and mild nuclear sclerotic cataract OS, and fundus exam revealed a pale, atrophic right optic nerve with a normal left optic disc.
Symptom Duration: 3 years
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Papilledema with bilateral optic nerve head edema, headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, elevated opening pressure (350 mm H2O), normal CSF contents, MRI showing enlarged optic nerve sheaths and empty sella, MRV normal, obese female (idiopathic intracranial hypertension)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Obesity
Alleviating Factors: Weight reduction (e.g., diet or surgically induced weight loss)
Associated Symptoms: headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, progressive visual loss, painless visual blurring, decreased color vision, visual field depression, proptosis, relative afferent pupillary defect
Prior Treatments: Right cataract surgery
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant visual impairment in the right eye (progressive unilateral vision loss, reduced acuity to 20/100, visual field depression, color vision loss) likely interferes with activities requiring depth perception, reading, driving; left eye largely preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, obesity
Risk Factors: Obesity (female sex and young to middle‑aged woman)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No evidence of lacrimal system abnormality.
External Exam – Orbit: Mild proptosis on the right (Hertel 22mm OD vs 19mm OS at base 95)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens on the right; mild nuclear sclerotic cataract on the left


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally, poorly reactive to light on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Pupils – Reaction to Light: right: poorly reactive; left: briskly reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive on the left; poorly reactive on the right
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally, poorly reactive to light on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, motility normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: a posterior chamber intraocular lens on the right and a mild nuclear sclerotic cataract on the left
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Pale, atrophic optic nerve on the right; left optic disc normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal abnormalities detected (periphery within normal limits)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Papilledema due to idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri) — obese middle‑aged woman with headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, bilateral optic disc edema on exam and MRI signs of raised intracranial pressure (enlarged optic nerve sheaths, empty sella), normal MRV, normal CSF composition, and markedly elevated lumbar puncture opening pressure (350 mm H2O). Management options discussed include weight loss and medical therapy with acetazolamide (1–2 g/day) and other diuretics, with surgical options (CSF shunting or optic nerve sheath fenestration) reserved for progressive vision loss or medical therapy failure; close ophthalmologic follow‑up with serial visual fields, disc photos/OCT, VA, color vision, and RAPD testing recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri) with papilledema
Differential Diagnosis: Idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri); compressive optic neuropathy (orbital/intracanalicular tumor such as meningioma); optic atrophy from chronic optic neuritis or ischemic optic neuropathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: vision loss


Plan
All Medications: acetazolamide; methazolamide; furosemide; corticosteroids
All Procedures: MRI brain and orbits with and without gadolinium; MRV; Lumbar puncture (spinal tap) with opening pressure measurement; Acetazolamide medical therapy (pharmacologic treatment); Weight reduction (including bariatric surgery) as treatment option; Ventriculoperitoneal or lumboperitoneal CSF shunt; Optic nerve sheath fenestration (ONSF); Serial formal visual fields, optic disc photography, and OCT RNFL for monitoring
Follow-Up Instructions: Return monthly for 6 to 12 months with careful evaluation of formal visual fields, stereo-optic disc photos, OCT nerves, visual acuity, color vision, and RAPD testing; lengthen intervals once stable but continue long-term follow-up for possible recurrence or shunt failure.
Patient Education: Explain that idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri) is increased pressure around the brain causing headaches, vision changes, pulsatile tinnitus, and swollen optic nerves; stress the importance of prompt monitoring and treatment to prevent permanent vision loss. Recommend weight loss as a core treatment (dietary changes, supervised programs or bariatric surgery for morbid obesity). Describe medical therapy options (acetazolamide as first-line to lower CSF production; possible add-on furosemide; short-term steroids only in select cases) and discuss risks (acetazolamide avoided early in pregnancy; diuretics/corticosteroids have side effects). Explain diagnostic steps already done (normal MRI/MRV, high lumbar puncture opening pressure with normal CSF contents) and that repeat neuroimaging or lumbar puncture may be needed if symptoms change. Outline potential surgical options if vision worsens despite medical therapy: optic nerve sheath fenestration to protect vision and CSF shunting (lumboperitoneal or ventriculoperitoneal) to lower pressure; note benefits and risks and that choice is individualized. Emphasize need for frequent follow-up with visual acuity, visual fields, optic nerve photos/OCT and prompt reporting of new or worsening symptoms (new visual loss, persistent headaches, worsening tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, or double vision). Advise discussion of pregnancy planning with neurologist/obstetrician before treatment changes. Provide reassurance that many patients improve with weight loss and medical therapy but long-term monitoring is required.
Referrals: Neuro-ophthalmology and neurosurgery consultation (for consideration of CSF shunting or optic nerve sheath fenestration)


CASE 62 — Visual Field Defect—Junctional Scotoma
================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Painless progressive vision loss in the right eye
DURATION: 8 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had an 8-month history of headache... over the last 2 months I noted a 'pulsating sound' in my head... over the last 2 weeks I had episodes lasting seconds of transient visual loss in the left or right eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 22-year-old woman with 8 months of almost-daily diffuse headaches (rarely with nausea), 2 months of pulse-synchronous tinnitus worse with posture change, and 2 weeks of transient seconds-long visual loss episodes in either eye; obese (250 lb), no diplopia, not pregnant, no medications causing increased intracranial pressure.
History Narrative: A 22-year-old obese woman with an 8-month history of almost-daily diffuse headaches, rarely with nausea, who over the last 2 months noted pulsatile (pulse-synchronous) tinnitus worse with posture changes and for 2 weeks had seconds-long transient visual obscurations in either eye. She denied diplopia or other neurologic symptoms, was not pregnant, and took no medications known to raise intracranial pressure. She weighed ~250 lb, blood pressure 135/85 mmHg. Visual acuity 20/20 OU, normal color vision, pupils 5 mm OU reactive without RAPD, motility normal. Visual fields showed bilateral enlarged blind spots; fundus exam revealed marked bilateral optic disc swelling with exudates, hemorrhages, and dilated vessels consistent with papilledema.
Symptom Onset: 8 months prior
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Right optic neuropathy with ipsilateral central scotoma and contralateral superior temporal (junctional) visual field defect due to compressive lesion at the right optic nerve–chiasm junction (pituitary adenoma)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Changes in posture (especially going from lying to standing)
Associated Symptoms: painless progressive vision loss in the right eye; right-sided central scotoma; transient visual loss in left or right eye (seconds); headaches (diffuse, almost daily); pulse-synchronous tinnitus (pulsating sound); no diplopia; weight gain/obesity
Previous Episodes: Transient visual loss episodes lasting seconds in either eye over the last 2 weeks
Prior Treatments: Surgical decompression
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent transient visual obscurations and progressive vision loss in the right eye causing functional impairment, but 20/20 acuity OU and normal color vision preserved most daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: Headache; pulsatile tinnitus; transient visual obscurations; obesity (weight gain)
Risk Factors: Obesity (female, 250 lb), young woman of childbearing age, possible pituitary adenoma (mass lesion)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 135/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance visual acuity testing)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet (no conjunctival injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, no cataract/not reported


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally, reactive to light, no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Pupils were 5 mm bilaterally and reacted well to light.
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to light
Pupils – RAPD: Right afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) present
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD)
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal anterior segment examination; no acute slit-lamp abnormalities detected
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Severe optic disc swelling with diffuse exudates, hemorrhages, and dilated vessels bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: dilated
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal periphery


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right optic neuropathy with junctional scotoma secondary to compressive lesion at the junction of the right optic nerve and chiasm—consistent with a pituitary adenoma causing ipsilateral optic atrophy, right central scotoma, right RAPD, and contralateral superior-temporal visual field defect.
Primary Diagnosis: Right optic neuropathy with junctional scotoma secondary to compressive lesion of the right optic nerve–chiasm junction (pituitary adenoma)
Secondary Diagnoses: Pituitary adenoma (compressive suprasellar lesion)
Differential Diagnosis: Pituitary tumor (sellar/suprasellar adenoma) causing junctional compression; Suprasellar meningioma; Craniopharyngioma; Supraclinoid aneurysm
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Optic atrophy; progressive permanent visual loss (right optic neuropathy with junctional scotoma); possible need for hormone replacement; potential recurrence or residual tumor requiring chemo/radiation; risk of blindness


Plan
All Procedures: Decompressive surgery (surgical removal of pituitary adenoma); periodic MRIs; serial ophthalmologic examinations with visual field testing every 4 months for 1 year then every 6 months thereafter.
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up ophthalmologic exam with visual field testing every 4 months for 1 year, then every 6 months thereafter, with periodic MRIs to monitor for tumor growth.
Patient Education: The visual loss is from a compressive pituitary tumor pressing where the optic nerve meets the optic chiasm, causing a right optic neuropathy and a junctional scotoma. Treatment usually requires surgical decompression and may include hormone therapy or medical treatment if the tumor is a prolactinoma; radiation or chemotherapy may be needed if surgery is subtotal. Prompt treatment can prevent further vision loss but some deficits may be permanent; regular ophthalmic follow-up with visual fields and periodic MRI surveillance are necessary (initially every 4 months for a year, then about every 6 months). Seek urgent care for sudden worsening vision, new double vision, severe headache, or hormonal symptoms (e.g., menstrual changes, libido changes, unexplained fatigue).
Referrals: Neurosurgery for decompressive tumor resection; Endocrinology for pituitary hormone evaluation and management; Neuro-ophthalmology follow-up.


CASE 63 — Visual Field Defect—Pituitary Lesion
==============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressive painless visual impairment in the right eye over 1½ years.
DURATION: 1.5 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had progressive painless visual impairment in my right eye for about 1 and a half years."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old man with progressive, painless visual impairment in his right eye over 1½ years; denied visual problems in the left eye and other neurologic or ophthalmologic symptoms.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man complained of progressive painless visual impairment in his right eye over a period of 1½ years. He denied any visual difficulty in his left eye and denied any other history of neurologic or ophthalmologic impairment.
Symptom Onset: 1.5 years
Symptom Duration: 1.5 years
Progression: Progressive over 1.5 years
Characteristics: Bitemporal hemianopia with superior bitemporal (superotemporal) field defects; junctional scotoma pattern with generalized diffuse depression in the right eye and superotemporal defect in the left; visual field defects respect the vertical meridian; painless progressive loss.


Subjective (cont.)

Associated Symptoms: progressive painless visual impairment in the right eye over 1.5 years, headaches, history of multiple car accidents
Impact on Daily Activities: Multiple car accidents and progressive vision loss causing impaired driving and daily functioning
Systemic Symptoms: Headaches; multiple car accidents (suggesting episodic vision-related incidents); no other neurologic or systemic symptoms reported.
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Age (middle‑to‑older adult), pituitary adenoma (parasellar mass)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart at 6 meters
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 14 mm Hg — applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal

Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Both reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Both reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: both reacted well to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Full motility; extraocular movements intact and normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Right optic disc: diffusely pale; Left optic disc: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 63-year-old with progressive, painless visual loss and headaches found to have a bitemporal hemianopia on formal visual field testing. Examination notable for decreased VA OD (20/80) with color vision loss and a right relative afferent pupillary defect, diffuse optic disc pallor OD, and normal left eye exam. Findings are most consistent with chiasmal compression from a pituitary macroadenoma on MRI. Management includes neurosurgical evaluation for decompression, endocrinologic assessment and medical therapy if a prolactinoma is suspected, and scheduled postoperative/serial visual field and MRI follow-up.
Primary Diagnosis: Bitemporal visual field defect secondary to pituitary macroadenoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Pituitary macroadenoma; Bitemporal hemianopia due to chiasmal compression; Junctional scotoma (right optic nerve involvement)
Differential Diagnosis: Pituitary macroadenoma; Craniopharyngioma; Meningioma; Chiasmal glioma
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: visual field loss (bitemporal hemianopia), optic neuropathy with optic disc pallor, progressive vision loss, potential hormonal/endocrine dysfunction requiring replacement, recurrence after surgery, need for postoperative radiation therapy


Plan
All Medications: bromocriptine
All Procedures: Neuroimaging (preferably MRI of the sella); surgical resection of pituitary adenoma; postoperative endocrinologic follow-up and hormonal replacement as needed; postoperative radiation therapy (if required); serial visual field and ophthalmologic examinations (postop at ~3 months, then 6-month intervals for 2 years, yearly for 5 years, then every 2 years); serial MRI studies (e.g., 6 months, 1 year, then yearly for several years); medical therapy for prolactinomas (bromocriptine)
Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative visual field and ophthalmologic examination as soon as tolerated; then visual fields and ophthalmologic exam at 3 months, then every 6 months for 2 years, yearly for 5 years, and every 2 years thereafter. Repeat MRI at 6 months, 1 year, and then yearly for several years.
Patient Education: You have a lesion compressing the optic chiasm (most likely a pituitary tumor) causing bitemporal visual field loss. This explains your peripheral (side) vision loss and symptoms such as accidents or unawareness of field loss. Recommended next steps: urgent neuroimaging (MRI of the sella) to confirm the diagnosis and assess tumor size; referral to a neurosurgeon and endocrinologist for evaluation of surgical and hormonal management; visual field and ophthalmic testing to document baseline vision. If the tumor is a prolactinoma, medications (e.g., bromocriptine) may be effective; many other pituitary masses require surgical removal (transsphenoidal surgery), sometimes followed by radiation. Post-treatment follow-up includes repeat visual fields, regular ophthalmic exams (e.g., 3 months, then serially), and periodic MRI (e.g., 6 months, 1 year, then yearly) to monitor for improvement or recurrence. Report new symptoms promptly (worsening vision, severe headache, nausea/vomiting, sudden vision loss) as these may indicate acute enlargement or pituitary apoplexy. Discuss hormone testing and possible replacement with your endocrinologist.
Referrals: Neurosurgery (for pituitary mass evaluation/surgical treatment); Endocrinology (pre- and postoperative hormonal evaluation and management)
Work Restrictions: Avoid driving and operating heavy machinery until visual fields improve and clinician clears; restrictions on night driving if persistent field loss; may require temporary work modification for tasks requiring full peripheral vision; follow-up ophthalmology/neurologic clearance before return to full duties.


CASE 64 — Visual Field Defect—Homonymous Hemianopia
===================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: unable to read well, “lose his place” and “miss lines,” and poor driving with collisions (difficulty parking)
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "lose his place" or "miss lines"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 64-year-old man with history of hypertension, diabetes mellitus, and hyperlipidemia status post bypass surgery who noticed he was unable to read well because he “lose[s] his place” and “miss[es] lines” and has bumped his left front fender several times when attempting to park in the garage; visual symptoms led to evaluation showing a left homonymous hemianopia from a right occipital lobe stroke.
History Narrative: A patient with a past history of hypertension, diabetes mellitus, and hyperlipidemia status post bypass surgery reported difficulty reading for an unspecified time because he “loses his place” and “misses lines,” and he had bumped his left front fender several times while parking; visual field testing revealed a left homonymous hemianopia and he was found to have a right occipital lobe stroke.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Slowly deteriorating over the last year
Characteristics: Dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia indicating a retrochiasmal (right occipital lobe) lesion


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Driving (parking) and reading activities
Associated Symptoms: unable to read well, "lose his place"/"miss lines", bumped left front fender when parking, blurred vision bilaterally, poor driving performance with multiple accidents, occasional frontal headaches
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty reading (loses place, misses lines) and impaired driving/parking with accidents (bumped left front fender); reduced ability to navigate and perform tasks requiring peripheral vision.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension, diabetes mellitus, hyperlipidemia, history of bypass surgery, frontal headaches, history of stroke (right occipital lobe infarction)

Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg

Objective Exam (cont.)

External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal

Assessment Narrative: Right occipital lobe infarction causing a dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia consistent with a retrochiasmal visual pathway lesion; risk-factor management for stroke and secondary prevention recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Right occipital lobe infarction causing a dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; hyperlipidemia
Differential Diagnosis: Right occipital lobe infarction (retrochiasmal stroke); Parietal lobe infarct affecting optic radiations; Optic tract lesion (contralateral homonymous hemianopia)
Severity: Dense
Laterality: Left
Complications: Difficulty reading (losing place/missing lines), impaired driving with motor vehicle collisions/bumper damage, and increased risk of accidents due to reduced visual field.


Plan
All Medications: Acetaminophen; aspirin (recommended for stroke prophylaxis)
All Procedures: MRI with and without gadolinium (sellar region)
Follow-Up Instructions: Repeat visual fields and ophthalmologic examination approximately 6 months after onset to assess for improvement.
Patient Education: You have a homonymous hemianopia—loss of the same half of the visual field in both eyes—due to a stroke in the right occipital lobe. This explains your difficulty reading (losing your place or missing lines) and problems with driving/parking. Key points and recommendations: - Cause and prognosis: This is from a retrochiasmal (brain) lesion; some spontaneous improvement can occur over months but large permanent deficits are common. Repeat visual fields and eye exam about 6 months after onset to check for recovery. - Safety: Avoid driving until formally cleared by your physician and local licensing authority. Be cautious with mobility—use extra care when parking, crossing streets, and navigating unfamiliar places. - Reading strategies: Use a ruler or line guide, enlarge text, increase contrast and lighting, and slow down. Practice systematic scanning (making larger saccades) toward the blind side to improve search strategies. - Rehabilitation options: Consider referral to low-vision services or occupational therapy for training in scanning techniques and compensatory strategies. Prism glasses (monocular sector prisms) may help shift images into the seeing field for some patients. - Medical care: Continue vascular risk factor control (blood pressure, diabetes, lipids, smoking cessation) and follow your neurologist regarding stroke prevention (e.g., antiplatelet therapy if indicated). - Resources: Ask your provider for referral to low-vision rehabilitation, occupational therapy, and driving-assessment programs. Provide printed or online materials about homonymous hemianopia and local support services. If you notice any sudden worsening, new neurological symptoms, or visual loss, seek immediate medical attention.
Work Restrictions: Avoid driving and operating motor vehicles; limit tasks requiring full peripheral visual fields (use visual aids and supervised rehabilitation); re-evaluate at 6 months for return-to-duty decisions.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate medical care only if new or worsening neurologic symptoms occur (sudden vision loss, weakness, numbness, speech disturbance, severe headache, or loss of consciousness). Otherwise follow-up with ophthalmology/neurology for visual fields and stroke risk‑factor management; call your doctor promptly for new symptoms.


CASE 65 — Transient Monocular Visual Loss
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Transient Monocular Visual Loss
DURATION: 6 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I would often 'lose my place' or 'miss lines' when reading."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Since coronary bypass surgery 6 weeks ago, the 57‑year‑old man has noticed difficulty reading—losing his place and missing lines—and bumped his left front fender while parking; he has no other neurologic symptoms but has hypertension, diabetes, and hyperlipidemia.
History Narrative: A 57-year-old man with hypertension, diabetes, and hyperlipidemia who underwent coronary artery bypass surgery 6 weeks earlier. Postoperative recovery was apparently uneventful, but since surgery he has noted difficulty reading—often “losing his place” or “missing lines”—and has bumped his left front fender several times when parking. He has experienced no other neurologic symptoms. He subsequently had transient monocular visual loss and was found to have a left homonymous hemianopia on visual field testing; visual acuity 20/20 OU, pupils 4 mm reactive without RAPD, normal motility, mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts, mild hypertensive retinal changes, and no hemorrhages or exudates.
Symptom Onset: 6 weeks prior to evaluation
Symptom Duration: 6 weeks
Characteristics: Left homonymous hemianopia, congruous, macular-involving (no macular sparing)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: losing his place/missing lines when reading; bumping his left front fender when parking; no other neurologic symptoms
Previous Episodes: Since surgery he noted that he would often “lose his place” or “miss lines” when reading and bumped his left front fender — intermittent visual disturbances beginning several weeks after coronary bypass surgery (transient monocular visual loss episodes).
Prior Treatments: Coronary artery bypass graft surgery (6 weeks prior)
Impact on Daily Activities: He often “lost his place” or “miss lines” when reading and bumped his left front fender several times when parking, indicating moderate impact on reading and driving/parking tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, diabetes, hyperlipidemia
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: hypertension, hyperlipidemia, diabetes, recent coronary artery bypass surgery (recent cardiac surgery)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/88 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20

Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to near

Primary Diagnosis: Retinal emboli secondary to right carotid stenosis causing transient monocular visual loss
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; hyperlipidemia; diabetes; prior coronary artery bypass graft (6 weeks prior)
Differential Diagnosis: Retrochiasmal (occipital or optic radiation) infarction (e.g., PCA stroke); Lateral geniculate body or optic tract infarction; Space-occupying lesion of occipital/temporal/parietal lobe (tumor, abscess); Demyelinating disease (e.g., MS)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right
Complications: Stroke


Plan
All Medications: aspirin
All Procedures: Carotid endarterectomy; Carotid stenting; Antiplatelet therapy (aspirin or other); Anticoagulation (for cardiac valvular/AF embolic source); Noninvasive carotid surveillance (carotid Doppler or MR angiography); Neuroimaging (CT in acute setting, MRI otherwise)
Follow-Up Instructions: Medical supervision of stroke risk factors and periodic reevaluation of the carotid artery for restenosis with noninvasive studies (e.g., carotid Doppler or MR angiography).
Patient Education: Explain that transient monocular vision loss (TMVL) can be caused by tiny cholesterol or platelet emboli from the carotid arteries, heart, or aortic valve and may signal increased stroke risk. Advise urgent vascular and cardiac evaluation (carotid imaging, ECG/echocardiography, and possibly hypercoagulable or inflammatory testing) and that neuroimaging (MRI/CT) is indicated. Emphasize controlling stroke risk factors—blood pressure, diabetes, cholesterol, smoking cessation, regular exercise, and adherence to antiplatelet or anticoagulant therapy if prescribed. Discuss symptoms that require immediate emergency care (persistent vision loss, new weakness, numbness, slurred speech, severe headache) and the importance of follow-up with vascular surgery/cardiology and ophthalmology for ongoing monitoring and possible carotid intervention if high-grade stenosis is found.
Referrals: Carotid vascular evaluation (carotid Doppler/MRA) and vascular surgery (carotid endarterectomy/stenting) referral; cardiology (evaluate cardiac/aortic embolic source, valvular disease, arrhythmia) referral; neurology/stroke service; rheumatology (evaluate for giant cell arteritis)


CASE 66 — Third Nerve Palsy—Ischemic

CASE 67 — Third Nerve Palsy—Aneurysm
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Binocular diplopia and right eyelid droop (ptosis) with right periorbital/retro‑orbital pain
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "stabilized" and in fact had "improved" since his right eye "dropped."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 70-year-old man with a 4-week history of binocular diplopia that developed and worsened over five days then stabilized and partially improved after his right eyelid drooped; associated severe right retro-orbital pain in the first two weeks that subsequently lessened; noted right eyelid droop/ptosis with marked impairment of right eye elevation, adduction, and depression but full abduction; no facial numbness, recent headache, jaw claudication, or other neurologic deficits; past history of hypertension, diabetes, bilateral cataract extraction, and one-pack-per-day smoking.
History Narrative: A 70-year-old man had a 4-week history of binocular diplopia. The diplopia developed and worsened over a 5-day period, but it then “stabilized” and in fact had “improved” since his right eye “dropped.” In the first 2 weeks, he had also noticed severe retro-orbital pain on the right but this pain became minimal. He had a history of bilateral cataract extraction but his vision had otherwise been “stable.” He had a past history of hypertension and diabetes. He smoked one pack of cigarettes daily. He denied any facial numbness, recent headache, jaw claudications, or other neurologic deficits. Examination revealed visual acuity of 20/30 on the right and 20/25 on the left. Pupils were 3mm bilaterally and both reacted well to light and near. There was no relative afferent pupillary defect. Visual fields were normal. He had complete ptosis of his right lid with markedly impaired levator function. He could not adduct, elevate, or depress the right eye but he could fully abduct the eye. Attempts at depression of the right eye resulted in mild incyclodeviation of the eye (consistent with an intact ipsilateral fourth nerve). Motility was normal in the left eye. There was no proptosis, facial sensation was normal, and general neurologic exam was otherwise normal. Slit-lamp exam revealed bilateral posterior chamber intraocular lenses. Fundus exam revealed mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy without optic disc pathology.
Symptom Onset: 4-week history
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Progression: Improved then stabilized
Characteristics: Left-sided (left) pupil-involving partial third nerve palsy with ptosis, impaired elevation/adduction/depression, anisocoria (left pupil larger), acute binocular diplopia, periorbital pain; due to posterior communicating artery aneurysm.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye movements (attempts at elevation, adduction, and depression) — i.e., attempted ocular motility (gaze with affected eye)
Alleviating Factors: Eye patching to relieve the diplopia
Associated Symptoms: binocular diplopia, left-sided frontal-periorbital pain, left-sided lid droop (ptosis), anisocoria, partial ophthalmoplegia with impaired elevation, adduction, and depression, retro-orbital pain, severe ptosis, diplopia worsening over 5 days then stabilizing/improving, mild headache (history mentions headaches minimal), no facial numbness, no jaw claudication, no other neurologic deficits
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent disabling diplopia and severe ptosis affecting reading, driving, and daily visual tasks; requires eye patching for function and likely limits activities until treated.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension; diabetes; smoker (1 pack/day)
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Hypertension; diabetes mellitus; cigarette smoking; age (older adult)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: JNB (jaeger 1)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right complete ptosis with markedly impaired levator function; left lids normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal (no conjunctival injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear; no corneal pathology observed
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris, no abnormality noted
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (bilateral)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: both reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Both reacted well to light and near.
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: 3mm bilaterally and both reacted well to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Right: unable to adduct, elevate, or depress; full abduction. Left: normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: bilateral posterior chamber intraocular lenses
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: without optic disc pathology
Fundus Exam – Macula: mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy without optic disc pathology
Fundus Exam – Vessels: mild diabetic retinopathy (nonproliferative)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Mild diabetic retinopathy (nonproliferative) bilaterally; periphery without retinal tears/detachments noted


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Partial third nerve palsy (TNP) of the left eye with pupillary involvement most consistent with compression from a posterior communicating artery aneurysm based on acute onset binocular diplopia, ipsilateral periorbital pain, ptosis, marked limitation of adduction, elevation and depression with spared abduction, and anisocoria. Neuroimaging (CT/CTA) confirmed a posterior communicating artery aneurysm. Pupillary involvement and motor deficits indicate a compressive etiology requiring neurosurgical/endovascular repair.
Primary Diagnosis: Partial third nerve palsy due to posterior communicating artery aneurysm
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Diabetes mellitus; Bilateral posterior chamber intraocular lenses; Mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy; History of smoking (current smoker, 1 pack/day)
Differential Diagnosis: Posterior communicating artery aneurysm causing compressive third nerve palsy; Ischemic (diabetic/hypertensive) third nerve palsy; Pituitary/sellar mass or other compressive lesion; Myasthenia gravis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: left
Complications: Aneurysmal rupture leading to subarachnoid hemorrhage (high morbidity and mortality)


Plan
All Procedures: CT/CTA; MRI/MRA (recommended as part of workup); cerebral digital subtraction angiography (catheter angiography); surgical or endovascular repair of the posterior communicating artery aneurysm; patching of the eye for diplopia relief
Follow-Up Instructions: Patching the eye to relieve the diplopia; monitor for any worsening (new pupil involvement or neurologic signs) and arrange neuroimaging/vascular repair as indicated (CTA/MRA and possible DSA) with ophthalmology/neurosurgery follow-up.
Patient Education: You have a third nerve (oculomotor) palsy caused by an aneurysm compressing the nerve. This explains your drooping eyelid, double vision, and pupil enlargement. Because aneurysms can rupture with serious consequences, definitive treatment (surgical clipping or endovascular repair) is recommended to prevent rupture. While awaiting or after repair, patching or occluding one eye can relieve double vision. If you develop worsening headache, sudden severe eye/face pain, sudden vision loss, increasing eyelid droop, or new weakness/numbness, seek emergency care immediately. Control vascular risk factors (blood pressure, blood sugar) and stop smoking. Follow-up with your neurosurgeon/interventional radiologist and neuro-ophthalmologist for treatment planning and postoperative vision/motility rehabilitation.
Referrals: Neurosurgery and interventional neuroradiology for aneurysm repair (surgical or endovascular)

CASE 69 — Sixth Nerve Palsy
===========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Binocular horizontal diplopia, primarily at distance and worse on left gaze.
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "below and to the side of the other"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 56-year-old with type II diabetes mellitus presenting with 3 months of binocular horizontal diplopia, worse at distance and on left gaze; found to have 4 PD esotropia and limitation of abduction of the left eye. No other systemic symptoms noted in HPI.
History Narrative: A 56-year-old with type II diabetes mellitus presented with 3 months of binocular horizontal diplopia, worse at distance and on left gaze. Examination showed 4 prism diopters of esotropia and limitation of abduction of the left eye consistent with a left sixth nerve palsy. The patient also had left facial numbness in the V1 distribution and left-sided headache. MRI revealed a cavernous sinus meningioma causing the left sixth nerve palsy and damage to the left ophthalmic branch of the trigeminal nerve.
Symptom Onset: 3 months
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Subacute
Characteristics: Left-sided sixth cranial nerve palsy causing limitation of abduction with 4 PD esotropia at distance, associated left V1 sensory loss and left-sided headache from cavernous sinus meningioma; diplopia binocular, horizontal, worse on left gaze.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Left gaze
Associated Symptoms: Binocular horizontal diplopia (worse on left gaze), left-sided headache, left facial numbness/sensory changes in V1 distribution, limitation on abduction (left), esotropia.
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty with distance vision tasks due to binocular horizontal diplopia, causing problems with activities like driving and reading signs; requires patching/prisms for symptomatic control.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies headaches, facial numbness, ptosis, or any other complaints
Risk Factors: Type II diabetes mellitus; cavernous sinus meningioma (compressive lesion)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity testing
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis, no periorbital swelling; limitation of left eye abduction (left lateral rectus paresis) with left facial numbness in V1 distribution.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear, deep, quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses (no cataract)

Pupils – Reaction to Near: equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Limitation of left eye abduction; ductions, versions, saccades, and pursuit otherwise intact.

Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Some degree of excyclotropia in the right eye


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 56-year-old patient with Type II diabetes presenting with 3 months of binocular horizontal diplopia, worse on left gaze. Exam notable for 4 PD esotropia and limitation of abduction of the left eye consistent with a left lateral rectus paresis. Associated left facial numbness in V1 distribution and left-sided headache. MRI demonstrated a cavernous sinus meningioma. Impression: left sixth cranial nerve palsy with involvement of the left ophthalmic (V1) branch of the trigeminal nerve due to a cavernous sinus meningioma; diplopia management options include patching, prisms, early ipsilateral medial rectus botulinum injection, and consideration of surgery or radiation directed at the meningioma with strabismus surgery later if persistent.
Primary Diagnosis: Left sixth nerve palsy secondary to cavernous sinus meningioma
Secondary Diagnoses: Damage to the left ophthalmic branch of the trigeminal nerve (V1) secondary to cavernous sinus meningioma; type II diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Left sixth nerve palsy secondary to cavernous sinus lesion (e.g., meningioma); ischemic (vasculopathic) sixth nerve palsy; myasthenia gravis; thyroid eye disease
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: left facial numbness and sensory changes in distribution of ophthalmic branch (V1); left-sided headache; persistent diplopia; potential residual ophthalmoplegia and need for strabismus surgery


Plan
All Procedures: patching; prisms; botulinum injection into the ipsilateral medial rectus; strabismus surgery; surgery or radiation therapy for the underlying lesion (e.g., meningioma)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe for recurrence of neurologic or ophthalmologic deficits; follow-up for ongoing symptomatic diplopia management (patching, prisms, botulinum injection, or strabismus surgery as indicated) and return for neuroimaging/neurosurgical or radiation consultation and further treatment of the underlying lesion as needed.
Patient Education: The diplopia (double vision) is caused by a weakness of the nerve that moves the left eye outward (left sixth nerve palsy) due to a tumor (meningioma) in the cavernous sinus. Treatment focuses on the tumor (observation, surgery, or radiation as recommended by your specialists) and on relieving the double vision in the short term with an eye patch, prism glasses, or temporary botulinum injection to the inner eye muscle. Strabismus surgery may be considered later if eye alignment and double vision do not improve. You should be monitored regularly for any new or worsening neurological symptoms (increased double vision, drooping eyelid, changes in facial sensation, headache, or vision loss) and follow up with neurosurgery/otolaryngology and ophthalmology for imaging and management decisions.
Referrals: Neuro-ophthalmology and neurosurgery (for cavernous sinus meningioma management; consider radiation oncology for radiosurgery)


CASE 70 — Internuclear Ophthalmoplegia
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: difficulty focusing her eyes
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "difficulty focusing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 27-year-old woman with a history of multiple sclerosis and prior right optic neuritis, not on medications, presents with “difficulty focusing” her eyes. She has a mild adduction deficit in the left eye, right abducting nystagmus, slow saccades in adduction in the left eye, and intact convergence consistent with a left internuclear ophthalmoplegia.
History Narrative: A 27-year-old woman with a past medical history of multiple sclerosis and right optic neuritis not currently on medications presents with “difficulty focusing” her eyes. She has a mild adduction deficit in the left eye, right abducting nystagmus, slow saccades in adduction in the left eye, and intact convergence consistent with a left internuclear ophthalmoplegia.
Symptom Onset: 3 months
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Characteristics: Adduction deficit in left eye, right abducting nystagmus, slow adducting saccades in left eye, intact convergence, binocular horizontal diplopia worse at distance and on left gaze, 4 PD esotropia at distance (nil at near), left abduction underaction, decreased left forehead sensation, slightly depressed left corneal reflex.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: worse on gaze to the left; primarily present at distance but absent at near
Alleviating Factors: Near fixation (diplopia absent at near)
Associated Symptoms: difficulty focusing, binocular diplopia (worse at distance and on left gaze), numbness and tingling in the left forehead, mild left frontotemporal headaches, decreased sensation over left forehead, slightly depressed left corneal reflex
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty focusing eyes and binocular diplopia (worse at distance), causing impairment in activities requiring distance vision; likely mild-to-moderate functional impact on daily tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: numbness and tingling in the left forehead; mild left frontotemporal headaches; diabetes controlled with oral agents
Risk Factors: Multiple sclerosis; diabetes mellitus; vascular risk factors (age-related)

CASE 71 — Diplopia—Ocular Myasthenia Gravis
===========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Diplopia
DURATION: 6 months
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "as the day goes on"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months that worsens as the day progresses, with intermittent left or right eyelid droop (fluctuating ptosis).
History Narrative: A 57-year-old woman presented with intermittent diplopia for 6 months that worsened "as the day goes on," and intermittent left or right eyelid droop. She had no other medications reported.
Symptom Onset: 6 months
Symptom Duration: 6 months
Progression: Intermittent over 6 months with worsening as the day goes on (fatigable)
Characteristics: Intermittent diplopia worsening as day progresses; intermittent bilateral ptosis with enhanced ptosis on contralateral lid elevation; variable ocular motor weakness (paresis of both lateral rectus muscles), fatigable symptoms, positive Tensilon test, negative AChR antibodies — consistent with ocular myasthenia gravis.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Symptoms worsen "as the day goes on" (fatigue/diurnal worsening), increased ptosis with elevation of the opposite eyelid (enhanced ptosis)
Alleviating Factors: Closing one eye or patching one eye; use of spectacle crutch for ptosis; responsiveness to anticholinesterase agents and corticosteroids (symptoms improve with these treatments)
Associated Symptoms: intermittent diplopia, eyelid droop (ptosis), worsening fatigue of eyelids as day progresses, orbicularis oculi weakness, fluctuating ocular motor weakness (variable diplopia), possible blurred/difficult focusing (from second case), mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, leg numbness, history of optic neuritis (visual changes)
Previous Episodes: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months with intermittent eyelid droop; no prior generalized myasthenic episodes reported.
Impact on Daily Activities: Intermittent diplopia and fluctuating ptosis worsening as day progresses causing difficulty with vision-related tasks (focusing, reading, driving) and possible need for eye patching or spectacle crutch — moderate functional impact on daily activities.
Risk Factors: Older age (though case patient was 57), female sex, autoimmune disorder (e.g., concurrent autoimmune disease), thymoma or thymic abnormalities, prior/underlying systemic myasthenia gravis (risk of generalization), variability/fatigability of symptoms; also factors associated with progression: absence of acetylcholine receptor antibodies does not exclude risk.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N13/14
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Bilateral ptosis (variable; increased with sustained upgaze/with contralateral eyelid elevation)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis, orbits unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses bilaterally

Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally and equally reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 5 mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Near: equal react to near
Pupils – RAPD: mild right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Paresis of both lateral rectus muscles; adduction weakness of the left eye with slowed adducting saccades; otherwise ductions full; convergence preserved; bilateral ptosis noted (in earlier case)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp examination was normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: mild temporal pallor in the right optic disc; left optic disc normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: otherwise normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ocular myasthenia gravis presenting with fluctuating diplopia for 6 months, bilateral ptosis with enhanced ptosis, paresis of both lateral rectus muscles, positive Tensilon test, and negative acetylcholine receptor antibodies — clinical picture consistent with ocular-only myasthenia gravis (evaluate for thymoma with mediastinal imaging; monitor for risk of generalization).
Primary Diagnosis: Ocular myasthenia gravis
Secondary Diagnoses: Multiple sclerosis (history of optic neuritis with residual right optic nerve pallor and small paracentral scotoma)
Differential Diagnosis: Internuclear ophthalmoplegia (MLF lesion, e.g., multiple sclerosis or brainstem infarct); Ocular myasthenia gravis (myasthenic pseudo-INO); Cranial nerve VI palsy (bilateral or alternating abducens paresis); Brainstem mass or infection causing MLF dysfunction
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Generalization of myasthenia gravis (development of generalized weakness including dysphagia and respiratory involvement); possible thymoma-related complications; exposure keratopathy from ptosis/crutch use; complications from treatments (steroid side effects, immunosuppression, plasmapheresis/IVIG risks).


Plan
All Medications: pyridostigmine bromide (Mestinon), corticosteroids (prednisone), azathioprine, cyclophosphamide, cyclosporine, intravenous immunoglobulin, plasmapheresis
All Procedures: Tensilon test single fiber EMG MRI (mediastinal CT or MRI for thymoma evaluation) mediastinal CT mediastinal MRI ptosis surgery placement of spectacle crutch (ptosis crutch) thymectomy plasmapheresis intravenous immunoglobulin (IVIG)
Follow-Up Instructions: Patients need close supervision of medications used to control symptoms. Warn about possible generalization and instruct to notify physician immediately if dysphagia, respiratory difficulty, or extremity weakness develop.
Patient Education: Warn about possibility of disease generalizing (report immediately if dysphagia, breathing difficulty, or limb weakness); medication supervision and side effects; options for symptomatic relief (eye patching, eyelid crutch on glasses); medical treatments (anticholinesterase agents like pyridostigmine, corticosteroids, immunosuppressants) and potential need for further tests (mediastinal CT/MRI to evaluate for thymoma); when to seek urgent care (respiratory compromise).
Referrals: Mediastinal CT or MRI for thymoma evaluation; neurology follow-up for myasthenia management and monitoring for generalization.
Emergency Instructions: Inform physician immediately if symptoms of generalization develop, such as dysphagia, respiratory difficulty, or extremity weakness.


CASE 72 — Thyroid Ophthalmopathy
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: mild to moderate eye irritation, diplopia, and eyes bulging
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "eye irritation" "foreign body–type" "diplopia" "eyes bulging" "two lines instead of one"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35-year-old woman with a history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine one year ago who presents with a 2-month history of mild to moderate eye irritation described as a foreign body sensation, diplopia, and progressive eye bulging (proptosis). She has associated eyelid retraction, lid lag, and symptoms consistent with dry eye (mild punctate keratopathy).
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with a past medical history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year ago presents with a 2-month history of mild to moderate eye irritation described as a foreign body–type sensation, diplopia, and bulging eyes. She reports worsening symptoms over the day and signs consistent with thyroid eye disease.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Subacute (progressing over months)
Characteristics: Proptosis, lid retraction and lag, diplopia, extraocular muscle restriction (limited elevation, depression, abduction), mild punctate keratopathy, scleral show, exposure-related eye irritation/foreign body sensation, restrictive strabismus (esotropia/vertical misalignment), eyelid retraction, chronic inflammatory orbital disease associated with thyroid dysfunction


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Activities that fatigue the extraocular muscles and prolonged upward gaze; diurnal worsening (symptoms worse as the day goes on); heat/elevated body temperature; smoking; recumbency (worse morning periorbital edema)
Alleviating Factors: rest, elevation of head of bed, ocular lubrication (methylcellulose tears/ointment), dark glasses with side protection, taping eyelids shut or goggles at night, smoking cessation, systemic corticosteroids (for severe inflammation)
Associated Symptoms: eye irritation, foreign body sensation, diplopia, eyes bulging, limitation of elevation and depression, limitation of abduction, lid retraction, lid lag, proptosis, mild punctate keratopathy, photophobia, corneal and conjunctival exposure, periorbital edema
Prior Treatments: Radioactive iodine (for hyperthyroidism) approximately 1 year prior; supportive ocular lubricants (artificial tears/ointment) and conservative measures (head elevation, sunglasses) inferred.
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty driving due to diplopia (seeing double lines), intermittent worsening throughout day causing impairment in tasks requiring sustained gaze; mild eye irritation and photophobia affecting comfort; overall moderate functional limitation.
Systemic Symptoms: history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine; otherwise no systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: Smoking; history of thyroid disease (hyperthyroidism, treatment with radioactive iodine); possibly radioactive iodine treatment; thyroid dysfunction (autoimmune thyroid disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J4+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: lid retraction, lid lag, bilateral ptosis, scleral show, mild periorbital edema
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild injection with punctate keratopathy consistent with exposure (conjunctival injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis, scleral show, lid retraction, lid lag, periorbital edema (morning), mild punctate keratopathy from exposure
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild punctate keratopathy
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: open angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic changes consistent with age-related cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 5mm and equally reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect.
Pupils – Other: 5mm and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Limitation of elevation and depression bilaterally; limitation of abduction bilaterally; duction testing suggested bilateral lateral rectus pareses; extraocular motility fatigues with sustained gaze (worsening esotropia on lateral gaze and development of left hypertropia with prolonged upgaze).
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild punctate keratopathy, otherwise slit-lamp exam normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; periphery normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine one year ago presenting with 2 months of bilateral eye irritation, diplopia, lid retraction/lag, proptosis, restricted elevation/depression and abduction, and mild punctate keratopathy. Findings and history are most consistent with active thyroid ophthalmopathy (thyroid eye disease) with extraocular muscle involvement causing restrictive motility disturbance and exposure-related ocular surface disease. No optic neuropathy is described. Management focuses on optimizing thyroid status, smoking cessation, ocular surface lubrication, conservative measures for exposure, consideration of systemic steroids or orbital radiation if inflammation worsens, and delaying strabismus/eyelid/orbital decompression surgery until disease is inactive (stable 6–12 months).
Primary Diagnosis: Thyroid orbitopathy
Secondary Diagnoses: Hyperthyroidism (history of radioactive iodine treatment); dry eye/keratopathy; lid retraction and lid lag; diplopia; restrictive extraocular myopathy (thyroid eye disease)
Differential Diagnosis: Thyroid (Graves) orbitopathy; Ocular myasthenia gravis; Cranial nerve VI palsy (bilateral); Idiopathic orbital inflammation (orbital myositis)
Severity: Mild to moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Exposure keratopathy; diplopia; compressive optic neuropathy leading to visual loss; globe luxation; cosmesis and eyelid retraction-related problems


Plan
All Medications: radioactive iodine, methylcellulose artificial tears, ointment (ocular), systemic corticosteroids, neostigmine (Prostigmin), edrophonium (Tensilon), botulinum toxin
All Procedures: methylcellulose artificial tears (day) ointment at night elevate head of bed/sleep partially supine dark glasses with side protection eyelid taping at night humidified goggles patching for diplopia prism correction for diplopia botulinum toxin injection into tight/stiff extraocular muscles and levator systemic corticosteroids (oral or IV) orbital radiation therapy orbital decompression surgery strabismus (extraocular muscle) surgery (muscle recessions/resections) eyelid procedures: lateral tarsorrhaphy, Müller's muscle/levator lengthening, lower eyelid elevation, blepharoplasty with orbital fat excision
Follow-Up Instructions: Close ophthalmologic supervision with monitoring of visual acuity, color vision, visual fields, and fundus for optic neuropathy; monitor for corneal epithelial breakdown and need for more aggressive treatment; coordinated, long-term follow-up with endocrinology and ophthalmology as part of a multidisciplinary team.
Patient Education: Stop smoking; maintain thyroid control with your endocrinologist. Use lubricating drops during the day and ointment at night; elevate head of bed and use dark glasses for photophobia. Protect eyes at night (taping lids or humidified goggles) if exposure symptoms. Use prism, patching, or botulinum toxin short-term for diplopia; report worsening vision, pain, or increased redness/foreign-body sensation promptly. Expect prolonged course; close follow-up with ophthalmology and endocrinology; surgery (decompression → strabismus → lid surgery) only after disease stabilizes.
Referrals: Endocrinology

Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: lid retraction and lid lag bilaterally
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild injection/conjunctival injection bilaterally
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis 22 mm OD, 24 mm OS (Hertel base 95 mm)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Right pupil 6 mm in dark with poor, segmental light reaction and vermiform iris movements; left pupil 3 mm, reactive normally.
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right pupil 6 mm (poorly reactive, segmental); Left pupil 3 mm (reactive)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light; left pupil reactive (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Light-near dissociation
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: right pupil 6 mm in dark, left pupil 3 mm; right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light; light–near dissociation; vermiform segmental iris movements
Extraocular Movements: Limitation of elevation and depression in both eyes; limitation of abduction in both eyes (worse on the left); adduction relatively normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal pathology; periphery normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Isolated, idiopathic tonic pupil — right anisocoria with a dilated, poorly light-reactive, segmentally reactive right pupil with vermiform iris movements and light–near dissociation consistent with Adie (tonic) pupil. Reassurance; no specific medical or surgical treatment indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: Isolated, idiopathic tonic pupil
Secondary Diagnoses: Anisocoria (tonic/Adie’s pupil)
Differential Diagnosis: Isolated idiopathic tonic pupil (Adie’s pupil); Holmes–Adie syndrome; Pharmacologic pupillary dilation; Oculomotor (third nerve) palsy
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: No significant complications; typically none — mostly benign with possible persistent accommodative paresis and induced astigmatism.


Plan
All Medications: thyroid supplements
All Procedures: Slit-lamp examination Static perimetry (visual field testing) Hertel exophthalmometry Duction testing Forced duction testing (recommended) Computed tomography (CT) or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the orbits (recommended) Dilute pilocarpine testing (pharmacologic testing for cholinergic supersensitivity)
Follow-Up Instructions: No treatment, except reassurance; use unequal bifocal reading aids or a unilateral frosted bifocal segment if accommodative paresis is permanent; observe for pupil size change over time and possible bilateral involvement (≈4%/yr).
Patient Education: This condition (tonic/Adie’s pupil) is benign and usually not sight‑threatening. Its main features are a pupil that reacts poorly to light but better for near vision, possible blurred near vision or difficulty focusing, and sometimes a mild, fluctuating eye discomfort. No specific treatment is typically required—reassurance is appropriate. For persistent near-vision difficulty, simple measures include unequal bifocals or a unilateral frosted bifocal segment to reduce symptoms. Monitor for any new symptoms (worsening vision, increased pain, double vision, or systemic symptoms like fainting or muscle weakness) and return for reevaluation if they occur. If the pupil causes cosmetic concern, discuss options with your ophthalmologist.


CASE 74 — Anisocoria—Horner’s Syndrome
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: anisocoria and left upper eyelid drooping (left ptosis) with left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "it could be due to a brain tumor."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45-year-old woman noted anisocoria two weeks earlier when a friend at a party pointed out one pupil was larger; she has occasional headaches for several years but no other illnesses, eye or head trauma, eye drop use, or visual symptoms.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman noted anisocoria two weeks earlier when a friend observed one pupil larger than the other. She reported occasional headaches over several years but denied other illnesses, significant head or eye trauma, use of any eye drops, or visual symptoms. She saw a neurosurgeon who obtained a normal brain MRI and suggested a possible cerebral angiogram. On exam visual acuity was 20/20 OU, color vision and visual fields were normal. The right pupil measured 6 mm in darkness and reacted poorly and segmentally to light; the left pupil measured 3 mm and reacted briskly. The right pupil constricted slowly to near and redilated slowly at distance; the left constricted briskly to near and redilated quickly. Extraocular motility was normal and there was no ptosis. Slit-lamp exam showed vermiform, segmental movements of a sector of the right iris; fundus exam was normal.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Characteristics: Anisocoria—Horner’s Syndrome


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches; drooping of left upper eyelid (ptosis)
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: Coumadin for 3 months, then maintenance aspirin
Impact on Daily Activities: None; no impact on daily activities noted.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies other illnesses; occasional headaches over several years.
Risk Factors: Hypertension; spontaneous internal carotid artery dissection (implied vascular disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/90 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen — 20/20 bilaterally
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left ptosis 2 mm; no eyelid or lash abnormality on right.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet, no injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; extraocular motility normal; no periorbital swelling or ecchymosis
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: vermiform, segmental movements of the iris; no iris injury
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic changes consistent with age (early cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right pupil 6 mm (dilated, segmental light reaction); Left pupil 3 mm (small, briskly reactive)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light; left pupil reacted briskly and symmetrically to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: The right pupil slowly constricted to near and then slowly redilated on looking in the distance.
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Right pupil 6 mm in darkness, poorly and segmentally reactive to light; left pupil 3 mm in darkness, briskly reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU; motility normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Vermiform, segmental movements of one sector of the right iris; no iris injury.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Postganglionic Horner’s syndrome of the left eye due to spontaneous dissection of the internal carotid artery — confirmed by pharmacologic testing (10% cocaine showed poor left pupillary dilation; 1% hydroxyamphetamine showed poor dilation of the left pupil consistent with postganglionic lesion). Ptosis ~2 mm on the left. No ocular motility deficit. Managed medically with anticoagulation (Coumadin for 3 months) then aspirin; Horner’s syndrome itself requires no surgical treatment.
Primary Diagnosis: Postganglionic Horner’s syndrome due to spontaneous dissection of the internal carotid artery
Secondary Diagnoses: Horner’s syndrome (postganglionic, left)
Differential Diagnosis: Tonic (Adie) pupil; Pharmacologic mydriasis; Third cranial nerve palsy; Iris structural damage
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: cocaine 10%, hydroxyamphetamine 1%, pilocarpine 1%, pilocarpine 0.125%, Coumadin (warfarin), aspirin
All Procedures: Topical 10% cocaine instillation in both eyes; Topical 1% hydroxyamphetamine instillation in both eyes; MRI of the brain; Anticoagulation with warfarin (Coumadin) therapy for 3 months; Maintenance aspirin therapy thereafter
Follow-Up Instructions: Cases in which no etiology is evident require close observation to investigate for the development of other neurologic or medical signs or symptoms.
Patient Education: Horner’s syndrome itself does not require treatment, but it is important to identify and treat the underlying cause (in this case a carotid artery dissection). You may notice an uneven pupil size and mild drooping of the eyelid; these signs may improve as the underlying problem is treated. Expect vascular treatment as recommended by your physician (this patient received anticoagulation followed by antiplatelet therapy). No surgery is typically needed. Close follow‑up is important to monitor for new neurologic symptoms or changes and to ensure the underlying cause is resolving.


CASE 75 — Anisocoria—Eye Drops
==============================

LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"

Pupil failed to constrict to dilute pilocarpine, consistent with pharmacologic mydriasis.

CASE 76 — Leukocoria
====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Leukocoria
