DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 3-year-old boy referred for persistent poor vision and esotropia; diagnosed at age 2 with right-eye esotropia and amblyopia with prescribed full-time patching of the left eye done sporadically for ~6 months without improvement. Parents noted leukocoria of the left eye. Current exam: best-corrected VA 20/200 OD, 20/30 OS; 25 PD constant esotropia at distance and near with full motility; 1+ RAPD in the right eye; cycloplegic refraction +1.25 D OU. Initial posterior pole exam suggested normal disc/macula/vessels but careful review revealed small, crowded, pale right optic disc with double-ring sign and smaller left disc. No significant medical history except height/weight at 15th percentile; no family history of strabismus/amblyopia.
History Narrative: A 3-year-old boy is referred by another ophthalmologist. The child was diagnosed with esotropia and amblyopia in the right eye at age 2 years. The referring ophthalmologist prescribed full-time patching of the left eye, which was done sporadically for approximately 6 months. No improvement in the child’s vision was noted, prompting referral for a second opinion and treatment recommendations. The child’s medical history is notable for a weight and height in the 15th percentile, but is otherwise unremarkable. The review of systems is completely negative with the exception of the ophthalmologic complaints and there is no family history of strabismus or amblyopia. The patient is shown in ▶Fig. 85.1. Best-corrected visual acuity is 20/200 in the right eye and 20/30 in the left eye. Motility evaluation reveals an esotropia of 25 prism diopters at distance and at near with full ductions and versions. Pupillary testing reveals a 1 + relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) in the right eye. Cycloplegic refraction is +1.25 diopters in both eyes and initial evaluation of the posterior pole reveals a normal disc, macula, and vessels. The ophthalmologist is concerned by the presence of the RAPD and performs a more careful evaluation of the posterior pole. The disc of the right eye appears slightly pale. The vessels on the nerve are crowded. The disc diameter is small, and a double-ring sign is noted (▶Fig. 85.2a, b). The left disc is also slightly smaller than normal.
Symptom Onset: Age 2 years (esotropia and amblyopia noted at age 2; leukocoria identified by age 3)
Symptom Duration: Approximately 1 year
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Leukocoria, bilateral retinoblastoma (left > right), vitreous seeding, exudative retinal detachment, optic nerve involvement risk, hereditary potential (RB1 germline mutation possible), presents with esotropia and RAPD, small/crowded/pale optic discs (optic nerve hypoplasia-like features).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Leukocoria, esotropia, decreased vision in right eye (20/200), relative afferent pupillary defect (1+ RAPD) in right eye, optic disc pallor/small crowded disc with double-ring sign, sporadic patching history, growth retardation (height and weight 15th percentile), history of amblyopia.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant visual impairment in the right eye (20/200) and leukocoria/advanced disease in the left with likely loss of vision, causing functional monocular vision affecting depth perception and activities requiring binocular vision; requires protective measures and rehabilitation.
Systemic Symptoms: Completely negative (no systemic symptoms)
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Hereditary RB1 germline mutation (family history of retinoblastoma or germline mutation), young age (infancy/early childhood), bilateral disease, prior radiation exposure, monocular status increasing trauma risk, increased risk of secondary malignancies (soft-tissue and bony sarcomas)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (best-corrected visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonometry (Icare rebound tonometry) under anesthesia


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal appearing conjunctiva and sclera (no injection or abnormality noted)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Left orbital mass with leukocoria and enucleation performed; left orbit involved greater than right with evidence of orbital involvement on CT.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris bilaterally (no neovascularization, heterochromia, or iris mass noted)
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear, intact lens with no cataract detected


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 1+ relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) in the right eye
Pupils – RAPD: 1+ RAPD in the right eye
Pupils – Other: 1+ relative afferent pupillary defect in the right eye
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc of the right eye appears slightly pale; vessels on the nerve are crowded; disc diameter is small with a double-ring sign; left disc is also slightly smaller than normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: crowded
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: large retinoblastoma with vitreous seeding and exudative retinal detachment (left > right), tumor involvement extending peripherally with retinal detachment and vitreous seeds


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Bilateral retinoblastoma: the left eye demonstrates advanced disease with leukocoria and greater involvement necessitating enucleation; the right eye has tumor involvement amenable to combined medical and focal surgical therapy. Examination under anesthesia and MRI confirmed bilateral intraocular tumors with left greater than right. Genetic testing for RB1 germline mutation and endocrine evaluation due to associated findings are indicated. Close follow-up with periodic examinations under anesthesia (every 3–4 months until age 5 if germline mutation present) and long-term surveillance for secondary malignancies recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Bilateral retinoblastoma
Differential Diagnosis: Optic nerve hypoplasia; Retinoblastoma; Persistent fetal vasculature (persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Increased risk of secondary malignancies (particularly soft-tissue and bony sarcomas), risk of tumor spread via optic nerve with orbital recurrence, vision loss/monocular status, and potential endocrine abnormalities (from associated septo‑optic dysplasia).


Plan
All Medications: steroids
All Procedures: Examination under anesthesia; Magnetic resonance imaging (brain/orbit); Genetic testing for RB1; Chemotherapy (intravenous, intra-arterial, periocular, and intravitreal as indicated); Focal therapies (laser photocoagulation, cryotherapy, radioactive plaque therapy); Enucleation; Fabrication/fitting of protective polycarbonate eyewear and prosthetic/ocular rehabilitation follow-up
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up examinations under anesthesia every 3–4 months until age 5 (if RB1 germline mutation present), then frequent exams for several more years with annual eye exams thereafter; examine enucleated socket at each contralateral-eye visit; long-term surveillance for secondary malignancies; protect remaining eye with polycarbonate safety glasses and provide eye-safety education.
Patient Education: Protecting the remaining eye (wear polycarbonate safety glasses), educate family on signs of recurrence and need for frequent follow-up exams (every 3–4 months under anesthesia until age 5 if RB1 germline mutation, then continued regular exams), importance of genetic testing and long‑term surveillance for secondary malignancies (soft‑tissue and bone sarcomas), and adherence to treatment and prompt reporting of new symptoms.
Referrals: Endocrinology (urgent) and pediatrician for endocrine testing and growth monitoring; oncology/ocular oncology for retinoblastoma management and genetic counseling/testing.


CASE 77 — The Child Who Sees Poorly Out of One Eye
==================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: The child’s left eye has looked “funny” for the last 3 months with a white pupil (leukocoria).
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "funny"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 9‑month‑old girl whose family noted her left eye had looked “funny” for the past 3 months; pediatrician detected leukocoria and urgently referred her to ophthalmology. She is otherwise healthy, born 2 weeks premature with birth weight 6 lb 8 oz, no systemic disease, animal exposures, or family history of childhood ocular disease. On exam she fixes and follows with the right eye but not the left, with a white vascular lesion and large serous retinal detachment in the left eye; only the posterior pole seen in the right eye. Strong objection to occlusion of the right eye.
History Narrative: The family of a 9-month-old girl noted that her left eye has looked “funny” for the last 3 months. The girl’s pediatrician detected leukocoria and immediately referred her to an ophthalmologist for evaluation and treatment. The child is an otherwise healthy 9-month-old with a negative review of systems with the exception of the white pupil. She was born 2 weeks prematurely and had a birth weight of 6 lb., 8 oz. There is no history of systemic disease or exposure to animals, and there is no family history of childhood ocular disease. Examination reveals a normal right eye, though only the posterior pole can be seen on retinal examination. In the left eye, a white, vascular lesion is obvious with a surrounding large serous retinal detachment. The child fixes and follows well with her right eye, but does not fix or follow with her left eye. Strong objection to occlusion of the right eye is noted.
Symptom Onset: 3 months prior
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Characteristics: Unilateral leukocoria with a white vascular intraocular mass and large serous retinal detachment in the left eye; right eye appears clinically normal except for a small inferior retinal tumor; left eye non-fixing/following; onset at ~6 months, noted for 3 months; suspicious for retinoblastoma (mass with calcification on imaging possible), unilateral presentation, associated visual loss and amblyopia risk.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: white pupil; poor vision/does not fix or follow with left eye; strong objection to occlusion of right eye
Impact on Daily Activities: Unable to assess — N/A
Systemic Symptoms: Negative
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Uncorrected anisometropia (significant hyperopia)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: NLP: light perception only
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: NLP: light perception only
Visual Acuity – Method: Fixation and fixation preference testing
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen (applanation) under sedation/anesthesia


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear, except for a posterior capsular cataract in the left eye


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: round, symmetric, normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: 1+
Pupils – Other: leukocoria
Extraocular Movements: Full with normal versions bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize macula due to large serous retinal detachment (macula off)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Large serous retinal detachment with a white vascular mass in the left periphery; right peripheral retina shows a small inferior tumor; right posterior pole only visualized.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Anisometropic amblyopia of the right eye due to uncorrected hyperopia; left eye leukocoria with a large vascular intraocular mass and serous retinal detachment consistent with retinoblastoma (massive tumor in left eye) with a smaller inferior retinal tumor in the right eye; right eye fixation and follow-follows, left eye nonfixating; need urgent MRI/orbit and genetic testing, ocular ultrasound, and ophthalmic oncology management with consideration of enucleation/pathology for the left eye and focal/systemic therapy as indicated, plus refractive correction and amblyopia therapy (spectacles ± penalization/occlusion) for the right eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Anisometropic amblyopia in the right eye due to uncorrected hyperopia.
Differential Diagnosis: Retinoblastoma; Coats’ disease; Persistent fetal vasculature (PFV); Toxocariasis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: atropine eye drops (topical)
All Procedures: Examination of the eyes under anesthesia; MRI of head and orbits; B-scan and A-scan ocular ultrasonography; genetic counseling/testing (RB1) and general physical examination; enucleation with pathologic examination (if performed)
Follow-Up Instructions: Ophthalmologic follow-up examinations as needed after amblyopia treatment; monitor visual acuity and refractive error periodically, update prescriptions as child ages, and continue/maintain patching or other therapy as indicated (may require maintenance patching until age 7–8 years) to detect and prevent recurrence.
Patient Education: Explain leukocoria is a serious finding needing urgent evaluation; discuss likely causes (retinoblastoma, Coats’ disease, PFV, others) and need for prompt imaging (MRI/orbit and ultrasound) and examination under anesthesia. Explain amblyopia risk and treatment: full cycloplegic refraction and glasses, possible atropine penalization, and patching of the good eye (often 2 hours/day for mild–moderate amblyopia) with regular follow-up to monitor vision and refractive changes. Discuss genetic testing and counseling if retinoblastoma suspected, examination of siblings/parents, and that early treatment improves visual and life prognosis.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmologic evaluation for any worsening vision, new eye pain, redness, or a change in the appearance of the pupil; if retinoblastoma is suspected, urgent referral to a specialist and prompt imaging (MRI of orbits/brain) are required.


CASE 78 — Childhood Torticollis
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Failed preschool vision screening test.
DURATION: 4 years
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 4-year-old boy referred after failing preschool vision screening; confirmed by pediatrician. No prior medical or eye history, no eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, or squinting. Parents report no symptoms; child has not had prior ophthalmologic exam. Technician found 20/40 OU on Allen figures; HOTV testing by ophthalmologist showed 20/50 OD and 20/30 OS. Cycloplegic refraction: +3.00 OD, +1.00 OS. Motility: orthotropia at distance, 4 PD exophoria at near, full ductions/versions; stereoacuity 60" ARC.
History Narrative: A 4-year-old boy failed his preschool vision screening test. The vision screening failure was confirmed by his pediatrician and he has been sent for ophthalmologic evaluation and treatment recommendations. The child is a robust 4-year-old with no history of medical or ophthalmologic problems. The review of systems is negative and, specifically there is no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or other ophthalmologic problems. The child has not previously had an ophthalmologic examination and there is no family history of amblyopia or childhood eye disease.
Symptom Duration: Chronic
Characteristics: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: head tilt, face turn, chin rotation, mild facial asymmetry, decreased vision in right eye (20/50), stereoacuity reduced to 60" arc, no pain or other systemic symptoms
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — minimal functional limitation; may have head tilt but generally able to perform daily activities without significant impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative
Risk Factors: Childhood superior oblique palsy associated with congenital/early-onset torticollis, anisometropic hyperopia (right +3.00 vs left +1.00) leading to amblyopia risk, uncorrected refractive error, and possible facial asymmetry from chronic head tilt.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/70 mmHg
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Method: HOTV acuity test
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing orbits (no proptosis, enophthalmos, or palpable mass)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal cornea (clear, no opacities)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal.
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to accommodation (normal)
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry.
Primary Diagnosis: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry
Differential Diagnosis: Amblyopia (anisometropic/refractive); Refractive error (anisometropia/ametropia); Strabismic amblyopia (including superior oblique palsy-related); Deprivational amblyopia (media opacity/ptosis)
Severity: mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Amblyopia (refractive/anisometropic or strabismic) - Persistent abnormal head posture and facial asymmetry - Diplopia (may develop later) - Residual or recurrent vertical deviation (over- or under-correction after surgery) - Postoperative Brown’s syndrome (limitation of elevation in adduction) - Need for additional strabismus surgeries


Plan
All Medications: 1% cyclopentolate
All Procedures: ophthalmologic evaluation and treatment recommendations cycloplegic refraction with 1% cyclopentolate visual acuity testing (Allen figures, HOTV acuity test, Snellen-type recognition testing) stereoacuity testing (Titmus fly) ocular motility evaluation (ductions/versions, cover testing) vertical/horizontal prism dissociation for fixation preference testing vertical prism correction (trial/prism therapy) inferior oblique weakening surgery (myectomy or recession) superior oblique strengthening surgery (tuck) combined oblique and rectus muscle strabismus surgery postoperative ophthalmologic follow-up for fusion, stereopsis, and visual acuity monitoring
Follow-Up Instructions: Parents should be advised at the time of surgery of the potential for over- or under-correction and the possible need for additional surgery in the future; expect possible transient Brown’s syndrome after superior oblique tuck; children should undergo periodic ophthalmologic evaluations focusing on fusion, stereopsis, and visual acuity to detect, prevent, and treat amblyopia.
Patient Education: Explain torticollis and that in children it can be due to eye muscle weakness (ocular torticollis) as well as nonmuscular causes; review that common signs include head tilt, face turn, or chin rotation. Describe that evaluation includes vision testing, eye alignment and movement exam, cycloplegic refraction, and stereo/fixation assessment to look for amblyopia or refractive errors. Explain that treatment options depend on cause: glasses for refractive error, patching/amblyopia therapy if needed, prism for small vertical deviations, and surgery (usually weakening of the inferior oblique or strengthening of the superior oblique) for symptomatic superior oblique palsy or persistent head tilt—surgery may require further procedures and can cause temporary movement limitations. Advise that many mild cases need no surgery and can be observed, and that close follow-up is important to monitor vision, stereopsis, and head posture to detect or prevent amblyopia. Recommend prompt treatment of significant refractive error or amblyopia and returning for regular ophthalmology visits; seek urgent care if new double vision, increasing head tilt, or sudden vision loss occur.


CASE 79 — Aniridia
==================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Anomalous left head tilt since infancy and intermittent eyes not moving together (strabismus)
DURATION: 5 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I strongly resist any attempts to straighten my head."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 5-year-old boy referred for evaluation of a constant left head tilt noted since infancy; parents report he has resisted attempts to straighten his head, frequent eye rubbing and blinking, and intermittent misalignment of the eyes for the past 2 months. He fixes and follows well with either eye and alternates fixation; no systemic symptoms and unremarkable family history. Cycloplegic refraction +1.75 +0.75 OD/OS.
History Narrative: A 5-year-old boy is brought to his ophthalmologist’s office at the request of his pediatrician for evaluation of an anomalous head tilt. The child has undergone orthopaedic evaluation and neck muscle abnormalities are absent. The child has had a relatively constant left head tilt since he first gained head control during the first year of life. His parents note that he strongly resists any attempts to straighten his head. During the last 2 months, they have noticed that his eyes sometimes do not appear to move together. Family history is unremarkable and the review of systems is notable only for frequent eye rubbing and blinking behavior.
Symptom Onset: Since infancy (noted since he first gained head control during the first year of life).
Symptom Duration: Since first gained head control during the first year of life (≈ ~4–5 years)
Progression: Progressive (risk of progressive corneal opacification, cataracts, and glaucoma)
Characteristics: Sporadic aniridia with WAGR features (aniridia, developmental delay, genitourinary abnormalities) due to 11p13 deletion; associated photophobia, foveal hypoplasia, risk of corneal opacification, cataract, glaucoma, and Wilms’ tumor.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Bright light/photophobia (worse in bright lighting)
Alleviating Factors: Occlusion of one eye (patching) is tolerated and reduces symptoms; wearing sunglasses or shaded contact lenses alleviates photophobia.
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, frequent eye rubbing and blinking, nystagmus (congenital), anomalous head tilt, abnormal head posture, alternating fixation, mild facial asymmetry, decreased visual development (foveal hypoplasia)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate impact — photophobia, frequent eye rubbing/blinking, and risk of progressive vision loss causing need for low-vision aids and educational intervention; periodic medical visits and abdominal ultrasounds adding burden.
Systemic Symptoms: frequent eye rubbing and blinking behavior; developmental delay; genitourinary abnormalities (renal mass/Wilms' tumor)
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: WAGR syndrome (11p13 deletion) with high risk for Wilms tumor; congenital aniridia (foveal hypoplasia) leading to photophobia, progressive corneal opacification, cataract, and glaucoma; family history unremarkable (sporadic case)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 92 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4°F
Vitals – Respirations: 18
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (J1)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen (age-appropriate / fixation and following assessment)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external examination of the orbits with no palpable masses or deformities; mild facial asymmetry (left side smaller) noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal cornea (though at risk for progressive corneal opacification and vascularization)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Absent iris (aniridia)
Anterior Segment – Angle: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic changes absent; lens clear (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Normal
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular movements with moderate overelevation of the right eye in adduction (right inferior oblique overaction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment and pupillary examination are normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Foveal hypoplasia
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina unremarkable/no peripheral pathology noted


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Sporadic aniridia associated with developmental delay and genitourinary abnormalities consistent with WAGR syndrome (11p13 deletion); high risk for Wilms tumor — recommend regular abdominal ultrasounds until ~3 years, ophthalmologic surveillance for progressive corneal opacification, cataract, and glaucoma, consideration of photophobia management (sunglasses/shaded contact lenses), early low-vision evaluation and educational intervention.
Primary Diagnosis: Sporadic aniridia
Secondary Diagnoses: Aniridia; Foveal hypoplasia; Photophobia; Progressive cataract (risk); Glaucoma (risk); Corneal opacification/vascularization (risk); WAGR syndrome components (developmental delay, genitourinary abnormalities); Wilms tumor (risk)
Differential Diagnosis: Superior oblique palsy; Brown’s syndrome; Dissociated vertical deviation; Congenital fibrosis syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Progressive corneal opacification and vascularization; progressive cataracts; glaucoma; foveal hypoplasia leading to reduced vision; risk of Wilms tumor (renal malignancy) due to WAGR syndrome.


Plan
All Procedures: Comprehensive ocular motility evaluation including three-step test; cycloplegic refraction; fundus examination; abdominal ultrasounds at regular intervals until ~3 years of age; MRI of abdomen if ultrasound abnormality; renal biopsy if MRI suggests Wilms’ tumor; referral to pediatric oncologist if Wilms’ tumor confirmed; periodic ophthalmologic screening examinations; low-vision evaluation; early childhood educational intervention; prescription of sunglasses or shaded contact lenses.
Follow-Up Instructions: Periodic ophthalmological screening exams to monitor for glaucoma, progressive cataract, and corneal opacification; abdominal ultrasounds at regular intervals until ~3 years of age for Wilms’ tumor screening (MRI and biopsy if ultrasound abnormal); referral to pediatric oncologist if Wilms’ tumor confirmed; early childhood educational intervention and low-vision evaluation.
Patient Education: - Explanation of aniridia: a congenital (present at birth) condition where the iris is absent or underdeveloped, causing light sensitivity, reduced visual acuity, and possible nystagmus. - Common associated risks: progressive corneal problems, cataracts, glaucoma, foveal hypoplasia (poor central vision), and, when part of WAGR syndrome, risk of Wilms’ tumor (kidney cancer). - Symptom management: wear sunglasses or shaded/contact lenses for photophobia; avoid eye rubbing. - Surveillance recommendations: regular ophthalmology exams to monitor vision, cornea, lens, and intraocular pressure; periodic low-vision evaluation and early educational/rehabilitation services. - Systemic screening (if syndromic/WAGR suspected): renal ultrasound regularly until about age 3 to screen for Wilms’ tumor; further imaging (MRI) and oncologic referral if abnormalities are found. - When to seek urgent care: sudden change in vision, new eye pain, redness, abnormal eye position, or any abdominal mass/abdominal pain.
Referrals: Pediatric oncologist for confirmed Wilms’ tumor; low-vision/early childhood educational services.


CASE 80 — Retinopathy of Prematurity
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: “jiggling eyes” and large pupils
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 9-month-old boy referred for “jiggling eyes” and large pupils first noted during the first month of life; parents report photophobia (closes eyes in bright sunlight) and apparent reduced vision compared with siblings; born at term with prenatal care and no perinatal complications; past medical history notable for hypospadias repair 2 months earlier and developmental delay.
History Narrative: A 9-month-old boy is referred to his ophthalmologist with a history of “jiggling eyes” and large pupils, both noted during the first month of life. The child closes his eyes in bright sunlight and does not appear to see as well as his two older siblings. The boy was born at term, the mother received prenatal care, and there were no perinatal complications. The child’s medical history is notable for a hypospadias repair 2 months earlier and for developmental delay. He has two healthy siblings and two healthy parents with no significant family history of ophthalmologic or medical problems. On examination, the child appears to fix and follow, but the fixation behavior is abnormal. A low-amplitude/high-frequency horizontal pendular nystagmus is noted. Examination reveals peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization. The child appears to have only a very small peripheral remnant of iris and an anterior pyramidal cataract is noted in both eyes. Retinal examination is notable for the absence of an obvious umbo or foveal pigmentation. The child’s parents and two older siblings were examined and found to have normal ophthalmologic examinations.
Symptom Onset: first month of life
Symptom Duration: 9 months
Characteristics: Type I retinopathy of prematurity (severe ROP: stage 3 with plus disease) characterized by abnormal retinal neovascularization with plus disease, requiring treatment (laser or intravitreal anti-VEGF); risk of progression to retinal detachment and associated high myopia, strabismus, amblyopia.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Prematurity (being born very preterm) and supplemental oxygen exposure
Associated Symptoms: jiggling eyes; large pupils; photophobia (closes eyes in bright light); decreased visual behavior (appears not to see as well as siblings)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate—visual impairment (nystagmus, poor fixation, likely reduced acuity) limits visual tasks; requires monitoring and potential visual rehabilitation.
Systemic Symptoms: developmental delay; history of hypospadias (genitourinary abnormality)
Risk Factors: Prematurity (low gestational age and low birth weight), neonatal intensive care with supplemental oxygen exposure, respiratory distress/oxygen therapy, and systemic neonatal complications associated with prematurity (e.g., sepsis, anemia, blood transfusion)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 28 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Fix and follow
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Fixes and follows (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Method: Grating acuity (Teller Acuity Cards)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tono-Pen applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal globes, without proptosis or enophthalmos.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Severely hypoplastic with only a very small peripheral remnant (near-aniridia)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Anterior pyramidal cataract in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: large pupils
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: large pupils
Extraocular Movements: Full but conjugate (low‑amplitude/high‑frequency horizontal pendular nystagmus)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization; severely hypoplastic iris with only a very small peripheral remnant of iris; anterior pyramidal cataract in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Macula: Absent foveal umbo and foveal hypoplasia (no obvious foveal pigmentation)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Avascular peripheral retina to the ora serrata in both eyes


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Type I retinopathy of prematurity (ROP) bilaterally — severe posterior vasoproliferative disease requiring urgent treatment (laser photocoagulation or intravitreal anti-VEGF). Close, frequent retinal follow-up until disease regression with continued screening up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age for possible late reactivation. Educate caregivers regarding risk of associated visual sequelae (amblyopia, strabismus, high refractive error) and coordinate neonatal/pediatric care for systemic monitoring as indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: Retinopathy of prematurity (Type I, both eyes)
Secondary Diagnoses: Retinopathy of prematurity; Stage 3+ ROP (Type I, both eyes); Prematurity-related complications (risk for amblyopia, strabismus, severe refractive error)
Differential Diagnosis: Aniridia; Retinopathy of prematurity; Congenital cataract; Albinism
Severity: Type I
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: cataract; subretinal hemorrhage; intraretinal hemorrhage; vitreous hemorrhage; severe myopia; amblyopia; strabismus; severe refractive error (myopia, astigmatism, anisometropia); late disease reactivation


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab
All Procedures: intravitreal injection of bevacizumab; cryotherapy for retinopathy of prematurity; laser photocoagulation (peripheral retinal photocoagulation); frequent ophthalmic examinations (post-treatment monitoring)
Follow-Up Instructions: Frequent ophthalmic examinations until ROP has regressed; for bevacizumab-treated infants, continuous screening up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age for late reactivation.
Patient Education: Premature infants treated for type I ROP or at risk should have frequent eye exams until disease regression; if treated with bevacizumab they need continued screening up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age because of possible late reactivation. Parents should be counseled about risks of amblyopia, strabismus, and significant refractive errors (myopia, astigmatism, anisometropia) and the need for long‑term ophthalmic follow‑up.
Referrals: Genetics consultation and pediatrician follow-up (for Wilms’ tumor screening/abdominal ultrasound)


CASE 81 — Childhood Esotropia
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 11-week-old former 24-week premature girl for retinopathy of prematurity (ROP) screening/follow-up (due for ROP screening)
DURATION: Recurrent (status post strabismus surgery) — duration not specified.
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 11-week-old former 24-week gestational age premature girl, birth weight 650 g, presented for ROP screening. Last exam 1 week earlier showed immature retina in zone 1 without plus disease. Since then she developed sepsis and respiratory compromise requiring IV antibiotics and re‑intubation/ventilatory support; currently intubated and receiving 50% oxygen. Has anemia of prematurity and a history of necrotizing enterocolitis 4 weeks ago. Blinks briskly to indirect light; fundus exam now shows dilation and tortuosity of posterior pole vessels (plus disease) in zone I.
History Narrative: An 11-week-old former 24-week estimated gestational age premature girl presented for consultation for retinopathy of prematurity screening. Birth weight was 650 g. She is a frail-appearing infant who is intubated and receiving 50% oxygen. Since her last retinal exam 1 week prior (which demonstrated immature retina in zone I with no plus disease), she developed sepsis and respiratory compromise requiring re-intubation/ventilatory support and intravenous antibiotics. She has anemia of prematurity and had an episode of necrotizing enterocolitis 4 weeks earlier.
Symptom Onset: 11 weeks old
Symptom Duration: 11 weeks
Progression: Progression: developed from immature retina in zone I without plus disease to zone I ROP with plus disease (type I ROP) within one week
Characteristics: Infantile esotropia with recurrent esotropia, left dissociated vertical deviation, bilateral inferior oblique overaction, and left-eye amblyopia


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Sepsis, respiratory compromise with re‑intubation and supplemental oxygen, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, prolonged ventilation, extreme prematurity and very low birth weight
Associated Symptoms: sepsis, respiratory compromise requiring re-intubation/ventilatory support, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, frail appearance, requires 50% oxygen, blinking briskly to light
Prior Treatments: strabismus surgery
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — requires ongoing medical/surgical care and visual rehabilitation; risk of recurrent amblyopia and strabismus affecting vision-dependent activities throughout childhood
Systemic Symptoms: sepsis; respiratory compromise requiring re-intubation/ventilatory support; anemia of prematurity; necrotizing enterocolitis; frail-appearing; requiring 50% oxygen; history of prematurity (24-week gestational age)
Recent Travel: No recent travel.
Risk Factors: extreme prematurity (estimated gestational age 24 weeks), very low birth weight (650 g), supplemental oxygen/respiratory compromise and prolonged ventilation, sepsis, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, prolonged intravenous nutrition (implicit), multiple/more unstable clinical course


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 140 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0°C
Vitals – Respirations: 50 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Fix and follow (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Fixing, no measurable near acuity (central, steady, unmaintained)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal appearing conjunctiva and sclera
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: briskly reactive, normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: briskly reactive to indirect ophthalmoscope light with either eye
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to view macula due to immaturity/zone I ROP with posterior pole vascular dilation (plus disease)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Dilation and tortuosity of the posterior pole retinal vessels (plus disease)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Zone I immature retina with dilation/tortuosity of posterior pole vessels (plus disease)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Infantile esotropia status post strabismus surgery, now with recurrent esotropia, dissociated vertical deviation of the left eye, bilateral inferior oblique muscle overaction, and amblyopia of the left eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Infantile esotropia status post strabismus surgery, recurrent esotropia
Secondary Diagnoses: Infantile esotropia status post strabismus surgery, recurrent esotropia, dissociated vertical deviation (left eye), bilateral inferior oblique overaction, left eye amblyopia
Differential Diagnosis: Recurrent infantile esotropia; Partially accommodative (refractive) esotropia; Ocular motor nerve palsy (vertical dissociation with IO overaction)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: recurrent esotropia; dissociated vertical deviation (left eye); bilateral inferior oblique overaction; amblyopia (left eye)


Plan
All Medications: intravenous antibiotics, supplemental oxygen, bevacizumab, spectacles (full cycloplegic refraction), bifocals
All Procedures: retinopathy of prematurity screening examination with indirect ophthalmoscopy and scleral depression; dilated fundus examination; cycloplegic refraction with full spectacle prescription; amblyopia monitoring and treatment as indicated; strabismus surgery options discussed (bilateral medial rectus recession or medial rectus recession with ipsilateral lateral rectus resection or bilateral lateral rectus resection)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in the next few months to recheck ocular alignment; regular follow-ups throughout childhood with interval determined by stability of the angle, visual acuity, and age; monitor vision for amblyopia and treat as needed; repeat strabismus surgery if deviation recurs or new deviation develops.
Patient Education: Provide full cycloplegic spectacle correction and recheck alignment in a few months; monitor vision and treat amblyopia as needed; consider bifocals only if fusion/stereopsis documented and child is orthotropic at distance but esotropic at near; if residual esotropia >10–12 PD at distance with full correction, discuss strabismus surgery options; arrange regular follow-up throughout childhood to monitor alignment, stereopsis, and need for repeat surgery.


CASE 82 — Childhood Exotropia
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Crossed eyes noted since 2 months of age; recent intermittent inward drift of left eye, worse when tired.
DURATION: 4 years, 10 months
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "her left eye has been noted to intermittently drift inward, especially when she is tired."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 5-year-old girl with history of crossed eyes since 2 months of age; had strabismus surgery at 8 months with good alignment afterward until recently when her left eye has been noted to intermittently drift inward, especially when tired. Never treated with occlusion therapy. Current glasses at least 1 year old. Amblyopia suspected with decreased vision OS. Family history: sister with esotropia. Current spectacles +2.00 OD, +2.50 OS.
History Narrative: A 5-year-old girl with a history of crossed eyes since 2 months of age presents for an examination. She has a history of strabismus surgery at 8 months of age. Her mother reports that her eyes were well aligned following surgery until recently when her left eye has been noted to intermittently drift inward, especially when she is tired. She has never been treated with occlusion therapy. Her current glasses are at least 1 year old. Her past medical history is otherwise unremarkable. The family history is notable for a sister with esotropia that required surgical intervention.
Symptom Onset: 2 months of age
Symptom Duration: Since 2 months of age
Progression: Recurrent (initially resolved after surgery, now intermittent inward drift of OS)
Characteristics: Intermittent exotropia; pseudodivergence excess type; onset in early childhood with intermittent outward deviation, better control at distance than near, associated with poor nasal-to-temporal OKN, reduced stereopsis, and possible amblyopia risk.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: tiredness
Associated Symptoms: Intermittent inward drift of the left eye (worse when tired)
Previous Episodes: Recurrent exotropia after initial surgery at 8 months of age; intermittent inward drift noted recently since alignment had been good until recently.
Prior Treatments: strabismus surgery at 8 months of age
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely minimal — occasional intermittent misalignment when tired causing minor cosmetic concern but no major functional impairment in daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Family history of strabismus (sister with esotropia), early onset strabismus (onset at 2 months), prior strabismus surgery, uncorrected/under‑corrected hyperopia (+3.00D OD, +3.75D OS), amblyopia (worse VA OS), abnormal OKN indicating early‑onset strabismus.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 90 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4°F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (with current spectacle correction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear and normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric reaction to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk and equal
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full versions with moderate bilateral inferior oblique overaction and a V‑pattern (esotropia worse in downgaze, better in upgaze)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The remainder of the ophthalmologic examination is normal.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Pseudodivergence excess type intermittent exotropia.
Primary Diagnosis: Pseudodivergence excess type intermittent exotropia
Secondary Diagnoses: intermittent exotropia, amblyopia (left eye), inferior oblique overaction, dissociated vertical deviation, V-pattern strabismus, asymmetric OKN (latent nystagmus)
Differential Diagnosis: Infantile (congenital) esotropia; Partially accommodative (refractive) esotropia; Sensory esotropia; Sixth nerve (abducens) palsy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: amblyopia; decreased stereopsis; recurrence of exotropia


Plan
All Medications: cyclopentolate, homatropine, scopolamine, atropine
All Procedures: strabismus surgery (performed at 8 months of age)
Follow-Up Instructions: If medical management: follow-up every 2–6 months for reevaluation. If postoperative: follow-up with healing over ~6 weeks and continued long-term follow-up for recurrence and monitoring for decreased stereopsis or amblyopia.
Patient Education: Intermittent exotropia is a lifelong condition caused by reduced fusion control; it may be managed without surgery if control is good but can worsen over time. Treatment options include updated full hyperopic spectacle correction, patching (alternate or dominant‑eye) to reduce suppression, and — for older children who can cooperate — convergence exercises (e.g., pencil pushups) or orthoptic therapy to improve control. Surgery is considered when control is poor (deviation present >40–50% of the time) or for cosmetic/symptomatic reasons, typically delayed until ≥4 years when possible; procedures depend on exotropia type. Regular follow‑up every 2–6 months during medical management and long‑term monitoring after surgery are important to detect recurrence, decreasing stereopsis, or amblyopia.


CASE 83 — Childhood Ptosis
==========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Intermittent outward drifting of the right eye for 2 years (worse when tired or looking at distance)
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "the deviation is worse when the child is tired or when he is looking at distant objects." "the eye is now deviated outward approximately 60% of the day." "right eye closure in sunlight." "the deviation is worse at distance than near."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 4‑year‑old boy with a 2‑year history of intermittent outward drifting of the right eye, worse when tired or looking at distance, initially occurring 2–3 times/day but progressively more frequent and now present about 60% of the day; no antecedent trauma, fever, or other medical problems; mother notes right eye closure in sunlight.
History Narrative: A 4-year-old boy presents with a 2-year history of intermittent drifting out of the right eye. His mother states that the deviation is worse when the child is tired or when he is looking at distant objects. His mother initially noted this deviation approximately two to three times a day; however, it recently has been getting worse. She states that the eye is now deviated outward approximately 60% of the day. There is no history of antecedent head trauma, febrile episode, or other medical problem. There is no family history of strabismus or amblyopia. Review of systems is notable for right eye closure in sunlight.
Symptom Onset: 2 years prior (onset at age 2)
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Worsening over time; increased from occurring 2–3×/day to present ~60% of the day
Characteristics: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid with anomalous chin‑up head posture and absence of amblyopia


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: tiredness/fatigue and looking at distant objects; bright sunlight (causing right eye closure)
Alleviating Factors: recovery of orthotropia on refixation or blinking
Associated Symptoms: Right eye closure in sunlight; intermittent drifting/outward deviation of right eye, worse when tired or looking at distance; anomalous chin‑up head posture.
Previous Episodes: The deviation was initially noted approximately two to three times a day and has progressed to occurring about 60% of the day.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent diplopia/eye deviation 60% of day, chin‑up posture and light sensitivity cause functional and cosmetic impact but no amblyopia; may interfere with activities when tired or in sunlight.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 100 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal, no ptosis
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: 3 mm, round, briskly reactive (no anisocoria)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3mm, round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk response to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk response to accommodation (brisk near response)
Pupils – RAPD: No afferent pupillary defect detected
Pupils – Other: 3mm, round, briskly reactive, no anisocoria, no RAPD
Extraocular Movements: Full.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal appearing optic nerves bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid with anomalous chin‑up head posture and absence of amblyopia.
Primary Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid
Secondary Diagnoses: Intermittent exotropia (pseudodivergence excess type), anisometropia (mild), stereopsis preserved, no amblyopia
Differential Diagnosis: Intermittent exotropia (pseudodivergence–excess type); Congenital exotropia; Sensory exotropia; Myasthenia gravis
Laterality: Right
Complications: early and late infection, suture granuloma, exposure keratopathy, retrobulbar hemorrhage, overcorrections


Plan
All Procedures: Fasanella–Servat procedure; levator resection; frontalis suspension (using sutures such as Supramid, nylon, Prolene, Gore‑Tex; silicone rods; banked or autogenous fascia lata; palmaris longus tendon)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow the child throughout childhood for surveillance of amblyopia and anisometropia until at least age 7–10 years; monitor visual acuity, stereopsis, and refractive status regularly, reassess after any ptosis surgery (wound healing ~1 month) with attention to lid height and nocturnal lagophthalmos; provide ocular lubrication at night for ~1 month postoperatively (continue long-term if Bell’s reflex absent); monitor for recurrence of ptosis and treat amblyopia promptly if detected.
Patient Education: Congenital ptosis is treated when it affects vision or head posture; watch closely for amblyopia and anisometropia with regular follow-up until at least age 7–10. If amblyopia develops it is treated with occlusion (patching) or pharmacologic/optical penalization and glasses for significant refractive error. Small ptoses may be managed with limited procedures (e.g., Fasanella–Servat); levator resection is versatile for mild–severe ptosis if levator function is adequate; frontalis suspension is used for severe ptosis with poor levator function (autogenous fascia lata gives best long‑term results). Discuss risks of surgery (infection, exposure keratopathy, overcorrection, granuloma) and need for temporary nighttime lubrication and follow‑up for possible recurrence.


CASE 84 — The Apparently Blind Infant
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Droopy right upper lid since birth.
DURATION: Since birth
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "lazy"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 17-month-old boy with a droopy right upper lid present since birth. Parents report he holds his chin up when walking or watching TV (preferred chin‑up head posture ~15°). No noted ocular motility disturbance. Occasionally the lid blocks his pupil. No family history of ptosis, strabismus, or hereditary muscular disorders. Child otherwise healthy, developing normally, negative review of systems. Good fixation and following in both eyes without correction, no fixation preference.
History Narrative: A 17-month-old boy presents with a droopy right upper lid. His parents state that this right upper lid has been “lazy” since birth. They also state that he holds his chin up when he is walking or watching TV. No ocular motility disturbance has been noted. His parents also volunteer that occasionally the child lets the lid block his pupil. There is no family history of ptosis, strabismus, or hereditary muscular disorders. The child is otherwise healthy and developing normally with a negative review of systems.
Symptom Onset: Since birth
Symptom Duration: Since birth (17 months)
Progression: Stable since birth
Characteristics: Congenital right upper‑lid ptosis present since birth; moderate–severe severity with absent lid crease, MRD1 right 0.5 mm (improves to 2 mm with chin‑up), palpebral fissure R 6.5 mm vs L 10 mm, levator function R 6 mm (L 12 mm), preferred 15° chin‑up head posture, full extraocular motility, equal brisk pupils (3 mm), no anisocoria, normal fixation/following, minimal anisometropia (–0.25+1.50×90 OD, +0.50 OS), consistent with congenital levator (congenital ptosis/DVM).


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: chin-up head posture
Associated Symptoms: chin-up head posture; occasionally lets the lid block his pupil; no ocular motility disturbance; otherwise healthy, normal development; no family history of ptosis/strabismus/hereditary muscular disorders
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal — child has good fixation/following, normal vision both eyes, mild chin-up posture but no functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative review of systems (no systemic symptoms)
Risk Factors: Congenital levator muscle hypoplasia (congenital ptosis) — present since birth, absent lid crease, reduced levator function, chin-up head posture; no family history, no trauma, no anisocoria, no systemic/neuro signs (isolated congenital ptosis)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 98/60 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: fixation and following (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: good fixation and following visual behavior in both eyes (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central, steady, maintained (fix and follow)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow (behavioral fixation assessment)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tono-Pen (handheld applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right moderate-to-severe congenital right upper eyelid ptosis with absent/right rudimentary lid crease; MRD1 R 0.5 mm, L 3.5 mm; palpebral fissure R 6.5 mm, L 10 mm; levator function R 6 mm, L 12 mm; adaptive 15° chin-up head posture; occasional occlusion of visual axis by the lid.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, normal conjunctiva and sclera
External Exam – Orbit: Unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: 3mm, round, briskly reactive to light (no anisocoria; no heterochromia)
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear, normal crystalline lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3mm, round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to light (implies reactive to near)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: round and briskly reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and unrestricted (versions are full).


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Isolated developmental visual maturation (DVM): 17‑month‑old boy with congenital right upper eyelid ptosis consistent with congenital levator hypoplasia (interpalpebral fissures 6.5 mm OD, 10 mm OS; MRD1 0.5 mm OD, 3.5 mm OS; levator function 6 mm OD, 12 mm OS; absent lid crease OD) but with equal, normal fixation and following in both eyes, no fixation preference, full versions, equal reactive pupils without APD, minimal refractive anisometropia (–0.25 +1.50 ×90 OD, +0.50 OS), and preferred 15° chin‑up posture. Findings consistent with isolated DVM; normal development and visual behavior indicate no current surgical indication. Recommend ongoing developmental/neurologic surveillance and follow‑up; pursue further testing only if vision fails to normalize by 6–8 months of age or if neurologic/developmental concerns arise.
Primary Diagnosis: Isolated DVM
Differential Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis (levator muscle dysgenesis); Horner syndrome; Third cranial nerve palsy; Marcus Gunn jaw‑wink ptosis
Severity: Moderate to severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Amblyopia (from visual axis occlusion or induced astigmatism), exposure keratopathy risk after surgery if Bell’s phenomenon abnormal, associated strabismus, anisometropia/astigmatism, potential underlying neurologic disease (if not isolated)


Plan
All Procedures: exam under anesthesia, funduscopy, refraction, corneal diameter measurement, measurement of intraocular pressure, cycloplegic refraction, slit-lamp examination, motility examination, vertical prism test, cover test (occlusion), electrophysiologic testing, neuroimaging
Follow-Up Instructions: For isolated DVM: expect vision to normalize by 6–8 months; no further evaluation if normal. If vision fails to improve by 6–8 months, obtain electrophysiologic testing and neuroimaging. Periodic developmental and neurologic assessments by the pediatrician; continued monitoring and follow-up if neurologic abnormalities, developmental delay, or ocular findings (visual response may be delayed up to 1–2 years).


CASE 85 — Optic Nerve Hypoplasia
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Poor vision/not responding to visual stimuli (suspected blindness)
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 3-month-old girl brought by her parents for concerns she may be blind; parents report poor eye contact, lack of response to parental facial expressions, and apparent nonresponse to bright lights. Prenatal and birth histories unremarkable. No prior ophthalmologic or medical problems. Parents note two older siblings who responded visually at this age. Developmentally appears normal. On exam she does not fixate or follow targets, does not respond to facial gestures, but blinks to bright light; occasional OKN beat with equivocal attention; pupils 5 mm, moderately reactive without APD; anterior segment and fundus normal.
History Narrative: A 3-month-old girl is brought to her ophthalmologist by her parents because of concerns that she may be blind. They report that she has poor eye contact, that she does not respond to parental facial expressions, and that she does not appear to respond to bright lights. The child’s prenatal and birth histories are unremarkable. Her past ophthalmologic and medical histories are unremarkable. The child has two siblings, aged 2 years and 5 years, who visually responded much more at 3 months of age than the patient does and who are developmentally and visually normal at this time. The review of systems is otherwise negative. The child appears to be normal from a developmental standpoint, though the child has not been seen by her pediatrician in several weeks.
Symptom Onset: 3 months of age
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Variable; vision may improve (if delayed maturation/amblyopia) or remain stable/worse depending on severity and associated endocrine/CNS abnormalities.
Characteristics: Septo-optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia, associated growth hormone deficiency (short stature, low weight) and absence of the septum pellucidum.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: poor eye contact; does not respond to facial expressions; does not respond to bright lights (only blinks to bright light); poor fixation and following; occasional OKN nystagmus beats; no spontaneous nystagmus
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant—visual impairment (potentially severe in one eye with better-seeing eye at risk), requiring ophthalmic, endocrine, and rehabilitative follow-up; may limit normal visual development and require protective measures and therapies.
Systemic Symptoms: growth hormone deficiency, short stature, low weight


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 90/60 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 30 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: NLP (no light perception)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: LP (light perception)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: No light perception
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central, cannot fix or follow (no measurable near acuity)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fixation absent; no consistent fixation or following (central, steady, maintained not present)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm, moderately reactive, no afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Moderately reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: none
Pupils – Other: Moderately reactive to light, 5 mm in size, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full versions in all directions (orthotropic on exam; eyes are orthotropic with full versions to oculocephalic testing)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment and cornea normal on slit‑lamp examination.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia (right smaller with “double ring” sign; left relatively normal)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Septo‑optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia. The patient demonstrates poor visual behavior consistent with optic nerve hypoplasia and has associated growth hormone deficiency causing short stature and low weight. Developmental and neurologic screening are otherwise normal. Endocrine evaluation and pituitary hormone replacement are indicated; ophthalmic management includes monitoring vision, consideration of amblyopia therapy for the better potential eye, protective eyewear for the functional eye, and delayed strabismus surgery after maximal amblyopia treatment.
Primary Diagnosis: Septo-optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia
Secondary Diagnoses: Septo-optic dysplasia; Bilateral asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia; Growth hormone deficiency resulting in short stature and low weight; Esotropia; Functional amblyopia (possible); Pituitary hormone deficiencies (hypopituitarism)
Differential Diagnosis: Delayed visual maturation; Cortical visual impairment; Bilateral structural ocular pathology (e.g., optic nerve hypoplasia, retinal or corneal/lenticular disease)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Variable visual loss up to blindness (range: no light perception to 20/20) - Nystagmus - Strabismus (esotropia) with risk of recurrence after surgery - Amblyopia (functional component in affected eye) - Endocrine/hypothalamic-pituitary dysfunction (growth hormone deficiency, other pituitary hormone deficiencies) - Associated intracranial malformations (septo-optic dysplasia, absence of septum pellucidum, corpus callosum hypoplasia) - Need for lifelong endocrine monitoring and risk of delayed-onset hormonal abnormalities - Increased risk to the better-seeing eye (functional monocular status)


Plan
All Medications: pituitary hormone replacement as needed; stress‑dose corticosteroids when indicated; botulinum toxin (for strabismus)
All Procedures: VEP testing EEG (if indicated) Electroretinogram (if indicated) Neuroimaging (MRI) Endocrinology evaluation and pituitary hormone replacement Amblyopia therapy (trial) Strabismus surgery or botulinum toxin injection Prescription of polycarbonate safety spectacles
Follow-Up Instructions: - Ongoing evaluation of growth and endocrine parameters throughout early childhood, even if initially normal (monitor for delayed-onset endocrine abnormalities up to 3–4 years of age) - Continue care with an endocrinologist and provide pituitary hormone replacement as needed with close growth‑parameter follow-up - Administer stress‑dose corticosteroids during acute illness, surgery, or serious injury if corticotrophin deficiency is present - Trial and maximize amblyopia therapy before considering strabismus surgery; defer surgery (often until school age) as appropriate - Prescribe polycarbonate safety spectacles to protect the better‑seeing eye
Patient Education: Explain optic nerve hypoplasia (small optic nerves) and that vision can range from near normal to severely reduced; discuss possible associated brain/pituitary problems (septo‑optic dysplasia) and need for endocrine testing and long‑term follow‑up. Reassure parents that some visual improvement may occur and that amblyopia therapy (patching/vision stimulation) can be tried if appropriate. Recommend neuroimaging and evaluation by an endocrinologist for hormone testing and replacement if needed. Advise regular developmental surveillance and coordination with the pediatrician/neurologist. Discuss eye protection for the better eye (polycarbonate lenses) and that strabismus surgery, if considered, is usually delayed until amblyopia treatment is optimized and school age. Emphasize signs that require urgent care (poor feeding, failure to gain weight, vomiting, lethargy, or signs of adrenal insufficiency) and provide instructions to seek immediate medical attention for these.
Referrals: Endocrinology (pediatric endocrinologist)
Emergency Instructions: Stress doses of corticosteroids should be administered in situations such as acute illness, surgery, or serious injury for patients with corticotrophin abnormalities.


CASE 86 — Ptosis
================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Ptosis
DURATION: 8 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old man with an approximately 8-month history of an enlarging skin nodule on the right upper eyelid near the medial canthal region that had grown fairly rapidly, with one episode of bleeding after he picked at it; no prior skin cancer but extensive sun‑exposure history.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man presented for evaluation of an enlarging skin nodule of his right upper eyelid. The lesion had been present for approximately 8 months and had grown fairly rapidly. The patient recalled one episode of bleeding from the area after he had picked at the lesion. While there was no previous history of skin cancer, the patient reported an extensive sun-exposure history. His ophthalmic examination was within normal limits except for the external examination. There was a large elevated nonpigmented nodule involving the skin of the right upper eyelid near the medial canthal region. The nodule measured approximately 15 mm × 12 mm × 7 mm and showed numerous small telangiectatic vessels on its surface. The edges of the tumor had a thickened pearly appearance. There was no ulceration present. The skin underlying the lesion was freely moveable, and the tumor did not appear fixed to the underlying deep structures. There was no regional lymphadenopathy. The skin of the patient’s face showed diffuse sun-exposure changes consisting of fine wrinkles and telangiectatic vessels and scattered areas of crusting.
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Enlarging over ~8 months
Characteristics: Droopiness of the upper eyelids from levator aponeurosis dehiscence (bilateral), causing superior visual field impairment; lids improved after external levator advancement.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Aging (levator aponeurosis dehiscence)
Alleviating Factors: taping of the eyelid; use of eyelid crutches attached to spectacles
Associated Symptoms: Impairment of superior peripheral vision; eyelid droop (upper eyelid ptosis)
Previous Episodes: One episode of bleeding after he had picked at the lesion.
Impact on Daily Activities: Improved — postoperative improvement in visual field with no complications (preoperative visual impairment interfering with superior peripheral vision, improved after surgery)
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: fair skin; northern European ancestry; extensive sun exposure; history of previous basal cell carcinoma; family history of skin cancer; age (over 50)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: 20/30 (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Large elevated nonpigmented nodule of right upper eyelid near medial canthus; skin freely moveable; no ulceration; numerous surface telangiectasias; edges pearly and thickened; surrounding facial skin with diffuse sun-exposure changes (fine wrinkles, telangiectasias, scattered crusting); bilateral upper eyelid ptosis due to levator aponeurosis dehiscence.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal.
External Exam – Orbit: NORMAL
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open/normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerosis, mild cataracts bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light and accommodation (pupils normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full without restriction (normal)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3 OU, discs sharp, pink, no pallor, no edema, no hemorrhages
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize due to lid surgery / N/A
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ptosis of both upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion; symptomatic visual field impairment improved after bilateral external levator advancement.
Primary Diagnosis: Ptosis of both upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion.
Secondary Diagnoses: Ptosis (bilateral, aponeurotic)
Differential Diagnosis: Aponeurotic (age-related) ptosis; Myogenic ptosis (e.g., myasthenia gravis or other myopathy); Neurogenic ptosis (Horner syndrome or CN III palsy)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: bilateral
Complications: undercorrection; overcorrection; asymmetric or unsatisfactory lid contour; lagophthalmos with exposure symptoms


Plan
All Procedures: External levator advancement on both upper eyelids
Patient Education: If ptosis is mild and not causing symptoms, observation is reasonable. Temporary measures include eyelid taping or eyelid crutches on glasses to improve vision. For symptomatic ptosis causing visual field impairment, discuss surgical options: anterior levator advancement (external approach to reattach/advance the levator and recreate the eyelid crease) or posterior Muller’s muscle procedure; risks include under- or overcorrection, asymmetry, contour abnormalities, and lagophthalmos/exposure. Explain that surgery is usually performed under local anesthesia with intraoperative adjustment, typically improves visual field, and requires follow-up to monitor healing and eyelid position.


CASE 87 — Thyroid Eye Disease
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: drooping of her upper eyelids
DURATION: 3 to 4 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "the drooping had slowly progressed over the last 3 to 4 years" "the drooping was now severe enough to interfere with her vision" "she often found herself manually lifting her lids to improve her visual field" "she did not complain of diplopia or significant fluctuation of the lid position during the day"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 72-year-old woman with 3–4 years of slowly progressive bilateral upper eyelid drooping, now severe enough to interfere with vision; often manually lifts her lids to improve visual field. No periocular trauma or surgery history. No history of muscle weakness, fatigue, neurologic disease, or diplopia; no significant diurnal fluctuation. Previous photos did not show ptosis. Had bilateral cataract extractions with IOLs 4 years ago.
History Narrative: A 72-year-old woman referred for slowly progressive drooping of her upper eyelids over 3–4 years, now interfering with vision and requiring manual lifting of her lids. No prior periocular trauma or surgery. Bilateral cataract extraction with IOLs 4 years earlier. No history of muscle weakness, fatigue, diplopia, significant diurnal fluctuation, or known neurologic disease. Inspection of old photographs showed no prior ptosis.
Symptom Onset: 3 to 4 years ago
Symptom Duration: 3 to 4 years
Progression: Slowly progressive over 3–4 years
Characteristics: Thyroid eye disease with eyelid retraction and bilateral proptosis, signs include proptosis, eyelid retraction, limited extraocular motility, increased IOP in upgaze, and potential optic neuropathy.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Manually lifting her lids to improve her visual field; frequent artificial tears and punctal occlusion; wearing a moisture shield at bedtime (for nocturnal drying)
Associated Symptoms: Drooping of the upper eyelids (progressive bilateral ptosis), visual field obstruction relieved by manually lifting lids, no diplopia, no fatigue, corneal exposure symptoms (dryness), lagophthalmos/nighttime dryness.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Bilateral cataract extraction with intraocular lens implantation 4 years prior; thyroid hormone replacement therapy.
Impact on Daily Activities: Required lifting of her lids with her hands to improve visual field; ptosis severely interfered with her vision and daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypothyroidism (on thyroid hormone replacement)
Recent Travel: None
Risk Factors: Smoking; autoimmune thyroid disease (Graves’/thyroid dysfunction); older age; prior thyroid dysfunction (hypothyroidism/thyroid disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
