Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Upper eyelid retraction with widened palpebral fissures and scleral show; deep superior sulci; absent/effaced upper lid creases.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: No significant abnormalities; no scleral injection or chemosis observed
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; puncta patent; punctal occlusion performed (temporary or permanent considered)
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis, eyelid retraction, widened palpebral fissures with scleral show, and evidence of corneal exposure (lagophthalmos)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no epithelial defect; mild exposure‑related punctate epithelial erosions treated with lubricants
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: No rubeosis; iris unremarkable (no neovascularization or other iris abnormalities detected)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk and symmetric constriction
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions; orthophoric (no motility restriction)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Unable to visualize periphery; no peripheral fundus abnormality documented.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy) with bilateral eyelid retraction and proptosis causing exposure symptoms; hypothyroidism on replacement therapy. Managed conservatively with lubricants and punctal occlusion, followed by 4‑lid recession after 4 months of stability with good cosmetic and symptomatic outcome.
Primary Diagnosis: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy) with eyelid retraction and bilateral proptosis
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypothyroidism
Differential Diagnosis: Aponeurotic ptosis; Neurogenic ptosis (e.g., Horner syndrome, myasthenia gravis); Myogenic ptosis (e.g., oculopharyngeal dystrophy, chronic progressive external ophthalmoplegia); Mechanical ptosis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: artificial tears; lubricating ointment; topical intraocular pressure–lowering drops; prednisone; orbital radiation (if steroids contraindicated)
All Procedures: recession of all four eyelids; punctal occlusion; frequent artificial tears (conservative therapy)
Follow-Up Instructions: Maintain thyroid hormone replacement and routine endocrinology follow-up; continue artificial tears and punctal occlusion as needed; monitor vision, ocular surface, and motility; follow up postoperatively for eyelid surgery outcomes and routine ophthalmic exams.
Referrals: Endocrinology


CASE 88 — Ectropion
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Pain around both eyes.
DURATION: Progressive for 18 months
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "gritty"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old woman with 18 months of progressive bilateral eye prominence and bilateral periorbital pain described as dull ache worse with eye movement. She reports bilateral gritty sensation but no diplopia or blurred vision, no prior ocular surgery or trauma. Review of systems notable for heat and cold intolerance without weight change, hair or voice changes. Examination elsewhere revealed 2 mm bilateral lagophthalmos, upper and lower eyelid retraction with palpebral fissures 16 mm (R) and 15 mm (L), exophthalmometry 22 mm (R) and 21 mm (L), mild inferior punctate corneal erosions, and mild conjunctival injection.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old woman was referred for evaluation of dull, aching pain around both eyes that worsened with eye movement and progressive prominence of both eyes over the past 18 months. She reported bilateral gritty sensation but denied diplopia, blurred vision, prior ocular surgery or trauma. Past medical history was unremarkable with no known thyroid dysfunction, though she noted heat and cold intolerance without recent changes in weight, body hair, or voice. No known cardiovascular or neurologic disease. Exam later showed 20/20 corrected vision OU, normal color vision, pupils reactive without RAPD, orthophoria with mild (-1) restricted upgaze bilaterally, upper and lower eyelid retraction with palpebral fissures 16 mm OD and 15 mm OS, 2 mm bilateral lagophthalmos, increased resistance to retropulsion without palpable orbital masses, exophthalmometry 22 mm OD and 21 mm OS, IOPs 21 mm Hg OD and 20 mm Hg OS, scattered inferior punctate epithelial erosions OU, mild bulbar conjunctival injection, and otherwise unremarkable anterior and dilated fundus exams.
Symptom Onset: 18 months
Symptom Duration: 18 months
Progression: Progressive over time
Characteristics: Involutional ectropion of the left lower eyelid with resultant ocular irritation


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: eye movement
Alleviating Factors: lubrication with artificial tears or lubricating ointment
Associated Symptoms: Pain around both eyes; dull ache worse with eye movement; gritty sensation; heat and cold intolerance; tearing; dryness; ocular irritation; blurred vision — none reported; diplopia — none reported.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — ocular irritation and gritty sensation affecting comfort; no vision loss but symptomatic with exposure signs, likely interferes somewhat with daily activities (reading/comfort).
Systemic Symptoms: Heat and cold intolerance
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Age-related eyelid laxity (advanced age/involutional changes)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jaeger 1 (J1)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (corrected)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 21 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Lower eyelid ectropion of the left greater than right with horizontal laxity and medial punctal malposition; bilateral upper and lower lid retraction and 2 mm lagophthalmos; temporal flaring of upper lids; no lash abnormalities.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild bulbar conjunctival injection bilaterally, most pronounced at the horizontal rectus insertions
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Patent, no punctal stenosis, puncta apposed poorly preoperatively causing epiphora; lacrimal drainage otherwise unremarkable
External Exam – Orbit: Mild bilateral proptosis (exophthalmometry 22 mm OD, 21 mm OS) with upper and lower eyelid retraction, 2 mm bilateral lagophthalmos, mild (–1) restriction of upgaze, increased resistance to retropulsion, and no palpable orbital mass.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Scattered punctate epithelial erosions inferiorly in both corneas
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: phakic with no cataract detected


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (normal size)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Both pupils reacted normally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Both pupils reactive, no RAPD
Extraocular Movements: Orthophoria with mild (-1) restriction of upgaze bilaterally
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Scattered punctate epithelial erosions of both corneas inferiorly; mild bulbar conjunctival injection bilaterally (most pronounced at the insertion of horizontal rectus muscles); anterior segments otherwise unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: flat without pallor
Fundus Exam – Macula: Flat without pallor
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; periphery otherwise normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Involutional ectropion of the left lower eyelid causing ocular irritation from poor punctal apposition and exposure-related corneal surface disease. Surgical correction with lateral tarsal strip and medial spindle was performed, resulting in improved eyelid position and symptom relief.
Primary Diagnosis: Involutional ectropion of the left lower eyelid with resultant ocular irritation
Differential Diagnosis: Involutional ectropion; Cicatricial ectropion; Paralytic ectropion; Mechanical ectropion
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Procedures: lateral tarsal strip procedure; medial spindle procedure
Follow-Up Instructions: Routine postoperative follow-up with the oculoplastics surgeon to assess wound healing and eyelid position; return for suture removal and exam within 1 week; continue lubricating drops/ointment as needed; seek prompt evaluation for increasing pain, redness, discharge, vision change, or wound dehiscence.
Patient Education: Involutional ectropion is caused by age‑related loosening of the eyelid support, which can make the lower lid turn outward and cause tearing, dryness, irritation, and corneal damage. Mild cases can be managed with frequent lubricating eye drops during the day and ointment at night to protect the cornea. If symptoms persist or worsen, surgery is the definitive treatment; procedures (e.g., lateral tarsal strip with possible medial spindle or skin graft if scarring is present) tighten the eyelid and restore punctal apposition. Protect your eyes from wind and dry environments, use prescribed lubricants as directed, avoid rubbing, and return promptly for follow‑up if vision changes, worsening redness, pain, increasing tearing, or inability to close the eyelid occur.


CASE 89 — Entropion
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: left eye irritation for 1 year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 70-year-old man presented with a one-year history of left eye irritation. He had prior bilateral cataract surgery 3 years earlier and no other history of ocular disease or trauma. On exam his corrected vision was 20/20 OD and 20/25 OS; external exam showed marked eversion (ectropion) of the left lower eyelid with diffuse tarsal conjunctival injection and 1+ inferior superficial punctate keratitis. Both lower lids had moderate horizontal laxity with delayed snapback.
History Narrative: A 70-year-old man presented with left eye irritation that had been present for the last year. He had bilateral cataract surgery 3 years previously, and there was no other history of ocular disease or trauma.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Chronic, gradual over ~1 year
Characteristics: Involutional (age-related) entropion due to horizontal laxity, retractor dehiscence, orbicularis overriding, and relative enophthalmos


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Aging-related horizontal eyelid laxity and overriding preseptal orbicularis (involutional changes); ocular irritation (causing spasm) and eyelid edema as potential aggravators
Alleviating Factors: Taping the lid; lubricating drops and ointment
Associated Symptoms: Left eye irritation, 1+ inferior superficial punctate keratitis, conjunctival injection of tarsal conjunctiva, epiphora (tearing)
Prior Treatments: Lubricating drops and ointment; taping of the lid (temporizing); temporizing everting sutures (Quickert‑Rathbun) mentioned as options; Wies procedure performed.
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild—intermittent irritation and discomfort from ocular surface exposure, relieved with lubricants or temporary measures; no significant vision loss or functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported.
Risk Factors: - Horizontal eyelid laxity (involutional aging changes) - Disinsertion/dehiscence of lower eyelid retractors - Overriding of preseptal orbicularis - Relative enophthalmos from orbital soft tissue atrophy - Aging (involutional changes) - Ocular irritation or lid edema causing spastic entropion - Cicatricial causes: Stevens–Johnson syndrome, cicatricial pemphigoid, posttraumatic scarring (chemical/thermal burns) - Prior eyelid surgery or trauma

Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg

External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Marked out-turning of the left lower eyelid; mild dermatochalasis of both upper lids; both lower lids with moderate horizontal laxity and delayed snapback.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Marked tarsal conjunctival injection diffusely of the left lower eyelid
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal

Severity: Moderate

Patient Education: Lubricating eye drops and ointment for comfort; temporary taping of the lower lid if needed for short-term relief; explanation that temporizing everting sutures can give immediate symptom relief but definitive surgical repair (options include Wies procedure, lateral tarsal strip/horizontal tightening, and reattachment of lower lid retractors) is recommended to correct the entropion and prevent corneal damage; discuss risks/benefits of surgery, expected recovery, and need for follow-up to monitor healing and vision.


CASE 90 — Trichiasis
====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe right eye pain progressively worsening for 2 years, relieved by manually retracting the right lower eyelid
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "if she manually retracts her right lower eyelid."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 75‑year‑old woman with a 2‑year history of progressively worsening severe right eye pain, relieved by manually retracting her right lower eyelid; she notes in‑turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes rubbing the globe.
History Narrative: A 75-year-old woman presented with complaints of severe right eye pain that has been progressively worsening for 2 years. The patient has discovered that she can alleviate her pain if she manually retracts her right lower eyelid. Examination shows “in-turning” of the right lower eyelid causing the lashes to rub against the epibulbar surface. Moderate horizontal laxity of the lower lid is present. With gentle downward traction on the right lower eyelid, the lid margin can be restored to its normal position; however, the in-turning recurs when the patient blinks.
Symptom Onset: 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Progressively worsening over 2 years
Characteristics: In-turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes rubbing the epibulbar surface; moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; lower lid entropion due to involutional changes with distichiasis (aberrant lashes), associated blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eyelashes rubbing against the cornea/epibulbar surface (lower lid entropion causing lash-cornea contact)
Alleviating Factors: manually retracts her right lower eyelid
Associated Symptoms: Severe right eye pain alleviated by manually retracting the right lower eyelid; epiphora and ocular irritation from lashes rubbing on the epibulbar surface.
Previous Episodes: Recurrent trichiasis/distichiasis with focal recurrences after initial cryotherapy (episodes over prior 2 years)
Prior Treatments: Wies procedure on the lower eyelid and cryotherapy to aberrant lashes; prior office epilation and lid hygiene/meibomian gland management (lubricating drops/ointment)
Impact on Daily Activities: Severe — significant pain impairing daily functioning; relieved only by manually retracting the eyelid, requiring treatment and ongoing management.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Age-related changes (advanced age/involution), horizontal lower lid laxity, lower eyelid retractor dehiscence, orbicularis overriding/spasm, chronic inflammation (blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction), posterior lamellar scarring (cicatricial changes), prior lid edema/trauma or surgery (spastic entropion risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J7
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart at 20 feet (unaided)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 16 mmHg — Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right lower lid entropion with trichiasis/distichiasis; moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction of all four eyelids; focal post‑cryotherapy changes after treatment.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White conjunctiva; no conjunctival injection or scleral abnormalities
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing, no proptosis or enophthalmos noted (orbit without abnormality)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Punctate epithelial erosions from lash-induced corneal irritation, with focal epithelial defects/scarring where lashes abraded the epibulbar surface.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric in the right eye when tested (normal direct and consensual light reflex)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to accommodation (normal)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular movements intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: In-turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes contacting/rubbing the epibulbar surface (trichiasis), moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; no restriction of lid rotation on eversion, signs of blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction of all four eyelids.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal appearing optic nerve with no acute pathology inferred.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 75-year-old woman with chronic right lower eyelid entropion causing trichiasis from aberrant lashes/distichiasis and associated blepharitis with meibomian gland dysfunction of all four lids. Exam showed in-turning of the right lower lid with lashes rubbing the epibulbar surface, moderate horizontal lid laxity, and lid margin easily restored with downward traction (involutional entropion rather than cicatricial). Lid not restricted on testing. Assessment: trichiasis of the lower eyelid due to aberrant lashes (distichiasis) and involutional lower lid entropion; concurrent blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction.
Primary Diagnosis: Trichiasis of the lower eyelid due to aberrant lashes (distichiasis) and lower lid entropion
Secondary Diagnoses: Blepharitis; Meibomian gland dysfunction (all four eyelids)
Differential Diagnosis: Involutional entropion; Spastic entropion; Cicatricial entropion; Distichiasis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: loss of skin pigmentation and eyelid notching


Plan
All Medications: lubricating drops and ointment
All Procedures: Wies procedure; cryotherapy to aberrant lashes; office cryotherapy retreatment of recurrent distichitic lashes; lid hygiene program for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction
Follow-Up Instructions: Retreated focal recurrent distichitic lashes with additional in‑office cryotherapy as needed; institute lid hygiene program for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction and routine follow-up for entropion/trichiasis monitoring.
Patient Education: Explain trichiasis and its causes (misdirected lashes from entropion or distichiasis), why lashes rubbing the eye cause pain and corneal damage, and that treatment options include temporary measures (lubricating drops/ointment, epilation), office procedures (cryoablation, electrolysis, laser) and surgical correction of the entropion (wedge excision, lid-splitting, Wies procedure). Discuss expected recurrence risk and possible need for repeat treatments, explain postoperative care and eyelid hygiene for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction (warm compresses, lid scrubs, topical lubricants), warning signs requiring prompt return (increasing pain, redness, vision change, discharge), and set expectations for follow-up to monitor for recurrence and treat as needed.


CASE 91 — Dacryocystitis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: chronic irritation of the left eye worsening over the last 2 years
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 64-year-old woman with no prior ocular history presented with chronic left eye irritation worsening over the past 2 years. She reported tearing and symptoms related to entropion of the left lower eyelid with lashes rubbing the globe, along with associated mild inferior corneal punctate changes and conjunctival staining. Symptoms were longstanding and progressive, attributable to lid malposition and abnormal posterior lashes causing trichiasis; there was associated diffuse lid-margin thickening, telangiectasia, and scattered lash debris.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman presented with chronic irritation of the left eye worsening over the past 2 years. She had no prior ocular history. Examination revealed left lower eyelid entropion that could be easily returned to normal with gentle downward pressure. The tarsal conjunctiva showed mild erythema without cicatricial changes, the inferior fornix was deep without symblepharon, and eversion revealed a row of fine nonpigmented lashes posterior to the gray line contacting the globe. Mild inferior punctate corneal changes and rose bengal staining of the inferior bulbar conjunctiva were present in areas touched by the abnormal lashes. Diffuse lid margin thickening, fine telangiectatic vessels, and scattered debris including collarettes on lashes of all four lids were noted. Symptoms were attributed to both the entropion and the abnormal posterior lashes (distichiasis/trichiasis) with associated ocular surface irritation; coexisting eyelid margin disease/meibomian gland dysfunction was suspected. Additionally, dacryocystitis of the right side secondary to nasolacrimal duct disruption from prior nasal surgery was later diagnosed and treated.
Symptom Onset: 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Resolved after treatment with oral antibiotics and subsequent DCR with silicone tubing; good resolution of symptoms.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Narrowing or obstruction of the nasolacrimal duct (e.g., prior nasal surgery) leading to lacrimal sac blockage and infection; warm compresses recommended (no specific external aggravators stated)
Alleviating Factors: Frequent application of warm compresses
Associated Symptoms: tearing, pain, redness, swelling in medial canthal area, epiphora, chronic irritation
Prior Treatments: oral antibiotics; dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with placement of silicone tubing; warm compresses
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — chronic left-eye irritation and epiphora interfering with comfort and activities, improved after antibiotics and DCR.
Risk Factors: Nasolacrimal duct obstruction from prior nasal surgery; chronic nasolacrimal obstruction leading to stasis; eyelid malposition/entropion causing tear dysfunction; chronic eyelid inflammation (blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction); age (older adult)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left lower eyelid entropion; diffuse lid margin thickening with fine telangiectatic vessels; row of fine nonpigmented lashes posterior to gray line (distichiasis) contacting globe; scattered debris and collarettes on lashes of all four eyelids.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild inferior bulbar conjunctival erythema with rose bengal staining inferiorly; punctate corneal staining inferiorly
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Patent right nasolacrimal system; left dacryocystitis with nasolacrimal duct obstruction (prior nasal surgery) treated medically and later left DCR with silicone tubing resulting in resolution.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing, no proptosis or enophthalmos; external orbital structures without focal mass or deformity.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild inferior punctate epithelial erosions with corresponding inferior rose bengal staining.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: PERRL
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: briskly reactive, equal
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU without restriction or diplopia
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The tarsal conjunctiva showed mild erythema without cicatricial changes; inferior fornix deep without symblepharon; mild inferior punctate corneal staining; inferior bulbar conjunctiva with rose bengal staining in areas contacted by abnormal lashes; diffuse thickening of the lid margin with fine telangiectatic vessels; scattered debris and collarettes on lashes of all four lids; eversion revealed a row of fine nonpigmented lashes posterior to the gray line contacting the globe.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Dacryocystitis of the right eye secondary to nasolacrimal duct disruption from prior nasal surgery, presenting with acute infection that responded to oral antibiotics; planned/definitive treatment with dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with silicone tubing placement, performed after resolution of inflammation, resulting in good resolution of epiphora.
Primary Diagnosis: Dacryocystitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Right nasolacrimal duct obstruction (post‑nasal surgery)
Differential Diagnosis: Dacryocystitis secondary to nasolacrimal duct obstruction; Chronic dacryostenosis with recurrent canaliculitis; Preseptal/orbital cellulitis (less likely); Lacrimal sac neoplasm (consider in recurrent/atypical cases)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: recurrent infection/epiphora; lacrimal sac abscess/pyocele; need for surgical intervention (dacryocystorhinostomy); potential spread to surrounding tissues (cellulitis)


Plan
All Medications: oral antibiotics antibiotic ophthalmic drops silicone tubing
All Procedures: oral antibiotics; aspiration of lacrimal sac abscess (if present); dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with placement of silicone tubing; warm compresses (therapeutic procedure)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up with ENT and oncology for management of underlying midline disease; continue postoperative follow-up after dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tubing for epiphora resolution.
Patient Education: - Explain that dacryocystitis is an infection of the tear (lacrimal) sac caused by a blockage in the nasolacrimal duct, which leads to tearing, redness, pain, and swelling near the inner corner of the eye. - Emphasize importance of completing prescribed antibiotics and contacting the clinic promptly if fever, increasing pain, spreading redness, or worsening swelling occur. - Instruct on frequent warm compresses to the affected area to reduce pain and promote drainage; teach how to apply (warm, clean cloth for 10–15 minutes several times daily). - Explain that topical antibiotic eye drops are usually of limited benefit for this condition. - Advise that if an abscess forms, drainage may be needed and cultures can help tailor antibiotics. - Discuss that definitive treatment to prevent recurrence is dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR); surgery is usually done after the acute infection resolves but may be performed sooner if medical therapy fails. - Review postoperative expectations if surgery planned: temporary silicone tubes may be placed, follow-up with ophthalmology and ENT is needed, and epiphora (tearing) typically improves after successful DCR. - Recommend routine eyelid hygiene if concurrent blepharitis/meibomian dysfunction is present, and use of artificial tears for surface irritation as needed. - Provide return precautions and contact information for urgent concerns.
Referrals: Otolaryngology (ENT) and oncology
Emergency Instructions: Apply warm compresses frequently to the inflamed site; seek urgent medical care for worsening pain, increased swelling, fever, drainage of pus, or vision changes.


CASE 92 — Idiopathic Orbital Inflammation
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: tearing and pain of the right lower eyelid medially
DURATION: 48 hours
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "constant epiphora of the right eye since the time of her surgeries"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old woman with chronic right-eye epiphora since prior nasal surgeries for lethal midline granuloma, now with recent onset tearing and pain of the right lower eyelid medially, intermittent redness of the right lower eyelid with tenderness and mucous discharge, constant epiphora since surgeries, previously treated with unknown topical antibiotic; on exam erythema and tenderness of right medial lower lid, whitish mucoid discharge from punctum, mild conjunctival injection, puncta present and patent, nasolacrimal irrigation showed distal NLD obstruction with reflux through puncta, large nasal defect with absent septum from prior surgeries.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old woman with a history of lethal midline granuloma status post multiple nasal surgeries (including removal of the nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies) was referred for tearing and medial right lower eyelid pain. Previous Episodes: Yes — constant epiphora of the right eye since the time of her surgeries and intermittent redness/tenderness with mucous discharge more recently (chronic epiphora with subsequent recurrent inflammatory episodes).
Prior Treatments: multiple procedures including removal of the nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies; previously treated with an unknown ophthalmic antibiotic drop

Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms
Risk Factors: Prior sinonasal surgery with nasal septum removal and frontoethmoidectomies (prior facial/sinonasal trauma/surgery)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min

Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40

Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Erythema and mild edema of the right lower eyelid medially; puncta present and patent.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival injection of the right eye; otherwise normal sclera.

Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: Present
Pupils – Other: relative afferent pupillary defect resolved
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; extraocular movements intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Conjunctiva mildly injected; puncta present and patent; whitish mucoid discharge from punctum; no other anterior segment abnormalities noted.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Idiopathic orbital inflammation of the left orbit with associated scleritis and secondary choroidal effusion, presenting with acute orbital pain and inflammatory signs, rapid dramatic response to high-dose intravenous corticosteroids; infectious and collagen-vascular causes excluded and MRI negative for neoplasm.
Primary Diagnosis: Idiopathic orbital inflammation
Secondary Diagnoses: scleritis; choroidal effusion
Differential Diagnosis: Infection (dacryocystitis/orbital cellulitis); Autoimmune orbital disease (sarcoidosis, rheumatoid arthritis, Wegener’s granulomatosis/GRS); Thyroid-related orbitopathy (thyroid eye disease)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: None reported


Plan
All Medications: methylprednisolone (IV, 250 mg every 6 hours); oral prednisone; ophthalmic antibiotic drop (unknown)
All Procedures: nasolacrimal irrigation; dacryocystography (discussed as a test option); dacryoscintigraphy (discussed as a test option); intravenous methylprednisolone administration; conversion to oral prednisone; orbital biopsy (not performed but indicated if no improvement); MRI (follow-up)
Follow-Up Instructions: Referred for systemic evaluation for collagen-vascular disease or lymphoma; slow taper of oral steroid therapy with outpatient follow-up and repeat MRI to rule out underlying neoplasm.
Patient Education: Idiopathic orbital inflammation is an inflammatory condition of the tissues around the eye that typically causes pain, redness and swelling but often responds rapidly to high‑dose corticosteroid treatment. Treatment plan: start high‑dose systemic steroids (often IV methylprednisolone followed by an oral prednisone taper) with close follow‑up to monitor response and side effects. Expect symptom improvement within 24–48 hours; if no clear improvement after ~48 hours, biopsy and additional testing may be needed to exclude infection or tumor. Additional evaluation: systemic workup for autoimmune diseases and lymphoma as indicated, and MRI to exclude an underlying neoplasm if signs persist or recur. Monitor vision, pain, intraocular pressure, blood glucose and other steroid side effects during therapy; report worsening vision, increasing pain, fever, new neurological symptoms or signs of infection immediately.
Referrals: Referred for systemic evaluation for collagen-vascular disease or lymphoma


CASE 93 — Orbital Cellulitis
============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left eye pain and decreased vision for 2 days
DURATION: 2-day
LATERALITY: Left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 63-year-old man with 2-day history of left eye pain and decreased vision, with no prior ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury; notable for poorly controlled hypertension and no diabetes, presenting with acute onset left periorbital edema, erythema, proptosis, pain with eye movements, decreased vision OS.
History Narrative: A 63-year-old man presented with a 2-day history of left eye pain and decreased vision. He had no prior ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury. Medical history was significant for poorly controlled hypertension; he denied diabetes mellitus, rheumatoid disease, or collagen vascular disease. Review of systems revealed no sinus disease, malignancy, immunosuppressive therapy, or recent trauma.
Symptom Onset: 2 days
Symptom Duration: 2-day history
Progression: Improved
Characteristics: Acute onset left orbital inflammation with pain, decreased vision, marked periorbital edema/erythema, 3 mm proptosis, resistance to retropulsion, diffuse chemosis, severe motility restriction with pain, 2+ RAPD OS, diffuse scleral and choroidal thickening, subperiosteal orbital abscess associated with ethmoid sinusitis


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye movement (extraocular motility) — pain and worsened with gaze; increased resistance to retropulsion/proptosis
Associated Symptoms: eye pain, decreased vision, periorbital edema, erythema, chemosis, proptosis, pain with eye movement, limited extraocular motility, headache (inferred)
Previous Episodes: No previous ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury.
Prior Treatments: Previous (unsuccessful) medical therapy prior to surgery; then intravenous ceftriaxone and clindamycin (48 h IV then converted to oral antibiotics)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — marked decrease in left-eye vision (20/400), pain, proptosis, chemosis, and severe motility restriction limiting normal visual function and likely interfering with activities of daily living until treated.
Systemic Symptoms: No fever; no history of rheumatoid disease, collagen vascular disease, or diabetes mellitus; poorly controlled hypertension.
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms
Risk Factors: Ethmoid sinusitis; recent eyelid trauma not present; poorly controlled hypertension; no diabetes mellitus; no immunosuppression; none obvious (sinusitis is primary risk factor)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/90 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 99.1 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: marked periorbital edema and erythema
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 3+ conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal appearing lacrimal system with no noted lacrimal gland enlargement or drainage abnormality
External Exam – Orbit: Marked left periorbital edema and erythema with 3 mm left globe proptosis, increased resistance to retropulsion, significant motility deficit in all directions with associated pain.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: No corneal involvement detected; cornea clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and diffuse chemosis with no mention of hyphema, cells, flare, or shallow/flat chamber; anterior chamber appears quiet and formed — within normal limits.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light OD; left pupil with 2+ relative afferent pupillary defect (appears dilated)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 2+ relative afferent pupillary defect OS
Pupils – RAPD: 2+ left
Pupils – Other: 2 + relative afferent pupillary defect of the left pupil
Extraocular Movements: Significant motility deficit in all directions of gaze with associated pain
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 3 + left conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 63-year-old man with acute (2-day) left orbital inflammation characterized by pain, decreased vision (OS 20/400), a 2+ left RAPD, marked periorbital edema/erythema, 3 mm left proptosis, resistance to retropulsion, painful global motility restriction, 3+ conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis, and diffuse choroidal/scleral thickening on exam and MRI. Findings were consistent with orbital cellulitis with a large subperiosteal orbital abscess and associated ethmoid sinusitis. Blood and intraoperative cultures were negative. Treated with surgical drainage and ethmoidectomy plus IV antibiotics (ceftriaxone and clindamycin) with marked clinical improvement and complete resolution of swelling and motility restriction on follow-up.
Primary Diagnosis: Orbital cellulitis with associated sinusitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Poorly controlled hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Idiopathic orbital inflammation; Preseptal (periorbital) cellulitis; Thyroid-related orbital inflammation (thyroid eye disease); Orbital abscess from sinusitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Subperiosteal orbital abscess with decreased vision (relative afferent pupillary defect) and choroidal thickening; potential vision threat (optic nerve dysfunction) prior to drainage


Plan
All Medications: ceftriaxone; clindamycin
All Procedures: Drainage of large subperiosteal orbital abscess; ethmoidectomy; preoperative blood cultures; intraoperative cultures of purulent material.
Follow-Up Instructions: Marked improvement noted; after 48 hours IV antibiotics converted to oral antibiotics and patient discharged for outpatient follow‑up with subsequent examinations showing complete resolution of periorbital swelling and motility restriction.
Patient Education: Orbital cellulitis is a serious infection behind the eyelid that can cause eye pain, swelling, redness, decreased vision, and limited eye movement. It most often comes from nearby sinus infections or skin/eyelid trauma and requires prompt medical treatment to prevent vision loss or spreading infection. Key points and instructions: - You will need urgent treatment with intravenous antibiotics; sometimes surgery is required to drain an abscess. Follow hospital and surgeon recommendations closely. - Watch for worsening symptoms (increased pain, new or worsening vision loss, fever, spreading redness, or increased swelling) and seek emergency care immediately if they occur. - Complete the full course of antibiotics as prescribed, even if symptoms improve. - Keep the head elevated to reduce swelling and avoid activities that increase sinus pressure (heavy lifting, nose blowing) until cleared. - Attend all follow-up appointments so the eye, vision, and sinuses can be rechecked; additional imaging or ENT follow-up may be needed. - If you have or develop fever, diabetes, immune suppression, or recent trauma/sinus disease, inform your providers—these affect treatment decisions. - Ask your care team for clear discharge instructions about medications, wound care, signs to watch for, and when to return to work or normal activities.
Referrals: ENT surgeon; Infectious disease consult (pediatric infectious disease)


CASE 94 — Dacryoadenitis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever for 4 days
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "No definite complaint of decreased vision."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 7‑year‑old boy with 4 days of right eyelid swelling, pain, and fever; initially diagnosed with preseptal cellulitis and started on oral antibiotics with progression over 2 days to complete right upper‑lid ptosis, worsening edema and erythema, lethargy, fever ~100°F, periorbital tenderness, conjunctival injection, decreased right eye motility in all directions, mild proptosis, and palpable preauricular and submandibular lymphadenopathy; no definite complaint of decreased vision.
History Narrative: A 7-year-old boy was referred for evaluation and treatment of right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever. His symptoms began 4 days prior to presentation. No definite complaint of decreased vision was elicited. The presumptive diagnosis of preseptal cellulitis had been made, and the patient had been placed on oral antibiotics. Two days later, the child was reevaluated and found to have progression of the right upper lid edema, now causing a complete ptosis. Because of the disease progression despite oral antibiotics, the patient was referred to a tertiary care center. Upon his arrival, it was noted that the child was somewhat lethargic and continued to complain of pain around the right eye. The patient’s temperature was 100 °F. There was marked periorbital edema and erythema with complete ptosis of the eyelid. Enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes were palpated. The eyelid was gently lifted to allow for testing of the visual acuity, which was found to be 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS. The conjunctiva appeared diffusely injected, and there was decreased motility of the right globe in all directions of gaze. No conjunctival discharge was noted. Mild proptosis of the globe appeared to be present; however, the diffuse lid edema and tenderness precluded formal exophthalmometry measurements. The pupils reacted normally with no relative afferent defect. The anterior segments were quiet, and the dilated fundus exam showed no significant abnormalities.
Symptom Onset: 4 days prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 4 days
Progression: Progression of the right upper lid edema over 2 days despite oral antibiotics, now causing complete ptosis.
Characteristics: Acute inflammation and enlargement of the lacrimal gland (acute dacryoadenitis) characterized by rapid onset upper outer eyelid pain, fullness/“S-shaped” eyelid deformity, erythema and edema of temporal upper eyelid and conjunctiva, tenderness over superotemporal orbit, often with regional lymphadenopathy; in this case EBV-associated, self-limited, may respond to topical/oral corticosteroids.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: eye pain; eyelid fullness/upper outer eyelid swelling; fever; lethargy; periorbital edema and erythema; complete ptosis of the right upper eyelid; conjunctival injection; decreased ocular motility (pain with eye movement); mild proptosis; regional (preauricular and submandibular) lymphadenopathy; tearing (implied in keywords)
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: oral antibiotics (initially for presumed preseptal cellulitis)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — limited by pain, eyelid ptosis and lethargy causing functional impairment (difficulty opening eye, reduced activity) but vision largely preserved (20/25 OD, 20/20 OS).
Systemic Symptoms: Fever, lethargy, enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Recent Epstein–Barr virus (EBV) infection; prior viral infections (e.g., mumps); bacterial infection (Staphylococcus, Streptococcus, gonococcus); regional lymphadenopathy; systemic immunologic/idiopathic orbital inflammation; age (pediatric patients prone to infectious causes)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 100
Vitals – Temperature: 100 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 18 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5/6
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 18 mmHg (Goldmann applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Marked right periorbital edema and erythema with complete right upper‑eyelid ptosis; S‑shaped deformity of the left upper eyelid notated in figure; no conjunctival discharge.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: diffusely injected
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Enlarged, tender lacrimal gland with erythema and edema of the superotemporal upper eyelid consistent with acute dacryoadenitis
External Exam – Orbit: Mild proptosis OD with marked right periorbital edema and erythema, complete right upper‑eyelid ptosis, tenderness over superolateral orbit, decreased right ocular motility in all gazes; preauricular and submandibular lymphadenopathy.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear natural lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive normally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Decreased motility of the right globe in all directions of gaze
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segments were quiet on slit-lamp examination.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular abnormalities detected
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions seen.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute dacryoadenitis of the right lacrimal gland, clinically consistent with inflammatory enlargement with eyelid erythema and edema; serology positive for anti-EBV capsid antigen and negative for EBV nuclear antigen supporting recent EBV infection as the presumed etiology. No evidence of orbital involvement on exam; management with anti-inflammatory therapy (topical or oral corticosteroids) and observation for resolution.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute dacryoadenitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Epstein–Barr virus infection
Differential Diagnosis: Acute dacryoadenitis (infectious - viral e.g., EBV; bacterial); Idiopathic orbital inflammation (dacryoadenitis subtype); Lacrimal gland tumor (primary or metastatic)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: oral antibiotics (unspecified; e.g., penicillinase-resistant penicillin such as oxacillin suggested) and topical or oral corticosteroid medication
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for resolution of symptoms.
Patient Education: Follow-up for resolution of symptoms; disease is usually self-limited; consider anti-inflammatory therapy (topical or oral corticosteroids) if symptomatic; seek return care if worsening vision, increasing pain, fever, or lack of improvement.


CASE 95 — Orbital Tumors of Childhood
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 12-year-old boy with a 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid associated with redness and fullness of the temporal aspect of that lid producing an S-shaped deformity; on manual lid elevation there was marked injection and chemosis of the lateral bulbar conjunctiva with enlargement, erythema, and moderate tenderness of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland; mild regional lymph node enlargement; otherwise healthy without chronic medical disease; ocular motility normal and no intraocular inflammation.
History Narrative: A 12-year-old boy presented with a 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid. The boy was otherwise healthy without known chronic medical disease. On examination, there was redness and fullness of the temporal aspect of the left upper lid that created an “S-shaped” deformity of the eyelid. Manual elevation of the left upper eyelid revealed marked injection and chemosis of the bulbar conjunctiva laterally with enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland. There was moderate tenderness over the enlarged lacrimal gland. Mild enlargement of the regional lymph nodes was also noted. The remainder of the ocular examination, including motility, was normal. There was no evidence of intraocular inflammation.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Benign, encapsulated dermoid cyst located in the orbit (superotemporal/temporal upper lid region) causing S-shaped eyelid deformity, redness, fullness, and localized tenderness with lacrimal gland enlargement; no bony erosion or soft-tissue invasion on imaging.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid, redness, temporal fullness causing an “S-shaped” eyelid deformity, conjunctival injection and chemosis laterally, erythema and enlargement of lacrimal gland, moderate tenderness over lacrimal gland, mild regional lymph node enlargement.
Previous Episodes: None reported
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — no major functional impairment; daily activities essentially unaffected aside from cosmetic swelling and mild tenderness.
Systemic Symptoms: Otherwise healthy without known chronic medical disease
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Congenital developmental sequestration of surface ectoderm (embryologic inclusion during suture closure) — i.e., congenital lesion arising from epithelial inclusion at orbital suture lines; no specific acquired risk factors.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 116/72 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (age-appropriate)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left upper eyelid fullness and redness with an “S-shaped” deformity; temporal fullness of the left upper lid; moderate tenderness over enlarged lacrimal gland; mild regional lymph node enlargement.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Injection and chemosis of the bulbar conjunctiva laterally; otherwise no scleral abnormalities.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the left lacrimal gland with tenderness; chemosis of the adjacent bulbar conjunctiva and mild ipsilateral preauricular/regional lymphadenopathy.
External Exam – Orbit: Left upper eyelid temporal fullness with S-shaped deformity; marked enlargement, erythema, and tenderness of the palpebral lobe of the left lacrimal gland with lateral bulbar conjunctival injection and chemosis; mild regional lymphadenopathy; otherwise normal motility and no intraocular inflammation.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear, quiet anterior chamber with no intraocular inflammation
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens, no cataract or lenticular abnormality noted


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal to light in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light bilaterally
Extraocular Movements: Full motility (normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: No anterior chamber inflammation; marked conjunctival injection and chemosis laterally; enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland; otherwise normal anterior segment on slit-lamp exam.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (no peripheral retinal abnormalities)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 12-year-old boy with 1-week history of left upper eyelid swelling. Examination showed temporal fullness of the left upper lid with an S-shaped deformity, tenderness, enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland, conjunctival injection and chemosis laterally, and mild regional lymphadenopathy. Ocular motility and intraocular exam otherwise normal. Imaging may show an enlarged lacrimal gland or orbital mass without bony erosion. Clinical presentation most consistent with an orbital dermoid cyst; surgical excision via lid crease approach performed with complete removal and good postoperative outcome.
Primary Diagnosis: Dermoid cyst of the right orbit
Differential Diagnosis: Lacrimal gland inflammation (dacryoadenitis); Dermoid/epidermoid cyst; Lacrimal gland tumor (pleomorphic adenoma or adenoid cystic carcinoma); Orbital cellulitis
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: inflammatory response from traumatic rupture of the dermoid cyst


Plan
All Procedures: Surgical removal (complete excision) of a dermoid cyst via lid crease incision
Patient Education: A dermoid cyst is a benign (noncancerous) tumor that commonly occurs near the eye in children. It is caused by trapped skin tissues and may slowly enlarge. Because it can rupture and cause inflammation or become cosmetically noticeable, surgical removal is generally recommended; surgery is often done through a lid-crease incision to minimize visible scarring. There is no effective medication to remove a dermoid cyst. Risks of surgery and anesthesia (discuss timing in infants) and possible complications such as infection, bleeding, scarring, or recurrence should be reviewed. Follow-up after surgery is important to monitor healing and vision; if you notice redness, increasing swelling, fever, new pain, or changes in vision, contact the clinic promptly.


CASE 96 — Orbital Tumors in Adults
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressive proptosis of the left eye and changing refractive error (mass behind the left eye)
DURATION: Progressive since shortly after birth (approximately 8 months)
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "the mass had been present since shortly after birth"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: An 8‑month‑old healthy boy whose parents noted a mass over the right eye present shortly after birth with slow continual enlargement; no associated color change or size change with crying or Valsalva; otherwise healthy, full‑term birth, no other masses, no measurable proptosis, central fixation and following in each eye.
History Narrative: An 8-month-old healthy boy presented for evaluation of a mass over the right eye that had been present since shortly after birth with slow continual enlargement. He was born full-term after an uncomplicated pregnancy and had no other medical problems. Parents reported no change in color or size of the mass with crying or Valsalva. On exam he had central fixation and following in each eye; a moderately firm, well-circumscribed mass over the superotemporal right eyelid measuring approximately 20 x 10 x 5 mm, fixed to underlying tissues with intact overlying skin and no discoloration or ulceration. Careful palpation revealed no defect of the superior orbital rim. There was no measurable proptosis, both orbits were normal to retropulsion, and no other masses were palpated.
Symptom Onset: Shortly after birth
Symptom Duration: Since shortly after birth (present for 8 months)
Progression: Progressive proptosis
Characteristics: Well-circumscribed, dark-red encapsulated retrobulbar tumor causing progressive proptosis and changing refractive error (pressing on posterior globe)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: painless progressive proptosis, changing refractive error (myopia −2.00 D), choroidal striae
Impact on Daily Activities: None; no impact noted.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: None identified; N/A


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/75 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Moderately firm mass over the superotemporal right eyelid; overlying skin intact without discoloration or ulceration; no ptosis described.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Left proptosis with a palpable, well-circumscribed retrobulbar mass causing axial displacement of the globe; resistance to retropulsion not noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None
Extraocular Movements: Full motility in all cardinal positions of gaze; following and central fixation in each eye
Fundus Exam – Macula: choroidal striae
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 96-year-old patient with progressive left orbital proptosis due to a well-circumscribed, encapsulated retrobulbar mass on imaging consistent with a cavernous hemangioma. No medical therapy available; recommended and performed lateral orbitotomy with complete excision. Histopathology confirmed cavernous hemangioma. Postoperative course was good with stabilization of left eye refractive error at –2.00 D and gradual resolution of choroidal striae.
Primary Diagnosis: Probable cavernous hemangioma of the left orbit
Differential Diagnosis: Dermoid/epidermoid cyst; Capillary hemangioma; Lymphangioma; Rhabdomyosarcoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Procedures: Lateral orbitotomy with excision of retrobulbar cavernous hemangioma (tumor removed in toto)
Follow-Up Instructions: The patient did well postoperatively; monitor refractive error and ocular status with follow-up visits to confirm stability of the left eye (including refraction) and resolution of choroidal striae.


CASE 97 — Benign Tumors of the Eyelid
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: increasing painless prominence of the left eye (left eye proptosis)
DURATION: many years
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "increasing painless prominence of the left eye without associated diplopia."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old man referred for evaluation of progressive, painless prominence (proptosis) of the left eye over many years with increasing prominence and two recent changes in glasses prescription over the past year; no diplopia.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old man was referred for evaluation of proptosis of the left eye. He had no previous medical history. He reported increasing painless prominence of the left eye without associated diplopia. His glasses prescription had been changed twice in the past year. Examination showed visual acuity of 20/20 OU with a manifest refraction of –2.25 D OD and +1.00 D OS. Motility and pupil examination were within normal limits. The palpebral fissure was widened on the left side, and Hertel exophthalmometry disclosed 4 mm relative proptosis OS. There was increased resistance of the left globe to retropulsion OS. No bony defects or definite masses were palpated. There was no regional lymphadenopathy and no bruits were auscultated over the orbits. The anterior segment was quiet by slit-lamp examination; the bulbar conjunctiva was unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity. Dilated examination of the left fundus revealed choroidal striae of the posterior pole. The optic nerve was normal, and no hemorrhage or exudate was present. The right fundus was normal.
Symptom Duration: increasing over many years
Progression: Very slow growth over many years
Characteristics: Very slow growth over many years; lack of skin ulceration, scaling, madarosis, or telangiectasia; usually painless; benign adnexal origins (e.g., intradermal nevus, seborrheic keratosis, syringoma, hidrocystoma, pilomatrixoma); may be observed or excised; small defects repairable primarily or by secondary intention.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Observation or surgical excision; lesion may be observed (no treatment) or excised (office procedure under local anesthetic)
Associated Symptoms: increasing painless prominence of the left eye without diplopia; change in glasses prescription (decrease in myopia)
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: None; no prior treatments reported
Impact on Daily Activities: None — no impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported


Objective Exam

Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm

Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Palpebral fissure widened on the left; upper eyelid lesion (excisional biopsy) with no madarosis or eyelid margin distortion; otherwise lids and lashes unremarkable.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: The bulbar conjunctiva was unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal system without palpable masses or discharge (within normal limits)

Anterior Segment – Lens: clear lens; no cataract findings


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Within normal limits
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Within normal limits
Pupils – Reaction to Near: brisk
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: within normal limits
Extraocular Movements: Within normal limits
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The anterior segment was quiet by slit-lamp examination; the bulbar conjunctiva was unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: choroidal striae of the posterior pole
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina flat and without hemorrhage, exudate, or lesions (no peripheral abnormalities)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 44-year-old man with progressive, painless left-sided eyelid/orbital prominence and 4 mm relative proptosis OS. Examination: VA 20/20 OU, manifest refraction –2.25 D OD and +1.00 D OS, normal motility and pupils, widened left palpebral fissure, increased resistance to retropulsion OS, choroidal striae OS, otherwise normal anterior and dilated fundus exam. Orbital CT: well-circumscribed retrobulbar lesion with marginal contrast enhancement and no bony changes. Clinical and radiographic features consistent with a benign eyelid tumor, most likely intradermal nevus. Management: excisional biopsy of upper eyelid lesion performed; histopathology confirmed intradermal nevus. No further treatment indicated; patient doing well without recurrence.
Primary Diagnosis: Intradermal nevus
Differential Diagnosis: Intradermal nevus; seborrheic keratosis; squamous papilloma; hidrocystoma
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Procedures: Excisional biopsy of the upper eyelid lesion (office procedure under local anesthesia)
Follow-Up Instructions: The patient has done well without evidence of recurrence.
Patient Education: This lesion is a benign intradermal nevus of the eyelid. It grows slowly, is not cancerous, and often needs no treatment unless it causes symptoms or cosmetic concern. Options include observation with periodic checks or simple surgical excision under local anesthesia in the office; excision specimens are sent for pathology to confirm the diagnosis. If removed, healing is usually straightforward but care is taken to avoid eyelid margin distortion; report increasing redness, pain, drainage, vision changes, or signs of recurrence. Follow-up visits will monitor healing and check for recurrence.


CASE 98 — Malignant Tumors of the Eyelid
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left upper eyelid lesion, slowly enlarging over several months
DURATION: several years (slowly enlarging for the last several months)
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had a lesion on my left upper eyelid for several years and it has been slowly enlarging for the last several months."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old man with a left upper eyelid lesion present for several years, slowly enlarging over the past several months, without redness, bleeding, prior skin disease or skin cancer, otherwise healthy and on no medications.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old man presented with a left upper eyelid lesion present for several years that had been slowly enlarging for the last several months. He reported no associated redness or bleeding and no history of prior skin disease or skin cancer. He was in good health and took no medications. Ophthalmic examination was normal except for a smooth, dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion measuring approximately 10 × 8 × 5 mm involving the preseptal skin of the left upper lid. The overlying skin was intact without ulceration or telangiectasia. There was no regional lymphadenopathy and no other skin lesions on the head or neck.
Symptom Onset: several years
Symptom Duration: several years (slowly enlarging for the last several months)
Progression: Slowly enlarging over several months after years of stability
Characteristics: 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth, dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion on the left upper eyelid preseptal skin with intact overlying skin, no ulceration, telangiectasia, or madarosis, slowly enlarging over years.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: No associated redness or bleeding; no regional lymphadenopathy; lesion slowly enlarging over several years.
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: None; no impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: None identified
Risk Factors: Sun exposure; fair skin; increasing age (though patient is younger than usual); prior skin cancer or actinic damage; male sex; immunosuppression; genetic susceptibility (e.g., basal cell nevus syndrome)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen distance acuity: 20/20 (OD and OS, with habitual correction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped amelanotic skin lesion on the left upper eyelid preseptal skin; overlying skin intact without ulceration, telangiectasia, or madarosis; no eyelid margin distortion.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal except for a 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion on the left upper (preseptal) eyelid; overlying skin intact without ulceration or telangiectasia; no regional lymphadenopathy.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (brisk)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: briskly reactive to light bilaterally
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; extraocular movements intact and full.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: External exam notable for a 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion involving the preseptal skin of the left upper eyelid with intact overlying skin (no ulceration, telangiectasia) and otherwise normal ophthalmic/slit-lamp examination.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 32-year-old man with a slowly enlarging, smooth, amelanotic 10×8×5 mm dome-shaped lesion of the left upper eyelid (preseptal skin) without ulceration, telangiectasia, madarosis, or regional lymphadenopathy. Clinical differential favored benign nevus vs other benign epithelial or adnexal lesions, but high suspicion for basal cell carcinoma prompted excisional biopsy. Intraoperative frozen section confirmed nodular basal cell carcinoma with clear margins. Defect repaired with full-thickness skin graft; postoperative course satisfactory. Patient counseled regarding increased risk for new skin malignancies and placed under dermatologic surveillance.
Primary Diagnosis: Lesion of the right upper eyelid (medial canthal region); probable basal cell carcinoma
Differential Diagnosis: Intradermal nevus; Sebaceous (sweat gland) adnexal tumor (e.g., hidrocystoma, syringoma); Seborrheic keratosis; Basal cell carcinoma
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: None
All Procedures: Excisional biopsy of left upper eyelid lesion with intraoperative frozen section analysis confirming basal cell carcinoma and full-thickness skin graft repair of the defect (reconstruction).
Follow-Up Instructions: Educated about increased risk for new skin malignancies and ongoing dermatology surveillance; routine postoperative follow-up for surgical site monitoring and graft assessment.
Patient Education: He was educated about his increased risk for the development of new skin malignancies and advised to undergo continued dermatologic surveillance.
Referrals: Dermatology (for continued surveillance)
